TECHNICAL NOTES
There is no transliteration system which is fully satisfactory for rendering terms originally written in Cyrillac, whether they be Russian, Altayic, or Mongolian. The system used here is that of the U.S. Board of Geographic Names, largely because of its comprehensiveness, its easy accessibility, and its frequent usage. Exceptions have been made, however, with terms which have come into English with a non-conforming spelling, as in the case of "Kiev," "Evenk," and "Saimaly-Tash." I have made exceptions in other cases where the expected transliteration would cause confusion in English. A case in point is the name for the Ket high god, here referred to as Es. Anyone working in scholarly materials relating to Eurasian geography quickly realizes that the English designation,"Central Asia," can be rendered in Russian in two forms, "Srednyaya Aziya" and "Tsentral 'naya Aziya," each with its own separate meanings in terms of physical and cultural geography. In this study I have tried to adhere to established precedents in Soviet archaeological literature. "Middle Asia" refers to present-day Turkmeniya, Uzbekistan, Tadzhikistan, and Kirgiziya; "Central Asia" refers to Kazakhstan and Mongolia. Parts of South Siberia are sometimes included by scholars in northern Central Asia. I will keep these geographical distinctions separate, however, and include in "South Siberia" the regions of present-day Gorno-Altayskaya A.O., Tuvinskaya ASSR (Tuva), Krasnoyarskiy Kray south of Krasnoyarsk (Minusinsk Basin), the Prebaykal (the cultural zone to the west of Lake Baykal), and the Transbaykal (the cultural zone to the east and southeast of Lake Baykal). In this study, "Early Nomads" refer to a specific cultural group within the larger Scytho-Siberian culture. The Early Nomads inhabited the region of South Siberia and northern Central Asia and can be identified by the eighth century B.C. When I refer to early nomads (lower case), I use the term generally to mean the earliest nomadic peoples of Eurasia, i.e. those who emerged with a mobile and stock-dependent economy by the beginning of the first millennium B.C. The illustrative material included at the end of the text is intended to clarify textual references to particular image types and to particular examples of artistic traditions. These illustrations cannot attempt to indicate the full context of any of the images, whether they derive froP"
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



xx TECHNICAL NOTES
petroglyphs or from horse regalia. In many cases I have indicated published resources in which colored reproductions and more reproduc-• tions can be found. In the case of the rock carvings and paintings, I can only caution the reader that while the drawings included here are accurate, all these images would ideally be considered within the larger context of rock wall and adjacent images. Many of the drawings have been done from rubbings or from line drawings published in archaeological reports. In those cases, I have indicated their immediate source in the List of Plates.

CHAPTER ONE
THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS
About ten years ago I turned my attention to the image of the deer in the art of the early Eurasian nomads. The recurrence and transformations of that motif across the Eurasian Steppe and throughout most of the first millennium B.C. indicated its centrality within the nomads' symbolic systems. It therefore had to be equally central, I reasoned, to their mythic traditions. However, the more I attempted to fit those appearances into existing interpretative approaches, the more perplexing the image became. None of those theories seemed to fit. While they might have explained some aspects of the deer image, they offered only contradictions and even nonsense with regard to others. As I pursued the deer image both forward and backward in time and across South Siberia, Central Asia, and southern Ukraine and Russia, it gradually became apparent that the image was leading me in the direction of a mythic tradition substantially different from any which had hitherto been advanced. It was appearing too regularly within particular patterns to allow one to ascribe its formulation either to mere ornament or to convention. I began to understand this patterned ordering of images as symbolic systems which retraced in imagistic form the shifting outlines of a tradition of myth and belief. It became apparent that the image of the deer was the key to the understanding of the ecology of emerging and evolving cultures. Considered in this light, the image shifted from being a relatively static icon to being a sign of a process in which underlying belief systems were continually adapting to new circumstances. With these considerations in mind, I undertook the investigation which has led to this study.
A) THE PROBLEM
Across the taiga and tundra of Siberia, the outlines of ancient cultures are traced in thousands of rock carvings and paintings, in stony mounds, altars, and monumental stelae. These images and structures testify to mythic traditions reaching back millennia. They indicate the movement and change of culture, from steppe to taiga and tundra. Nowhere are
these inherited traditions richer than in South Siberia, the region com
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



2 THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS
prising present-day Gorno-Altayskaya Autonomous Oblast' (A.O.), Tuvinskaya A.O. (Tuva), Khakasskaya A.O., and the area around Lake Baykal. Within funerary and ritual complexes and from the dictionary of images carved and painted on rocky walls and burial slabs emerges a symbolic vocabulary which continues from the Neolithic down to the Bronze Age and from there into the age of the Early Nomads and the larger Scytho-Siberian world. That symbolic vocabulary is usually treated as a series of discrete objects and images dimly reflecting a mythic tradition related in some manner to what we know of modern shamanistic traditions or decipherable in terms of Indo-European myths. The discussion which follows takes another approach. It assumes that within the history of South Siberia images which recur together necessarily qualify the meaning invested in any single element; and that as those linked images are modified in space and time, they reveal the outlines of cultural change and the transformation of myth. Those linked images and their placement relative to each other, on rocky walls or within a funerary construct, function as symbolic systems. As deliberately organized elements of larger constructs, they carry a symbolic load reflective of a specific time but also reflective of the passage of time and the modification of myth. This book, therefore, is about the search for meaning in visual symbols where there are no texts to guide our inquiry. The symbols which will be the object of our attention are representational images of a particular kind: antlered deer, horned caprids and horses, panthers and tigers, wolves and bears, birds of prey and waterfowl, all rendered in forms both fantastic and realistic and frequently intertwined in acts of predation. These images were repeated over and over again in the art of the Scytho-Siberian culture-a culture which dominated the vast Eurasian Steppe and Forest-Steppe in the first millennium B.C. Individually and collectively, lodged within recurring patterns of relationships and traced over a period of several hundred years, these images effectively functioned as an elaborate semiotic system carried on the bodies of humans and their horses. The very continuity of images and of image relationships across the Scytho-Siberian world argues for the existence of a commonly-held mythic tradition as the basis of Scytho-Siberian artistic imagery. The imagery to which I refer constituted the essential elements of symbolic systems which continually reexpressed, reaffirmed, and reinterpreted a collective understanding of the nature of individual existence and of cosmic processes. At the center and source of those cosmic
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS 3
processes appears a particular figure-female and deer-like, an image which gradually shifted in appearance as it was contradicted by the inflow of new cultural elements and as it was transformed to accommodate and absorb those contradictions. In her most ancient incarnation, she took the form of a monumental and unantlered elk (New World moose). Over centuries and even millennia, her representational form shifted to an antlered animal and then to an antlered but thoroughly syncretic form, until finally her animal aspect was eclipsed in the last centures before our era. Where the deer had once been the source of life and death, there emerged a female figure, enthroned and honored; she, also, gradually lost her sway, however, and was slowly reduced in power and in privilege. If the animal images of the Eurasian Scytho-Siberians effectively functioned as visual.symbolic systems, the fact remains that we know those images only as bits and pieces embellishing objects retrieved from the broken remains of burials. We know them as disjointed, disconnected, even physically crushed elements, their artificial isolation from each other obstructing the enterprise of decoding the meaning of their forms and of their patterned interrelationships. Reconstructing the complex arrangements of animal prey and predators is not simply a matter of fitting together pieces on the basis of a visually satisfying pattern or according to principles drawn by analogy from other, neighboring cultures. The reconstruction of symbolic systems undertaken here assumes that even in the absence of written texts, regularly recurring patterns of imagery over time reveal a logic and an evolving signification. Lacking written maps by which to trace the sense of the patterns, meaning may still be fathomed in the persistence and change of images and in the relationships of one visual image to another within the symbolic systems to which they are bound. Meaning thus emerges as an organic process with its own impulses toward variation, transformation, ambivalent negation, and resolution. As surely as within cultures clarified by writing, the load carried in pre-literate cultures by images joined together into patterned order is rooted in history and qualified by the mythic traditions and assumptions of the past. Thus the reconstruction of symbolic systems involves a subtle dialogue between cultural history revealed through archaeologically and ethnographically retrieved materials and the deciphering of the import of change within visual symbolic systems. On one level, then, this book is about the reconstruction of symbolic systems. Central to that inquiry is the reconstruction of meaning lodged
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



4 THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS
in a single image, that of the deer of the Early Nomads of South Siberia and thus of one of the principle Scytho-Siberian cultures. The image of antlered animal elaborated in gold, wood, felt, fur, and stone was at the very heart of the mass of images with which the Early Nomads embellished their dead and the horses of the dead in preparation for the journey to another realm. The deer was not, certainly, the only significant image within the art of the Early Nomads. Like their cousins across the Eurasian Steppe-the Scythians, the Sakas, the Tagar peoples, or those who inhabited the grasslands of Mongolia-the Early Nomads bracketed the image of the deer with feline and wolf-like predators, with eagles and griffinated eagles. They dissolved the antlers of the deer into bird heads, transformed its head into that of a great, beaked animal, or even melted its body into a half-deer, half horse shape. At times they simply dismissed the image, substituting that of an elk or of a horned caprid: sheep, or goat. Throughout that first millennium B.C. and throughout the nomadic hegemony of Eurasia, however, the art of the Early Nomads was dominated by the image of the deer. Isolated or as a part of complex zoomorphic references, the image of the deer is the ultimate key to understanding Early Nomadic culture and that of the larger ScythoSiberian world. The image of the deer of the Early Nomads did not spring fresh and whole with the appearance of these people. It was rooted deep in the prehistory of South Siberia, going back as far as the Neolithic period. When t11e Early Nomads inherited the motif, it had already undergone enormous iconic and symbolic shifts. It had become laden with the interwoven symbolism of cultures in fundamental economic transition. Having emerged within a forest-bound hunting economy and having adapted to the requirements of a new Bronze Age pastoralism, the motif was finally incorporated into the stock dependent economy of the early Iron Age. By then the deer image had come to function as an elaborate text of condensed beliefs and myths. In its articulation in gold, bronze, or carved wood and in its gradual disintegration and replacement, the deer image serves as a key to reading history and myth. This book, then, cannot only be about the deer image or about the reconstruction of the Early Nomads' symbolic systems. It is necessarily about the prehistory of South Siberia as well and about mythic traditions as they are revealed in petroglyphs, in ritual and funerary structures, and in monolithic stone images.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS 5
B. THE BACKGROUND: PEOPLE, LAND, AND CULTURES
The Early Nomads were the South Siberian branch of the ScythoSiberian culture. In the first millennium B.C., this culture controlled the vast steppe and mountainous regions between China to the east, Persia and Greece to the west, and north to the edge of the Siberian Taiga. The Early Nomads were centered in the region of present-day GornoAltayskaya A.O.. The designation, "Early Nomads," however, has often been used to include all the contemporary nomadic and semi-nomadic peoples of South Siberia. The names by which this broad cultural grouping was divided varies depending on the scholarly approach to the archaeology of South Siberia between the eighth and first centuries B.C. Thus, the Early Nomadic culture of the northern Allay Mountains is frequently divided into the Mayemir (seventh - sixth centuries) and Pazyryk (sixth - first centuries) cultures. 1 The contemporary culture of the Minusinsk Basin is known as the Tagar culture. It emerged at some time in the first third of the first millennium B.C. and was succeeded at the end of the millennium by the Tashtyk culture (Kiselev 1951; Kyzlasov 1960, 1979). Within Tuva, those cultures within the Yenisey drainage and contemporary with the Tagar and Pazyryk cultures are frequently referred to as Aldi-Bel' and Saglin while that in the more southern part of the region and extending into present-day northern Mongolia is usually known as Uyuk (Grach 1980; Volkov 1967). 2 For the sake of clarity, the general term, Early Nomads, will be used throughout this book to refer to the Scytho-Siberians located in South Siberia and in parts of northern Mongolia, while regional cultural designations will be used as appropriate. By virtue of their economy and art, the Early Nomads were close cousins of the Sakas of Kazakhstan, who with their successors, the Wusun, dominated eastern Kazakhstan between the eighth and first centuries B.C (K.A. Akishev and Kushayev 1963; K.A. Akishev 1973, 1978; Bernshtam 1952; Vishnevskaya 1973). Further to the west, the Massagetae controlled the region between the Caspian and Aral seas. Although the archaeological picture of these people is unclear, Herodotus has left a vivid account of how under the leadership of their queen, Tomyris, the Massagetae fought and defeated the armies of Cyrus of Persia and in the process killed the ruler himself ( Herodotus [1954]: Bk. I). To the north
1 Useful resources include Kiselev 1951; Jettmar 1967; Rudenko 1960; Gryaznov 1969. 2 The Uyuk culture has been called Kazylgan by Vaynshteyn (1966).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



6 THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS
of the Massagetae, the Sauromatians dominated the steppe between the Volga and Ural mountains from the middle of the millennium to its end (Smirnov 1964). The most western of the Early Nomads' relatives and those most familiar to the modern observer were the Scythians. They are believed to have arrived in the lands of the Caucasus, the Crimea, and the rich steppe north of the Black Sea by the sixth century B.C. The Scythians claimed a major portion of The Histories of Herodotus by driving the armies of Darius out of their territory. By virtue of that account and because of the pictorial realism of the goldwork found in Scythian burials, we have a clearer picture of these people than of any of their Scytho-Siberian cousins. Perhaps because of that relative clarity, there is a frequent tendency to apply the specific term "Scythian" to all the related groups across the Eurasian Steppe. Throughout this book, however, the term Scythian will be used only in reference to those ScythoSiberians who inhabited the Crimea and the steppe region north and east of the Black Sea-that region formerly known as "Scythia" (Artamonov 1969; Minns [1913] 1971; Rostovtsev 1922). In the late years of the first millennium B.C., the successors to the Early Nomads and Sakas served as the vehicles for the transformation of Eurasian nomadism into statehood. The Xiongnu of Mongolia gathered an array of nomadic peoples together into a confederation which became the major geopolitical opponent of the Chinese Han Dynasty. In the same period, the second and first centuries B .C., the Yuezhi established hegemony over the vital steppe region west of the borders of Han China and extending through Bactria to the borders of India. Indeed, after their conversion to Buddhism, the Yuezhi-by then known as the Kushans-proceeded to conquer India and to establish the great Kushan Dynasty. Despite these political transformations and despite significant distinctions in language and physical anthropology between the Xiongnu and their Early Nomadic relatives, they as well as the Yuezhi/Kushans may properly be considered to have belonged to the larger Scytho-• Siberian culture. All the members of this larger grouping shared to varying degree a dependency on herd animals-primarily sheep and cattle, secondarily horses, goats, yaks, reindeer, and camels, according to the specific conditions of any given region. All-with the exception., perhaps, of the Tagar culture-were skillful horsemen and known for their skill by the settled peoples to the south. Cultural cohesion throughout the Scytho-Siberian world is attested to by a common weapon typology involving daggers, knives, short and long swords, the three••
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS 7
winged arrowhead, a modified form ofgorytus, and three-holed cheekpieces and jointed canon (horse bits). No less common across the ScythoSiberian world was the mirror, either of the Chinese type with a central knob handle, or of the long-handled Greek type. 3 Finally, the art of all the Scytho-Siberian groups was founded upon a particular style and upon a particular zoomorphic vocabulary which affirm a certain commonality of taste and of mythic traditions. Across the Eurasian Steppe, by varying degree and depending on climate and the character of the land, the Scytho-Siberians added to their pastoral economy a reliance on the cultivation of grains as well as a reliance on hunting and fishing. Chance finds and burials have revealed tools and weapons appropriate to these economies. The remains of furs, woven fabrics, felt, and grains have also been found within those Altay burials which were flooded and frozen. The mixed economic system indicates that the term generally used to describe these people, "nomads," is not really accurate. While groups like the Scythians had presumably traveled a great distance from a homeland in South Siberia or Central Asia, once they arrived in the region of the Black Sea they turned to the cultivation of grain as well as to fishing and stockraising. Herodotus refers to the "agricultural Scythian tribes, growing grain not for food but for export..." He distinguishes them from Alizones and a "Graeco-Scythian tribe" called the Callipidae, in the region west of the mouth of the Dnieper. Both these groups "resemble the Scythians in their way oflife, and also grow grain for food, as well as onions, leeks, lentils, and millet" (Herodotus [1954]: 248). Most of the other Scytho-Siberians practised transhumance rather than true nomadizing. The nomads living in proximity to the Pamir, Tyan-Shan, Altay, and Sayan mountain ranges probably lived as people do today in those regions. During the winter months they inhabited relatively stable settlements in protected valleys. They may well have lived in solid log and plank structures such as those preserved in the case of the Pazyryk burials (Rudenko 1970). In spring, the nomads would begin to move their animals up the slopes to alpine pastures where they would stay until the beginning of fall. During that period of the year, they almost certainly lived in portable yurt-like structures. In this respect, the lives of the Early Nomads and of the Sakas may have closely resembled those described for modern pastoralists of
3 Within the archaeological sites of the South Siberian and northern Mongolian nomadic cultures have been found several object types for which there are known Chinese parallels. These include, for example, a bronze yoke-shaped object similar to the Chinese pang but of unknown function.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



8 THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS
the Altay, Tuva (Vaynshteyn 1980), and Mongolia. The petroglyphs at the Great Boyar cliff from the Minusinsk Basin, dating to the late Tagar or Tashtyk culture, include scenes of herdsmen, yurt-like structures, and more solid log structures (Devlet 1976). Among almost all the Scytho-Siberian groups, burial within a wooden chamber under a stone or earthen barrow (kurgan) was the general rule, although even here one finds variations which may reflect the time of year of death or the nature of the soil in a specific area. In Tuva, for example, the frequent appearance of simple burials above ground but under a stone mound suggests that death took place when the ground was frozen. The frozen burials at Pazyryk demonstrate how the burial chamber could be as large, sturdy, and elaborately furnished as a regular dwelling. While these burials certainly refer to the way of life of wealthy members of Scytho-Siberian society, they also point to a general truth: that the Scytho-Siberians went to death within structures which replicated the dwellings of the living and were equipped with the utilitarian objects, the dress, the food, the furniture and even the horses they would presumably need in another world. The composition of burials also indicates a belief that in some manner the realm to which the dead departed must mirror that of the living. Although individual burials were common, it was equally common for several individuals to be laid out in the same burial chamber, together with their ornaments, their tools, their weapons, and their household vessels. The number of paired burials is particularly striking. Although the five large barrows at Pazyryk were plundered and some of the bodies may have been removed, it seems as if almost all those burials were originally paired, including adult males and females. The Ulandryk burials, also, were often if not always paired (Kubarev 1987a). In other cases one finds burials which seem to reflect nuclear families or lineages .. In the Saglin culture of Tuva, burial chambers might contain from three: to five bodies. The combination of adults and children suggest the members of a family or limited lineage group. Presumably the buria!l chamber was only temporarily covered until a whole family unit, or a part thereof, had been laid to rest (Grach 1980). Similarly reflective of possible family groupings are the burials in the Ulangom cemetery of western Mongolia, dated between the fifth and third centuries B.C. Here, also, it is probable that the bodies of specific lineage groups would be gradually added over time (Novgorodova 1989: 257-315). The repeated appearance of such paired or multiple burials raises the possibility that the "secondary" individual was killed in order to be
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS 9
buried with the primary individual. In the case of double or triple burials involving mixed sexes, the general assumption has tended to be that it must have been the male who died first and then was buried with the body of the murdered female. This has been, for example, the assumption regarding the paired burials at Pazyryk: that the male must have died first and been buried with his consort as well as with his finest horses and belongings. Such pairing was not necessarily dependent, however, on the murder of one individual. It could have been acheived by the same methods which appear more clearly in the Saglin or Tagar burials. There the burial chambers were loosely closed or covered until they had been filled. In some Tagar multiple burials, the bones of the dead had evidently been cleaned and the skeletons disarticulated in order to allow the deposition of many remains in one crypt over an extended period of time (Kiselev 1951; Martynov 1979). Even in such a case as that of the eighth century B.C. burial at Arzhan, Tuva, where several individuals were buried, self-immolation rather than sacrifice may have been the case (Gryaznov 1980: 45-46). Among the Scythians, the principal individuals among the dead seem to have been connected in some significant manner: a male and female pair or even a number offemales suggestive of a particular group of priestesses. Here, as among the Early Nomads, it is possible that the burials would be incompletely closed until they could be filled with the bodies of related individuals. Occasionally the arrangement and combination of bodies strongly points to the killing of retainers at the time of the death of the master or mistress. A case in point may be found in the great "royal" burials at Chertomlyk and Kul Oba. In the case of paired burials of males and females-as at Kul Oba-the general assumption has been that the woman as wife or concubine was killed at the time of her husband's death, presumably to accompany him to the next world. But absent the traces of mortal wounds, one may not conclude that the males died earlier than did their female companions. Indeed, barring death in warfare (which has certainly been attested to, as in the cemetery of Ulangom), the rigors of childbirth would have dictated that females could well have died earlier than males. To date, no one has yet proposed that the male of the pair would be sacrificed at the time of the female's death.4
4 Despite the Scytho-Siberians' reputation in the non-nomadic world for ferocity, there are only a few instances which seem to refer to human sacrifice accompanying the primary dead. This has been attested, for example, in a few collective burials of the Tagar culture (Martynov 1979).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



10 THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS
Throughout the world of the Scytho-Siberians, the most spectacular indicators of cultural continuity may be found in habits of dress and ornamentation. In the frozen Altay burials of Pazyryk, in the gold ·representations of Scythians from Scythian burials in the South Russian Steppe (e.g., Piotrovsky 1987: pis. 118, 126, 128-129, 184-187), in the intact Saka burial at Issyk (K. A. Akishev 1978), and in the late Yuezhi burials at Tillya-Tepe (Sarianidi 1985, 1989) a mode of dress which was common throughout the nomadic world of the Eurasian Steppe is attested to. Males wore clothing appropriate for horse-back riding: soft, close-fitting pants and boots and a belted short jacket. Their headware usually took the form of a soft, peaked hood. Women seem to have worn long robes, close-fitting over the bodice, and tall headdresses of somewhat varying shape, although in some instances their hair was piled up on their heads on a bone framework and ornamented. The men and women buried at Issyk and Tillya-Tepe confirm what has been repeatedly indicated in the gold representations of Scythians: that the clothing of the Scythe-Siberians was covered with sewn-on gold plaques ornamented with the images of animals, of people, or of purely decorative elements. The frozen burials at Ulandryk in Gorno-Altayskaya A.O. further testify that at least in death even non-aristocratic members of Early Nomadic society wore their hair in an elaborate mounding, secured by gold covered carved ornaments and hairpins (Kubarev 1987a). The most obvious and distinctive common denominator of ScythoSiberian culture, however, is its so-called "animal style," a tradition of symbolic representation and ornamentation in which the zoomorphic image is paramount. In burials early disrupted by robbers, such as that. at Arzhan, remaining bronze objects include a magnificent coiled feline (pl. Ila) and poletops in the shape of powerful, stylized mountain sheep. In the plundered burial at Tuekta in the northern Altay, Rudenko found an abundance of beautiful wooden carvings of felines, eagle-griffins, elk, and plant-like forms (e.g., pl. Ilb - e). Originally these images were probably covered with gold. They ornamented horse bridles, chestguards,. and saddles. The five large barrows of Pazyryk reaffirm the domination of zoomorphic representations. Despite the fact that all five burials had been plundered shortly after they were completed, the remains of horse regalia, of human dress and ornament, and of the furnishings of the burial chambers themselves were extensive. Shabracks were also ornamented with animal imagery. In some cases, the horses carried masks or crests. The men and women buried at Pazyryk wore robes and headdresses of fur, leather, felt, and gold, all ornamented with deer heads, saiga heads,
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS 11
and birds. The jewelry they wore was most certainly torn from their bodies by the individuals who broke into the burials shortly after they were sealed. That jewelry must have carried abundant zoomorphic references. It is possible that the objects taken by the robbers were similar in size and richness to the great gold plaques preserved in the Siberian Treasure of Peter the Great (Rudenko 1962). But the wealth found in the Pazyryk and Scythian burials was shared to some extent by commoners. Burial remains at Ulandryk and Tashanta, in eastern GornoAltayskaya A.O., indicate that even these members of Early Nomadic society wore neck bands and armlets which were painted or covered with gold and which carried symmetrically arranged images of deer, caprids, birds, and feline or wolf-like predators. Within Early Nomadic burials, simple furnishings were also ornamented with zoomorphic elements: a table, for example, from Pazyryk 2 was fitted with legs carved in the shape of rampant feline caryatids (Rudenko 1970: pis .. 50, 51). At Pazyryk and at the later Early Nomadic burial of Bashadar, even the coffins were embellished with animal imagery. The Bashadar coffin is especially interesting in this respect. It was carved out of a single larch log and its outer sides were carved with a row of standing tigers distinguished by their huge jaws and claws. Under the feet of some of the tigers and in proximity to their jaws are the images of sheep, boar, and saiga antelope or female elk, crushed or twisted under the weight of the cats ( Rudenko 1960: fig. 21). The impression of humans wrapped in animal references wrought in gold is fully confirmed by the single intact burial of Issyk in the Tyan-Shan Mountains, and the gold jewelry and gold swords of Scythian burials confirm that even on the borders of the Hellenic world, animal imagery was considerably more important than human or foliate references down to the fourth century B.C. Crowning and centering this zoomorphic pantheon were images of deer, rams, coiled or crouched felines, birds of prey, and wolves. Juxtaposed and intertwined, these images were most often organized to represent scenes of animal predation: wolves, felines, or birds as predators, and deer, caprids, and horses as prey. Varying in specific elements of style, the repetition of these images on clothing, shields, weapons, utilitarian household objects and horse regalia asserts more clearly than any other cultural tradition a common belief system and set of values binding the Scytho-Siberian world from the borders of China to the borders of Persia and Greece. The apparently rootless, restless, horseborn life of the Scytho-Siberians allowed ancient observers such as Herodotus and Sima Qian to conclude that they were innately ferocious,
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



12 THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS
predatory barbarians; but for the modern observer, it is probably the representations of animals and of animal predation which perpetuated that reputation and elevated it into myth. The general continuity of Scytho-Siberian culture across the Eurasian Steppe may also suggest a continuity of ethnicity, but that conclusion would be incorrect. Both contemporaneous representations and craniological evidence indicate that the Scythians and Sakas were Europoid in racial stock; it is generally assumed, also, that they were Iranian in language.5 This assumption is supported by the numerous representations of nomads which have come out of the Scythian burials, as well as by representations of nomads in such Central Asian materials as the socalled Treasure of the Oxus (Dalton 1964). On the fine gold cup from Kul Oba, for example, or in the figures of Scythians adorning the Tolstaya Mogila pectoral, the Scythians represented in attentive interaction define the male physical type one finds on materials from the royal burials (Piotrovsky 1987: pis. 121, 184-187). The men were stocky and powerfully built. Their hair was long and often wavy, their beards full. They seem to have had prominent, straight noses and deep-set eyes. Unfortunately, no such visually descriptive images of Scythian women are available. The Sakas seem to have been similar in appearance to the Scythian men, although with perhaps more prominent noses (cf. Jettmar 1967: pl. 30). The picture of the Early Nomads is much less clear; or, rather, the population of South Siberia seems to have been considerably more mixed in racial stock. There are only a few human representations which have been preserved and almost all come from the Pazyryk burials. The most famous is certainly that of the fine horseman repeated several times across the large felt hanging from Pazyryk 5 (pl. Via). The individual has short curly black hair, a fine curling mustache, and a prominent nose. In general, the physical type represented here in stylized form is similar to some of the faces preserved on plaques which had ornamented the tenth bridle of Pazyryk 1 (Rudenko 1970: pis. 91-92) and to the figures found on the anecdotal plaques from the Siberian Treasure of Peter the Great (pl. VIIb). All the faces on the carved plaques have thick, curly hair and beards. Beyond that, the facial types vary between several which are wide and flat and one which seems to be more Europoid in type. This variation in facial type accords with the apparent variation in craniologi
5 But regarding the problems of reaching conclusions regarding the physical anthropology of the Scythians and Sakas, see Alekseyev and Gochman [Gokhman] 1989: 206-241.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS 13
cal indications from Pazyryk and other Altay barrows of the early Iron Age. Although there was a strong Europoid element in the population of the Early Nomads, there appears, also, to have been a clear Mongoloid element (Rudenko 1970: 45-53). Evidence for a mixed population has been confirmed in broader studies of early Iron Age materials from Siberia and Mongolia. These studies indicate that during the ScythoSiberian period, Mongoloid peoples spread to the west and mixed with the dominant Europoid type in South Siberia and Central Asia (Alekseyev and Gochman [Gokhman] 1983: 37-60). The very complexity and mixing of populations evident in craniological studies to date urges us to exercise extreme caution in assuming linkages between physical type, language, and culture. South Siberian in origin, the Early Nomads emerged out of a succession of cultural traditions which reached back through the Bronze Age to the Neolithic period. Those traditions were not simply Siberian in derivation: there is overwhelming evidence that early Iron Age South Siberia was the culmination of a long-evolving and complex interweaving of autochthonous Siberian cultures with other cultures which came into South Siberia from northern Central Asia, from the west and from the southwest. For over two thousand years these influxes resulted in the recurring mixing of Europoid and Mongoloid racial stocks and of their cultures. Furthermore, most of the cultures which preceded the Early Nomads in South Siberia may be identified with a distinctive tradition of artistic expression. It is this cumulative tradition which must form the basis of any inquiry into the reconstruction of the Early Nomads' symbolic systems. The homeland of the Early Nomads may be visualized as a triad of shallow bowls joined at the Sayan-Altay Ridge. One bowl slopes northwards into the Minusinsk Valley, bisected by the northwardflowing Yenisey River. Another falls to the west of the first, cradled by the Sayan Ridge on its east, by the high Altay Mountains on its south, and open on the north to the plain of Central Siberia. The third bowl slopes southeast to encompass northern Mongolia, abutted on the east by the valley of the Selenge River and its Transbaykal extension, the Selenga. Over the millennia, cultures from the north, southeast, southwest, and west were continually sliding into those bowls in overlapping layers, jostling others up and over the sides. Ultimately it was the pastoral nomadic culture of the Early Nomads which emerged as the dominant tradition, leaving its imprint of stock-dependency north and south of the Altay-Sayan Ridge and throughout northern Mongolia. The
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



14 THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS
significance of this region in the history of Bronze and Iron Age Eurasia is underlined when one recalls that the grasslands of Mongolia also spawned the successors to the Early Nomads, the Turkic and Mongoll cultures of the first and early second millennia A.D. Although they are outside the scope of this study, both cultures again demonstrate how the South Siberian-Mongolian region functioned for thousands of years as a cultural vortex and eruptive point, within which traditions from Siberia's forest-bound North met and merged with traditions moving out from the vast Central Asian Steppe of grass and desert. 6 The images exploited by the Early Nomads were rooted in an ancient pantheon first carved and painted on the cliffs over the great Siberian rivers. This tradition will be examined in more detail in Chapter IV. It was concentrated in the region of the upper Tom', Yenisey, Angara, and Lena rivers with extensions south into the Katun' Valley of GornoAltayskaya A.O. Here it should be noted that its most important image was that of the female elk: a figure often represented in monumental size, or at least with a realism and power which was monumental in aspect. During the Baykal Neolithic period a tradition of locating sacrificial sites at the base of cliffs, in what are believed to be sacred places, was also established (Okladnikov 1950; Tivanenko 1989). The Early Nomads also inherited an ancient tradition of metalworking, one which went back to the earliest copper-working culture of South Siberia, the Afanasevo. Dated between 2500 - 1500 B.C., the Afanasevo culture was centered in the Minusinsk Valley, with significant extensions southwest to the Altay and west to the steppes north of the Aral Sea and as far as the northeast shores of the Caspian. Because the Afanasevo people belonged to a Europoid racial type, there has been a predisposition to associate them with a Proto-lndo-European tradition (Gryaznov 1969: 51; Kyzlasov 1986). The investigations of physical anthropologists over the last few decades, however, should give one pause: throughout its South Siberian range, the Afanasevo culture appears to have been carried by a population of mixed Europoid and Mongoloid stock (Alekseyev and Gochman [Gokhman] 1983: 33). Afanasevo was a culture in transition from a hunting economy to a pastoral economy. The population lived in small groups and depended on wild animals, fish, and stock animals such as cattle, sheep, goats, and
6 The region described here is somewhat broader than that included in the two best known sources on the pre- and early history of South Siberia, those of Gryaznov (1969: 9-13) and Kiselev (1951). The latter, however, briefly includes Central Asia in his introduction to a consideration of the "Hunnish-Sarmatian" period.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS 15
horses for meat. Afanasevo burial structures seem to have established the beginnings of the South Siberian tradition of using logs, stone slabs, and mounds to construct the burial chamber and to mark it on the surface of the ground. Within the Minusinsk Valley, Afanasevo graves took the form of subterranean pits roofed by a layer of logs and surmounted by an earthen mound sometimes covered with a layer of stone slabs. The burials would frequently contain several individuals as well as simple furnishings. A tall wall of slabs would be used to surround the mound. In the Altay, a similar treatment of the surface would occur, but the burial pit would frequently be constructed from carefully worked thin slabs of stone and was intended for individual burials, several of which would be marked on the surface by a single mound (Gryaznov 1969: 46-48; Kubarev 1988). The artistic legacy of the Afanasevo culture thus includes the building of surface mounds faced in some manner by stones or slabs. Beyond this, however, that legacy is confused by a number of issues contingent on as-yet unascertainable theories: the date of great carved monoliths found within the Minusinsk Basin; the dating of the earliest images of carts found on those monoliths; the question of the exact relationship of the Afanasevo culture to the Proto-Indo-European Yamna culture of western Siberia. Within the Minusinsk Basin, the Okunev culture succeeded the Afanasevo culture by the early second millennium B.C. In contrast to the Afanasevo, the Okunev people were Mongoloid of the Central Asian type (Gryaznov 1969: 51). Burial pits were made of carefully sized stone slabs laid horizontally to build up the walls. They were roofed at ground level by other stone slabs. The most significant art form associated with the Okunev culture are the huge monoliths carved with a variety of quasianthropomorphic images and animal masks (Vadetskaya, Leont'ev, and Maksimenkov 1980).7 These sculptured stones will be discussed later in this book. For the moment it may be said that within a series of cultures which used stone extensively in the construction of mortuary and ritual sites, the Minusinsk stones are remarkable for being the earliest carved monoliths in South Siberia. Furthermore, the images on their facesboth two and three-dimensional-establish a tradition of representing masks which lie between the animal and the human in aspect.
7 There is still no general agreement regarding the dating of these monumental sculptures, although most scholars do accept the Okunev date. Kiselev assigned them to the Karasuk period (Kiselev 1951). Gryaznov, also, first believed they were Karasuk in date, but later put them back to the Andronovo (Gryaznov 1950a). Most recently, Kyzlasov has made a forceful argument for assigning them to the culture he calls Tazmin, preceeding both Afanasevo and Okunev in the Minusinsk Basin (Kyzlasov 1986).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



16 THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS
By the middle of the second millennium, the Okunev culture in the Minusinsk Basin was confronted by a new and fully Bronze Age culture, the Andronovo. Most scholars consider the Andronovo to have been the eastern extension of the Timber-grave (Shrubnaya) culture, which was centered in the lower Volga Basin and stretched southwest from there to the shores of the Black Sea. 8 The Timber-grave and Andronovo cultures are also related to the Tazabag'jab culture located east and south of the Aral Sea (Chernikov 1960). Whether or not the center of Andronovo was in the region of Lake Zaysan, in eastern Kazakhstan, its eastern boundary was in the area of the upper Yenisey. Andronovo sites have been found extensively between the Yenisey and the Ob rivers, but none have been found either in Tuva or in Gorno-Altayskaya A.O. The high level of Andronovo bronze technology has been indicated by knives, awls, spear heads, celts and needles found in Andronovo graves. Depending on the abundance of pasture in any particular region, this culture practiced both cultivation and stock-breeding. Sheep and cattle seem to have been particularly important, with sheep, cattle, and horses used for food as well as for the sacrificial joints of meat placed in the graves of the dead. The great majority of Andronovo burials appear to have been individual. Typically, interment would be in stone chambers in the center of a circular or rectangular area, bordered by a stone "wall." More rarely in the Yenisey region but commonly in the region of the Ob, burial chambers were constructed of stout logs. The chambers would be covered by an earthen mound bordered by stones (Maksimenkov 1978). In the use of wooden chambers and mounds, the Andronovo is obviously related to the Timber-grave culture of Eastern Europe, as well as to the later Scytho-Siberian culture. On the other hand, the cart burials associated with Timber-grave sites west and east of the Urals (Piggott 1975) are not repeated in any known Andronovo burials: neither the imprint of carts and wheels nor the bronze remains of wheels and axles have been attested to. Only to the west, in the region between the Syr-Dar'ya and Amu-Dar' ya and in the region north of the Aral Sea, where the Andronovo culture mixed with the Timber-Grave culture to the west and with the Abeshevo and Gorbunovo cultures in the Urals, do finds of bone psalia indicate the beginnings of the use of horses for riding and hauling
8 The Timber-grave people are considered by Gimbutas and others to have been "prot<~Scythians," predecessors of the Scythians of the early Iron Age (Gimbutas 1965: 576-577). By contrast, Terenozhkin believes that the Scythians were not related to the Timber-grave culture; rather, they arrived in the Pontic region from somewhere "in the depths of Asia," sometime around the eighth century B.C., bringing with them the bronze and iron implements and weapons from the Karasuk tradition (Terenozhkin 1975).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS 17
sometime between the twelfth and ninth centuries B.C. (Chernikov 1960). The succession of Afanasevo and Okunev cultures in the Minusinsk Basin functioned as an alternating overlay of Europoid and Mongoloid populations. This pattern of ethnic overlay seems to have characterized much of the prehistory of South Siberia and northern Central Asia. It was repeated when the Andronovo culture was displaced by the Karasuk culture in the second half of the second millennium B.C. As in the case of the Okunev culture, the center ofKarasuk was located in the Minusinsk and Kuznetsk valleys, with extensions indicated into Mongolia and as far as the region of the Syr Dar'ya in south-central Kazakhstan. The Karasuk culture is most noted for its bronze technology, with the sophisticated shaping of knives and daggers and the elaboration of handles and butts displaying an invigorated sense of ornament. Many of these objects display incised and openwork motifs reminiscent of the geometric decor on Andronovo pottery. A number of weapons are crowned by horse or deer-headed terminals, or with the representation of standing animals. On the basis of these weapon types and their ornamentation, Kiselev has argued that the origins of the Karasuk culture lay somewhere in the south, in association with the Northern Bronze culture contemporary with, but distinguished from, the culture of Shang China (Kiselev 1951; Lin 1982).9 On the other hand, significant parallelism can also be found between the zoomorphic and geometric ornamentation of Karasuk bronze daggers and knives and those of the Sejma-Turbino bronze industry (ca. 1500- 800 B.C.) in the North Russian region of the Kama Basin (Gimbutas 1965; Sulimirski 1970a). Similarities in tool and weapon typology and in zoomorphic and geometric ornamentation connecting such farflung regions as the northern Urals, South Siberia, and northern China argue for vital trade and for the movement of peoples carrying bronze technology and their artistic taste throughout the second half of the second millennium B.C. Karasuk mortuary structures and ritual varied according to local culture, but typically they took the form of a stone-lined or simple earthen pit, the roof of which was level with the surface of the ground and then covered by a small mound. Several burials would be found within a usually rectangular enclosure formed by upright stone slabs. In
9 Gryaznov ignores the obvious relationship between the bronzes of the Karasuk and Northern Bronze culture, as well as the related issue of the derivation of the Karasuk people. Sulimirski, following Chlenova, finds the argument for Karasuk origins in the region of the Tyan-Shan to be convincing (Sulimirski 1970a: 308).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



18 THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS
the large cemetery of Malye Kopeny 3 in the Minusinsk Valley, dated to the late Karasuk period, a number of burial chambers were located individually within the center of round enclosures, at one side of which might be an "entrance" (Zyablin 1977). In so far as the usually disrupted burials indicate, furnishings were modest, limited to a few joints of meat (sheep, cattle, rarely horses), a single pottery vessel, and a bronze knife. Such furnishings were accorded all dead, male and female, adult and children. The high quality of the rounded and ornamented Karasuk pottery suggested to Kiselev that, like the Andronovo, the Karasuk people were stock-breeders who moved within only a relatively circumscribed area (Kiselev 1951: 159). 10 The general aspect of Karasuk society is that of a peaceful, stock-dependent culture, in which there are almost no signs either of hunting tools or of weapons. Sheep were the most frequent source of meat, cattle were used for traction and possibly functioned prominently in rituals. Horses were used only for food (Zyablin 1977: 33-38; Kiselev 1951: 161-170). There is no clear division between the Karasuk culture and its successor in the Minusinsk Basin, the Tagar culture. By the eighth century B.C., variations in burial ritual, in bronze technology, and in weapon typology indicate the emergence of that culture, contemporaneous with and artistically related to the culture of the Early Nomads. The very large number of bronze plaques in the shape of standing or recumbent deer indicates the pre-eminent position of that image, while the typology and ornamentation of Tagar mirrors, plaques, and animalcrowned pole-tops affirm the underlying unity of object and image within nomadic culture all across the Eurasian Steppe. Of the fate of the people who created the Karasuk-Tagar culture, little is known. Tagar archaeological remains indicate that the culture disappeared by the late: first millennium B.C., pushed north-northwest out of the Minusinsk Basin by the influx of another more aggressive culture, most likely that of the Xiongnu ("Huns"). Those who fled to the north may well have been the ancestors of a remnant Yenisey group of peoples of which only the Ket survive. 11 They moved northwards along the Yenisey, carrying
10 Gryaznov has emphasized the increasing importance during the Karasuk of the family as the basic social unit; within that emerging social structure, he claims to find evidence of increasingly patriarchal values. However, in the cemetery of Malye Kop<;lny 3, one finds a striking evenness in the treatment of the mortuary context and in the distribution of burial goods. In only one burial out of 287 did the position of a young woman's body facing that of a large man suggest to the excavator of that site the possibility ofincreased male authority (Zyablin 1977: 19). 11 The other recorded members of this group were the Kot, Asan, and Arin (Jakobson 1957: 222; Pulleyblank 1963). THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS 19
in their mythic traditions elements which may clarify the overwhelming significance of the deer image within the iconographic tradition of the Tagar culture and of the Early Nomads of South Siberia. The history of Karasuk-Tagar culture and the association of Tagar zoomorphic imagery with that of contemporary nomadic cultures of Eurasia suggest that the zoomorphic imagery of the Scytho-Siberians may owe many of its stylistic characteristics to elements associated with Karasuk bronze ornamentation and with the related tradition of North China. These characteristics include the use of knife and dagger terminals in the form of stylized deer, elk, or homed animals. Antlered or horned, the animals were usually characterized by large, soft muzzles and a rounded, cellular treatment of the eyes and nostrils. The remains of inlay in bronze objects found in North China and associated with the styles of both the Northern Bronze culture and with Karasuk bronzes indicates that stone inlay was originally used to add coloristic emphasis to the eyes and ears of the animal headed terminals. 12 The Bronze Age of the northern Altay Mountains remains unclear, although recent archaeological excavations promise to change that situation. The early Bronze Age burials at Karakol have revealed a tradition of painting grave slabs with figures of quasi-animal, quasianthropomorphic aspect. These images suggest connections with the imagery of the Yenisey Valley to the north; they also suggest to some scholars possible connections with a cultural impulse coming from the Southeast. It is certain that a large number of the petroglyphs from the Chuya Valley in southeastern Gorno-Altayskaya A.O. belong to the Bronze Age and that their combinations of cattle, deer, carts, and figures in mushroom-shaped hats obscurely reflect the myths, rituals, and economies of contemporaneous life in that region. Furthermore, the parallelism between the human images here and those found in many sites of northern and western Mongolia and even in central Mongolia, certainly refers either to the gradual spread of a single culture over a large region or to the passage of representatives of that culture from one area to another. One must assume that in the Altay region, as in the Minusinsk Basin and northern Mongolia, pastoralism was the dominant way of life, supplemented by hunting.
12 Because the Karasuk representational style has been chronicled largely through chance finds, its particular character and its independence from the bronze tradition associated with the northern China borderlands remains problematic. Despite the desire of many scholars to find the origins of the Scythe-Siberian zoomorphic imagery in the Karasuk bronze tradition, available material does not yet justify such a conclusion, Regarding the Karasuk and Tagar traditions, see Chlenova 1967, 1976; Kiselev 1951.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



20 THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS
The grasslands of the Transbaykal and northern Mongolia seem to have also given rise to a pastoralism by the first millennium B .C. and to a characteristically semi-nomadic way of life (Dikov 1958; Volkov 1967; Grishin 1981; Okladnikov 1950). Petroglyphs from the Selenga (Selenge) River dating to the first millennium B.C. reflect a life of stockraising mixed with hunting and some cultivation. Those from the Chuluut Gol in northern Mongolia seem to indicate a continuing dependency on the hunting of wild animals, as well as a preoccupation with herds of animals, wild and domesticated. These scenes may reflect a tradition of reindeer herding as well as religious beliefs which required the pictorial invocation of herds of animals. In the Transbaykal and in northern and central Mongolia, the culture of the first millennium B.C. is associated with rectangular slab-graves, so-called because the slabs of stone set into the earth suggest the shape of large graves. These structures are regular in size and are organized in rows running north-south. Their lack of structural differentiation suggests a general evenness of wealth and authority, but the burials themselves have been almost completely plundered. Perhaps the most striking feature of these slab-graves are great monoliths covered with the carved representations of stylized deer. Sometimes they were placed at the corners of the slab graves; in other cases, they formed a part of the slab wall or were set on the east side of the grave complex. At times these stones have a decidedly anthropomorphic reference, leading many scholars to postulate that they represented warriors of the "after world" or warrior-ancestors ( Okladnikov 1959). 13 The deer images with which they are enwrapped are distinctive, their heads lengthened into birds' beaks, their bodies elongated, their folded legs thin and small, and their great racks rising like waves over the animals' backs (pl. XIXa). From these distinctive images has come the general appelation- "deer stones." Unfortunately, however apt the term may be in the case of the Mongolian stones, its usefulness becomes dubious when applied to related traditions of carved monoliths in the Sayan-Altay region. Although the location of their origin and the direction of their spread is not certain, the Mongolian deer stones do appear to indicate a general unity ofculture across northern Mongolia and throughout the Transbaykal during the early first millennium B.C. In the east and center of Mongolia, even as far as the Khangay Mountains, the deer stones were associated with slab-graves. In the northern part of Mongolia and toward the
13 Sec below, Chap. VI.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS 21
northwest, however, in association with the region of the Uyuk culture, the deer-stones were rather associated with a particular kind of elaborated stone mound known as khereksur (Mannay-ool 1970), a term derived from the Mongolian word khargias-ur (Khudyakov 1987: 136). The khereksur take the form of huge mounded spoked wheels or mounds surrounded by walls, gates, and ritual altars or circles. They seem to have been ritual rather than funerary in function. Apparently contemporaneous with the slab-graves and deer stones of northeastern and northern Mongolia was a particular type of ritual complex in northwestern Mongolia, Tuva and southeastern GornoAltayskaya A.O. These combined anthropomorphic stones carved with the representations of deer, horses, felines, and weapons, with ritual circles, kurgans, or khereksur (Kubarev 1979; Grach 1980; Vaynshteyn 1974: 28-32; Volkov 1981; Novgorodova 1989: 201-226). Most scholars agree that the deer stones of the Mongolian type and their counterparts in Tuva and Gorno-Altayskaya A.O. should be dated to the end of the Bronze Age. If that date is correct, than one may conclude that their appearance coincided with the emergence of Early Nomadic culture. Beyond that uncertain association of deer images, monoliths, and Early Nomads, the deer stones remain a major enigma. I shall return to a more extensive discussion of this material, for it is central to the reconstruction of the symbolic systems centered on the Early Nomadic deer image. The foregoing overview indicates that the Early Nomads inherited a number of significant artistic traditions: the carving and painting of images on living stone; the carving and raising of monolithic figures; the construction of ritual sites with circles of stones or with mounded stones and earth; the construction of elaborate funerary structures including log and stone burial chambers and elaborate stone mounds or barrows. In addition, it is possible to document a vital ceramic tradition from the Bronze Age, one which nonetheless seems to have had virtually no impact on the functional ceramic tradition of the Early Nomads. Most important by far is the tradition of bronze casting and, specifically, that of the casting of zoomorphic elements in bronze and gold. This metalurgical tradition itself points back to what must have been a significant tradition of carving in bone, horn, antler, and wood. It is virtually certain that in those perishable materials we would have found the roots of much of the zoomorphic representations of the Early Nomads and of their relatives across the Eurasian Steppe. 14
14 Some bone carvings have survived, such as the important carvings of elk from Bazaykha (Okladnikov and Martynov 1972: 180-185).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



22 THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS
C. THE ART OF THE ScYTHo-SrnERIANS AND THE IMAGE OF THE DEER
The Scytho-Siberians came out of the center of Eurasia and evolved over centuries into a powerful political and economic force in the history of late Zhou and Han China, of Assyria and Achaemenid Persia, and of the Hellenic world. Over the course of several centuries, they were drawn from regions of grassland and forest-steppe southwards toward the borders of the settled civilizations which ring the Eurasian heartland. Paralleling that expansion was the transformation of their social and political order. The transformation of their artistic tradition, however, also paralleled those changes and traced the evolution of cultural references away from the forest-steppe and steppe toward the tastes of the Hellenic, Achaemenid, and Chinese worlds. Established in its beginnings through monumental images of deer, caprids, panthers and birds of prey, the evolution of their art reveals the nomads' gathering interest in that other world to the south. The archaic severity of their early images gradually gave way to a syncretic and curvilinear treatment reminiscent of Persian and Chinese traditions, and to a realism and anthropomorphism which announced the intrusion of Hellenic taste. Just as the remote Siberian roots of the Scytho-Siberians were lost in the final transition to urbanization and hegemony over the steppes, so too was the unique vitality of their archaic images finally smothered by ornament and by convention.By the period of the Xiongnu and Kushan hegemonies, all vital signs of that ancient imagistic tradition had virtually disappeared. However powerful the nomadic kingdoms in the last few centuries B.C. were, it is not through their political achievements that most people know of the Scytho-Siberians. It is their artistic achievements, wrought in gold, wood, and fabrics, that have attracted both scholarly and general attention over the last one hundred years. The great gold objects found in Scythian barrows-the "golden man" of Issyk kurgan in the TyanShan and the magnificent horse trappings carved in wood and covered in gold from the Altay burials of Early Nomads-have in recent years established Scytho-Siberian antiquities as a major field of archaeology and as the source of a great artistic tradition. At the center of that tradition, shared across the Eurasian steppe, was the image of an antlered animal-sometimes a deer, at other times more closely resembling an elk. In the earliest period of Scytho-Siberian culture (seventh - early fifth centuries), this image shared a dominant position with a coiled or crouched feline and with an eagle-like bird. Depending on the geography
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS 23
of the region, the deer image would be replaced by a horned animal, goat, or sheep. However its position may have varied within the ScythoSiberian symbolic system, the deer remained over time as the dominant image in the nomads' visual pantheon and the image which underwent the most radical transformations. The Scytho-Siberian deer is best known as it is displayed in grand isolation within museum exhibitions and catalogues. These displays tend to emphasize the splendor of the nomads' goldwork, the ferocity of the scenes of animal predation, and the essential "barbarism" of these people from the Greek and Chinese point of view. In its original context on human clothing or carried by horses, the deer image was rarely represented alone. Intact burials and unbroken objects from the North Pontic region, from Kazakhstan, and from South Siberia teach us that the deer was part of a regularly recurring web of images, bound to these others by postures indicating opposition, aggression, and zoomorphic transformation. So regular were the patterns encompassing the deer image and those of other animals and so repeated were the structures by which they were ordered on the bodies of horses and humans, that one begins to sense that meaning must lie in the relations ofelements as much as within the elements themselves. Meaning, however, must also have resided within the process of image transformation indicated by changing patterns of representation. The repetition and modification of the deer image over time suggests that within the vital iconography of the Early Nomads and of their relatives, the antlered animal serves as a key to the understanding of the Early Nomads' belief systems-that is, to their understanding of the source, nature, and processes of their world. The most famous example of the nomads' deer is offered by the great recumbent stag from the Scythian burial at Kostromskaya (Piotrovsky 1987: pl. 16). This image is believed to have ornamented the shield of the man buried there. In its size and in the beauty of its archaic formulation, the Kostromskaya stag is unique. The same image, however, in a less powerfully conceived form, can be found on a multitude of gold and bronze objects from sixth and fifth century Scythian barrows. It reappears on the gold gorytus cover from the sixth century Kelermes 4, there juxtaposed with a crouching panther (Piotrovsky 1987: pl. 23). In the same burial the remains of a gold plaque with four fine deer of the Kostromskaya type were also found. From Kelermes 1 a hollow gold finial was recovered, also in the shape of a recumbent deer (Piotrovsky 1987: pl. 20) and in the Crimean barrow of Ak-Mechet, dated to the fifth century B.C., a single gold plate ornmented with a recumbent deer
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



24 THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS
(pl. Va) was found. In this case, the animal retains the sculptured stylization of body volumes reminiscent of a carved matrix, but the treatment of the eye has become more realistic, the neck and chest have acquired a stylized fringe, and the great antlers have been transformed into the heads of large-beaked birds. This appearance of a new representational realism mixed with a fantastic zoomorphic transformation surely marks the beginning of the end of the archaic deer image in Scythia. Thereafter, the deer image was fated to become further interpreted away from its ancient mythic formulation. By the fourth century, it had become either burdened by the clash between its archaic formulation and the requirements of a Hellenized taste, as in the case of the shield plaque: from the royal burial of Kul Oba (Piotrovsky 1987: 213) or, as on the: great vase from Chertomlyk, it had become thoroughly transformed into the realistic animal of the Greeks (Piotrovsky 1987: 265-268). Within the scanty remains of the Saka artistic tradition, it is more difficult to perceive the process by which the archaic image of the deer devolved. From the plundered sixth century burial at Chiliktin, in eastern Kazakhstan, a number of gold deer with turquoise inlaid ears have been recovered (Chernikov 1964; Frumkin 1970: figs. 3, 4). Crushed and small, these plaques nonetheless retain the monumental aspect of an archaic prototype. Their heavy bodies and pronounced foretines suggest that the reindeer lies somewhere behind the image. When the deer reappears within the gold magnificence of the Issyk burial, at least one or two centuries later than Chiliktin, it has been deprived of that early monumentality and has been incorporated into the plaques of the dead man's belt and into a plaque adorning the dagger sheath. With an elk-like head and exaggeratedly curvilinear antlers, the animal seems to have been sacrificed to decorative considerations (Basilov 1989: 32-33). The deer image is markedly absent from the plaques of the Siberian Treasure of Peter the Great. The images incorporating syncretic deer/ horse/bird forms from burials in the China border-lands (Tian and Guo 1986) indicate the relatively late date of this material within the artistic production of the Scytho-Siberian world. Only in the art of the Early Nomads of the Altay, of Tuva, and of the Minusinsk Basin is it possible to trace the devolution of the deer from a relatively early image type to a late expression. Here the transformation of single image types over the course of several hundred years does not appear to have been as dramatic as was the case in Scythian art. The earliest known Early Nomadic burial, that of Arzhan, was severely plundered and little remains of image and ornament. Certainly no images of deer survived the robbery
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS 25
and fire which destroyed this burial, but the single great bronze plaque of a coiled feline (pl. Ila) and the powerful images of rams from the tops of bronze finials indicate clearly the monumental style of the archaic tradition. One can only assume that the images of deer, had they survived, would also have exemplified that early stage of the Early Nomadic tradition. It is through the materials retrieved from the Early Nomadic burials of Gorno-Altayskaya A.O. and from those of the Tagar culture of the Minusinsk Basin that we can reconstruct the history of the deer image in the South Siberian part of the Scytho-Siberian world. Throughout that period-that is, from the fifth century to the first century-the deer remained the most frequently repeated element within the imagistic pantheon of both areas. Provenanced and unprovenanced materials from the Tagar culture indicate quite clearly that the deer image, particularly in its recumbent form, was more frequently repeated than any other animal image. Bronze images of deer have been found in many Tagar burials, laid on the bodies of the dead (female as well as male) as if to mark a significant association of forces or to call down to the dead a particular poweror relationship (Chlenova [1962] 1963; Bobrov 1973). These plaques certainly carried a more vital significance than the caprids did on the Tagar openwork standards. Throughout the period of the Tagar culture, the handling of the deer image seems to have been fairly consistent, as least as far as can be ascertained at this stage of Tagar archaeology. Within the artistic production of the Early Nomadic culture of the Altay, the deer's stylistic rendition was also remarkably consistent over time. Taking into consideration the variation in quality which one might expect between the art of a social elite (as in the five large burials at Pazyryk) and that of commoners (as in the burials at Ulandryk), the handling of the cervid image continues to emphasize the animal's lithe, alert character as well as distinctive differences between elk and deer. The consistency of the cervid's rendition until the disappearance of Early Nomadic culture in the last two centuries B.C. attests to the relative insularity of that part of
the Scytho-Siberian world (Bobrov 1973). The appearance in Altay burials of Chinese silks and mirrors and of Achaemenid fabrics and motifs indicates the intrusion of new tastes but no profound change. The art of the late burials at Ulandryk, Tashanta, and Ak-Alakh-all in Gorno-Altayskaya A.O.-demonstrates a steady, albeit conventionalized, continuation of the styles and motifs which appeared earlier in the art of Tuekta and Pazyryk.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



26 THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS
Throughout the Scytho-Siberian world, however, the image of the antlered animal also reflects a keen sense of the living animal. Even when the rendition reflects intense stylization, one has the impression that the artist observed the animal in the wild. The alert but fearfu] expression of its raised head and wide-open eyes and the tension in its neck and body, indicate not only the hand of an anonymous master, but also the memory of a hunter. Likewise, the heavy bodies and antlers of the Chiliktin deer seem too close to those of a reindeer to be accidental, just as the vital applique of elk from the Pazyryk shabracks (Rudenko 1970: figs. 108, 109, 113) surely reflect the understanding of an artist who observed the animal in the wild and saw its anguished twist under the impact of a predator. In the case of the early Scytho-Siberian deer images it seems possible to identify the very species of the animals represented. These species include the elk (Akes alces Linnaeus), known in North America as a moose; the reindeer (Rangifer tarandus), a close relative of the North American caribou; a variation of the red or noble deer (Cervus elaphus Linnaeus) or of the maral (Cervus elaphus canadensis). At times in the following discussion it will be possible to determine the exact nature of the animal represented; at other times it will be considerably more difficult to do so. Whatever the ultimate reference of the image may be, we will find that the deer image was enclosed in the signs of change. Usually placed at the center of an axis or arranged in mirror imagery on either side of that center, it might be the intended victim of anticipated attack from a feline, a wolf, or a great bird or its antlers, head, or even body would be represented in a state of transformation from deer to bird, from deer to horse, or from horse/deer to tree. Within the Early Nomadic and Scythian traditions, the patterns in which these images were arranged were strikingly consistent. Although materials from Tagar and Xiongnu burials are considerably more fragmented, there, too, one finds the repetition of similar images bound in regularly recurring patterns of opposition and transformation. Clearly these patterns of representation were not merely ornamental in intent. They functioned as symbolic systems: that is, as systems of images and relationships which carried complex symbolic significance. The deer was the key to those symbolic systems within the age of the Early Nomads, just as it was the key to the symbolic systems of the larger Scytho-Siberian culture. The following pages will investigate the nature of those symbolic systems and attempt to excavate from the archaeology and mythic traditions of South Siberia the source and nature of the deer image itself.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE RECONSTRUCTION OF SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS 27
In Chapter II, I will discuss the current scholarly understanding of the deer image and of its historical and cultural sources. Chapter III will examine in considerable detail the Early Nomads' symbolic systems as carried on the bodies of horses and humans. By comparing that material to related materials from the Scythian and Saka traditions it will be possible to see how widespread those symbolic systems were and yet how their variations accorded with chronology and with cultural ecology. In Chapter IV, I will tum back to the history of the deer image in South Siberia during the Neolithic and Aeneolithic periods, continuing in Chapter V with its history during the Bronze Age, as part of a larger pantheon of images. Chapter VI will address the tradition of carved monoliths in South Siberia and Mongolia, particularly as they relate to and complicate our understanding of the so-called "deer stones" of that region. This examination will again draw our attention beyond the world of the Early Nomads to that of the Pre-Scythians and Scythians. In Chapter VII, I will tum to Siberian ethnographic sources for the reconstruction of archaic mythic and ritual symbolic systems which involve a deer or deer substitute. Finally, in Chapter VIII, I will draw these several traditions together. At that point it will become apparent that the deer image inherited by the Early Nomads and elaborated into the center of their symbolic systems was a sign of the ecology of belief. It referred back not to a solar hero or to Indo-European values but rather to the emergence and gradual disappearance of a truly Siberian cosmogonic source-the Animal Mother, the source of life and death.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



CHAPTER TWO
THE IMAGE OF THE DEER: THEORETICAL CONSTRUCTS
