The image of the Scytho-Siberian deer has prompted considerable theoretical speculation regarding its origins and meaning. One quickly finds that within the study of Scytho-Siberian art and culture, as well as within the art and culture of the nomads' Bronze Age predecessors, inquiry into any one area of style and iconography leads to the question of meaning. Thus, for example, the gold in which the deer was cast and with which the nomads arrayed their dead has prompted a particular focus on the symbolic significance of gold within their cosmology. The warrior signification attributed by most scholars to the anthropomorphic deer stones has given rise to an assumption that the deer image must be interpreted in connection with values validating the role of the warrior in nomadic society. These arguments frequently turn on themselves and lead to the assumption that the deer image was the sign of the warrior. Such associations have been too easily supported by assumptions regarding the warlike character of those early nomadic peoples and their supposed cultural dependency on Inda-European traditions. What emerges from most theoretical approaches to the study of the deer image, and of Scytho-Siberian culture in general, is a web of intertwined threads join ing the motifs of deer, gold, and warrior with Inda-European, Inda-Iranian, and Siberian mythology, but without the benefits of the linguistic evidence on which Inda-European and Inda-Iranian constructs depend. There are three principle theories regarding the origins of the deer motif and of the archaic Scytho-Siberian triad. The first theory, articulated most clearly by M. I. Artamonov, locates those origins within the cultural world of the ancient Near East. Artamonov argued that the origins of Scytho-Siberian iconography and styles were rooted in the art of the Medes and of earlier peoples of Iran and Mesopotamia. Thus the deer, the crouched feline, and the bird of prey, as the primary motifs within the archaic Scythian pantheon, were to be found within that ancient Near Eastern tradition and were borrowed by the Scythians during their sojourn in the northern Iranian plateau and in the region of Urartu (Artamonov 1968). Artamonov's arguments have been seconded by many scholars of Scytho-Siberian history and culture: those who have
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE IMAGE OF THE DEER 29
wished to see the period of the Scythian sojourn in that area as particularly formative both with respect to their artistic traditions and with respect to their culture. If, as some believe, the Scythians spoke an Iranian language and shared in the artistic heritage of the Iranian plateau, then it becomes possible to interpret their motifs in light of lndo-lranian mythic traditions. 1If they were Indo-lranian, then they owe their artistic tradition to Near Eastern sources. Yet despite these arguments, there exist no convincing Near Eastern paradigms for the archaic Scythian deer, crouched or coiled feline, or bird-of-prey. The position staked out by Artamonov has had a powerful influence even on scholars of South Siberian Early Nomadic art. V. V. Bobrov has proposed that the deer motif of the Tagar culture in particular, as well as that found throughout the Scytho-Siberian world, was derived from the Near East and transmitted to the Siberian nomads through an influx of Central Asian Sakas in the fifth century B.C. (Bobrov 1973, 1974). The idea of such an influx is not altogether new. Many have attempted to explain the "sudden" appearance of Arzhan in the eighth or seventh centuries and of the great Altay barrows in the sixth or fifth centuries as evidence of the intrusion of a horse-dependent and warlike people from out of Central Asia. However, whether one speaks of Arzhan, Tuekta, Pazyryk, or the Tagar culture, such an influx remains purely hypothetical and based upon theories of analogy. It is true that burial furnishings of the Early Nomads have revealed clear evidence of regular trade and interchange with the Iranian world of Central Asia. Other than those traded silver objects and rugs and those artistic motifs which indicate the intrusion of a foreign taste, however, there exists little clear evidence that there was a specifically Saka influx as far north as the northern Altay and certainly not as far north as the Minusinsk Basin. The dominant style of the Altay barrows is Siberian, not Central Asian, and the dominant aspect of the Tagar material is rooted in the fauna of the North as well as in an archaic northern tradition of carving wood and bone. Those who have argued for Near Eastern stylistic roots depend on fragile theories of cultural transmission in order to explain the appearance of a confident and integrated artistic tradition as far to the northeast as South Siberia. None except Bobrov have attempted to address the issue ofreception, of why those distant peoples would have been willing, and even eager, to embrace foreign motifs and foreign styles. Bobrov has suggested that the way for such reception in the Tagar culture was paved
1 See, e.g., the studies by Abayev, Rayevskiy and Kuz'mina.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



30 THE IMAGE OF THE DEER
by an ancient belief in the elk image symbolizing the sun, but beyond that suggestion, he has offered little concrete evidence. The issue of transmission and reception remains the primary stumbling block for the theory of Near Eastern origins. Except in the case of passing fads characteristic of societies bombarded by modern media, elements of style and iconography are never given and never adopted unless the receiving culture is receptive to the foreign element. That state ofreceptivity involves a complex set of cultural, economic, and political circumstances. Given the vastly different worlds of the settled Near East-politically centralized and urban-based-and the loose pastoral society of South Siberia, it is unlikely that motifs developed in the ancient Near Eastern arena would have had any meaning for the peoples of that distant Siberian world. Only by the middle Early Nomadic period., as is attested by the great burials of Pazyryk, do foreign-made objects indicate that the nomadic culture was sufficiently developed and selfconfident that its members sought foreign objects as signs of the wealth befitting the well-born. 2 In an early study of the deer motif, N. L. Chlenova argued that the image emerged from traditions located between the borders of northern Iran and Lake Zaysan in eastern Kazakhstan (Chlenova 1962b, 168). She developed this position in the context of an extensive stylistic analysis of Tagar deer plaques, but the title of her study-"The Scythian Deer"indicates how her argument with regard to the Tagar motif is intended to refer back to a discussion of the deer motif in the larger ScythoSiberian culture as well as to the origins of the "animal style." Like others writing before the extensive excavations of Tagar materials in a more recent period, Chlenova was dependent upon chance finds. Her insistence on seeing the deer image as an individual element, unconnected with any larger symbolic system, prompts her particular conclusion. Within the Near Eastern tradition, Chlenova argues, the recumbent deer, goat, and bull were almost always associated with a representation of the Tree of Life, itself a symbol of the Great Mother Goddess (Chlenova 1962b: 195). In the Scythian world, however, the deer is never represented with either that tree or with any elements which would appear to refer to a Mother Goddess. Rather-and here she follows the arguments of Abayev (1949)-the deer image within the Scythian world
2 Minasyan has argued vigorously that the technical aspects of the archaic ScythoSiberian coiled feline and recumbent deer clearly contradict a Near Eastern origin. Rather, they refer to a tradition of wood and bone carving which in turn points to a northern forest homeland (Minasyan 1988, 1990).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE IMAGE OF THE DEER 31
had been reformulated as an image of the Scythian totemic animal. As the totemic animal of the Scythian and Saka groups within the ScythoSiberian world, the deer image-presumably with the rest of the "animal style"-must have emerged somewhere in the vast region between northern Iran and Lake Zaysan, a region bound linguistically and culturally to Iran but distanced from it and enjoying a marked degree of cultural autonomy. The argument for a Central Asian origin has also been supported by K. F. Smirnov, the scholar of Sauromatian antiquities, and by S. S. Chernikov, the excavator of the important burials at Chiliktin in eastern Kazakhstan (Smirnov 1964; Chernikov 1960, 1964). The early dateseventh or sixth century-assigned to Chiliktin 5 and its plaques of recumbent deer and coiled felines certainly suggest a location closer to the origins of the Scytho-Siberian tradition than can be argued for Near Eastern motifs and treatment. On the other hand, either because of isolation or due to plundering, the objects recovered from the severely disrupted Chiliktin burial are not sufficient in number to demonstrate an eastern Kazakhstan origin for the deer image. Furthermore, by virtue of the shape of their heads and bodies and by virtue of their forehead tines, the Chiliktin deer appear to refer to the reindeer, an animal which was not native to eastern Kazakhstan. Rather, it points back to the foreststeppe of the Sayan Ridge. G. I. Borovka, E. H. Minns, and M. Loehr have all argued that the deer image and the Scytho-Siberian artistic tradition had to have emerged in Siberia (Borovka 1967; Minns [1913] 1971; Loehr 1955). Gryaznov, also, believed that the deer motif of South Siberia derived from a local, Sayan-Altay artistic tradition rooted in the image of the maral but associated with an economic dependency on reindeer. The source of the so-called "Scythian deer," according to Gryaznov, was within that Sayan-Altay image of the maral, but tempered by Scythian stylizations (Gryaznov 1978; and see Barkova 1990). I have argued in the past for the theory of Siberian origins of the deer image and of the Scytho-Siberian artistic tradition (Jacobson 1983); this position will be developed in the following chapters. The general reasoning behind the "Siberian connection" is straightforward: the animals which dominate the archaic Scytho-Siberian style are all animals of the northern forest or forest-steppe. Furthermore, it can be reasoned that the archaic nature of the early nomadic styles and images indicates not only a tradition of bone and wood carving but also a tradition of zoomorphic representation that goes back as far as the
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



32 THE IMAGE OF THE DEER
Siberian Neolithic. The particular combination of cervids (deer, elk, reindeer), felines (panthers and tigers), caprids (probably representing a variety of sheep and goats), and birds-of-prey, evident in the early art of the Early Nomads and that of their immediate predecessors, depends upon the emergence ofan artistic tradition among the hunting-dependent Bronze Age cultures of South Siberia and Mongolia. While the deer image does, of course, occur in Near Eastern art, it is never the image of the maral, reindeer, or elk, and the feline in that ancient tradition is the lion, an animal that was known to the South Siberian Early Nomads only as an exotic conception, capable of considerably more playful elaboration than the panther image was ever granted. By contrast, the representations of panther-like felines from shabracks from the same barrow (Rudenko 1970: figs. 110-112) convey the power and efficiency of the cats without the slightest suggestion of playfulness.. Within these representations of predator and prey, one senses an accu-• racy and vitality which could only be the result of a lifetime of observation on the part of the artist. I have already referred to Chlenova's belief that the Scytho-Siberian world borrowed the Near Eastern deer motif and adapted it to their totemic tradition. It is V. I. Abayev's identification of the deer with the totemic animal of the Sakas which has had the greatest influence on general assumptions regarding the meaning of the deer. In his study of the language of the Scythians, Abayev analysed the term "Saka" as meaning deer, and "because of that, the totemic tribal name saka, 'scyth,'' 'belonging to the totem deer."' (Abayev 1949: 179). From this interpre-tation the assumption that the antlered deer image referred to the male progenitor of the nomadic people and the conclusion that the deer image of the Scytho-Siberians refers to totem, tribal ancestor, male warrior, and maleness have been derived. Hardly less influential than the theory of the deer as a totemic animal deriving from Near Eastern traditions has been Okladnikov's account of the Siberian mythical deer with golden antlers (Okladnikov 1964; Okladnikov and Martynov 1972; Martynov 1974, 1990). This account was based on an amalgam of Siberian traditions regarding a cosmic dee:r associated with gold and, hence, with the sun. The wingedness of the Tagar deer images refers to flight and prowess. The gold with which they were wrapped indicates their solar signification. The association ofdeer, wingedness, and gold has led to the conclusion that the animal represents the sun: it is a solar, cosmic animal, imaged as deer and as (solar) disk, but ultimately replaced by the horse.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE IMAGE OF THE DEER 33
Unfortunately, it is virtually impossible to find in the raised withers of the Tagar deer image any consistent suggestion of wingedness. This is all the more telling when one realizes that a number of other animalsfelines, caprids, perhaps horses--do acquire wings in Saka art, but deer do not. 3 Furthermore, neither Okladnikov and Martynov' s association of deer/gold/sun nor that of any succeeding scholars have taken into account the stubborn fact that the deer is no more or less frequently represented in gold than is any other animal (Okladnikov and Martynov 1972: 221-229; Martynov 1990). Felines, caprids, horses, birds, and humans have been represented as magnificently in the precious metal work of the Scytho-Siberians as have been deer; but few have argued that in those cases the animal or human represented should be identified with the sun. 4 Related to the association of gold/deer/sun has been the tendency to join gold and the indications of clan genesis. This connection first appears in the account by Herodotus of the Scythians' origins. In the opening pages of his chapter on the Scythians, Herodotus established the mythic association between clan origins, gold, and place. According to Herodotus,
The first man to live in their country ... was a certain Targitaus, the son of Zeus and of a daughter of the river Borysthenes ... Targitaus had three sons, Lipoxais, Arpoxais, and Colaxais, the youngest; and during their reign in Scythia there fell from the sky a golden plough, a golden yoke, a golden battleaxe, and a golden cup. The eldest of the three was the first to see these treasures, and as he went to pick them up the gold caught fire. At this he retired, and the second of the brothers approached; but the gold caught fire and blazed, just as before. Lastly... the youngest came along, and this time the fire went out, so that he was able to pick up the golden implements and carry them home. The elder brothers accepted this as a sign from heaven and made over the whole kingdom to Colaxais. The descendants of ... the youngest brother are the Royal Scythians ... the Greeks call them Scythians (Herodotus [1954]: 243244).
In this tale, gold is associated with clan genesis, but so, also, are the several implements which fell from heaven-plough, yoke, battle-axe, and cup. Unfortunately, no gold ploughs or yokes have been found in Scythian burials, and only two gold-covered battle-axes and a few gold
3 See, e.g., the treatment of felines and caprids in the plaques of Issyk kurgan. The closest one comes to finding winged deer is in the case of plaques of thoroughly syncretic deer from the same barrow. These images have large bird-heads curling up from their withers (Basilov 1989: 32-33). 4 Others have found reason to believe that certain elements, such as wings, horns, or antlers, confer solar value on the animals to which they are added, and that this solar aspect derives from Indo-lranian traditions; cf. Barkova 1985.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



34 THE IMAGE OF THE DEER
vases and rhyta have been found. Obviously, none of these objects relate gold to the specific image of the deer. The solarization of the deer image derives in part from the general belief within Scytho-Siberian scholarship that the deer was gradually replaced by the horse and that the horse itself was a solar symbol. The "replacement theory" is certainly suggested by an Early Nomadic practice indicated at both Tuekta and Pazyryk: that of masking sacrificed horses as deer and caprids. It is also supported by the fact that within the art of the Scythians, the body of the deer gradually assumes a more horselike heaviness. The outright interpretation of the horse as a solar animal is based largely on the report by Herodotus regarding the Massagetae, the branch of the Scytho-Siberians living between the Caspian and Aral seas. According to that historian, the Massagetae worshipped the sun and sacrificed to it the horse, "the swiftest animal" (Herodotus [1954]: 101 ). From this association of horse and sun, parallel to that of deer and sun,. has followed the association of deer, horse, and sun, and the conclusion that the deer-horse is a solar animal. 5 The association of Scytho-Siberians and horses, articulated by both Herodotus and Sima Qian, has tended to be exaggerated in the modern mind: the horse undoubtedly lends to the nomad a greater romance than would be the case if we had to imagine him trudging in the dust on foot beside his sheep and cattle. But it is also true that its visual evidence is substantial and persuasive. The two ends of a superb torque from Kul Oba (fourth century B.C.) are shaped in the form of two bearded Scythians riding their horses (Piotrovsky 1987: pis. 126-127). On the shoulder frieze of the great silver-gilt amphora from the fourth century barrow of Chertomlyk are embossed scenes of Scythians and their horses (Piotrovsky 1987: pis. 266-268). Perhaps the loveliest representation of Scythians amid their animals is found on the magnificent gold pectoral from the fourth century burial at Tolstaya Mogila. In the upper frieze and in the center, two bare-chested Scythians prepare a fleece, while on either side images of milking and of cattle and horses nursing their young create scenes remarkable for their expression of quiet contentment. In the lowest frieze of this pectoral the mood has changed; there, one finds superb renditions of lions and eagle-griffins savaging boar, deer, and horses (Piotrovsky 1987: pis. 118-121). In all these cases, of course, the
sThis position informs such studies as those by Volkov, Chlenova, Novgorodova, Kubarev, and Bobrov, among others. The extent to which it continues as an unexamined assumption may be seen, for example, in Martynov 1990.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE IMAGE OF THE DEER 35
representation is in gold. By contrast, there are few provenanced gold representations of horses and men from Siberia. The objects in gold and felt referred to above attest to the importance of horses as steeds and as sources of milk. Horses, however, were also used for sacrifice at the time of an individual's death. At Arzhan, as many as 160 horses had been sacrificed (Gryaznov 1980). In the rich first burial of Pazyryk, eight horses had been laid out in elaborate regalia in the northern part of the shaft above the chamber (Gryaznov 1950). Horse sacrifice within the Early Nomadic world was not, however, a question of wealth. In a number of commoner burials at Pazyryk, for example, one or more horses were laid outside the burial chamber (Rudenko 1970: 326-327). The commoner burials at Ulandryk, even those of females, were regularly accompanied by one, more rarely two, horses, laid carefully just outside the north wall of the burial chamber. The commoner burials of the Saglin culture in Tuva demonstrate a tradition similar to that attested to at Ulandryk (Grach 1980; Kubarev 1987a). The use of horses within the funerary ritual was not consistent within the Scytho-Siberian world. In many cases there is no sign of horse sacrifice at the time of interment and in other cases the only sign of such sacrifice is the remains of a leg or head. In Mongolia, before the late first millennium B.C., the considerable disruption of burials offers an unclear picture of the importance of the horse (Volkov 1967, 1981 ). In the Minusinsk Basin, burial goods indicate considerably less use of the horse than in the neighboring areas to the south, except as a probable source of food. Despite Herodotus' account of how the Massagetae so valued their horses, in the burials associated with the nomads ofKazakhstan there is no clear evidence for horse sacrifice at the time of a person's burial. In the one unplundered burial at Issyk, that of the remarkable "golden man," there was no preserved indication of horse sacrifice (K. A. Akishev 1978). In the case of the Scythians, horse sacrifice was frequent and lavish but still inconsistent. At Kelermes, dated to the sixth century, at least twenty-four horses had been carefully placed in the burial mound of barrow 1. At the ritual site of Ulsky, possibly also dated in the sixth century, 360 horses had been arranged around and in proximity to hitching posts (Balonov 1987). By contrast, in the burials at Axjutintsy and Volkovtsy, the only horse indicators were the gold and bronze objects used in horse trappings (Minns [1913] 1971: 182-187). The inconsistent use of horse sacrifice across the Scytho-Siberian world suggests that whatever the meaning of the horse was within the culture, it was not essential at the time of death. Nor, clearly, was the
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



36 THE IMAGE OF THE DEER
horse the prerogative of the male warrior. Although the horse was valued for its milk, funerary ritual and visual representations reaffirm the archaeological testimony that the animals were primarily used as mounts. Whether that aspect of the horse was sacralized, however, is not altogether clear. The regalia in which the Pazyryk horses were buried refer to the functional trappings of riding horses. At the same time, the elaborate ornamentation of that regalia and its impractical elaborations raise basic questions: what kind of mounts were these intended to be, and for what purposes? A number of the horses in Pazyryk 1 were fitted with headdresses or masks which effectively disguised them as other animals or as combinations of animals. Kiselev has argued that the deer masks at Pazyryk refer to the social transition from a deer herding dependency to a dependency on horse and cattle. In his early presentation of Pazyryk 1, Gryaznov also surmised that the use of the mask on some of the horses indicates a reminiscence of earlier days when reindeer were the most valuable animals of the South Siberians (Kiselev 1951: 375; Gryaznov 1950). It would be incorrect to assume that the Pazyryk horses, or those at Tuekta, were intended to be disguised as deer or sheep. There are only two cases, both from Pazyryk 1, of deer-antlered headdresses. In other cases, from Pazyryk and Tuekta, the masks and headdresses refer to caprids or to a combination of animals and point to the use of the horse as a vehicle for some complex of mythic ideas lodged in antlers, in horns, and in the theme of animal predation. I shall return to this regalia in the next chapter. For the moment, it is sufficient to note that despite the wear attested to on some of the bridle parts, it is highly unlikely that the horse regalia was regularly used in riding. The delicacy of the workmanship would not have supported the resulting wear and tear, and the masks and headdresses would have been simply impractical in movement at any significant speed. As a whole, the sacrificed horses and the horse regalia from Pazyryk and Tueka point to the use of horses as steeds, but within a ritual context where "horseness" per se was not necessarily the greatest value. In some areas of the Scytho-Siberian world, horses were evidently plentiful in supply and could be sacrificed in abundance. In other areas, however, one must assume that the horse was too valuable an animal to be sacrificed, but the nature of that value is unclear. The horse's absence from the burials of the Tagar culture or from the rich Issyk barrow may also indicate that within the spiritual realm, the horse itself had no particular function. Similarly, where horse sacrifice appears, we may
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE IMAGE OF THE DEER 37
conclude either that it was valued as a mount in both life and death, or it may have represented wealth and fealty, or that it may actually have referred to another kind of steed, or simply to the journey that accompanied death. The very appearance of sacrificed horses-one, two, or many-within any burial really tells us very little about the kind of value the animal carried. It is clear that the archaeological record disputes any easy association of horse, deer, gold, and rank and gender within the Scytho-Siberian social order. Equally problematic, I believe, is the addition of a male warrior association to the solar complex of deer/gold/horse. Arguments for this position have emerged from within the study of the anthropomorphic monoliths of Scytho-Siberian Eurasia; they will be reviewed in Chapter VI. In both Siberia and Scythia, belts girdling the stones and the tools and weapons carved as if hanging from the belts indicate that the stones represented males, and possibly males as hunters or warriors. The association of these stones with deer images has led to the conviction that the deer was the sign of the warrior. Thus a line of reasoning was established which associates deer with horse and sun, deer with totemic origins, and, finally, the deer, horse, and male warrior with the sun and with tribal generation. This reasoning has led to a consistent privileging of the male warrior and solar animal (deer/horse) throughout the archaeological record of the Scytho-Siberians in general and of the Early Nomads in particular.6 Herodotus attended to the history of the Scythians because they entered into his primary purpose: to record an account of the Greek and Persian wars. He looked at the nomads as the Greek that he was, however, and they were, in his eyes, barbarians, beyond the walls of Greek civilization. What emerges from his account is a story that has endured: that these were a people whose only achievement was in stopping the Persian invasion of Scythia. 7 Beyond that one act of historical significance, the Scythians offered little of value to the Hellenic world. They were" ...without fortified towns ...accustomed, one and all, to fight on horseback with bows and arrows..." "It is not their custom to make statues, or to build altars and temples, in honour of any
6 See, especially, A. K. Akishcv 1984; Kubarcv 1979; Volkov 1981; Chlcnova 1962b, 1984; Nowgorodowa (Novgorodova) 1980. 7 A feat which he actually credits to the Scythians' frivolous pursuit of a hare at the point when they were to engage the Persians (Herodotus [1954]: 285-286). Almost every scholar who has written of the Scythians has turned to Herodotus for supposedly authoritative information. None has weighed seriously the ancient historian's reliance on hearsay, nor has any attempted to consider what information Herodotus does give and what he omits.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



38 THE IMAGE OF THE DEER
god except Ares," to whom the Scythians made bloody sacrifices of animals and prisoners of war. Moreover, according to the account of Herodotus and impressed deeply into the imagination of the modern world,
...the Scythian custom is for every soldier to drink the blood of the first man he kills... (The Scythian warriors) have a special way of dealing with the ...skulls ... of their worst enemies: they saw off the part below the eyebrows, and after cleaning out what remains stretch a piece of rawhide round it on the outside. If a man is poor, he is content with that, but a rich man goes further and gilds the inside of the skull as well. In either case, the skull is then used to drink from (Herodotus [1954]: 257-262).
In fact, no such skulls-gilded or otherwise-have ever been found in any of the burials of Scythians, including even those which are apparently the burials of "warriors."
In the foregoing discussion I have indicated that there are major problems within the prevailing interpretations of Scytho-Siberian culture. More subtle and more difficult to disentangle is the complex web of understandings which finds in the art and archaeology of the ScythoSiberians clear indications of the mythic traditions and cultural dynamics of the nomads and of their predecessors. These understandings are founded on the persuasion that Iron Age nomadic culture depended upon the Bronze Age intrusion of a strong Europoid element into South Siberia. This intrusion occurred when the Andronovo culture entered South Siberia with an admixture of lndo-European values and social characteristics. Since (according to these ideas) Indo-European society was indeed male and warrior-centered, this process is believed to have resulted in a privileging of the male warrior and chieftain, in the increasing organization of nomadic society for the purpose of aggressive expansion, and in the emergence of a belief in male dieties associated with the sun and heaven and with male tribal progenitors. It is certainly these assumptions regarding the Indo-European character of ScythoSiberian society which have encouraged many scholars to consider the nomads' horse sacrifices as a sign of the esteem in which the horse was held by a warrior society and as the sign of the projection of IndoEuropean traditions into the society of the Eurasian nomads (Kuz'mina 1977a, 1977b). These assumptions will be examined more carefully in succeeding chapters. For now it is sufficient to note that they depend upon the persuasion that something is as it appears, according to prevailing
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE IMAGE OF THE DEER 39
cultural assumptions: that a stone image carrying weapons and tools necessarily refers to a male warrior as ancestor and as the object of worship, and that the appearance of a deer image on a "warrior" figure indicates the subordination of the deer to the warrior, transforming the animal into the sign of the male progenitor. I have pointed to the problems attending the solarization of gold, deer, horses, and warriors. But the constructs of Indo-European (and Indolranian) traditions are based primarily on linguistic evidence, for which there are no parallels among the Scytho-Siberians. Furthermore, there is no basis for believing that certain cultural innovations-e.g., horseback riding or carts-carried with them the cultural load indicated by language. Language and cultural innovations do not diffuse simultaneously or with the same cultural impact. This in itself should caution us against presuming an Indo-European basis for nomadic society, even if we find there evidence for a value placed on horses, gold, and warriors (of some kind) (cf. Hencken 1955; Schmitt 1974; Lincoln 1981). This theory, however, is most clearly contradicted by that very sign of the warrior: the deer image. Whether represented in apparent isolation or with other images, the deer is virtually always indicated as the intended or actual prey of a predator. Even on the deer stones, small images of predators are often interspersed with those of the deer, as if to reaffirm the fact that literally or figuratively the deer was a sign of animal predation, real or symbolic. The representation of the deer as victim would seem, in itself, highly inappropriate as the sign of a (presumably successful) warrior, and the bodies of deer twisting under the impact of felines is a peculiar way of referring to the supremacy of a solar power. Furthermore, the intact burials at Ulandryk in the northern Altay indicate that gold-foil covered deer were worn on headdresses as often by women as by men. This fact, in itself, undercuts the ready association of both deer and gold with the male warrior. Underlying the interpretative strategy which joins deer/horse, gold/ sun, warrior/tribal progenitor is a second category of assumptions closely associated with the first, but heavily influenced by modern political concerns and particularly those which affirm Marxist-Leninist principles of social development. These assumptions underpin the position most clearly articulated by one of the leading Soviet archaeologists, M. P. Gryaznov. Gryaznov has tended to seek and find evidence within the mortuary context of South Siberia to support his thesis that society during the Bronze and early Iron Age became increasingly "patriarchal," increasingly organized around a male warrior elite (Gryaznov 1969).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



40 THE IMAGE OF THE DEER
According to this point of view, distinctions within the mortuary context necessarily reflect distinctions in social rank based on political power. The more elaborate the burial furnishings and funerary ritual, the more certain is a concentration of political power in the hands of a male leader. Weapons are considered to be reaffirmations of the warlike nature of a society and, thus, of its organization around a warrior-elite. These assumptions require reconsideration in terms of the archaeo-• logical context itself and in terms of recent developments in theories of the archaeology of death (Chapman 1981; Jacobson 1987). In too many cases, the reading of indicators of ranking has been premature or lacking sufficient evidence, particularly in the face of heavily disrupted contexts. Where bodies are completely lacking from burials and burials have been robbed of much of their valuable metal-as is the case with most Karasuk burials, with Arzhan, with most of the great Altay barrows and with virtually all the Mongolian slab-graves-conclusions regarding the possible relationship of gender, status, and social ranking are hypothetical at best. Moreover, assumptions regarding the nature of ranking have been made without determining certain basic measures. Ranking has been considered, in a sense, absolute: the wealthier the burial, presumably the more powerful the individual within the political sphere. The possibility of ephemeral indicators of power has not been considered, nor has the issue of wealthy paired burials been addressed satisfactorily, except to assume that rank and wealth were dependent on the male of the pair and not on the female. For example, the burial of Arzhan has been cited by Gryaznov to demonstrate the full emergence of a nomadic society centered on a warrior-chieftain to whom many different tribes owed hegemony (Gryaznov 1980). Arzhan has been dated by Gryaznov to as early as the eighth century B.C. It was built of large timbers in the form of a great spoked wheel, with a central chamber and over 60 chambers spreading outward from that point. The timbers by which this palatial burial space was constructed had been laid directly on the ground. Found in the burial were the bodies of at least eleven individuals, identified by Gryaznov as a king, his chief wife, and nine followers, as well as the bodies of 160 horses. The presence of a number of bone and bronze three-holed psalia as well as the find of a magnificent bronze coiled feline and bronze ram poletops allowed Gryaznov to date Arzhan to the very beginning of the Scytho-Siberian period.Unfortunately, no other burials of this type have yet been excavated either in Tuva or in any other part of South Siberia and Central Asia, although there are ritual khereksur which in their
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE IMAGE OF THE DEER 41
wheel-shaped structures are analogous to Arzhan. 8 Moreover, Arzhan had been extremely disrupted by early robbers and the bodies of the dead had been pulled from their log coffins and scattered. There remained a few pieces of gold foil and turquoise, some bits of richly woven fabrics and sable, and a few pieces of gold jewelry to indicate that the dead had been lavishly dressed. In other words, there was nothing to indicate that this was the burial of a great chief other than by the theory applied to a ruined funerary site. The five main burials at Pazyryk offer another case in point (Gryaznov 1950; Rudenko 1970). In barrow 1 only one coffin was found, but no body. The dead had apparently been pulled from the burial by grave robbers. The furnishings remaining in that burial chamber were inadequate for surmising the sex of the individual or individuals who had been buried there. In Pazyryk 2 the bodies of a male and female were found. Since there was only one large coffin, one must assume that it had been used for both individuals. The burial chamber still contained many objects indicative of the wealth and well-being of this couple. The burial also had an associated horse burial. Barrow 3 contained only one treetrunk coffin and one skeleton, a male's. But there were two headdresses found in the burial chamber, one left undescribed and another of a type clearly associated with a female. One must assume, therefore, that another body, that of a woman, had been pulled from the burial by the robbers, in order to more easily strip it of valuable clothing and gold. Barrow 4 contained two coffins and two skeletons, those of a man and a woman. In barrow 5 were also found a pair of bodies, male and female. Like the other burial chambers, this one referred in structure and furnishings to a lavish domestic context. It is possible, of course, that the grave robbers had taken all the metal weaponry and tools, thereby stripping the burials of their specifically male markers. What remains is simply inadequate to support the assumptions of Rudenko and almost all others who have considered these burials: that they represent a society dominated by a male warrior aristocracy. The broken barrows give no clues regarding the relative ranking of males and females, nor the source of social ranking. Distinctions between their size and furnishings and those of other nearby barrows only indicate that, at least in death, Pazyryk society was characterized by social distinctions of some kind. When the burial structures, furnishings, and burial rituals of the Early Nomads are looked at in terms of an integrated context, it becomes clear that the assumptions which have filtered the interpretation of the deer
8 E.g., the khereksur of Ulug-khem, from the Saglin culture (Grach 1980).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



42 THE IMAGE OF THE DEER
image need to be reexamined. We can neither conclude that the head of the household, of the lineage, or even of the tribe was male, nor can we claim to have the materials that would allow us to assert that military activities dominated nomadic society and were restricted to males. Where one has a rich burial involving male and female, surviving goods do not indicate that the male was necessarily the principle interred and the woman the consort. If one were to base one's conclusions only on what has actually been found, than it would have to be admitted that Pazyryk 5, with its elegant carriage and with the wall hanging dominated by a seated female figure, would seem to be more certainly the burial of a great lady and her male consort than the reverse. The assumption of male dominance in Scytho-Siberian burials has been greatly dependent on the appearance of weaponry within the burials: that is, weapons are assumed to belong to the males and are assumed to be indicators of higher rank than, for example, the bowls, needles, pottery, and tools associated with women (but also with men). This kind of privileging, however, is problematic. It is clear that battle axes and long swords were useful as weapons, but neither appear in Early Nomadic burials until rather late.9 Moreover, we do not know their value as indicators of rank or status. It is also true that daggers, knives, bows and arrows-all of which have actually been found in proximity to female bodies as well as to male, although not as frequently-may have functioned as both weapons and tools; their appearance within a burial does not automatically indicate the burial of a warrior or of a male of superior status. Among a number of Scytho-Siberian groups, women as well as men handled knives and daggers. Kiselev has found weaponry associated not only with the "warrior-males" of Tagar burials but also with the females (Kiselev 1951: 227; cf. Kulemzin 1982). The Sauromatian women-most certainly the source of the Greek myth of the Amazonsappear to have functioned as both warriors and priestesses (Smimov 1964, 1971). Tomyris, queen of the Massagetae, succeeded her husband after his death and successfully led her warriors into battle against Cyrus. Her actions and her words of challenge to the Persian ruler suggest a woman as comfortable in that role of leadership as a man might be (Herodotus [1954]: 96-101). The assumption that weapons indicate males, warriors, and superior
9 Both do appear in early Scythian burials, Kelermes and the Melgunov kurgan being prime examples (Piotrovsky 1987: pls. 32, 36-40). Their absence from other burials may, of course, be due to the plundering of robbers. On the other hand, they are not attested in the preserved burials of Ulandryk, admittedly those of commoners.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE IMAGE OF THE DEER 43
ranking is further challenged by the possibility that female ranking may have been indicated through ephemera: charisma, personal power, or objects which have disintegrated in the course of time, such as elements of clothing. The problem of determining rank and status in regard to burials also applies to the analysis of images and their symbolic formulations, including those involving the deer. The signifying load of any image or of any structure of images will shift in time, depending on a number of factors. Some social or religious traditions inherited by a society from the past may be retained but others may be modified or lost. This could occur, for example, in the event of shifting climatic or geophysical factors, as when a social group moves elsewhere in pursuit of food or shelter. In that case, the force behind a particular image or structure may simply be lost or forgotten, or the original source of that image being absent, the image becomes mythologized, in the process gaining characteristics it never had earlier, or losing elements with which it had formerly been associated. The reindeer is a case in point. Even in its predomesticated state, it must have served as a primary source of food and clothing for South Siberian peoples of the Bronze Age. There is much to indicate that the reindeer was distantly behind the retention of the deer image within the Scytho-Siberian pantheon, long after the nomadic groups had left reindeer territory. One must assume that as Early Nomadic society shifted out of a Bronze Age balance between hunting/fishing and pastoralism to a greater dependency on livestock, the values with which specific images were laden would also have shifted. We know that such values and their institutionalization within symbolic systems may not proceed together. Nor can one necessarily find a ready consonance between changing symbolic formulations and new dimensions of social order and ranking. As is the case of artistic styles, so too in the case of the lives they embellish: old forms and old rituals may be retained, perhaps only for the sake of a sentimental attachment to what has been. Or they may be retained, perhaps, because of a cultural idealization of the past. At the same time, symbolic systems reflective of changing religious beliefs or changing social ranking may lag behind significantly, retaining traces of earlier value systems long after the latter have disappeared from effective functioning within society as a whole. This has been fully demonstrated in the case of mortuary practices, which may represent a "freezing" of earlier traditions and values that are effectively rejected in the practical communication of a given society (Pearson 1982). An image
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



44 THE IMAGE OF THE DEER
may entirely divest itself of earlier signification and gain new meaning. In the case of the transition from Bronze Age to early Iron Age South Siberia, the regular pairing of individuals may or may not reflect a contemporary relative equality between males and females. The fact that horses or horse trappings have been found with females and children alone rather than only in the presence of males also gives one pause: in light of the general scholarly contention that horses were the sign of the male warrior in Scytho-Siberian society, we have here either a direct contradiction of that hypothesis or a delayed retention of outmoded associations and rituals into a new age. Specific objects such as a sword or an ax may also shift from a reference to martial power to that of political power, without that shift leaving any other trace within the mortuary context. No less obscured by essential human conservatism could be a changing social order involving the shift in centers of power within household and lineage from females to males. Given the fact that the furnishings of Early Nomadic burials are overwhelmingly domestic in reference, there may be no way of ascertaining how, when, or if such a complete change ever occurred. The point is that contemporary realities of social organization and ranking may not be clearly visible through the mortuary or ritual context. The anthropomorphic deer stones associated with the slab-graves of Mongolia are also associated with the ritual khereksur, as well as with ritual enclosures. As I have indicated, because the monoliths seem to repre~ent mll.le wllrrior figurei; the lli:rnmption hlli: prevlliled thllt the khereksur refer to some aspect of a socially validated viricentrism, perhaps to a cult of the male tribal progenitor. In fact, in their structure and in the lack of other surface and subsurface materials, the khereksur give no clues regarding social ranking or gender reference. There is nothing to allow us to associate them with tribes rather than with clans or lineages. If the khereksur do refer to ranking, we cannot know from anything within their context whether they presuppose a centralization of leadership either in the political or spiritual realms. While the nature and sizeoftheritualstructures may indicate social willingness to accord individuals or groups conventional signs of authority (Tainter 1978; Brown 1981), it is not possible to say how, if at all, those signs will leave "readable" distinctions within the surface or subsurface archaeological record. Nor is it possible to determine the nature of that authority or the source of that ranking. In other words, there is nothing in the Early Nomadic mortuary or ritual context which would demonstrate a dominating and determinant belief in a male ancestor or tribal founder.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE IMAGE OF THE DEER 45
Finally, one must recognize the limitations of assuming that there is a self-evident relationship between a sign and the signified. A sign may represent one set of values associated with one social being, but depending on the context, it may shift those values to a very different reference. The bull and the lion of Sumerian and Babylonian traditions refer, on the one hand, to male vitality and fertility, but they were also frequently associated with the female goddess and were subordinated to her power. Separated from her, these images continued to serve as indicators of her presence, as well as of her domination. Chariots also referred to the presence of both gods and goddesses, since they were understood to be vehicles of both. Within the context of late Bronze-early Iron Age South Siberia, it is no less possible that the multivalency of any one symbol involved shifting attributes from one reference to another. While the anthropomorphic deer stone may on one level represent the male warrior, it may also indicate the presence of a female progenitor of the lineage. The result is similar to the way in which a Buddhist dvarapala arrayed in the attire of a warrior referred not to warriors, but rather to the presence of the Buddha, the embodiment of non-action and non-violence. Within Early Nomadic society, a sign could serve equally as icon and to indicate the presence of a power or individual that was otherwise absent. The powers indicated by that sign could thereby shift from one referent to another. This, indeed, is what happened to the deer image in its passage through time and through the cultural layers of Neolithic, Bronze Age, and early Iron Age South Siberia.
There is yet a third set of assumptions which has tended to prejudice scholarly examination of the Early Nomadic culture. These assumptions are characterized by the understanding that from the time of the early Bronze Age, the ancient cultures of South Siberia were shamanistic (e.g., Brentjes and Vasilevsky 1989). Petroglyphic images of horned figures, "masks" with the characteristics of fantastic creatures, and dead men laid to rest in suits of gold are all taken to indicate the cultural importance of the shaman figure in Siberian and Central Asian prehistory. Even mirrors have been taken to refer to a shamanistic ritual (Martynov 1979: 143145). Since shamanism indicates the concentration of spiritual power into the hands of a few individuals, the ascription of shamanism to an early society likewise would indicate that the society had evolved to the point where access to spiritual powers was indirect, and had to be facilitated through a small and select group. Moreover-and here one may return to a modern tendency to romanticize the past-"shamanism"
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



46 THE IMAGE OF THE DEER
functions as a useful catch-all to explain monstrous images (masks) or funerary rituals and furnishings which suggest a belief in an afterlife. "Shamanism" is a way of saying that we are unable to recapture the early, primitive belief in a journey to the land of the dead and in life in the hereafter. The modem perspective has erected "shamanism" as a barrier between "us" and "them," a sign of unity between humans and the natural world which can be known only by primitive societies. Assumptions regarding the shamanistic character ofBronze and early Iron Age society in South Siberia ignore basic questions: when, after all, did shamanism emerge as a religious institution and how can we know the answer in the absence of written texts? There are no references to shamanism in South Siberia before the Turkic period. No shamans' gowns, drums or drumsticks-or at least nothing recognizable as such-have ever been recovered from early burials. If the signs of shamanism were other than those associated with modem shamanism and if we knew what they were with certainty, we might be on more solid ground. The fact ramains, however, that the analogy between ethnographic materials gathered in the last few centuries and presumed characteristics in the society of the Early Nomads and their predecessors breaks down in the face of a lack of archaeological evidence. Stated simply, mask-like petroglyphs, mirrors, animal images, and sacrificed horses do not make shamanism, or at least not shamanism as we know it. The challenge facing the scholar who would use ethnography to tease out the meaning of images from South Siberian prehistory is allowing the possibility that shamanism as we know it may only be a late expression of a tradition which at that time had a vastly different form. Within the ethnographic record, as within the archaeological record, experience has been laid down in strata and over millennia; only the most archaic elements, within the most ancient layers, will offer some possibility of revealing meaning.10
This book is the result of the conviction that existing theories regarding the mythic traditions and social organization of the Early Nomads and of the other Scytho-Siberian groups do not account for their most impressive imagery, nor do they account for the embedding of that imagery within symbolic systems which persisted for centuries.None of these theories serve to reveal either the significance of the deer image or the significance of the symbolic systems of which this image was part. The position taken here is that the deer is indeed the key to the recon
10 I have discussed this question elsewhere (Jacobson 1990), as well as in Chapter VII.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE IMAGE OF THE DEER 47
struction of Scytho-Siberian culture in general and of the Early Nomads' in particular. While the deer image may have carried reference to the sun, that was only one limited aspect of the realm it represented. Moreover, if the deer image of the Early Nomads functioned as the emblem of the male warrior, that was a late and ambivalent development. I believe that maleness and warlikeness were not its original attributes and males were not its primary reference. The meaning of the deer image was, rather, rooted in an ancient identification of the animal with cosmogenesis and with the source and end of a clan's lineage. Beginnings, endings, and regeneration were all associated with this animal, as were the heavenly bodies, the rivers and mountains of this world. That unity, however, existed only in the Neolithic period and in the early Bronze Age. The history of the deer image is also the history of change in form, in context, in meaning. To investigate the history of an image and of the symbolic systems in which it was embedded is to study the process of cultural change itself. The deer of the Early Nomads was wrapped in layers of myth and ritual, hinted at in the ritual and burial structures of the Bronze Age, traced obscurely in the petroglyphs along the great Siberian rivers, and distantly echoed in the mythic traditions of remnant peoples such as the Tungusic Evenk and the Paleo-Siberian Ket. As the ancestors of those peoples left the regions of Mongolia and the Sayan Ridge, pushed northwards by the expansion of other peoples in the first millennium B.C., they carried with them mythic traditions which, when excavated from layers of story telling begin to reveal the outlines of a vital, generative being. In unwrapping the layers that obscure the image of the deer of the Early Nomads, one finds that it was rooted in a symbolic system revolving around the Animal Mother: the deermother as Tree of Life and as source of life and death. Essentially female, it was gradually arrogated to the male as a sign of his power; with time, however, that function also disappeared. By the end of the first millennium B.C., the deer had lost its visual and mythic energy; it had degenerated into meaningless ornamental scrolls, it had been translated into the image of a woman, or its absence was indicated only through the presence of a male warrior. With the disappearance of Scytho-Siberian culture, the deer was destined to persist only through the shamanistic tradition of Siberia and Central Asia. In seeking to reconstruct the symbolic systems represented by the deer of the Bronze and early Iron Ages, it is possible to consider this animal as a text: within its elaboration and modification is written the emergence, florescence, and decline of a once powerful symbolic system.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



CHAPTER THREE
IMAGES AND THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES IN THE ART OF THE EARLY NOMADS
A. OBJECTS, IMAGES, AND STYLES
Interest in the art of the Eurasian nomads was first aroused in the early eighteenth century when Tsar Peter the Great was honored with a gift of gold plaques said to be from Siberia. Cast in gold and studded with semi-precious stones, they represented realistic and fantastic animals, alone or locked in mortal combat. Peter's interest turned into fascination; he encouraged his representatives in Siberia to gather whatever gold pieces they could acquire. The resulting Treasure of Peter the Great, now housed in St. Petersburg's Hermitage State Museum, attests to the satisfaction of Peter's intense interest, as do the plundered burials across Siberia. Not a single object entered into Peter's collection with any scientific documentation. Great quantities of gold objects were spirited out of Russia and subsequently lost. One must assume that an even greater number were simply melted down for the personal enrichment of robbers and merchants (Rudenko 1962; Gatsie 1975). As a result, the magnificent objects belonging to the Sih~rio.n Treo.!lure of Peter the Greo.t have limited value within scholarship. At best, they can be used only to support hypotheses based upon excavated materials. The attention that had been focused on the Siberian gold was revitalized late in the eighteenth century by the 1763 excavation of the Litoi kurgan in the southern steppe of the Ukraine. Interest in Scythian antiquities was also encouraged by a contemporary fascination with Greek antiquities. By the mid-nineteenth century, more orderly excavations had been encouraged by the Imperial Archaeological Commission in St. Petersburg. Throughout the succeeding period and up to the present, first Russian and then Soviet archaeologists have uncovered the so-called "royal" burials of Scythia, as well as many lesser burials. Although many of the early excavations were done in a disorderly, even unscientific manner, the results of more careful excavations have permitted the development of a detailed understanding of provenance and chronology (Jettmar 1967). During the last fifty years, Soviet, Mongolian, and Chinese archae
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES 49
ologists have continued to uncover major finds across the Eurasian Steppe and Forest-steppe. The sheer number and richness of these finds defies description. Countless cemeteries have been excavated, and their small finds fill out our understanding of artistic traditions within a larger cultural context. More stunning are the large burials of individuals who must have held a privileged position in nomadic society. These large burials include the great burials at Arzhan in Tuva, and the large burials at Tuekta, Pazyryk, Berel, Bashadar, and Katanda in Gorno-Altayskaya A.O. The burials at Chiliktin and lssyk in eastern Kazakhstan, as well as excavations in the Chinese Tyan-Shan (cf. Jacobson 1985) have offered a wealth of new information about the Saka tradition. Chinese excavations at the sites of Aluchaideng, Xigoupan, Taohungbala, Yulongtai, Sujigou, and Maoqinggou in Inner Mongolia have added immeasurably to our understanding of the complexity of the art of those nomads who crowded China from the north in the late Zhou and Han periods (Tian and Guo 1986). Finally, mention must be made of the spectacular finds at Tillya-Tepe, in northern Afghanistan, excavated by the Soviet archaeologist V. I. Sarianidi (Sarianidi 1985, 1989). The thousands of gold pieces recovered there have immensely enriched our view of the art of the latest Scytho-Siberians in Central Asia, the Yuezhi. The cumulative effect of these discoveries and their publication has permitted as close a study of the art of the Sakas and Early Nomads as has been made of the Scythians. Nonetheless, the sheer wealth of material now available has discouraged the publication of comprehensive studies of Scytho-Siberian art. To date, there have been only a few studies which consider the Scytho-Siberian artistic tradition as a stylistic whole (Jettmar 1967; Borovka [1928] 1967), and none has attempted a reconsideration of Scytho-Siberian iconography. Of all the regional artistic traditions, only those of the Scythians and those at Tillya-Tepe have been well-published and analyzed with respect to the chronological development of styles and techniques. 1 The beginnings of Scytho-Siberian art are reflected in materials from the burials at Arzhan, Chiliktin, Kelermes, Litoy (Melgunov Treasure),
1 The conviction that much of the best of Scythian gold work actually reflects Greek workmanship has encouraged a greater interest in Scythian art than in any other of the Scytho-Siberian regional traditions. Several studies have focussed on artistic and iconographic impulses from Near Eastern traditions, such as those of the Medes and Urartians. Others have concerned themselves with Greek elements in Scythian art, usually assuming that the goldwork which strongly reflects Hellenic taste is the result of Greek workmanship. A smaller number of scholars have attended to issues of technology, the relationship of iconography, taste, and social change. See, inter alia, Artamonov 1969; Grakov 1971; Minns [1913],1971; Piotrovsky 1987; and writings by Rayevskiy, Kuz'mina, Mantsevich.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



50 THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES
Kostromskaya, and in the hoard recovered from Ziwiye in the Iranian plateau (cf. Godard 1950; Dyson 1965). Within this body of gold and bronze materials, the most significant animal images were those of the deer, of an eagle-like bird with large-beaked head, of a horned caprid, and of a coiled or crouching feline. The style in which these animals are represented may be described as monumental and archaic. It is best represented by the great deer of the Kostromskaya shield (Piotrovsky 1987: pl. 16), by small reindeer plaques from Chiliktin (Chernikov 1964: fig. 7), by small bird plaques from the Litoi kurgan (Piotrovsky 1987: pl. 19), and by heavy bronze ram poletops from Arzhan (Gryaznov 1980: figs. 25, 26). All these images are rendered with sharply juxtaposed planes reflective of a wood or bone-carving paradigm. Equally frequent are indications that the cells of eyes and ears were originally inlaid with colored stones. In contrast to the lavish inlay represented by plaques from the Siberian Treasure of Peter the Great, that of the early and archaic style appears to have been as restrained as the images were monumental in effect. The most powerful examples of coiled or crouching felines include the great bronze plaque from Arzhan (pl. Ila). In that plaque, originally mounted on the breast strap of a horse, a concern with modeling by means of sharply juxtaposed planes is again found. The cellular eyes, ears, and nostrils, as well as the small cell in the animal's tail, point to the original insertion of colored paste or stones. This feline is matched by an unprovenanced gold plaque from the Treasure of Peter the Great in which the modeling is fuller and the cells for inlay insertion are considerably larger (Rudenko 1962: pl. VI/1). Other early examples of the archaic feline image include crumbled gold foil images found in 1911 by Adrianov under a stone near the Mayemir kurgan in Gorno-Altayskaya A.O. (Rudenko 1960: 10-12) and the 29 small gold felines from Chiliktin in eastern Kazakhstan, dated to the sixth century (Chernikov 1964: fig. 7).2
The Arzhan feline type was frequently represented throughout the early Scythian period, but typically it is crouching rather than coiled. The
2 Two similar felines in bronze, one obviously used as a pendant, have been recovered from the early Saka finds of barrow 33 at Uygarak (VII - VI c.
