Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES 51
best known example is the gold shield plaque from the sixth century burial at Kelermes (Piotrovsky 1987: pl. 17). Closer to the South Siberian version and to the felines from Chiliktin are the small coiled feline, in relief, marking the central handle of a mirror from Kelermes 2 (Artamonov 1969: pl. 35) and that represented on a bone quiver ornament from the seventh century burial at Temir-Gora, near Kerch (Piotrovsky 1987: pl. 7). Many other crouched or partially coiled felines in the art of the Scythians (e.g., Grakov 1971: pl. XX; Artamonov 1969: fig. 17) confirm what is suggested by the monumentality of the Arzhan and Kelermes plaques: that in its early coiled or crouching form, the feline was associated with a centralized placement within a symmetrical organization of images. A number of representations of coiled animals suggest that the coiling of a predatory animal may have been more significant than the actual nature of the animal itself. A bridle plaque from Kulakovsky barrow in the Crimea, dated to the early fifth century, indicates that, at times, the feline could become remarkably wolf-like in appearance (Piotrovsky 1987: pl. 61). From the very inception of the Scytho-Siberian artistic tradition, therefore, there was estabished a small number of powerfully defined images: the deer, the feline, the caprid, and the eagle-like bird, often expressed in terms of a large beaked-head. In so far as we can judge from recovered materials, these images were virtually always represented individually, in a kind of monumental isolation. When images of predators and prey were juxtaposed-as on the gold casing from Kelermes kurgan (Piotrovsky 1987: pl. 23)-physical interaction would be implied and psychological interaction was signalled by conventional means: the posture of the feline would indicate imminent attack and the head of the deer would be raised, as if in alarm. Such compositions lend clarity to individual images such as the plaques from Arzhan, Kostromskaya, and Kelermes. In their isolation and stylization is lodged much of their monumentality; but their postures implicate the assumed presence of other animals. The archaic style represented by the Arzhan, Kostromskaya, and Kelermes plaques was fated to change. As the Scythians, Sakas, and Early Nomads developed closer ties with Greece, Persia, and China, the monumentality of the early formulations gave way to a greater sense of the animal observed in a state of nature. Realism and a sense of immediacy replaced the expression of a hieratic permanence. On the bridle trappings and shabracks recovered from the Pazyryk burials, deer twist and bolt, griffinated eagles stoop and hover, wolves and felines
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



52 THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES
sniff, leap, and pounce. On the other side of the nomadic world, in Scythia, an Hellenic concern for realism gradually overwhelmed the archaic style. Realistic proportions and organically modeled surfaces transformed the stylized animals of the northern world into images of immediate flesh and blood. Even the archaic animal types were transformed. The eagle head gave way to an eagle-griffin imported from the Near East and the Mediterranean world. The horse made its appearance beside the deer as the preferred victim of predation. The panther, or Siberian tiger, disappeared, replaced by a lion. This translation of archaic motifs was never completed in South Siberia. From the eighth to the first centuries B.C., the deer, the caprid, the raptor and the panther-like feline remained persistent elements. Nonetheless, the dilution of the archaic style is indicated by the intrusion of foreign formulations. Already at Tuekta, and more decisively in the materials from Pazyryk, the raptor becomes griffinated. Whimsical lionized animals and lion heads are introduced, probably from Achaemenid art, as ornamental elements in native felt designs ( Rudenko 1970: fig. 116, pl. 148). A lion-bull and a four-legged eagle-griffin from Pazyryk 1 suggest a playful translation of Near Eastern motifs even though the ferocity of their predation on the bodies of deer or goats belies that lightheartedness. Except in the case of objects which surely seem to be of foreign craftsmanship, human images did not make their appearance in ScythoSiberian art until the fifth, or more likely fourth, century. 3 Finds from the late fifth century barrow of Solokha and from the fourth century Scythian barrows of Chertomlyk, Kul Oba, and Elizavetovskaya, among others, indicate the Hellenistic nature of human representation in Scythian art. That human representation became increasingly common, to the point where it virtually overwhelmed the representation of animals. This is well illustrated by the superb gold vase from Kul Oba, decorated with large images of seated Scythians speaking and tending to each other and to their weapons (Piotrovsky 1987: pis. 184-187; and see related vessels from Gaimonova Mogila and Chastiye 3, Piotrovsky 1987: pls. 166173). Despite the fact that the men carry weapons, the scenes themselves are quiet and lyrical. So, too, are the images of men and boys on the pectoral from Tolstaya Mogila (Piotrovsky 1987: pl. 118). These and other representations in Scythian art stand in stark contrast to the florid
3 Earlier they appear on objects which were probably done by Urartian or other Near Eastern craftsmen. Regarding the introduction of human images in Scythian art, although with a line of reasoning which is open to serious challenge, see Raevskiy 1978 and 1983.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES 53
Helle- nic treatment of battling Greeks and Amazons on a gold scabbard casing from Chertomlyk and to the equally overdone images on the gold gorytus cover from the same burial (Piotrovsky 1987: pls. 221-222, 224225). Among the Early Nomads, the earliest human images within a native idiom are found in bridle plaques representing human heads, from Pazyryk 1 and on the great felt hanging from Pazyryk 5 (pl. Via; Rudenko 1970: pis. 91, 92, 154). There on the hanging, repeated eight times in two friezes, a seated woman holding a branch with one hand is approached by a fine male rider. The very confidence with which this scene is repeated on a hanging of monumental effect suggests that this very unusual scene belonged, in fact, to a common cultural understanding.By contrast to the confident presentation here, human representation is absent from the Ulandryk and Tashanta materials (fifth- first c. B.C.) except for a small gold-foil horned mask and a human head attached to the side of a composite deer ( Kubarev 1987a: pl. LXIl/12, 13). In contrast to the few anthropomorphic images from South Siberia, in the art associated with the Xiongnu human representation appears to have become somewhat more important. This is indicated by a number of belt plaques on which are represented wrestlers or cart drivers and carts. Unfortunately, the vast majority of this material-like the anecdotal plaques from the Treasure of Peter the Great-are chance finds and lack precise dating (Salmony 1933; Devlet 1980). In the latest material associated with the last of the Scytho-Siberians, the Yuezhi, zoomorphic imagery is totally submerged by ornamental considerations and by a Hellenized preoccupation with the representation of humans, heros, and anthropomorphic dieties (Sarianidi 1985, 1989). Throughout Scytho-Siberian art, the motif of animal predation constitutes the central representational formulation. This theme cannot be dismissed as a reflection of the fears of primitive hunting and herding peoples or as an immediate echo of the natural world. For example, it is certain that within the Altay Mountains, wolves as predators were as significant in antiquity as they are said to be today. Yet in the art of the Early Nomads, the wolf has an ambiguous role, appearing more frequently as an opponent to predatory felines and raptors than as a predator itself. The eagle-like bird also plays an unclear role within the pantheon of the Scytho-Siberians: quasi-predatory, quasi-protective, at times merged into the very body of another animal. In contrast to the actuality which surrounded the Early Nomads, herd animals never appear in the role of prey. The predation that constituted the central motif in Scytho
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



54 THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES
Siberian art was entirely acted out in the realm of wild beasts or fantastic animals. Predation was expressed in three basic formulations. The most archaic was certainly that represented by the Kelermes gorytus cover with its images of crouching felines and recumbent deer: predation implied through the juxtaposition of independent animals, the time of its occurrence frozen into an eternal potentiality. A similar predation is suggested by the bronze panther from Arzhan, placed on the chest of a living horse as if to indicate the fall of predator on its prey. The second form ofrepresenting predation is best offered by two pairs of wolves and deer carved ata site called Alty-Katyndoi on the south bank of the Chuya River in southeastern Gomo-Altayskaya A.O. Datable by style to the Pazyryk period, these images represent ferocious wolves confronting startled deer. This treatment is almost non-existent, however, within material recovered from actual burials. Far more common is the third formulation of predation, that of explicit attack. This is represented by a gold plaque from Ilyichovo, in the Crimea (Piotorovsky 1987: pl. 70), and more powerfully and explosively by the sets of animals on the shabracks from Pazyryk (Rudenko 1970: figs. 108-113). If predation, implied through juxtaposition, freezes the act into eternal potentiality, explicit representation freezes it into a perpetual actuality. Implicit or explicit, predation indicates the essential motif of the symbolic system of the Scytho-Siberians in general and of the Early Nomads in particular. It is a predation without conclusion, however. Within the eternal repetition of that act there is no death. Only at the end of the Sey- tho-Siberian period do we find animals once antlered and horned hang- ing lifeless or tom apart and devoured (e.g. ,details from the Chertomlyk and Elizavetovskaya scabbards; Artamonov 1969: pls. 185, 330). One of the most distinguishing aspects of the art of the ScythoSiberians is the manner in which animals are transformed into other animals or are joined in a complex pattern of zoomorphic junctures. This tendency appears within the archaic tradition of the seventhsixth century, the most striking example being the large crouching panther from Kelermes: its feet and the joints of its tail take the form of small, coiled panthers. Small bridle ornaments from that and other early Scythian barrows suggest that the fusing of sheep and birds, or of predators and birds, was a significant aspect of the archaic style (Piotrovsky 1987: pis. 1, 6, 10-12). A similar if more vigorous process of juncture and transformation appears in the art of the Early Nomads
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES 55
of South Siberia; examples of this material will be discussed below. The image of a deer with bird-headed antler tines offers the most important example of the Scytho-Siberian tendency to represent the process of zoomorphic transformation (Jacobson 1984). Within this transformation, the tines of the deer end in the heads of large-beaked birds, resulting in the fusing of deer, branched antlers and birds. This transformation is exemplified in Scythian art in plaques from the fifth century barrows of Ak-Mechet and Ilyichovo. The Ak-Mechet plaque (pl. Va) reveals a sturdy animal with raised, alert head and a spray of antler tines, three of which terminate with the heads of large-beaked birds. The realistic treatment of the animal's eye and ear and the ridging along the underside of its neck indicate a departure from the archaic tradition revealed in the treatment of its body. In the Ilyichovo representation (Piotrovsky 1987: pl. 70), the large deer still retains the powerfully modeled body of the archaic style, but the ornamentation on its throat and the treatment of its eye and ear indicate the intrusion of Hellenic formulations. Rearing up before the raised head of the deer is a great serpent, while a wolf or bear-like animal curls round the deer's upper body and bites into its chest, and an eagle pecks at its tail. Faced with such ferocious predation, the deer's raised neck and head and its firm body still express enormous vitality. As if they were an aspect of that vitality, its antler-tines have been transformed into the heads of birds with long curled beaks. Even the hoofs of the deer and the tail terminate in bird heads. The image clearly joins the processes of predation and transformation, centering them in the body of the antlered deer. The theme of the fusing of deer and bird takes on other variations: the head of the deer may become bird-like (e.g., a bridle frontlet from the fifth century Seven Brothers barrows; Piotrovsky 1987: pl. 68), or the bird heads may become griffinated and may sprout on the body of the deer, as in the case of a deer image from Axjutintsy (Jacobson 1984: pl. 11 ). In a number of instances, images of deer and birds are suggestively juxtaposed rather than fused. A gold horse-frontlet from Zhurovka (Piotrovsky 1987: pl. 71) demonstrates this pairing of elements. In the upper part of the frontlet we see a recumbent deer, its powerful body and the treatment of its body surfaces indicative of the archaic tradition of representation. In the center of the frontlet and facing in the same direction as the deer is a flying eagle, its body also formed by broad juxtaposed planes. Two bird heads with huge eyes terminate the lower section of the frontlet. In this object, then, deer and bird are joined to the horse which wore the frontlet, and although none of these elements refer
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



56 THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES
to transformation, the combination here of animals which elsewhere are fused is noteworthy. Whatever the specific variation, the motif of the deer with birdheaded antlers may be found repeated across the Scytho-Siberian world. Among the most impressive examples are the fantastic deer on the: tattooed man of Pazyryk 2 (Rudenko 1970: figs. 53-54), the composite deer on the wooden plaques from Katanda, and the unprovenanced Verkhne-Udinsk plaques from the Treasure of Peter the Great (pl. Ve). A number of these examples will be considered in more detail below. If the deer was the animal most frequently fused with a bird, however, it was not the only one. This merging of zoomorphs was echoed in other transformative motifs in Scytho-Siberian art. A bronze bridle plaque (early fifth century) from the Kulakovsky barrow in the Crimea represents a wolf-like animal coiled like a Siberian feline. Superimposed on the body of the animal are a caprid and small bird head, while its paws and tail terminate in long-beaked heads (Piotrovsky 1987: pl. 61). A horse-shoe shaped bridle plaquefromZhurovka (fifthcentury) is ornamented with a crouching feline. The animal's body is rendered with juxtaposed planes and its tail terminates in the head of a bird (Piotrovsky 1987: pl. 65). On a number of gold plaques from the Siberian Treasure, wolves. sprout bird heads or griffinated bird heads from the tips of their tails and on their necks (Artamonov 1973: pls. 178,280). Similar forms appear on the tattooed man from Pazyryk 2, as well as in late material from the grasslands of Inner Mongolia and northern China. The griffin-headed creatures with elaborated antlers from Xigoupan (Tian and Guo 1986: 356) only distantly recall the deer of the Pazyryk tattooes or those on the Verkhne-Udinsk plaques. The inlaid gold wolves crested with griffinheaded antlers from Aluchaideng (Tian and Guo 1986: pl. XVl/1) seem crushed beneath the weight they carry. One of the latest examples of the crested wolf transformation can be found on the carpet of the Xiongnu burial at Noin-Ula; in this last statement of the motif, dated to the first century A.D., the theme has become reduced to an ornamental flourish (Artamonov 1973: pl. 203). This brief overview of the evolution of style and motif in ScythoSiberian art indicates the outlines of a general pattern across the Eurasian steppe: the closer the nomadic groups came to centers of settled civili-· zations and the more they became absorbed into those cultures or emulated their aggressive statehood, the more completely any traces of the archaic pantheon were extinguished. The motif of predation disintegrated into conventionalized and grim reflections of death while the
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES 57
signs of transformation either disappeared or became conventional, like the rote incantation of a prayer emptied of meaning. By contrast, in those nomadic cultures which remained further from the settled crescent on the south and which never emerged into statehood, zoomorphic imagery continued in a tradition of realism or conventionalization. In the gold and bronze work from the Inner Mongolian late nomadic sites of Aluchaideng and Xigoupan and in numerous chance finds from the northern Chinese borderlands, the motifs of the predation of wolf or feline on horned animals, deer, or horses continued as a major theme, albeit rendered in terms of an increasingly lifeless conventionalization. The pattern of change across the nomadic world of Eurasia and over a period of several hundred years suggests that mythic traditions rooted in cultural patterns untouched by the traditions of settled civilizations were lodged in the archaic triad and in the combined motifs of predation and transformation. Central to those early formulations was the deer. Whatever the meaning of these aspects of the deer were, they were always articulated within a larger structure: one which postulates the vertical axis and bilateral symmetry as the sign of essential or ideal order. Even within the gradual transformation of the deer into horse, and within the final disintegration of the animal into conventionalized realism or meaningless ornament, the essential principles lodged in the representation of predation, transformation, and axial order are retained, like distant echoes of the archaic traditions.
B. PREDATION, TRANSFORMATION, AND AXIAL ORDER
Throughout the Scytho-Siberian world, but best represented by materials from the frozen burials of the South Siberian Early Nomads, the themes of predation and transformation were organized around an implicit central vertical axis. This axis was usually coextensive with the head or body of a human being or with the head and body of a horse. This axis, however, was also indicated by the pairs of plaques which were intended to be carried on the bodies of horses, men, or women. 4 The
•Unfortunately, the finest such plaques all derive from the Siberian Treasure of Peter the Great. They are therefore unprovenanced and cannot be used as primary documentation, but their general reliability is established through the finds of wooden plaques which replicate the images on those from the Siberian Treasure. Their authenticity, if not their date and provenance, is also indicated by the well-established tradition among the Xiongnu of wearing paired or individual plaques on their belts. One can only surmise that the lavish use of gold attested in the plaques of the Siberian Treasure accounts for the fact that similar plaques have not been found elsewhere; they were most likely among the gold materials plundered by ancient robbers from Early Nomadic burials such as Tuekta and Pazyryk.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



58 THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES
plaques carrying the representations of animals would have been worn in such a manner that the antlers or horns of the prey would coincide with, or frame, the central vertical axis; on either side of that axis and facing toward it the predatory animals or the larger elements of the scene would be arranged. When these plaques are paired, one finds that the central axis coincides with the representation of antlers, bird wings, and trees. That axis is reaffirmed by the positioning of the animals of prey and of predation itself. I would maintain that this material, late and unprovenanced as most of it is, nonetheless points to the direction in which scientifically excavated material will lead us.
1. Arzhan
Arzhan offers too little material that is too disrupted to allow the reconstruction of the image patterns as they were arranged on the bodies of horses or human figures. A possible exception is offered by the great, bronze, coiled feline which is believed to have been mounted as a breast ornament on a horse's cheststrap (pl. Ila). If the Arzhan plaque was used in this fashion, then the animal would have been "represented" as being coiled around its prey, much as the wolf-like animal on the Ilyichovo plaque is coiled around the deer. The Arzhan plaque thus raises the possibility that when this motif is found elsewhere, it was set on the central axis and its posture served as the sign of a centrally-located predation.
2. Tuekta
Although badly plundered, the four large barrows within the cemetery of Tuekta are believed to be among the earliest representing the Altay Early Nomads. Burials 1 and 2 establish the type which comes to represent later burials of this region. The chambers of single or double walls were constructed at the bottom of a deep pit. The dead had been placed in tree-trunk coffins and the horses killed in sacrifice were laid either outside the walls of the wooden chamber (burial 1) or above the chamber (burial 2) (Rudenko 1960: 93-108).5 No human ornamentations were found in either ofthe first two Tuekta barrows, but there are many indications that the horses sacrificed there
5 Tuekta was first excavated in 1935 by S. A. Sergeev and again in 1937 by S. V. Kiselev. A more complete excavation and study was undertaken by Rudenko, beginning in 1949.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES 59
had carried elaborate headdresses. These headdresses and the fine, carved bridle ornaments together indicate a pattern of dressing the sacrificed horse in such a way that the animal became the vehicle for carrying a set of elaborate images. By those ornaments, the horse was transformed into an antlered or horned animal, but it could also be said to carry a landscape which reaffirmed the processes of predation and transformation. For example, from Tuekta 1 were recovered two sets of large wooden horns, curved and fringed with half disks to recreate the horns of a mountain ram (pl. Ild).6 There were also four wooden structures, each composed of a curved wooden stand into which had been set a large carved antler shape (pl. Ile); these may have been assembled on the horse's head together with the large carved horns (Rudenko 1960: pl. LXXI). 7 From the same horse burial was recovered another pair of wooden horns with one interesting variation: on the top ofeach "hill" had been set the standing figure of a feline (Rudenko 1960: LXXII). The remains of gold foil on these horns indicates the original splendor of these large and magnificent structures. In some as yet unclear manner, each of these structures had been set on the head of a horse, transforming the animal into a ram or deer or into a combination of the two. The feline/ horn construction created an even more complex transformation. As the horse "became" a ram through the headdress, the felines between the hills of the horns articulated the theme of predation within a landscape setting. Bridle ornaments from Tuekta 1 and 2 reiterate the theme of transformation: from antler to leaf-like form, from antlered animal to griffinated bird (pl. Ile). One senses an almost playful willingness to let shapes and signs slide from one reference to another, yet the range of references is intentionally delimited. Many of the carved wooden bridle ornaments take the shape of assymetrical foliate forms rendered in large recurving shapes. Others seem to hesitate between a reference to plantlike foliage and to antlers, as though deliberately slurring the distinction between the curves of the two. The full curves of the foliate and antler-like ornament reflect the applique ornamentation on the felt shabracks that were poorly preserved in the Tuekta burials and superbly preserved in the burials at Pazyryk (Rudenko 1960; 1970: pls. 160-162). This similarity between fabric and carved ornamentation suggests that the apparently abstract
6 A similar but smaller pair was found in Tuekta 2; see Rudenko 1960: pis. LXIX, LXX, CIX.) 7 This placement of the antlered structures has been suggested to me by L. L. Barkova.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



60 THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES
curvilinear shapes rendered in felt also referred to plants or to antlers or to both. Several of the Tuekta 1 bridle ornaments hover between griffinated bird heads and the heads of female (?) elk and between griffinated bird heads and foliage. In many of the ornaments, the feline motif reappears, usually in the form of a frontal panther head with huge jaws and gripping in its mouth or attached at the neck to the fronds of a bird-foliate or antlerfoliate ornament. Regardless of the variation in the ornament, in every instance it is problematic how it should be read. In some cases, the juxtaposition of feline head and foliate-form suggests a highly stylized statement of predation. In other cases, there is at least the suggestion that the feline head vomits the shape from its mouth. However interpreted, both versions reaffirm complex references to transformation. Like the horns topped by felines and worn by the horse, these bridle ornaments transform the horse into a vehicle for the location of change and for the change which is located in and by predation. The Tuekta ornaments clearly establish a necessary and vital relationship between antlered animal, bird, and plant form, and a possible relationship between horned animal and feline.
3. Beret, Bashadar, and Katanda
At the plundered burials ofBerel in Gorno-Altayskaya A.O. a few horse: ornaments which also refer to deer antlers, plants, or to a combination of these were found (Sorokin 1969). In the horse burial of Bashadar 1 a pair of wooden horns covered with gold and silver were found. They had apparently been used as part of a horse headdress (Rudenko 1960: pl. XXXVIII). From the same horse burial two superb hovering eagles, carved from wood and originally covered with gold foil, were recovered (Rudenko 1960: pl. LI). They are believed to have been placed on the front and rear horns of a saddle, thus recreating the theme of a bird grasping in its talons its prey or the meat of a dead animal. This placement of the bird of prey directly on the horse recalls the original placement of the feline plaque from Arzhan. In both cases, the horse has become a vehicle for the representation of predation as an essential symbolizing process. If one consults the strength of the Ilyichovo deer, however, with its head raised high despite the predation of wolf and bird, then the Bashadar and Arzhan constructions of plaques and horses could be seen as ambiguous. We may here be presented with the idea of predation by bird or feline on the horse, or of transformation, with the
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES 61
horse or the antlered or horned animal "becoming" the bird through the merging of antlers and wings, or signified through the bird's eating of the prey's flesh. The large Katanda burial is usually dated to the Shibe phase of the Altay Early Nomad culture (third century B.C.) (Rudenko 1960; Kiselev 1951). Among the scattered indications of what had been a rich double burial were found several carved animals in a bundle of fabrics and fur. Like so many carved images from other Altay burials, these animals look like horses but have holes in their heads for the mounting of leather antlers. They thus suggest an animal in metamorphic process, between horse and deer. 8 A carved wooden plaque from Katanda is of particular interest (Artamonov 1973: pl. 196). The plaque is rho-shaped and was probably one of a mirror-image pair. It carries the representation of a bear-like feline attacking a large composite animal with the forebody of a horse but the hindquarters of a wolf or feline (pl. Vd). The animal's head is beaked like an eagle and from its antlers and tail sprout the heads of eared eagles. In shape and subject the Katanda plaque relates to and confirms the reliability of the unprovenanced Verkhne-Udinsk plaques from the Siberian Treasure (pl. Vc). 9 I have said that paired plaques such as those from Katanda or Verkhne-Udinsk would have been worn so that the antlers of the animal faced inward toward the implied central axis of the person's body. In this respect, therefore, when one recreates the pairing of plaques, one finds that the act of predation occurs at the center of the axial area and that the animal which is the object of attack seems to explode into a variety of forms while its branched antlers sprout the heads of birds. This combination of elements echoes the symbolic structures suggested by the materials from Tuekta: the conflation of antlers and birds; the resulting conflation of bird, antler, and tree mediated by the similarity of shape and placement; the association of composite horse-deer and predator; the juxtaposition of the construct of predation with that of the horse/deer/tree.
8 Kubarev has proposed that these animals were part of an elaborate human headdress similar to those more clearly indicated in the Ulandryk finds (Kubarev 1987: 110). If this is the case, then these deer would have been carried on the head of a person. 9 See the discussion of these and related plaques, provenanced and not, from Mongolia and Liaoning, in Jacobson 1984. In the wooden Katanda plaque, the masked horse has the hindquarters of a wolf, while in the Verkhne-Udinsk plaques, and others from Inner Mongolia and Xichagou, the wolf element is lacking.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



62 THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES
4. Pazyryk
The richest source of material on the Altay Early Nomads is unquestionably that recovered by Gryaznov and Rudenko from the five large burials at Pazyryk. Located in a high alpine region in the Ust Ulagan Valley, between the Bashkaus and Chulyshmanrivers in eastern Gorno-Altayskaya A.O., the burials were part of a large cemetery that included many small kurgans as well as the five large ones, all generally dated to the fifthfourth centuries B.C. 10 The burial chambers of the five large kurgans had already been thoroughly plundered in antiquity. In their haste to seize objects of value, the robbers scattered the furnishings of the chambers and mangled or dragged away the bodies. Stripped of their gold-foil covering, beautifully carved pieces of furniture, richly appliqued clothing, and other objects of both a utilitarian and decorative nature were left behind. As a result, the remains of the Pazyryk burials are extremely important for the understanding of Early Nomadic traditions of working wood, fur, leather, and wool. Within Pazyryk 1 the excavators recovered no materials which seemed to relate to headwear or systematic body ornament. A man and a woman had been buried in Pazyryk 2 and from the remains there Rudenko was able to reconstruct a woman's headdress. Shaped like a hood or flat-topped cowl, it was made of black colt's skin ornamented with an overall pattern of leather diamond shapes. At the top was a crown, or diadem, of standing leather cocks. Beyond this crested headdress, a number of carved wooden objects found in the same burial suggest other crests for human use, but how they would be mounted and on what kind of base remains a mystery. Included in this material were two bird heads with inserted leather ears and two deer heads originally fitted with leather antlers. In addition to those remains of human headdresses, Rudenko found
10 Pazyryk l was first excavated in 1927 by M. P. Gryaznov, a member of a larger expeditionary force led by S. I. Rudenko to explore the archaeology of the Altay (Gryaznov 1950). Subsequent excavations of Pazyryk 2, 3, 4, and 5 were carried out by Rudenko in 1947-49. The five large burials are dated by Rudenko to the fifth century B.C. (Rudenko 1970: 293-309). On the basis of radiocarbon and dendrochronology, Rudenko has suggested a detailed dating for the five burials within a period of48 years. Others, such as Jettmar, have proposed a broader horizon, between the fifth and fourth centuries (Jettmar 1967: 135). More recently, Marsadolov has used the scientific analysis of organic materials to propose dates for the five large burials between the late fifth century and the first halfof the fourth century, with the fifth burial being perhaps the last completed (Marsadolov 1984, 1988).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES 63
two complex and finely carved structures. One is a beautifully carved head of a griffinated eagle with leather ears and wings, holding in its beak the head of a deer. In a manner reminiscent of the composite deer of the Katanda and Verkhne-Udinsk plaques, the tines of this deer's leather antlers are transformed into small heads ofcrested eagles, suggesting the interconnection between the act of predation and the process of transformation. Assuming this was used as a head crest, the size of the construct (23 cm. long) indicates the necessity for a careful setting on some kind of stiff headgear. The other structure recovered from barrow 2 takes the shape of a carved griffinated eagle head holding a deer head in its beak. On each side of the griffin's neck a sculptured relief of an eagle with a goose in its talons is affixed. Pazyryk 3 contained only one body, that of a man, but the recovery of two headdresses from that chamber suggests that there had originally been another body. Rudenko describes only one of those headdresses, a balaclava-like covering of leather and felt topped with a gold-covered wooden structure (Rudenko 1970: 90-91). No headdresses were found in barrow 4. In barrow 5 a woman and man had been interred in one long log coffin. A wooden headdress with attached pigtail probably belonged to the woman. As a type, it is similar in appearance to that worn by the seated woman on the large felt hanging found outside the north wall of this burial chamber (pl. Via). One other group of images should be mentioned: those tattooed on the body of the man buried in Pazyryk 2. Rudenko has described the condition of this individual, whom he calls a chief, in some detail (Rudenko 1970: 110-113). On the relatively well preserved right side of his body were images of wolf and feline-like animals; twisted animals of horse, onager, or caprid type, with beaked-heads and with horns and bird-headed antler tines (pl. Vb); an antelope-like animal, confronted by a strange clawed, toothed and snouted creature with a wing-like appendage and curled up tail. Similar beasts were visible on the man's poorly preserved left arm, while on the left side of his chest were found the traces of a large predatory animal. The left leg of the man's body had been too damaged to make out the tattooed images. On his right leg were discernible a large fish, a row of four running goats, and a snouted and horned wolf-like animal with bird heads sprouting from its body. In so far as it is possible to reconstruct the imagery tattooed on the man from Pazyryk 2, there appears to have been a distinct order in its organization. The forequarters of the predatory animals on his chest had faced in towards the center. The primary images on his arms were of
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



64 THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES
composite animals. Their postures replicate the twisting of elk, deer, and rams under the impact of a predator. The tattooed images thus recreate the theme of predation, but with the predator either absent or merely indicated by juxtaposition. The heads of all the animals of deer or onager aspect face in the direction of the central vertical axis marked by the man's head and chest. This pattern reaffirms the principle advanced earlier: that the organization of predators and prey would refer to a central vertical axis, here carried by the man. Moreover, the animals represented as prey epitomize the process of transformation attendant on predation. From their heads sprout horns and antlers, the tines of which terminate in minature griffin heads. In this fantastic combination of elements, the composite animals echo the main image on the VerkhneUdinsk and Katanda plaques. 11 The transformation of a horse by the imposition of antlers or horns echoes the transformation that had been effected by the crowning of the Tuekta horses with antlered and horned headdresses. If the plundered human burials at Pazyryk add only a limited set of materials to our knowledge of the Early Nomads' headdresses, the horse burials add an immense wealth of information. Although the Pazyryk materials did not reveal horse headdresses exactly analogous to those indicated by the Tuekta finds, there are a number of parallel forms which suggest a similar tradition of elaborate headgear and similar principles of transformation underlying the ornamental motifs. The unbroken and frozen horse burials at Pazyryk allow one to reconstruct with great certainty the organization of motifs on the bodies of the horses, carried on masks, headdresses, crests, bridles, and body harness. 12 Unquestionably, the elaborate regalia in which these horses were buried goes far beyond the requirements of an ornamental display of wealth. Every horse sacrificed in Pazyryk 1 was sent to its death carrying references to the themes of predation and transformation-all organized around the vertical axis of the horse's body and head. So, too, were the horses in burial 2 and at least one in burial 5 elaborated. Each of the horses within Pazyryk 1carried a bridle, a chest strap, and a shabrack. Several of the animals were originally fitted with elaborate headdresses or masks. In each case the standing horse in regalia established a central vertical axis, on either side of which were symmetrically
11 They also recall the magnificent gold animal from Shenmu, Shaanxi province, which probably crowned a stiff headdress (pl. IVb) 12 The clearest representations of the reconstructed horse regalia may be found in Gryaznov 1950.

arranged individual elements and image constructs. Horse "A" is a good example (Gryaznov 1950: fig. 13). The axis located through his head and chest was implicitly defined by the symmetrically arranged leaf forms on the bridle. The images of eagle-griffins perched on twisted elk were reproduced on each side of the shabrack. The resulting system of images described the emergence of foliage from the predation of eagles on elk:

of leather, felt, fur, and gold. The mask took the form of a feline in combat with a lion-griffin; the griffin's head and wings became the horse's crest. Thus the horse carried a complex organization of images. At the top of the axis, a bird-animal struggled with a feline, as if for possession of the horse. At the horse's head, the juxtaposition of caprids and foliage suggests both the place of the animal in its natural world and the alternative form of that animal. On the axial center of the horse's chest hovered an eagle, while on either side of the axis was repeated the predation of feline on cervid:

The bridle of horse "E" was ornamented with carved plaques demonstrating a considerable sophistication in the reduction of a ram's body and head to a symmetrical statement of the whole animal (Gryaznov 1950: fig. 17). Full ram heads ornamented the psalia ends. On the chest
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES 67
harness, ram plaques alternated with a plaque that condensed a ram head in the jaws of a homed and snouted animal (pl. Ille). The horse's shabrack carried lion-bull images on either side, while from the shabrack hung pendants of rampant fantastic lions. This array of images was crowned by the elaborate mask worn by the horse. The mask recreated the attack of a feline laid over the face of the horse, as if attacking that animal's head, but the horse itself had been transformed into a deer by antlers ofleather and fur (Gryaznov 1950: fig. 16). The feline's body was made of blue fur studded with gold and originally must have made a extraordinary impression. It would be rash to assume that the mask was intended to transform the horse into a deer. Its purpose, rather, like that of the mask on horse ''.C," was to transform the horse into a vehicle for retelling the mythic opposition of feline and winged animal or of feline and homed or antlered animal. The result was a system of images which repeatedly affirms the association of predation with the vertical center:

A similar configuration of images was carried by horse "F" and by horse "G" (Gryaznov 1950: figs. 15, 12). In the latter case, the carving of deer on the bridle and of elk on the chest strap is particularly outstanding. The artist has skillfully conveyed the alert and nervous power of these animals. At the same time, by elongating antlers and wedging palmettes between split bodies, the artist has emphasized the synonymy of antlers and plant-like forms, organizing them symmetrically on either side of the axis implied through the head and body of the horse. The result is a powerful construction within which the conflation of antlered animals and foliage emerges from the base established by the predatory animals:

The horses buried in Pazyryk 2 had evidently also been elaborately arrayed to recreate the conjoining of predation, transformation, and central vertical axis, but little remained intact. A crested eagle head of painted leather was apparently part of a disintegrated horse headdress. Another headdress in the form of a horse cowl carried an elaborate sculpture of leather, felt, wood and gold foil representing a bird with raised wings over the head of a large horned ram (Rudenko 1970: fig. 95). In both cases, the winged bird was set at the top of the horse's central axis. Four of the five saddle horses buried in Pazyryk 5 were fitted with harnesses less elaborate than those of Pazyryk 1, but they did carry beautifully ornamented felt shabracks, including one which ingeniously incorporated a fine Chinese silk. The ornamentation of the shabracks combines references to birds (wings and beaks), antlers, and floral forms (Rudenko 1970: 160-162). Only the fifth horse carried an elaborate outfit, including a full face mask crested by a beautifully carved antlered deer head (pl. Illb). The head effectively established the deer at the top and center of the axis of the horse. The ornamentation of the bridle
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES 69
described elegantly curled recumbent deer confronted by the heads of felines; this combination of animals was juxtaposed by forks representing the jaws of wolves opening towards psalia tipped with the heads and claws of eagles (pl. Illa). The elaborately constructed saddle and its chest guard reiterated in several forms the motif of felines confronting antlered animals (Rudenko 1970: fig. 88). In effect, the horse carried a system of images which joined antlered animals and feline and wolf predators around the vertical axis of its body:
Pazyryk 5 - Horse 5 Horse Head--mask:--

felines/ elk felines/elk
While the masks and bridles from Pazyryk might have been used in ceremonial situations, they would have been too awkward and fragile for anything like everyday use. It is possible that these beautifully carved constructs were intended for burial only. They clothed the horse in a set of images which effectively established a map of significant relationships around a central vertical axis. That axis must have referred to the joining of realms of being, and the acts of predation and the processes of transformation evoked by the horses' regalia were reaffirmed as essential parts of the journey signed by that axis.
5. Ulandryk and Tashanta
The symbolic systems which were arranged on the bodies of horses at Tuekta and Pazyryk reappear in the regalia of commoners buried at Ulandryk-Tashanta, in the high and barren steppe of southeastern GornoAltayskaya A.O.(Kubarev 1987a). The burials are dated from the fifth to the first centuries B.C.; the largest number of those excavated by Kubarev belong to the third - first centuries B.C. They thus represent a late phase in the Altay nomadic tradition.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



70 THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES
Like the large burials at Pazyryk, these at Ulandryk and Tashanta are all chamber and horse burials. Unlike those at Pazyryk, however, only one or two horses were sacrificed with each burial. They were laid just outside the north wall of the chamber and did not carry any elaborate trappings. Moreover, the burial furnishings laid out with the dead were modest. They were also well-preserved, thanks to the early seeping of water into the burials and their subsequent congelation. Because of that freezing, the bodies ofthe dead lay undisturbed until excavation, making possible the reconstruction of the elaborate headdresses in which both males and females were laid to rest. The style with which the images of deer, caprids, birds, and felines were carved reveal a close connection with that found in the carvings of the Pazyryk burials. They also reveal a similar organization of images and of the motif of predation symmetrically around an implied vertical axis. This situation is examplified by finds from burial 2 in Ulandryk cemetery IV. Dated by Kubarev to the third century B.C., this was a wellpreserved paired burial ( Kubarev 1987a: 186-189, pis. LXXI-LXXV). Both bodies lay on their right sides with their heads to the east. The male was placed against the south wall of the chamber, the female just in front of him, to his north and facing north. Outside the north wall of the chamber had been laid the body of one sacrificed horse. Congelation preserved the general order of objects with reference to the bodies of the dead. Close to the man's neck were found the terminals of a neck-piece; they had been carved in the form of crouching horned felines. Near his head were found three wooden images with the body types and throat fringes of caprids. Above his head, against the east wall, was found the wooden carving of a spread-winged eagle. Nearby lay a wooden standing deer with pendants. All seem to have been originally covered with goldfoil. In immediate proximity to the dead man's skull were two images which Kubarev refers to as horses, perhaps because they lack the throat fringe found on certain caprids. However, these "horses" had holes in the top of their heads, perhaps for the insertion of leather horns. The woman's head was also surrounded by objects which had once ornamented her elaborately worked hair. A large dark area around her head indicated the remains of black dye used either generally or specifically in the elaboration of funerary regalia. On her head had been set a gold-covered diadem ornamented with swan-like birds and terminating in sheep heads.13 A gold-covered wooden horn and two other half
t3 This diadem was found in the immediate area of the woman's head, but the text description and the excavation drawing do not agree on its exact location.

cylinders were found at the back of her head. Kubarev believes that they had been used to build her hair into a mass capable of carrying the objects with which it had been adorned (Kubarev 1987: 94 ff.). Above and in front of her head lay a small diadem composed of a deer with split body, so that the head of the animal must have been placed at the front and center of her hair. Just at the top of her head, behind the deer diadem, lay the wooden carving of a caprid with gold-covered leather horns. It was set on a bronze pin stuck into the top of the woman's piled-up hair. When one reconstructs the organization of the headdresses and of the neck ornamentations worn by the couple buried in Ulandryk IV/#2, the following patterns emerge:
Headdresses from Ulandryk N/2

The headdress of the male from IV/2 carries no representation of actual predation by one animal on another. However, at the top of his head he had worn the figure of a spread-winged eagle. The felines crouched on the neck torque suggest the rhythms of the natural world: the attack of predator on prey and the subsequent contest between predator and hovering bird for the flesh of the dead animal. In the proposed reconstruction of the female's headdress, deer again occupy a central position on the vertical axis, while caprids and birds fill out the symmetrical organization of images. The burial of Ulandryk 1/12 is also dated to the third century B.C.; in it were found the bodies of a male and female, laid out as in the burial of Ulandryk IV/2 (Kubarev 1987a: 163-165, pls. XXV-XXVIII). In this case, the woman seems to have carried the more elaborate head ornamentation, as well as the images of rampant felines at her neck. In association with the simpler ornamentation of the male was found a small head of an animal, most likely a caprid (with lost horns) and hanging objects. A reconstruction of the headdresses along a vertical axis described from neck to the top of the head would result in the following ordering of images:

The single male buried in the third century grave of Ulandryk IV/1 had also been fitted with an elaborate head regalia. This included a particularly fine diadem composed of two recumbent facing deer separated by feline heads(?) and "attacked" at each end by felines with horns terminating in bird heads (Kubarev 1987a: fig. 45/1). Other head ornaments included three small carved animals; the lack of chest fringe suggests they are horses, but the general conformation of their bodies and the holes in their heads indicate that they were understood as caprids and were originally fitted with leather horns. The large standing deer above the man's head had been hung with four pendants. Four standing caprids found just above and in back of the head had perhaps been placed to either side of the deer, allowing the following reconstruction:

One other headdress may be referred to here: that worn by the single female in the burial of Tashanta 11/3, dated between the third and first centuries B.C. (Kubarev 1987: 199-100; pls. XCI-XCII). The woman was accompanied by the body of one horse, its head to the east. The remains of black dye under the woman's head indicated that her hair had
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES 73
been piled up in a tall cone-shape, at the top of which had been stuck a pin decorated with the head (and body?) of what Kubarev calls a horse but which has the small muzzle of a caprid and holes to indicate missing horns (or antlers). Somewhere in the vicinity of the lower front of her conical hair had been set a diadem of a double-bodied, single-headed animal with holes in its head to receive leather horns or antlers. The fringe under the animal's throat suggests a goat or ram, or possibly a deer. At the woman's neck were the rampant felines terminating the ends of a neck-piece. The result was a tall, conical structure with the following display of imagery:
Female Headdress from Tashanta II!3
caprid deer/caprid feline-feline
The material recovered from the frozen burials of Ulandryk-Tashanta confirms what is indicated by the horse and human regalia from Pazyryk: that within Altay traditions of the early nomadic culture, the head of the dead would be elaborately ornamented with a system of zoomorphic images usually wrapped in gold. The Ulandryk-Tashanta finds, however, broaden the implications of the Payzryk material. They indicate that elaborated headdresses were as much a part of the death-right of commoners as of the wealthy members of nomadic society, and as much a part of a woman's death-right as of a man's. The reconstructed headdresses from Ulandryk-Tashanta also indicate that the bird motif was a possible, but not necessary, part of such headdresses and was most likely to be placed in the upper part or at the top. Predatory animals such as felines were used as frequently as elements of female regalia as of male regalia. The same gender non-specificity is true of deer (standing or recumbent), caprids, and possibly even of horses, although the distinction between horses and caprids is occasionally problematic. The materials from Ulandryk-Tashanta further indicate what was also apparent in the poorer burials at Payzryk: that woman were as likely to have been buried with horses as were men. The horse headdresses and crests from the Pazyryk burials demonstrate that headdresses could become quite complex. A similar complexity is suggested by the reconstructed headdresses of those people buried at Ulandryk. In a number of cases, the crowning elements in a headdress had removable heads, suggesting that they were intended to move with
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



74 THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES
the movement of the wearer. This is true of the deer which may have crowned the head of the woman buried in Ulandryk II/5, of a standing deer found near the jaw of the man buried in Ulandryk IIl/4, and of the deer diadem which crowned the woman buried in Ulandryk 1/12. The resulting movement of elegantly carved and gold-covered ornaments must have been quite striking. However, assuming that the conviction existed that there was another life after death, we cannot be certain that these ornaments were intended for use in this world. Gold may have been valued as ornament or because of some mythic signification, but whatever its meaning, the finds from Tuekta, Pazyryk, and Ulandryk-Tashanta make abundantly clear that headdresses, crested masks, and related regalia carried significance beyond the requirements of ornamentation. 14
If the Early Nomadic materials establish a pattern of symbolic systems carried on the bodies of humans and horses, materials from other parts of the Scytho-Siberian world elaborate on that pattern. They indicate that such systems had to have been considered essential at the time of death and interment. While their elaboration indicates a finely developed sense of the ornamental, the very repetition of specific themes consistently organized around a central vertical axis indicates that these symbolic systems were not driven by ornamental considerations. Their existence and function were part of an enterprise attendent on death. The materials deriving from the larger Scytho-Siberian world describe several processes of change. These changes occur in the formulation of the images themselves and reflect the intrusion of tastes and values from outside the nomadic world. However, they also reflect the way in which concepts rooted in the Siberian world of forest and foreststeppe were gradually reformulated under the influence of Hellenic, Persian, or even Chinese traditions. Landscape implied became landscape represented. Animals of the forest were replaced by those of the Near Eastern and Greek world or even by human images. Nonetheless, within all those changes, the pattern of organization around a central vertical axis continued to reaffirm the essential principles established in the Pazyryk materials.
14 The finds of Ulandryk and Tashanta are expanded by Kubarev's recently published excavations at Yustyd (Kubarev 1991). Particularly relevant to this discussion may be included burials XII/ 8, XIl/12, XIl/16, and XII/21 from that site.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto
