6. The Crown Ornament from Aluchaideng
In 1972, a hoard of more than 218 pieces of gold was discovered in the sandy soil ofa commune near Aluchaideng, Inner Mongolia. Aluchaideng is located in the Ordos plateau at the north edge of the Mu Us desert, in that region of valuable grassland which was much contested by nomads and Zhou-Han Chinese. It is believed that the objects came from two graves belonging to the Bai Yang or Lin Hu of the Xiongnu confederacy in the late Warring States period. 15 The most impressive object within the hoard was a gold cap topped by a standing goldeagle with amoveable head ofturquois and naturalistically patterned feathers (pl. IVc). 16 The cap over which the eagle spreads its wings is scalloped and scrolled on its outer edge. Within each of the four equal sections of the cap, a wolf interlocks with the twisted body of a wild caprid. Associated with the cap was a three-piece gold crown belt modeled in a braided rope pattern and terminating in the recumbent bodies of a horse, a caprid, and a crouching, snouted wolf. Cap and belt were probably sewn onto a large headdress of fur or fabric, of the kind worn until recent times by such peoples as the Burials, Tuvinians, and Mongols. By style and by subject matter the Aluchaideng crown is closely associated with Early Nomadic traditions. 17 The appearance of the eagle at the top of the crown recalls the placement of a bird at the top of the man's headdress from Ulandryk IV/2. The Aluchaideng crown also suggests the original use of two gold eagle-like birds in the Siberian Treasure of Peter the Great (Artamonov 1973: pls. 267, 270). The smaller of the two birds is naturalistically rendered. It stands on a broken plaque fitted with three holes for attachment. The larger of the two birds
15 Tian and Guo 1986: 342-350. This find included 218 gold pieces, 5 silver pieces, and about 45 stones for inlay. As a group, the Aluchaideng finds are of immense importance in reconstructing the artistic traditions of the Ordos before Xiongnu political hegemony and outside Chinese cultural hegemony. Together with materials from other sites in Inner Mongolia--e.g., Xigoupan, Taohungbala, Yulongtai, Sujigou, and Maoqinggou-they offer the opportunity for an informed reconsideration and redefinition of Ordos art. 16 The eagle is 7.3 cm. high; the weight of the whole crown is 192 grams, while the weight of the three segments together is given as 1202 grams. 17 Other objects within the hoard confirm this association. Among the most interesting are twelve inlaid gold plaques in the form of crouching wolves, their tails and neck crests terminating in the heads of griffinated eagles (Tian and Guo, pl. XVl/1). This motif obviously echoes, albeit in much clumsier form, the crested wolf tattooed on the right foot of the man buried in Pazyryk 2, and the magnificent crested wolves from the wolf/tiger plaque pair (unprovenanced) from the Siberian Treasure of Peter the Great (Rudenko 1962, pl. VI/3,4; and see Jacobson 1984).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



76 THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES
was originally inlaid with turquoise; it holds in its talons the stylized body of a swan. At its base are holes and hooks by which to attach it to a larger object. Like the eagle on the Aluchaideng crown, this bird also has a moveable head. On the Aluchaideng crown ornament, the representation of wolf predating on caprid is repeated four times. In every case the representation is stylized to the point ofunrecognizability. Although the wolf heads cross the bodies of their victims, none of the animal pairs reflect the psychological impact of predation.
7. The Issyk Headdress
The most lavish human headdress from the early nomadic world is that from the undisturbed burial at Issyk, in the Tyan-Shan territory of the nomadic Sakas (K. A. Akishev 1978). In burial ritual and in the lavish use of gold plaques and gold covered objects, the Issyk burial is easily associated with both Altay and Scythian precedents. The dead man had been laid out on his back, his head to the west. His short jacket and boots had been entirely covered with sewn-on gold plaques. By his side had been placed a gold-sheathed sword and long sword. This headdress, however, was the most remarkable find (pl. IVa). It took the form of an exaggeratedly tall nomadic hood covered with representational gold plaques. According to Akishev's reconstruction, the front of the hood was adorned with composite animals combining horses with winged caprids and facing out from either side of an implied central axis. Above these composite creatures were arranged large gold feathers, goldwrapped and tipped arrows, wing-like motifs and vertical objects of gold with a painted pattern. Around the hood's sides and back plaques of twisted and winged felines, frontal feline heads, tri-peaked and zigzag mountains, profile caprids standing with feet together as if poised on .a ridge, and pole-trees topped by profile birds were distributed. At the top of the headdress a standing gold ram was somehow affixed. However many plaques were actually attached to the Issyk hood, 18 the
18 Different reconstructions of the hood have been proposed by K.A. Akishev and by his son, A. K. Akishev (1984). The setting of the solid gold ram at the top of the headdress would have necessitated a stiff internal frame. The interrelationship of caprids and horses on the front of the hood is also problematic. Although this is the only such well-preserved headdress, finds from plundered Saka burials at Tenlik and Alagou, in the Tyan-Shan, indicate that this may well have been a particular type of headdress that was not uncommon. Issyk has been dated to the fifth century, also, but for a number of reasons that dating seems early by one or two centuries (Jacobson 1985).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES 77
ensemble would have recreated a mountain setting, the top of which was crowned by a horned ram. On the sides of the mountain-hood, birds sat on trees and felines lived and predated on mountain caprids. On its front, a composite of caprids and horses was mediated by wings. The Issyk headdress thus combined images of predation, transformation, and axial order within a structure rooted in a pictorial realism. It translated into the realistic terms of a mountainous setting the mythic events implicitly and mythically referred to in the Aluchaideng headdress ornament and in the Tuekta and Pazyryk horse outfits. In the case of the Issyk headdress, the implied axis was that of a mountain, and the ram with the curved horns was associated with the top of that mountain. In this respect, the Issyk headdress recalls the caprid horns which had been mounted on a horse from Tuekta 1, and the horns with standing felines from the same barrow which suggested the transformation of horn into a hilly landscape. In the case of the earlier Tuekta headdress, the wooden horns served as a metaphor for the axial mountain and the felines referred statically to the existence and function of the predator within that setting. In the case of the Issyk headdress, representing later Saka culture, the mountain image is explicit and the representation of predatory felines within a mountainous setting is unmistakable. Juxtaposed, the Tuekta horns and the Issyk headdress suggest an evolution in the representation of mythic traditions, within which the mountain was a central reference point. Considered as a group of organizing systems, the horse and human headdresses from Tuekta, Pazyryk, and Issyk indicate that there existed at least two models for articulating the vertical axis. In addition to the mountain form recreated by the Issyk headdress, there was the vertical axis created by symmetrically arranged foliage, wings, and antlers. The transformation of antlers into tree-like forms, the branches of which end in bird heads, suggests that antlers and wings were a metaphor for trees or for the central axial tree. The Issyk headdress suggests that the explicit representation of the mountain may have been a late development, one dependent on the emergence of a concern for realism.
8. Late Scytho-Siberian Headdresses and the "Woman under the Tree"
Within the Early Nomadic funerary regalia, the headdress was clearly a central element. It could take one of several forms-diadem, cowl, hood, or crown. The simplest headgear was a diadem, usually fashioned from wood or leather and often covered in gold. They were frequently carved
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



78 THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES
with scenes of animal predation organized around the implied central axis. Diadems were associated with females and males and they might have been used to crown a cowl of perishable material. As far as can be judged from surviving materials, diadems seem also to have been a common form of ornament among the far western relatives of the Early Nomads, the Scythians. Fine gold examples have been recovered from Kelermes, Kul Oba, Olbia, and the Three Brothers barrows near Kerch (Piotrovsky 1987: pls. 45-46, 183; 223; Minns [1913] 1971: figs. 288289). These diadems, however, are all ornamented with the floral elements, fantastic beasts, and technical intricacies which indicate Greek taste and possibly Greek workmanship. They carry no reference to the Siberian images of animal predation nor does their ornamentation refer to a single central axis. The cowl recovered from Pazyryk 2 indicates the Early Nomadic use of a head cover of perishable material. It seems to have been a variation on the nomadic hood, of which the Issyk version offers a magnificent, if impractical, version. 19 There are many scattered materials which indicate that the hood and the cowl were a standard form of head cover among the Scytho-Siberians. The most vivid representation of the Scythian hood may be found on the gold vessel from Kul Oba. A number of the figures there wear what appear to be soft pointed headcoverings which fit closely down over the wearers' ears and neck. Males were not, however, the only ones to wear hooded head gear. At Ulandryk and Tashanta, both males and females could carry pyramidal structures or hair piled up in pyramidal form. The woman in the late burial of Tashanta II/3 had worn her hair in a large conical shape around an internal frame. At the top of her headdress had been placed the head (and body?) of a deer or horse. At the front of her hair was a large carved deer with split body and a single protruding head (Kubarev 1987a: pl. XCil/11). Hooded shapes were sometimes worn by Scythian women. The woman found in the fourth century barrow of Chertomlyk had also been buried in some kind of a hood or pointed headdress (Minns [1913] 1971: 161; fig. 45/16). The headdress had been ornamented with 57 gold plaques showing a seated female holding a mirror up before a smaller male figure who drinks from a rhyton (pl. Vic). The seated woman wears a soft cowl which falls over her head to her shoulders. In another plaque
19 A hardened crested hood recently recovered from Ak-Alakh, Gorno-Altayskaya A.O., may represent a simple funerary version. That crest indicates the probable manner in which the crests from Ulandryk and Yustyd were mounted on headgear (cf. Kubarev 1987a, 1991).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES 79
associated with the woman buried in Chertomlyk, a similar headdress is worn by a frontally seated woman (pl. Vld). In this case, the woman is approached by a smaller male on her right, while on her left stands an altar or branch. Yet another gold plaque from the Scythian traditionthis one cruder and of seemingly late date-was found at Merdzhana in the Kuban region (Artamonov 1969: pl. 331). Here a woman is depicted sitting frontally, holding a bud-shaped object in her hand. Her headdress takes the form of a long cowl, falling on either side of her head. To her right stands a tree; to her left, a post is topped with the head of a horse. Approaching from the left is a man on a horse. Artamonov has pointed to the non-Scythian aspect of this figure's clothing (Artamonov 1969: 82), but his hair and beard are Scythian in style and texture. Moreover, the image of the seated woman in relationship to a male is reminiscent of the images on the Chertomlyk plaques. These several instances of the same scene are certainly related to the images on the large hanging from Pazyryk 5. In every instance, a male on foot or on horseback addresses a seated woman as if in the position of suppliant or worshipper. In each instance, also, the woman is associated with a tree, a branch, or an altar. A young woman interred in the third century Karagodeuashkh burial, in the Kuban, had also worn a tall and pointed headdress ornamented with a triangular gold plaque (Piotrovsky 1987: pl. 232). The plaque appears to have been cut out of a larger composition divided into three horizontal friezes. In the lower section a seated woman wearing a tall pointed headdress is represented; she holds a large cup while on either side three males (one visible only through his arm) pledge her with raised cups or rhyta (pl. Vie). Behind her stand two woman in cowls. In the middle frieze a robed woman stands between two horses, and in the topmost section stands a single frontal female. Although much in this burial reflects Hellenic taste and workmanship, the images in this gold plaque seem to refer not to Greek representational traditions, but rather to representations found elsewhere in the nomadic world-as in the Chertomlyk plaques and the Pazyryk hanging. The hood and the cowl were not the only significant forms of head gear among the Scytho-Siberians; no less important was a kind of blocky crown associated with women. One such crown was found with the woman buried in Pazyryk 5. According to Rudenko, the woman had been partially shaven, but to her headdress of wood was attached a long pigtail composed of her own hair and horse hair (Rudenko 1970: 104; pl. 66/a). Curiously, one finds the same kind of blocky headdress and shaven head in the case of the woman repeated eight times on the wall hanging from
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



80 THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES
the same burial (pl. Via). It is not simply the great size of this hanging nor the clearly superior position of the woman which impresses th1! viewer. The hanging conveys a powerful sense of solemn purpose, carried in large measure by the female image. She holds one hand to her mouth in a gesture which might indicate speech. In her other hand she grasps the large branch with fanciful foliage. The images on the Pazyryk felt hanging certainly refer to some mythic tradition and in some manner to the processes catalyzed by death. We do not know how this hanging was used, but Gryaznov has suggested that it was hung around the bier of the dead during the actual construction of the burial chamber. 20 It is significant that an almost identical grouping of male and female occurs in the plaques from late Scythian burials, referred to above. Equally evident is the correspondence between the images on the hanging and those on the pair of anecdotal plaques from the Siberian Treasure of Peter the Great (pl. Vlb). Those plaques, also, convey a similar sense of solemnity. Like the woman in the hanging, the woman seated beside the tree is shaved and wears a blocky crown terminating in a long projection which loops up into the branches of the tree like a flexible rope or pigtail. The fine young horsemen recall the handsome rider on the Pazyryk hanging. Gryaznov is certainly correct in seeing a narrative purpose in these plaques (Gryaznov 1961). The scene creates the impression that death has called one of the riders, and that the moment of death is somehow bound to the figure of the woman under the tree. Similarly, the felt hanging from Pazyryk 5 can be associated with death by its placement outside the north wall of the burial chamber; its imagery in turn joins the female figure and tree, just as the plaques from Chertomlyk, Merdzhana, and Karagodeuashkh indicate that representations of seated women addressed by males carrying rhytons or cups and in association with altars or branches were in some manner appropriate to the rituals accompanying death. No blocky wooden headdresses have been found in Scythian burials, but gold crowns of a similar blocky shape have been found. A particularly impressive example was found in association with the woman buried in Chertomlyk. Standing 25 cm. high, it includes four friezes with tendrils and battling animals and two rows of hanging buds or nuts. Another version was found in the fourth century burial of a "priestess" at Great Bliznitsa, but there the images represent Amazons battling eagle-griffins (Piotrovsky 1987: pls. 226-228, 233).
20 I am grateful to L. L. Barkova for this information.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES 81
The most striking example of the block-shaped crown actually comes from a post-Scythian burial at Khokhlach in the Don-Dniepre steppe. The find is traditionally called the Novocherkassk hoard; it is associated with a Sarmatian woman, that is with a high-ranking member of the group which displaced the Scythians in the last years of the first millennium B.C. Despite the lateness of its date, the crown is of great interest-not only because it represents the outflow of the ScythoSiberian tradition, but more because it indicates the extent to which an Hellenic tradition of realism had by then transformed the bird, caprid, and deer images of the earlier nomads. 21 The center of crown is marked by a carved stone female bust. On either side of the stone portrait were arranged large bezeled stones and modeled gold eagles, each set frontally and inlaid with stones. Along the upper edge of the crown were arranged elaborate cast and inlaid elements: at the center, a large leafy tree, and on either side of the tree, antlered deer, horned caprids, smaller trees, and water birds. The leaves of the tree hung on rings to allow movement with the movement of the crown's wearer. Some other pendant must also have hung from the noses and beaks of each of the animals and birds since they were all fitted with gold rings. The organization of images on the Novocherkassk crown reaffirms the association of deer and tree (or of caprid and tree), and of tree, bird, and antlered or horned animal. Equally important is the association of a tree and female image with the central vertical axis. This organization recalls the implied axis created by the pairing of plaques from the Siberian Treasure, where the woman sits under a tree, bound by the extensions of her headdress to the branches above her. There are two other crowns or crown substitutes which fill out this discussion and confirm the probability that the tree and the female represent a late translation of images earlier rendered in other forms.
21 The hoard has been dated by Minns as late as the second-third centuries A.D., primarily on the basis of the chalcedony bust of an "empress" mounted on its face (Minns (1913] 1971: 235-236, 280-282). The identification of this image, however, and the consequent late dating are insecure. Sulimirsky associates the hoard with an Alanic group of Sarmatians from east of the Urals and proposes a date in the mid-second century A.D.. He refers to a tradition associating women with elaborate diadems or crowns auested to in finds from Eastern Europe as early as the second millennium B.C. and the widespread continuation of that tradition among Alanic peoples (Sulimirski 1970b). The crown was damaged when found and has been variously reconstructed. According to the version published by Minns and taken from N. P. Kondakov, two caprids would flank the central tree on either side, followed by the deer. In the reconstruction of the crown in the Hermitage Museum and as published by Sulimirsky (Sulimirski 1970b: fig. 57), deer flank the central tree, followed by caprids and another pair of deer.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



82 THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES
Both crowns come from the burials at Tillya-Tepe in northern Afghanistan. The site is dated between the first century B.C. and the first century A.D. and appears to have belonged to a ruling group of the pre-Buddhist Yuezhi centered in Bactria. As such, it may be characterized as the latest intact burial site associated with the early nomadic peoples of Central Asia. The materials found in the Tillya-Tepe graves reflect the eclectic tastes of a group of nomads who were poised to become the center and force behind the Indian Kushan dynasty. Nonetheless, within those eclectic references are clear traces of the older nomadic artistic tradition, transmitted in the form of richly inlaid gold ornaments and scenes of animal predation. The female buried in Tillya-Tepe 6 was laid in a coffin with her head to the west and pillowed in a shallow, silver dish. The crown she wore was made of a flexible gold band to which were attached five magnificent cut-gold trees (Sarianidi 1985: 46-48, pls. 12-15; Sarianidi 1989: 117-120, 144). In the upper boughs of the trees were cut-gold birds. Soldered to each of the trees were flowers with central bezels for inlay surrounded by granulation. Disks were attached by twisted gold wire to the petal and to the lateral branches of the trees. Similar flowers with attached disks were soldered to the forehead band; in some of the central bezels traces of the original turquoise inlay inlay. In its overall dimensions and generalized shape, the crown must have resembled the large headdress found in Pazyryk 5, those worn by the woman of the Pazyryk 5 wall hanging, and that worn by the woman in the plaques from the Siberian Treasure. In the case of the plaques and wall hanging, the tree either shades the woman or is held by her. In the case of the Novocherkassk crown, the tree rises above the image of the woman (and the presumed female wearer of the crown). In the case of the Tillya-Tepe crown, the tree is the crown and crowns the axis of her head. The delicacy of the crown's workmanship, its deliberate fabrication to reflect the movement of the wearer, and its imagistic reference to the natural world recall the earlier Altay and Saka traditions of headdresses, the delicate movement allowed in the Aluchaideng headdress and in one of the birds of the Siberian Treasure, as well as that of the Novocherkassk crown. Every aspect of this crown refers to a specifically nomadic tradition rather than to any crown tradition associated with Greco-Bactrians, Seleucids, or Parthians. 22
22 According to Sarianidi, Greco-Bactrian, Seleucid, Parthian, and dynastic Kushan rulers wore narrow fillet-diadems rather than crowns. Parthian noblewomen wore veils secured by narrow tiara (Sarianidi 1985: 48). Kushan coins indicate a great variety of helmet types and crest ornaments, but nothing analogous to the tree crown.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES 83
The other headdress recovered from Tillya-Tepe was apparently a construction intended for use within the burial ritual. This crownsubstitute (Sarianidi 1985: pis. 112-121; Sarianidi 1989: 89-90, 135135) was found in the male burial of site 4, where a number of objects done in a clearly nomadic tradition were also found. According to Sarianidi, the head of the deceased was laid on a small cushion cradled in a gold phiale which carried an inscription in Greek. Attached to the side of the bow1was a golden tree with dangling disks and pearls and the beautifully modeled figurine of a ram with full curl horns. Sarianidi believes that the ram had been fabricated by a Greco-Bactrian master in the period preceding the Yuezhi hegemony and that it was inserted into the crown construction because of its status value. But if the ram were foreign-made, it nonetheless fit within the representational traditions of the nomadic world. 23 It is not clear how the tree and ram would have been attached to the phiale. The base of the tree has four perforated wings, while the feet of the ibex were each fitted with a disk for attachment. The rim of the phiale, on the other hand, shows no perforation by which to attach tree or ram. Originally, both tree and ram must have been attached to some other objects but were reassembled here in proximity to the man's head in response to some compelling ritual concern. The Tillya-Tepe headdresses confirm the proposed association of tree and death. The resulting organization of elements-tree, birds, horned or antlered animals, and the vertical axis marked by the head of the dead-recalls the earlier Issyk hood and the Novocherkassk crown. In the Novocherkassk crown, the tree at the center was combined with deer and flanked by horned caprids. In the Issyk headdress, the hood referred to a mountain and the head of the central axis was marked by a horned animal. The tree, the central axis, and the associated birds on the TillyaTepe woman's crown translate into realistic images the principles of order embodied in a number of the Altay headdresses and plaques. Within that order, the horse could be transformed into a deer or a caprid. Considered in light the symbolic systems indicated by head wear from Issyk, Novocherkassk, and Tillya-Tepe, it is possible to understand the deer's bird-headed antlers as a metaphor for the branches of trees, just as the Tuekta wooden horns with standing felines are revealed as a
23 The identification of the species of animal is difficult. Its compact body, large curled horns, and neck ruff suggest an urial or possibly a tur, but the pronounced curl of the horns and their particular ridging is more reminiscent of an argali. The accurate identification of this animal would have to take into consideration, among other elements, the ecological niche occupied by the intended animal and the relationship of that animal to a total mythic tradition among the nomads (cf. Geist 1971; Schaller 1977).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



84 THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES
metaphor for a mountainous setting. The Novocherkassk and TillyaTepe crowns, however, are far distant from the archaic visions of the early nomadic world. What earlier had been symbolic metaphor became translated into direct representation. Gone were any indications of the animal predation around which the earlier Altay headdresses were developed. The realism submerged itself in decorative flourishes and reasserted throughout the Tillya-Tepe finds reflects a specifically GrecoBactrian taste: a taste informed by urban sophistication and imperial ambitions rather than by the archaic mythic order of the northern Altay. The use of a tree as a crown is not known within Greco-Bactrian traditions, nor can such ornamental trees be found outside the early nomadic world during the first millennium B.C.24 The trees from TillyaTepe may be compared with three unprovenanced gold trees with movable hanging leaves in the Siberian Treasure of Peter the Great (Artamonov 1973: pis. 274-276). One was intended to be attached to some kind of a base; the others were perhaps mounted on staffs. The sculptural treatment of all three recalls the tree from Tillya-Tepe 4, while the realistic leaves echo those hanging on the trees of the Novocherkassk crown. The Tillya-Tepe finds suggest that the unprovenanced trees were used in a similar manner: in life the purpose unknown, but in death to mark the head-the significant axis-of the individual buried. Although no headdresses have been found which can be dated earlier than those from Pazyryk and Tuekta, the sophistication involved in the imag.._ry and organization of those examples indicates an established and thus presumably older tradition. The repetition of archaic crouched and coiled felines, large beaked-heads, recumbent caprids and deer throughout the materials of seventh and sixth century barrows-Arzhan, Chiliktin, Kelermes-indicate the outlines of an archaic mythic formulation. Objects from these early sites and others join individual images into instructive relations. Thus, the Kelermes gorytus cover (Piotrovsky 1987: pl. 23) combines the crouched feline and recumbent elk, drawing together, as it were, the separate prey and predator. The gold horse frontlet from Zhurovka, barrow 401 (Piotrovsky 1987: pl. 71) joins archiac images of a recumbent deer, an eagle, and beak-heads. It is only
24 The single possible exception to this rule comes from the Han dynasty. These were the so-called "money trees" associated with the goddess Xiwangmu and her cult, but they were not used as crowns (Wu 1987). By contrast, the gold tree crowns from the Silla Kingdom of Korea, fifth-sixth centuries A.D. (Choi 1979: pis. 1-37), offer a fascinating analogy to the nomadic tree crowns, just as the timber chamber burials from that period offer parallels to early nomadic burial rituals. The difficulty with the Korean material is that it is apparently considerably later than the materials from Tillya-Tepe.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES 85
in the material from Tuekta and Pazyryk that one begins to find a certain indication of the complex symbolic systems which joined these images conceptually as well as visually. Within those systems, animals alone or joined through the act of predation are repeatedly referred to a central vertical axis. The consistency with which that structure reasserts itself in the Altay material indicates that significance was accorded to vertical axial order, to bilateral symmetry, and to the ordering of images in terms of predation and transformation. One is led to conclude that these symbolic structures referred to a significant mythic order, the reiteration of which was essential at the time of a person's death. The continuity of the tradition established in the materials from Tuekta and Pazyryk is marked in later finds. The Aluchaideng headdress ornament, dated to the early fourth century B.C., belongs also to that Altay tradition as it extended south into the Ordos. The Issyk headdress clearly reaffirms the significance of the central vertical axis and of the bilaterally symmetrical arrangement of predation and transformation motifs. The burials at Ulandryk have been reliably dated between the fifth and first centuries B.C. The headdresses from Tillya-Tepe and Novocherkassk are the latest in our chronology. In their imagery they join realism to the patterns of a mythic order. Taken as a specific artifact type, the early nomadic headdress for humans is represented by only a small group of examples-too small to allow any absolute conclusions regarding meaning. The consideration of the headdress proper can be enlarged, however, by the consideration of other forms of head ornamentation, such as diadems, cowls, and hoods. When to _this material is added the elaborate horse regalia from Tuekta and Pazyryk, clear patterns in the ordering of images begin to emerge. Those patterns become even more clearly defined when the horse outfits and their structural order are compared with recovered sets of human burial regalia and with unprovenanced and provenanced plaques. The headdress was part of a ritual regalia carried by humans and by horses. It reaffirmed a central vertical axis around which were elaborated the structures of symbolic animal predation and attendant animal transformation. Within that transformation, the association of antler, foliage and bird was critical, as was also the association of horn and mountain. The headdresses from Issyk, Tillya-Tepe, and Novocherkassk, however, rooted as they are in realistic imagery, intimate that the earlier vertical axis represented by horse and human and elaborated by images of animal predation was in fact a tree or a mountain: the Tree of Life or the World Mountain, joining the realm of the living with that of the spirits. Within
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



86 THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES
such a system, wolves and felines were the symbols of that change which was catalyzed by death but which in fact was the process of metamorphosis from one form of being to another, and thus from one realm to another. The horned caprid of the mountain and the antlered deer of the tree were thus the most fitting symbols of the unity of destruction and renewal encompassed by metamorphosis and passage. Griffinated, the eagle-like birds referred to raptors which seize their prey on the ground and carry the meat to their nesting young. From its participation in this visible cycle of renewal, bound to earth, tree, and the heavens, the raptor functioned as the being that most appropriately symbolized and sanctified the process of death and renewal. These considerations illuminate the bird's ambiguous presence on the Aluchaideng headdress, its feathers adorning the lssyk headdress, its griffinated forms in the Pazyryk headdresses, and its frontal prominence on the Pazyryk chest straps and bridles and on the Novocherkassk crown. Within such a symbolic system, horses were incidental to the underlying mythic structure, at least in the early period. They served as the steeds on which the dead made their journey to the next world, and they served to carry significant imagery, particularly that referring to deer antlers and the Tree of Life. The horse trappings from Tuekta and Pazyryk and plaques such as those from Verkhne-Udinsk and Katanda argue, however, that the horse in regalia was a practical solution to a mythic need: the requirement for an antlered animal to serve as the dead man's vehicle. The materials from Ulandryk indicate that it was only in the late nomadic period that horses in their own right may have become a legitimate part of that mythic process of death and renewal. Moreover, within the late first millennium B.C., realism became a value in and of itself. That emergent realism, unquestionably joined to profound changes in political and religious life, may ultimately have forced the submergence of the animal-spirit which was both the source of life and the guardian of the dead: that spirit from which emerged the World Tree or which existed as a metaphor for the World Mountain. The axis revealed in the headdresses of the Early Nomads and in the regalia of their horses was rooted in the certainty that death was a process ofchange that occurred along or through, or by reference to a tree (of life) or a (world) mountain. The process of the journey represented by tree or mountain could also be indicated symbolically by other image structures: by antlers of leaf-shaped form and by antlers with bird-headed tines; by horns or by horns which assumed the configuration of mountains; by the forms of animal predation. At the same time, the process or
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE SYMBOLIZING ORDER OF IMAGES 87
journey symbolized by axis, tree, mountain, antlers, or horns was associated with an animal-spirit, one which ruled over both life and death. In its archaic stage, this animal spirit was an antlered or horned animal, cervid or caprid. Its antlers were a metaphor for the Tree of Life while its horns may have referred to the World Mountain, or axis mundi. Working backwards, however, through the gradual emergence ofimages referring to the real world and through the gradual appearance of anthropomorphic representation indicates that the original animal-spirit was female in gender. Represented as a deer or caprid in the art of South Siberia and as a deer throughout much of the early Scytho-Siberian period, with the passage of time and the influx of Near Eastern and Hellenic traditions of representation she gradually assumed the form of a human female. She might be seated holding a branch or represented under a tree, or she might be recreated as the dead under the tree crown. It is certain that this being was rooted in ancient Siberian traditions and that her original substance was that of an antlered or horned animal. Across the Early Nomadic world, the reiteration of a seated female in association with a post or a tree and addressed by one or more men insists again upon the existence of a common mythic tradition. The woman's appearance in the Pazyryk felt hanging does not indicate that she was a sudden import from outside the nomadic world. It more likely refers to the circumstance of survival of otherwise perishable materials. It also refers, however, to the anthropomorphization of mythic powers which earlier had been given only zoomorphic form. 25 The material detailed in this chapter suggests that the tree and the mountain mediated between the symbolic systems ofvertical axial order, zoomorphic predation, zoomorphic transformation, and the figure of a female. But who was this female and what was her place in the world of the dead? The clarification of these questions requires a broader range of material: that embedded in the stone carvings and paintings of Neolithic and Bronze Age South Siberia.
25 If one excludes the human imagery which derives from Near Eastern (perhaps Urartian) sources, (e.g., that on the gold sword scabbard and silver mirror from Kelermes), then it is only in the fifth century barrow of Solokha that one finds in Scythian art the intrusion of human representations where there earlier had existed only the representations of animals. It is also in this period that the archaic images of the early nomadic world begins to give way to Hellenized forms: the panther begins to turn into a lion, the eagle or beak-head into an eagle-griffin, and the deer is gradually replaced by a horse.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



CHAPTER FOUR
ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS FROM THE NEOLITHIC AND AENEOLITHIC PERIODS
A. METHODOLOGICAL CONSIDERATIONS
Rock carvings and paintings constitute the most substantial body of information for the study of ancient South Siberian artistic and mythic traditions. Yet this resource is accompanied by significant obstacles. The first such obstacle is obvious: the great number of recorded images still does not insure inclusiveness. While it is possible that the most important petroglyphic complexes have been recorded, every year brings the discovery of yet more sites containing new material: material capable of upsetting carefully constructed understandings of chronology and cultural diffusion. More problematic is the destruction of known sites. In many cases that destruction has occurred through time and by the elements. Cliffs formerly covered with images now lie crumbled as a result ofrock falls or due to the more prosaic processes induced by freezing and thawing. 1 However, time and the elements are not, unfortunately, the most effective agents in this destruction. Human acts, particularly those of this century, have been far more efficient in wiping out the petroglyphic record of millennia. The construction of the Bratsk hydroelectic station on the Angara River inundated many superb Neolithic petroglyphic complexes between Ust'-Uda and Zayarsk. The construction of the Sayan-Shushensk hydroelectric project on the upper Yenisey has resulted in the inundation of the Mugur-Sargol complex in Tuva. The Krasnoyarsk reservoir on the Yenisey submerged many of the richest petroglyphic sites dating to the Neolithic and Bronze Ages in the Minusinsk Basin, such as those at Ust' -Tuba, Shalabolin, and Oglakhtin. Such destruction prevents significant reconsideration of the original images within their original settings.
1 The site of Saimaly-Tash in the Fergana Range, surely one of the most important petroglyphic complexes in Central Asia, is also one of the most spectacular examples of destruction by earthquakes and rock fall (Bernshtam 1952; Frumkin 1970: 44-47). The small site of Yalbak-Tash over the Chuya River in Gorno-Altayskaya A.O. exemplifies the more prosaic and more common crumbling of smooth surfaces as water and plants work their way into the cracks of the rocky faces.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS 89
More complex than the accidental or purposeful destruction of sites are the problems created by deliberate overcarving and recarving. When in modern times such overcarving occurs we are likely to call it vandalism. This kind of overcarving, however, has been occurring for thousands of years, complicating and enriching the petroglyphic record. At times such reworking actually helps to clarify chronology. At SaimalyTash, for example, the carving of a two-wheeled cart over the images of a deer and bull indicate a chronological sequence which could facilitate more generalized, relative dating (Sher 1980: fig. 110). An elk carved over the images of two boats at Ust' -Tuba III on the middle Yenisey also indicates patterns of chronological sequence (Sher 1980: fig. 80). To be meaningful, however, such superimpositions must be repeated on a regular basis. Only then does circumstance depart from the accidental and indicate the outlines of a reliable historical process. More troublesome are those innumerable instances where later images are inserted around or between earlier images, thus creating the impression (for the modern viewer) that certain representations would actually occur together. Since the technique of carving may not vary radically from one period to another-at least not before the early Iron Age-and since wear and tear may obscure subtle distinctions in execution, it is not necessarily possible to determine which images go with others. An example of this is offered at Kalbak-Tash by the juxatposition of an ordered row of deer with large figures arranged in ritual postures. There is no way of telling if the two groups of images were done at the same time and refer to some common ritual practice or myth. It is not difficult to imagine a Bronze Age artist taking advantage of the row of deer to carve a new group of figures who have some reference to wild animals; it is also possible, however, to imagine that such an artist simply took advantage of a large empty space on the east-facing wall-a space that happened to be next to a row of deer. Two other complications of an intellectual character must be mentioned here. One has to do with the established tradition of assigning to particular images a predetermined symbolic load. This approach concludes by shaping the emerging "new" culture in terms of known traditions originating outside the particular region being studied. The newly interpreted image becomes a sign of that foreign culture's intrusion. In some cases, these assumptions seem reasonable. For example, the female elk images associated with the Baykal Neolithic are sufficiently consistent in style and techniques of execution that their appearance suggests a continuity of culture, rather than some kind of fortuitous
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



90 ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS
coincidence. When these images appear within South Siberia but outside the established perimeters of the Baykal Neolithic one may safely conclude that they are signs of the intrusion of the more northern culture. On the other hand, the appearance of female elk images in the Ural Mountains and in parts of Karelia and Scandinavia does not necessarily indicate an actual continuity of culture; it may rather indicate certain cultural commonalities among Neolithic peoples of the northern Eurasian forest zone. Similarly problematic are the so-called "masks" of Mugur-Sargol in Tuva, the images of wheeled vehicles ("chariots") found throughout the petroglyphs of northeast Central Asia and South Siberia, the mushroomhatted figures so frequently arranged in ritual poses, and the rows of dancing figures like paper-cut dolls. In considering the trails of these images across broad regions, we must bear in mind that at each stage of removal from their homelands, the myths and rituals underlying imagery are necessarily reformulated. Distance, changes in climate, changes in physical geography, and the resulting pressures placed upon traditional economies must have deposited their own sediment of meaning over cultural layers already represented by older images. Thus, stability of outer form does not predetermine a continuity of meaning; indeed, such stability more likely masks constant change within the symbolic load of images. Related to the issues of image diffusion and the concommitant modification of symbolic load are the interpretative distractions inherent in the very naming of images. "Chariots," "sun-heads," "bulls," and "warriors," offer but four examples of the manner in which terminology effectively ends by prejudicing critical discourse. Such terms are invariably freighted by references to other traditions where texts exist to clarify images. In those cases, the texts help one to ascertain the existence of, for example, solar worship, a warrior class, or the association of chariots and aristocracy. In light of Indo-European and Near Eastern traditions, "chariots" conjure up a warrior, aristocratic elite with its own social and political presumptions. The term "sun-head" or "solar-mark" assumes the nature of the referent-the sun-and shapes the assigned meaning in terms of the solar cults of textually known traditions from the Near East and lndo-European traditions. Throughout the intellectual discourse surrounding the study of South Siberian and Central Asian petroglyphs, the cultural load carried by terminology has gradually been shifted from old references, derived from distant traditions, to new images. This discussion cannot promise to evade altogether
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS 91
the dangers involved in naming images. The necessity of creating a common vocabulary may frequently seem to outweigh a concern for refining the relationship of terminology to image. To the extent possible, however, I will attempt to avoid the pitfalls of assigning culturally-laden terms to image types.
B. THE FIRST TRADITION:
THE ELK OF THE SIBERIAN NEOLITHIC PERIOD
The most characteristic image of the Baykal Neolithic period is that of the female elk. Monumental in effect and often in size, usually represented as if in full stride to the right, the thousands of repetitions of her figure attest to her centrality in contemporary religious beliefs. The region in question included, to the east, Lake Baykal and the lower Selenga Valley, and to the west that region defined by the upper Lena, the Angara, and the middle Yenisey, with extensions west to the Tom' River. Okladnikov has divided the Baykal Neolithic into a number of stages: the Isakovo stage (fourth millennium); the Serovo stage (fourththird millennium); Kitoy stage (third-early second millenium B.C.) (Okladnikov 1959a). The great petroglyphic representations of elk and in particular of female elk are dated to the Serevo stage; during the Kitoy, that image lost its impressive monumentality. While that dating may put the great images of the elk far too early, there is no question that theyor rather, she-is a product of the Neolithic period and that she had effectively disappeared by the beginning of the second millennium B.C. The image complex centered on the figure of the female elk and so characteristic of the Baykal Neolithic is best represented by petroglyphs found on the upper reaches of the major Siberian rivers. Along the Tom' River, major sites include cliffs south of Tomsk, another site further south, nearTutal'skoe, and a third group further south, near Novoromanovo (Okladnikov and Martynov 1972). The Angara River was particularly rich in Neolithic imagery, with major sites located throughout its flow from a point north of Irkutsk to just north of Bratsk, and again along the lower Angara in the region of the inflows of the Irkineeva and Kamenka rivers (Okladnikov 1966).2 Okladnikov has also identified a number of important sites in the region of the upper and middle Lena River (Okladnikov 1977; Okladnikov and Zaporozhskaya 1972). 3
2 Much of this material has now been lost under the so-called Bratsk Sea. 3 This Neolithic tradition may be compared with that recorded in the northern Ural Mountains (Chernetsov 1964, 1971) and in Karelia (Ravdonikas 1936; Formozov 1969;
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



92 ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS
The petroglyphs of the Tom' River typify this Baykal Neolithic imagistic complex. In most cases, the images were scraped and chippedinto cliffs which faced to the south. The elk are almost always antlerless and thus certainly female. The outlines of their bodies and four legs are represented with an extraordinary realism, revealing the vitality of this majestic animal (pl. Vllf). Their heads and bodies are characteristically outstretched, the legs bent, the whole form expressive of the animal's surging power. Sometimes the elk are only outlined; at other times their heads are treated in silhouette but their outlined necks and chests are treated with internal lines, in a so-called "x-ray" style. To the same broad period are dated the images of boats with vertical lines which are believed to refer to human figures or human souls (Okladnikov and Martynov 1972: fig. 113).4 Because these early and monumental elk images usually occur on south-facing cliffs and because they are frequently accompanied by enigmatic circles, Okladnikov and Martynov have concluded that sun cults and solar rituals figured importantly in the life of Neolithic Siberia.. The boats frequently underlying or overlaying the elk refer to the passage of the dead from one world to another (Okladnikov and Martynov 1972: 251-252).5 The fact that the elk images are predominantly female would indicate that the religious beliefs in question referred to that animal as the source of life. Assuming that the boats do refer to the passage after death, one may conclude that the juxtaposition of elk and boats indicates that the female elk must also have been invoked in funerary rituals. The finest images from the region of the Tom' River are certainly those of the Tom' and Tutal' sk groups, while those of the Novoromanovo cliff are considerably cruder. By virtue of their frequent placement over the images of boats or elk, the scattered and strange anthropomorphic figures certainly belong to a later period, most probably that of the early Bronze Age. As for the scattered images of anthropomorphic figures
Savvataev 1970, 1977). These traditions, in turn, suggest significant connections with those of Neolithic Scandinavia, with its characteristic images of elk, boats, and horned anthropomorphic figures. 4 Also included in this early layer of images are bear images, stripes, disks, and possibly circles with inset circles or with spokes, as well as heart-shaped masks which suggest panSiberian connections both with the Amur Basin and Neolithic imagery from the northern Urals (Okladnikov and Martynov 1972: figs. 185,217,242, 244, 272; Okladnikov 1971; Okladnikova 1978). 5 Okladnikov's interpretation is certainly strengthened when one recalls that still today, among many northern Siberian peoples, the dead are "buried" above ground by placing the box in which they are laid on top of their sled, or by placing the dead person in his boat; see, e.g., Gracheva 1971.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS 93
with bird-like heads, Okladnikov and Martynov date them to the Bronze Age, primarily on the basis of their comparison with images on pottery shards from Samus IV, as well as with the bird-headed figures engraved on a stone from Tas-Khazaa, in the Minusinsk Valley, dated by most scholars to the Afanasevo culture ( Kyzlasov 1986: figs. 159, 233; Okladnikov and Martynov 1972: 187-191; Lipskiy 1961; Leont'yev 1978).6 The second great depository of Baykal Neolithic imagery was certainly that of the Angara River before the creation of the Bratsk Sea. The images datable to the Neolithic period include representations of elk, fish, and seals, characteristically carved and chipped-in. 7 The great variation in the size of the Angara elk (pl. Vllh) and the constant overlapping of one figure by another also suggests that a ritual significance resided in the visual repetition of these animals, who normally do not move in herds. Like those of the Tom' River, the Angara elk are virtually all female and are represented in profile with four legs, striding (usually to the right) or standing still. The finest images are rendered with an intense sense of the living animal: its powerful body, the great hump over its withers, its massive head, and soft drooping upper lip. The images of both elk and seals8 testify to the economic centrality of hunting within the Baykal Neolithic. No less monumental or expressive of the natural animal than the Tom' River images, these elk of the Angara Neolithic nonetheless demonstrate a regional stylistic differentiation. Here the animals are somewhat bulkier, the hump of their shoulders more softly curved, their necks and heads heavier. Images of boats appear again among the Angara petroglyphs, as do those of frontal figures; both types of images are frequently done in reddish pigment or in a engraved technique different from that of the massive elk figures. These differences indicate a somewhat later period. A composition from the Second Stone Island includes a massive female elk and an anthropomorphic figure on skis behind her. In this case, the difference in engraving techniques would indicate that the human figure
6 Cf. similar "bird-headed" anthropomorphic figures from the Shaitan group, Middle Urals (Petrin 1987). 7 For the sake of simplicity, all rock images-carved, chipped, or painted-will be referred to as petroglyphs. was added to the earlier animal image. 9 A group of curious mask-like images from the same island was done in an engraved technique. The images are crude and broken in their remaining patterns, but what remains does suggest a relationship with the strange mask-like faces of the pre-Okunev or Okunev culture of the Minusinsk Basin (Okladnikov 1966: 37, 134-135, pl. 32). In both cases one finds adistinctivereduction of an anthropomorphic entity into the mask-like head; the face is marked by a curious and often faint reference to eyes, nose, mouth; and the masks carry horn-like projections. A number of strange, frequently homed frontal figures from sites such as First Stone Island, Second Stone Island, Third Stone Island (pl. VIie), Balya Sukhaya, Bol'shaya Kada, and Manzya are dated to the Bronze Age by Okladnikov on the basis of their comparison with anthropomorphic figures on pottery shards from the Bronze Age site of Samus IV, near Tomsk (Okladnikov 1966: pls. 29, 90/3-4, 95/9, 103/ 1,105/2, 135, 159, 161, 164, 168; Okladnikov and Martynov 1972: 189). These figures bear an interesting similarity to a frontal horned figure found in 1962 on the upper Yenisey (Lipskiy 1970: 164, fig. 2b), as well as to figures painted on the slabs of the Aeneolithic (late third-second millennium B.C.) Afanasevo burial at Karakol, in Gorno-Altayskaya A.O. (see below). Like many who have looked at these images, Okladnikov refers to the Angara images as shamanistic figures. I shall return to this interpretation in Chapter VII. The cliffs over the upper Lena River were also carved and painted with the images of animals and anthropomorphic figures from the Neolithic and Bronze Ages, as well as with many representations of animals and horsemen from the Iron Age Kurikan tradition. Among the earliest images are female elk, their full bodies distinguishable from elk rendered with antlers and in a more slender style. 10 The horned and occasionally ithyphallic figures are certainly from a later, Bronze Age date. The repetition of images of female elk and boats along the valleys of the great Siberian rivers indicates the extent to which belief systems may
9 Elk were not always engraved into the rock: within the Second Stone Island representions, the rendition of both large female elk and boats in reddish pigment indicate that technique is not necessarily a firm indicator of relative chronology (Okladnikov 1966: 38, pl. 34). 10 In the case of the images at Tai' ma, Okladnikov fails to distinguish these two types of elk (cf. Okladnikov 1977: pis. 56-64). Tal'ma 9 includes a number of anthropomorphic figures which Okladnikov assigns to the Aeneolithic Glaskov period (c. 1800 - 1300 B.C.) and the Bronze Age because a number of them significantly overlay the images of Neolithic elk (cf. Okladnikov 1977: pis. 60, 62).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS 95
be shaped by existential realities. The rivers were one of the two most important lifelines of the Siberian Neolithic cultures: they were a rich source of food, they were the roads that led to the land of the sun, the south. They were, however, also the roads which led to the land of cold and darkness, the north. The forests surrounding the rivers were the natural habitat of elk, animals which must have been a primary source of food, skin, bone, and antler. It is not difficult to imagine how boats could become a metaphor for the passage of the soul after death into another world, and how elk could function as metaphor for the everrenewed source of human sustenance. This world of the north was not, however, immutable. The appearance of horned anthropomorphic figures indicate the elaboration of belief and ritual in the ensuing Bronze Age. These images and the appearance of masks at the Second Stone Island in the Angara may also indicate the infiltration of another tradition in the late third and early second millennia B.C. If figures and masks indicate a northward penetration of cultures into the region of the Baykal Neolithic, there is also ample indication of the penetration southwards of that culture. That process is indicated in the rich mix of images found at a multitude of sites along the upper Yenisey River. At a site near the confluence of the Sosnovki and Dzhoy rivers with the Yenisey, for example, a frontal figure with rayed-head, a series of stripes of the type associated with the Angara-Lena tradition, three female elk in the Tom' -Angara style, and a boat have been recorded (Lipskiy 1970: 165, fig. 2). Elk images with large bodies, heavy heads, and softly curving upper lips have been found at II 'inskaya and at the site of Tepsey II. In the latter case, the outlined body of a large female elk lies below the silhouetted and striped-style bodies of cattle, indicating the overlay of a northern culture by a cattle-dependent culture, probably in the Aeneolithic or early Bronze Age (Sher 1980: figs. 62, 71 ). At a number of other sites on Mt. Tepsey many elk images of the Tom' and Angara type, as well as overlaid images of cattle on elk and of elk and boats have been recorded. Such combinations of images probably reflect the contemporaneity of elk and boats and the intrusion of a culturecertainly post-Neolithic-for which cattle imagery was more significant (Sher 1980: 150, figs. 74, 76, 77,79, 80, 83).11 Shalabolin, on the right bank of the Tuba River in Krasnoyarskiy
a Other sites along the Yenisey where images of elk-virtually all characteristically female and facing toward the right-have been recorded include Oglakhtin, Bychikha, and Cheremyshnyi log, four km. above the village ofBaykalovo (Sher 1980: 160 ff, figs. 90, 93, 94, 95.)
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



96 ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS
Krai, includes an unusually rich layering of images in which elk and boats appear below other imagery-most notably, the images of strange masked figures and cattle. The vast majority of elk images are lacking in antlers and thus may be considered representations of females (pl. Vllg). 12 In other cases, in terms of relative size or the manner in which the animal is arranged with deer and cattle (pl. Vlld) of similarly realistic representation, but relatively static posture, the antlered elk appear to be of a later, Bronze Age date (pl. VIie). Boats also appear regularly in the Shalabolin complex. In one instance, for example, a boat with vertical stripes crosses over the upper body of a powerful female elk moving to the left (Pyatkin and Martynov 1985: fig. 83). In another case, two boats appear beneath the full image of a female elk. It is possible that one of these boats originally crossed over the body of another elk, of which only the head and neck are now visible (Pyatkin and Martynov 1985: fig. 28). Among the southern-most instances of Baykal Neolithic imagery are the paintings of female elk at Turochak on the Biya River, 60 km. from Teletskoe Lake in Gorno-Altayskaya A.O. (Okladnikov and Molodin 1978). These images are rendered in a style most comparable to that established by the Tom' River complex: the female elk are represented with four legs, moving to the right, their bodies sometimes done in openwork and striped patterns. Turochak is not, however, the most southern occurence of individual examples of the Neolithic elk. In the burials at Karakol were found a number of slabs which carried painted and carved images. The partial or complete images of female elk underlay all the other images on the stones. Moreover, a number of these stones had been reversed or turned in their reuse, so that the positioning of the older elk images was rendered senseless. Kubarev dates the burials to the late third- early second millennia B.C. and thus to the Aeneolithic or early Bronze Age (Kubarev 1988). Because of the complexity and importance of the images at Karakol, I will consider them in more detail below. The elk images at Shalabolin, Turochak, and Karakol indicate a finger-like penetration of the Baykal Neolithic culture up the Yenisey and into the northern Altay. The obvious chronological primacy of the female elk image over a vast stretch of South Siberia surely indicates an ancient belief system in which that animal held a central place. As the source of life for her own species and as sustenance for other animals and
12 A parallel problem of dating elk to the Neolithic or Bronze Age by reference, in part, to the appearance of antlers occurs in the case of petroglyphs from the Lena River.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS 97
humans, it is reasonable to suspect that she was viewed as a magna mater, as an essential element in the survival of individuals and communities. At the same time, the frequent overlay of the elk by boat images and the relationship of the Neolithic elk to major rivers suggests that if the rivers were considered by Neolithic cultures to be the paths souls took to the world of the dead, then the female elk in turn came to be associated as closely with death as they were with life. The petroglyphic tradition repeatedly confirms the proposition that the elk image had essentially disappeared before the appearance of the images of realistic cattle and antlered deer.
C. THE SECOND TRADITION:
ANTHROPOMORPHIC IMAGERY OF THE AENEOLITHIC AND
EARLY BRONZE AGE
We have seen that at some time in the early second millennium B.C. a new set of images made its appearance in South Siberia. Represented by petroglyphs along the Tom', Angara, and Yenisey rivers, and in a distinctively varied form at Shalabolin, at Karakol and Kalbak-Tash in Gorno-Altayskaya A.O., and along the Chuluut Go! in Mongolia, these images focus our attention on forms which seem to combine human and animal elements in what could only have been mythic references. The images stand frontally or in profile, hierarchically still or as if in rapid motion. At times they are reduced to masked conventions; at other times, they are detailed down to their feet and hands. Some are clearly ithyphallic, others are not. Many are female in reference, combining allusions to anthropomorphic females with elements from birds, bovines, and snakes, and sometimes with references to bears. Since the images appear in a number of variations at Karakol in Gorno-Altayskaya A.O., that site offers a useful starting point. In 1985-86, Vladimir Kubarev and his colleagues excavated five burials which had been partly disrupted by bulldozers preparing the site for construction of a community building. The burials took the form of small cists formed by stone slabs. The slabs themselves had been elaborately painted and carved with layers of images, each layer referring to a separate period of time. By virtue of their preservation for thousands of years, the Karakol slabs offer an uncontaminated record of pre- and early Bronze Age cultural layers (pl. VIIIa-f). It is true that most of the Karakol imagery has little to do with the deer image: only the lowest layer, that marked by the image of the elk, testifies to a cultural
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



98 ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS
preoccupation with the cervid. On the other hand, Karakol serves two important functions, both related to the ultimate concern of this book. By offering a controlled opportunity to examine layers of imagery in a pre-• and early Bronze Age context, it allows a more certain unraveling of the chronological layers in other petroglyphic contexts. Secondly, the excellent state of preservation when the graves were excavated may help to clarify the imagistic references carried by general carvings and paintings from other, open-air, sites. All the burials were constructed from fitted stone slabs, similar to those associated with the Neolithic Isakovo culture of the Angara Valley and very much like the burial structures characteristic of the Aeneolithic Okunev burials of the Minusinsk Valley (early second millennium B.C.). A number of the slabs were marked with carved and painted images on both their inner and outer faces. The images represent several generational layers, with the lowest level of images referring to the: Neolithic period. Furthermore, in a number of cases the slabs had been turned, so that older images were inverted and then overworked as if to obscure, if not obliterate, their appearance. As a result, one can "excavate" layers of imagery on the Karakol slabs, some of which may be separated by generations, others by only a short period of time. A good example of the resulting complexity is offered by the slab which had been set behind the head of the dead man, to the west, in burial 2, kurgan II. 13 In its found position, the slab indicated the reuse of a surface which had been turned clockwise 90 degrees. Its lowest layer, in found position facing downwards, included at the top of the slab a full figure of an elk moving powerfully to the right. In the center and on the right was the head of another elk, this one possibly carrying a small antler. Both images were rendered by a chipping technique and seem to have been done at the same period of time. Kubarev believes that three sturdy caprids in the lower half of the stone may have been done at the same time (Kubarev 1988: 39-40). The next layer over that of the elk included three anthropomorphic figures which had also been chipped-in. Two were represented with frontal heads, great "feathered" headdresses, and legs suggesting they were striding to the right. The headdress of the figure on the left is rendered with particularly long feathers and an outer rim of circular elements, reminiscent of the "eyes" in the feathering of some large birds.
13 This is stone #3. The analysis of layers follows that of Kubarev, but with variations. Kubarev distinguishes five layers of images, but I believe that there may actually only be three principle layers; cf. Kubarev 1988: 37-44, figs. 26-29.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS 99
Another figure with frontal head and two horns may also have been striding to the right, and in the lower right corner of the slab were the remains of a chipped-in head with feather headdress. This second layer of imagery also included a particularly strange figure with long legs bent and stretched as if running to the right. Although badly damaged, the head of this chipped -in figure was marked by a great double loop projecting up and ahead. Similar loops were visible in the lower right comer of the slab and to the right of the running figure's front leg. The overlay of images indicates that the anthropomorphic figures were chipped-in later than the elk. The fact that all the images discussed so far are set so as to move in the same direction also indicates that whatever the original location of this slab--carved when it was still part of a cliff face or used as a dressed slab---the original directionality was honored, if not the integrity of the images. In the case of the topmost layer, however, this was not true. In preparing the slab for its placement in the burial, the maker turned it to the right and superimposed a new figure over all the existing images. This was a large frontal figure with outstretched arms (pl. VIIld). Its body and arms were rendered in darkred color and its large round head and huge bovine-horns were scratchedin. The figure's decisive and formal frontality lends it a distinctive monumentality. A similar chronological process is indicated by the imagery on stone 1, burial 2, kurgan II (pl. VIiia). The lower level includes the chippedin figures of two "loop-headed" figures moving to the right. Between their rendition and the final reuse of the stone for placement in the burial, the slab had been turned 90 degrees to the right. The topmost layer included four figures done in red, black, and white coloring and standing in hieratic frontal postures. The heads of the two on the left of the stone were surrounded by feather-like rays. The preserved face of one was rendered in schematic lines indicating eyes, a mouth, a vertical line between the eyes, and a horizontal line above the mouth. The arms of both these figures terminated in long shapes reminiscent of heavily furred tails. The two figures to the right carried on their heads projections reminiscent of horns or rabbit ears. Their faces were indicated in a schematic, mask-like manner and their raised arms terminate in large three-clawed "hands." Stone 1 of burial 5 had originally carried the chipped-in outlined body of a fine female elk moving to the right (pl. VIIlb). In reusing the slab, however, the artists had again turned it 90 degrees to the right. Three large figures dominated the final upper layer (Kubarev 1988: fig. 43). On
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



100 ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS
the left was painted a frontal figure with feathered headdress similar to that of the left-most feathered figure from stone 3, burial 2. The feathers around its face are reduced and stylized, producing a masklike appearance, and its hands are replaced by long furtails. On the right of the slab and placed over the figure of the inverted elk was painted another frontal figure with arms raised and extended to the sides, its head reduced to a conical shape with blunt upper end and no features. The central figure combined a profile treatment of the head, of the placement of the arms (to the front and back of its body), and of its legs, thus suggesting movement to the right. Hanging from each "hand" was a branched stick. The most peculiar element is the figure's head. It wears the same kind of feathered headdress as does the figure to its left, but the sideview of the head indicates a distinctly animal-like aspect reminiscent of a bear. From the forehead of the creature emerges a small double loop similar to those of the large loop-headed figure on stone 3 of burial 2. This slab indicates that profile and frontal positioning could exist within the same layer of imagery and thus within the same cultural layer. Furthermore, the appearance of the loop-heads here on the topmost layer and elsewhere on earlier layers suggests that reuse of the stone may actually have occurred within a relatively short period of time and that layers do not necessarily indicate cultural distinctions. The appearance together of painted and carved feathered headdresses also indicates that distinctions in technique may not indicate distinctions in cultural levels. Featfo,red headdresses, animalistic heads, claws, branched "hands," and tail-like hands indicate that the beings represented here combined anthropomorphic and animal characteristics. The masklike treatment of many of the faces is of particular interest. It could refer to the actual wearing of masks by ritual performers, but it could also refer to the face of a deity: terrible, awesome, inhuman. In the case of all of these figures there is no indication of sex. This contrasts with the plainly ithyphallic figures found in the Tom', Angara, and Lena complexes and indicates either that there was no sexuality associated with these non-human beings, or that they were understood as female. A few other slabs from the Karakol burials are covered with similarly complex images. On one slab was found the image of a full frontal figure with outstretched arms, three-fingered hands, and large crescent shaped horns (Kubarev 1988: fig. 49). To its left were the remains of a large figure with a mask-like head and large crescent shaped horns similar to the large figure on the topmost layer of stone 3, burial 2 . On a broken slab from the same burial another large frontal figure with scratched-in
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS 101
roundhead and horns was painted (Kubarev 1988: figs. 58-59). The body of this figure was somewhat different, with large straight shoulders from which a streamered robe seemed draped. To its right can be seen a scratched-in and painted figure with what seem to be the remains of looped horns. On stone 3, burial 5, faint painted remains revealed a large frontal figure with furtail hands and a large feathered headdress (Kubarev 1988: 66-68, figs. 50-54). Beneath this figure and again indicating that the stone had been turned clockwise 90 degrees were several other elements, including the carved-in figure of a large moose and a featherheaded figure walking to the right. Here again, the appearance offeatherheaded figures on two different levels and in two techniques indicates at most a distinction in generational levels within the same culture. The larger and more hieratic frontal figures are clearly the "latest" in the series represented on these slabs. On the upper section of another badly damaged slab from the fifth burial, it was possible to make out the remains of three figures rendered in a dark raspberry color (Kubarev 1988: fig. 55). Two stood frontally with spread legs and hanging "arms" or sashes. Similar images, painted, scratched, and chipped-in, may be found on yet other slabs; they frequently overlay the images of elk and those of the loop-headed or feather-headed figures. The layering of engravings and paintings on the Karakol slabs and the inverting, reversing, and reworking of used surfaces allow a number of tentative conclusions. The imagery of realistic elk and, perhaps, of realistic caprids, had been devalued by the time of the construction of the graves. The culture responsible for these burials seems to have valued beings which combined certain animal and human elements. The zoomorphic references carried by these beings are to birds (feathers, wings, claws) and cattle (horns). Secondary references may be carried by bear, wolf, or wolverine elements (heads, tails). The loop-headed figures (pl. VIIIa, e) are strange, but not unique. Several such striding images have been recorded at Shalabolin, on the Tuba River (pl. VIia). The meaning of the loop-shape is not clear. Kubarev believes that it refers to a bull horn and indicates a cult of a bull deity, related to the solar cult indicated by the feather-headed figures (Kubarev 1988, 94-101 ). 14
14 A number of petroglyphic motifs are recorded which could support a bovine interpretation of the loops even if they do not necessarily indicate a bull cult. At Dalangash, on the border between Gorno-Altayskaya A.O. and northern Mongolia, a group of square-bodied cattle with loop-shaped elements on their head, as if they were loop-horns filled in, has been recorded. The cattle-definition is provided by the bodies and the tails (Okladnikova 1976: fig. 2).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



102 ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS
Novgorodova has related the appearance of a post-Neolithic bull cult (Novgorodova 1989: 85-86) in Mongolia to images of frontal figures, outlined and frequently represented with raised hands, small or absent heads, and bodies which include the backbone and ribs in a fish-spine manner, with a lower body composed of a "skirt" of hanging lines. In some cases there is also added a pronounced indication of the pubic area (Novgorodova 1989: 89-113). There can be no mistaking the female aspect of these figures, and for that reason Novgorodova refers to them as images of the ancestral mother, associated with the (male) hoofed totemic ancestor. She also calls attention to the treatment of the hands of the "mother" images in terms of three-claws, and the fact that the occasional horn or antler-like projections on their heads suggest a conflation of male and female progenitor. Most of these frontal female images have been recorded within the Chuluut Gal complex, but a number of fine examples may also be found at Kalbak-Tash, a rocky ridge in Gorno-Altayskaya A.O. above the Chuya River, about 14 km. east of the Chuya's confluence with the Katon'. The frontality and the unusual treatment of the hands and headgear of these images (pl. IXa,b) suggest that they represent a regional variation on the theme of frontal, horned, or feather-headed figures from Karakol; their presence indicates a cultural connection with the culture represented by the Chuluut Gol figures.
