The skirts of the Kalbak-Tash figures are reminiscent of the spread tail feathers of a raptor, while their small heads and claw-hands raise the possibility of referring to them as "bird-women." Among this group of images is also a frontal figure which suggests a human in a long-feathered robe (pl. IXd). This particular image is directly comparable to several surviving figures from Karakol: the large frontal figure in streamered robe from burial 2, described above, another from the same burial, and a third from burial 5 (pl. VIiie, f). In contrast to the "bird-women" from Chuluut Gol, those at Kalbak-Tash are not associated with bovine images but rather with the large images of deer. These clearly belong to a later layer of petroglyphs, as is demonstrated by the deer carved over the skirt of one of the women (pl. IXc). In the petroglyphic imagery of Chuluut Gol, Kalbak-Tash, and Karakol, hieratic frontality seems to have carried a greater significance than did the profile or three-quarter positions; but that distinction is blurred by other circumstances. In the imagery of Karakol, both frontal and profile figures could wear similar feathered headdresses. The movement suggested by some of the walking figures from Karakol is puzzling:
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS 103
where are they going and with what purpose? Perhaps here, as in the case of the Neolithic elk images, is indicated the conviction that the vitality and power of the being represented-zoomorphic or anthropomorphic--could only be expressed in movement. This vitality is also expressed in the frontal figures, but in another manner: their erect postures, forward gaze, and extended arms and legs suggest a vitality held in arrested tension. In considering the extraordinary wealth of information encompassed in the imagery of Karakol, it is also useful to consider another aspect of that information-namely, the elements which are absent. None of the images offer any indication of their sex, except in the inclusion or exclusion of antlers in the case of the elk. There are no images of bulls or of deer, and only three representations of caprids. There are no boats or boat-like elements. On the other hand, the culture responsible for the late Karakol images conceived of its significant deities in terms of quasibird, quasi-bovine anthropomorphic forms, or it assumed that those deities had to be addressed through the mediation of such animal beings. The appearance of these images in burials further suggests that the images themselves, or the beings to which they referred, had to be invoked at the time of death, either as a sign and instrument of death or as a sign and instrument of renewal. Karakol leads one in search ofrelated imagery as a way ofdiscovering relevant cultural connections and as a way of seeking out meaning. In the immediate region of Gorno-Altayskaya A.O. or to the southeast or southwest the search ends only in tentative connections. I have related the frontal images from Karakol-those with aspects of both birds and bovines-to the "bird-women" from Kalbak-Tash and Chuluut Gol. I believe that all these images represent local variations upon the same theme. But there are also similarities between the feather-headed figures of Karakol and images from other petroglyphic complexes. One of the most intriguing sites is that of Tamgaly, located in the Dzhambul' region of Kazakhstan, 170 km. to the northeast of Alma-Ata. At Tamgaly figures with anthropomorphic bodies and large round heads have been recorded (pl. VIIIg, h). Instead offacial features, the heads are filled with geometric patterns, or with dots, or even left "empty" or "filled." Around their heads are extended rays, rows of dots, or combinations of such patterns which produce an effect similar to that of the feathered heads of the Karakol figures. There are, however, significant differences. A number of these figures are associated with animals: several of the figures are placed above animals, as if standing on them, some have tails,
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



104 ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS
and a few may be ithyphallic. These figures have been referred to as "sun-headed" deities, unquestionably because of their similarity to childlike drawings of the sun; they have been described as indicators of aregional solar cult oflndo-Iranian derivation (Maksimova, Ermolayeva, and Mar'yashev 1985: 9-10). The recorders of the site date the round-headed images from Tamgaly to the Bronze Age, but the evidence for that conclusion is weak. It rests on the conviction that these images are solar deities and that they must therefore reflect Bronze Age Indo-European or Indo-Iranian traditions of the second millennium B.C. Similar conclusions have been assumed regarding the so-called "sun-faced gods" at Saimaly-Tash in the Pamirs (Bernshtam 1952; Sher 1980: 233-234). These images combine stick bodies and short stick legs with circular "heads" from which project rays. Occasionally these "heads" are marked on the interior by small "rayheads," or by crossed lines (Golendukhin 1971: fig. 205). Bernshtam and Sher date these images to the Bronze Age and associate them with a solar cult which came into Central Asia with the Aridronovo culture. Golendukhin dates them earlier, to the third-second millennium B.C., and believes they reflect the calendrical rituals of a pre-Indo-European agricultural people (Golendukhin 1971). Both Golendukhin and Sher are unquestionably correct in finding cultic references in some of the Saimaly-Tash petroglyphs, particularly in those with representations of individuals with their arms raised as if in prayer or in ploughing scenes. Concluding that these should be seen as reflections of solar cults, however, depends on assuming that early cultures were satisfied to represent the sun through a childish joining of a circle and extended rays. The ploughing scenes seem more accessible to understanding. Many of these images combine a number of curious elements. The drivers are frequently bull-tailed; sometimes they are distinctly ithyphallic and have long "hair" streaming back from their heads (Sher 1980: figs. 107, 108). Some of the ploughs are drawn by two different animals, e.g., a bullock and a horse, a goat-like animal and a horse, or by bulls with exaggeratedly long horns. Golendukhin is possibly correct in finding here images of ploughing that refer to calendrical cycles. But the focus of the ceremony referred to by the ploughing may have been the field itself, the embodiment of Mother Earth, whose fertile growth is brought forth by the bull's ploughing of the field in early spring. Cults revolving around the Mother Goddess as the source of the fruitfulness of earth and flocks were wide-spread and even dominant in
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS 105
the Near Eastern world and in its outlying regions from before the Neolithic period until well into the Bronze and even Iron Ages (Levy c. 1946; Berger 1985; and see below, Chapter VIII). The region of SaimalyTash is rather different from those riverine cultures which spawned the Near Eastern cults, however. The site is located in a rocky and mountainous region removed from the agricultural environment of the valleys of Ferghana. The Saimaly-Tash petroglyphs might seem to mediate between the agricultural lands on the outskirts of the ancient Near East and the dependency on stock increasingly necessitated by dry," rocky, and mountainous lands. Similar ploughing scenes have not been found further to the northeast, although representations of wheeled vehicles do appear at Tamgaly. For the moment it is possible to suggest, I believe, that the ray-headed images and the ploughing scenes may well be associated, that they relate to a pre-lndo-European period, i.e. to the late third-early second millennium B.C., and that the ploughing and planting of the fields as well as the bull elements refer to cults revolving around the earth as Mother Goddess. As the underlying cult was carried to the northeast, regional economic realities dismissed the ploughing ritual and replaced it with animal references to a pastoral dependency. Only with such a dynamic understanding of cults and of cult images could one hope to trace a meaningful relationship between the images of Saimaly-Tash, Tamgaly, Karakol, and the "bird-women" of Kalbak-Tash and Chuluut Gol. It is curious that two of the closest visual parallels to the Karakol feather-headed figures and to the Tamgaly images derive from a site far from the Altay. Among a group of engraved signs and images in a Chalcolithic shrine at Timna, in ancient Palestine, is one figure with stick body, arms, and legs and a head composed of a circle with expanding concentric circles. From the "forehead" of the face extend two long hornlike projections. The excavator of this site believes that this image is "probably female" (Rothenberg 1972: 55, figs. 12-14). From the adjacent site of the temple dedicated to the horned goddess, Hathor, and dated between the fourteenth and twelth centuries B.C., a pottery shard was recovered with the partial image of a similar figure. This image has an upper body which might be indicative of a female figure, a hand with five(?) claw-like fingers, and a head of concentric circles out of which project ray-like elements (Rothenberg 1972: fig. 47/4). What these images mean is not clear. There is no question about their being solar referents-they are not-but they do appear to be female in reference and they were found within a shrine dedicated to a goddess. Within the
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



106 ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS
Timna context, that goddess would presumably be related to the horned goddess, Hathor, of whom more will be said in Chapter VIII. For the moment, more useful analogies to the Karakol imagery may be found to the north of Gorno-Altayskaya A.O., in the valley of the Yenisey River. 15 The first relevant site is that of Shalobolin, to which I have already referred. There, scattered among the elk and boats which testify to a Neolithic layer of petroglyphs are a number of images clearly comparable to those found at Karakol. In addition to the loop-headed figures (cf. pl. VIia and pl. VIIIa,e) there are two others of considerable interest (pl. Vllb). One is only a head with a strange mask-like appearance, with feather-like rays projecting around its circumference and bordered by an arc. Beneath the head and connected to either end of the bow is a horizontal latticed section from which project downward a number of short vertical lines and to the side, diagonal lines. The second image is best preserved in its head section. It also has a mask-like round head marked by horizontal dividing lines. From the sides of its head long feather-like rays project, while the top of its head is marked by tall horns and a central vertical shaft. Although neither image is identical to the feather-headed or horned frontal images at Karakol, there are significant similarities to the latter: in the feather motif itself, in the stylized and mask-like treatment of the round head, and in the combination of horns and feathers. Assuming the possibility ofregional variations in the visual formulation of significant images, it is possible to conclude that Shalabolin reflects a continuity of culture of two image types from Karakol and thus of two temporal levels of culture. I have indicated that frontal images with large bovine horns are less frequent within the Karakol complex than are the feather-headed figures. However, their hieratic postures on the upper surface of the slabs suggest a particular significance within a larger pantheon of figures. Several of those Karakol figures also have distinctive wedge-shaped markings on their faces. The faces of other "rabbit-eared" figures with claws are marked in the same manner. Similar facial markings, usually in conjunction with horns, appear on many of the carved masks recorded at MugurSargol in the upper Yenisey (Devlet 1976b, 1980). Not all the masks from this complex are the same. Many have more recognizably anthropomorphic features, others are simply circles or facial features. Among the large number of such images, the majority carry bovine horns.
15 But see, too, the two or three images with "feather-heads" and "winged" bodies from the petroglyphic site of Oroktoy, on the Katun' River (Okladnikova 1984: pl. 26).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS 107
Several, also, have antennae or even a small human figure emerging from between the horns (Devlet 1976b: figs. 4-7). A small number carry antlers in place of horns. Devlet dates the Mugur-Sargol masks to the Bronze Age, primarily by reference to the Bronze Age images of homed figures associated with the Tom', Angara, Lena, and Selenga petroglyphs. She sees in the masked images reflections of shamanistic traditions which had emerged by that period and were to be continued up into the present (Devlet 1980: 225-243). If the analogy between the horned and wedge-shaped figures of Karakol and the masks of Mugur-Sargol is more than coincidental, then the date of the latter should be placed at least as early as the early Bronze Age. An earlier date would also be indicated by analogies with frontal faces with horns and antlers from Chuluut Gol. However, none of these connections can be considered certain, and as in the case of the horned figures from the Baykal Neolithic, assumptions regarding their shamanistic significance are premature. At the most one may conclude that the masks of Mugur-Sargol are yet another regional variation of the mythic traditions glimpsed in Shalabolin and Karakol, images datable to the early Bronze Age and possibly reformulated in the bird-women of Kalbak-Tash and Chuluut Gol and in the "feather-heads" of Tamgaly and Saimaly-Tash.
D. CARVED MONOLITHS OF THE MINUSINSK BASIN
The most powerful images comparable to those from Karakol are carved on giant monoliths associated with the Minusinsk Basin (pis. X, XI). These huge stones rose over the surface of the ground as much as 6 m. Over the millennia many of the stones have fallen and broken, others have been reused and recarved, and yet others have been rubbed into illegibility by grazing animals. Despite the muted aspect of the carvings, however, they retain a distinctive monumentality.16 When seen from the side, almost every stone describes a rising arc, its highest point terminating above the main, eastern face of the stone. That eastern face is dominated by a great mask image which protrudes from the surface of the stone.
16 See especially the work of Vadetskaya, Leont'yev, and Maksimenkov. A large number of these stones have been gathered into the Historical Museums in Abakan and Minusinsk. This relocation has protected the stones and allows one to view many of the most famous and impressive of the stelae in one place. In neither location, however, does the display auempt to recreate the original settings.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



108 ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS
Gryaznov, one of the first scholars to consider these stelae, began by believing they were datable to the Karasuk culture, but later associated them with the Andronovo culture (Gryaznov 1950a). Since Gryaznov first called scholarly attention to these monoliths, most scholars have associated them with the Okunev culture, usually considered to be an Aeneolithic culture of the late third or early second millennium B.C. (Formozov 1969; Khlobystina 1971a; Leont'yev 1978; Vadetskaya 1967, 1986). Against this prevailing opinion, Kyzlasov has proposed that the great stelae with masked images date to a Neolithic third millennium culture which he names Tazmin (Kyzlasov 1986). Regard-less of when they were actually carved, many of the stelae were reused in the context of Okunev period burials (e.g., pl. Xb). Whether that reuse indicates the disrespect accorded the works of an earlier culture by a later one, whether it indicates, rather, respect and honor, or whether it indicates a simple variation in the purposes for which such carved stelae could be used is not resolvable at this time. For the sake of clarity, I willl refer to the large monoliths with engraved masks as Okunev, but with the understanding that their actual cultural identification and dating remains debated. The several dozen stelae recorded in recent decades represent an unending variety of several specific themes. The general characteristics may be briefly reviewed. The faces themselves, usually found approxi-mately 1/3 or 1/2 up from the actual base of the stone, combine stylized anthropomorphic elements-eyes, noses, mouths-with decidedly non-human features. These include stripes or bands across the face in a manner reminiscent of the banded faces at Karakol. Circles suggestive of extra eyes or nostrils appear on many of the masks. Some of the masks. are surrounded by ray or feather-like projections, such as that on a stele: found in the Sorga Valley or in the case of the mask from a slab reused in a Tagar period burial on Black Lake (Kyzlasov 1986: figs. 59, 97). Most of the masks, however, have distinctly horn-like projections or "antennae" (e.g., pl. X a, b, d). In this respect, the masks are clearly similar to the masks of Mugur-Sargol. On a stone from Shira, masks and mask-like elements form a vertical band extending upwards from between the horns of the head (pl. Xe). In some cases (e.g., pl. Xie), the central projection over the head of the mask takes the form of a complex ray or feathered element; in other cases, the undulations of the ribbons or bands have a snake-like character (Vadetskaya, Leont'yev, and Maksimenkov 1980: nos. 33, 35, 53,64, 79; and see Lipskiy 1970: fig. 4). In addition, there is the frequent appearance of a four-cornered circle
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS 109
on various parts of the stelae (e.g., pl. Xd, e; pl. XI c). Under several masks may be found large circular elements. These often coincide with a distinctly protruding area, suggestive of a swollen belly or female breasts (e.g., pl. Xd, e). On the Shira stele, a large beast's head and forearms emerge from a side view of the proturding breasts and belly (pl. Xe), while on a broken stele from Bile, the eyes of a jawed beast are merged with the mask's breasts (pl. Xe). Most of the Minusinsk stelae are tapered at the upper end, but a number are carved into massive sculptural forms. The upper end of a stone from Verkh-Bidzhin (pl. Xia) takes the form of a massive horned ram head facing in the same direction as the mask (presumably, originally, to the east). The horns are carved into the sides of the stele in the form of two full-curves (Kyzlasov 1986: 137-139). Another huge stone found at Kulagash has a roughly shaped upper section recalling the head (unfinished?) of an animal such as a ram (Kyzlasov 1986: figs. 69, 70). A stone which had been reused as part of the enclosing wall of a Tagar kurgan in the Sorga Valley is particularly interesting (Kyzlasov 1986: 102-103). The upper portion of the stone, above the mask, is carved in the form of an elk's head, antlerless, its nostrils pointing up to heaven (pl. Xlb). In the case of all these stones, it is unclear whether or not the animal heads were carved at the same time as the masks and were intended to be a part of the same iconographic program. 17 A few stones have attracted considerable attention, since they suggest to some scholars the appearance of Proto-Indo-European elements within the Minusinsk Basin. These elements include bovines and wheeled vehicles: The most famous example of the occurence of these elements is that from the village of Znamenka on the Erbe River (pl. Xld). 18 Calling these animals "bulls" in association with a cart, Khlobystina dates the animals, cart, and stele to the Okunev period. She argues that the cart represents the sun in its heavenly course; bulls are a sign of the
17 Similarly problematic is the image of an elk carved above the partial mask of a stone believed to have been found along the Sagaich River (pl. Xlf). The drawing reproduced by Kyzlasov gives the elk antlers and suggests that the carving is different from that used to render the partial mask (Kyzlasov 1986: fig. 124). The drawing reproduced by Vadetskaya makes no such distinction and characterizes the elk as female (Vadetskaya, Leont'yev, and Maksimenkov 1980: no. 70). In both cases, however, there is the suggestion that the antennae springing up from the face of the mask actually cross at least one of the elk's legs, thus indicating that the mask was done after the carving of the elk. 18 The Znamenka stone was first considered in an article by Gryaznov (1960). It is also extensively discussed by Kyzlasov, as are two other stelae with dubious "carts," one from Usl'-Byur' and another, now-lost, from Krasnyy kamen' (Kyzlasov 1986: 178-179, 193194; figs. 112,116, 121).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



110 ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS
emergence of the Bronze Age in the early second millennium, and the bulls and carts together represent the emergence of agriculture in the: Minusinsk Basin (Khlobystina 1971b). Kyzlasov also believes that the cart and associated "bulls" are contemporaneous with the mask and refer to a solar cult. Gryaznov dated the stone itself, with its mask and solar mark, to the Andronovo and the cart, bulls, and "antelope" to the Karasuk. While these dates may be too late, he is unquestionably correct in seeing a sequence in time between the cart and bovines and the upper group of animals (Gryaznov 1960). The images of carts and bovines introduces the larger issue of interpretation. Khobystina is only one of many scholars who identify the masks on the stelae as indicative of a syncretic female-ancestor, originally the combination of woman and bull (Khlobystina 1971a, 1971b). Over time, the female ancestor began to play a greater role than did her animal partner, coming finally to dominate the slabs with engravings from the Okunov period. Echoes of this tradition persist in Siberian mythology and trace the origins of a people to the marriage betwen a woman and an animal: within Okunev art, that animal-husband is the totemic bull. Kyzlasov has developed a similar but more elaborate interpretation of the motifs on the Minusinsk stones. Associating the masked stelae with sacrificial grounds and altars, he calls the deity to which are dedicated both grounds and stelae Mother Earth. Each stone recreates the axis of the World Tree and World Mountain, their divisions associated with the underworld, the world, and the heavenly sphere. The female thus becomes the First Mother, and her worship in antiquity explains the now widespread Siberian worship of mountains, menirs, and stones. The cosmological cult of the World Mountain, said by Kyzlasov to have been brought to South Siberia from the ancient Near East, was grafted onto a local Neolithic cult of fertility and abundance (Kyzlasov 1986: 188192).19
It is probable that the stelae reflect the merging of two cultural traditions: one from the outside, probably from the southwest, and the other authochthonous. Whether it is then possible to interpret masks, carts, bovines, and "homed-circles" in terms of solar and fertility cults is, however, dubious. One may argue-as I will in Chapter VIII-that
19 Kyzlasov rather enigmatically argues that the element "contributed" by the local tradition was the conception of the Mother Earth; she assumed her role on the stelae as the, conflation of two traditions. He gives no basis for his theory about the local tradition and the imported tradition of a world mountain.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS 111
there are significant parallels here with traditions known textually from the ancient Near East, but it is unlikely that the iconographic programs on the Minusinsk stelae can be understood as directly derived from ancient Near Eastern and Proto-Indo-European traditions. Lacking any clear indication of how such a menu of beliefs could have been imported from such a distance and rearranged with such confidence on the stelae, it would be much wiser to withdraw from a detailed interpretation based on loose analogies and consider the regularly repeating, interlocking patterns found on these monumental stones. Most of these stelae are marked on their main or east face with a mask of somewhat anthropomorphic aspect. Whether superimposed upon a modeled pier or on a flattened slab, these masks are frequently marked by horizontal stripes or divisions. Most, if not all, have characteristics which in some respects are bovine: large and wide-set eyes, large nostrils and mouth. Ears are suggested by circles or loops on the sides of the mask and virtually all the masks are marked by projecting elements: horns in particular, but also antennae, vertical streamers, and feather-like elements. Under a considerable number of the masks are the modeled indications of breasts. In many cases, the thickening of the stone below the mask suggests the full womb of a female body. Many of the stones have earred circles marked either along the sides of the shaft or below the mask. The absence of these elements directly above the masks at the top of the stelae would argue against their interpretation as sun disks or as the sun over and under the world. If they were suns and if the solar aspect of these stones were as pronounced as Kyzlasov believes, one would certainly expect to find solar disks at the top of the shaft, thus reflecting the passage of the sun through the heaven. For the time being, these marks-like many others on these stelae--defy interpretation. Similarly, the association of the very few wheeled vehicles carved on the stones with a solar chariot (theoretically brought from Near Eastern traditions) with the bull and the mask as a symbolic grouping cannot be supported: first, because if the mask faced to the east, the cart on the Znamenka stele would also be facing to the east in its passage through heaven; second, because the carts and the bovines, here and on other stelae, are either later than the masks in execution or are known in such fragmentary or damaged form that their authenticity and authority must be considered uncertain; third, because there are too few representations of either wheeled vehicles or bovines to come to any confident conclusion regarding their intended meaning. Kyzlasov is certainly correct in emphazing the significance of the
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



112 ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS
mask-carved stone within the context of a place of ritual sacrifices. It is also probable that the deep burying of the stelae into the ground so that the mask might appear only above the surface of the earth, was intended for ritual as well as for practical purposes (Kyzlasov 1986: 85-135). As a result, the stone shaft would appear to connect the world under the soil., the earth's surface, and the upper sphere, as Kyzlasov argues. In this respect the shaft could function as a variation on the pan-Eurasian concept of the World Mountain or World Tree by which the three spheres are joined. Whatever their original function and however their original symbolic significance was nuanced, the elements on the Minusinsk stones taken altogether strongly suggest the presence ofa female deity. This deity was associated with animal characteristics which denoted either her animal-other or her actual animal nature. The animal in question was much more likely to have been the horn-bearing cow than the bull, and the fullness of the deity's breasts and womb much more certainly refers to the cow as the source of life and of sustenance than to the bull. The placement of the deity's mask on the face of the stelae, and the deep planting of the stelae into the ground, suggests that the deity was intended to mediate between the upper and lower spheres of existence. She emerges from the earth as if rooted there. The undulating ribbons may indicate snakes, a common corolary to chthonic deities, while the more feather-like elements may indicate her avian aspect. The appearance of the stelae or menirs within the confines of sacrificial grounds indicates the significance of this deity within the life of the community and the belief that on her timely propitiation depended the order and well-being of the universe. If her powers extended to the sun as a source of warmth and light, then that was only one area of her domain. Given a common cultural horizon, the frontality of mask-like images and the particular combination of animal elements indicate some significant connection between the images on the Minusinsk stelae, the main images on the Karakol slabs, and, possibly, the masks of Mugur-Sargol. The variations in treatment of the facial area and the slurring between horns, feathers, and snake-like elements may indicate regional or generational idiosyncrasies in the handling of a similar set of religious conceptions and references. The Kalbak-Tash and Chuluut Gol frontal figures are more specifically female in reference than those from Karakol; in this respect they recall the enlarged wombs and breasts on the Minusinsk stelae. By virtue of their appearance in burials, the Karakol figures are clearly related to life after mortal death; in that respect, they
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS 113
appear to be properly chthonic. The horns of some of the Karakol images are analogous to the more imaginative horns of the Mugur-Sargol and Minusinsk masks, while their feathered heads and clawed hands echo the bird-like aspects of the "bird-women" of Kalbak-Tash and Chuluut Gol. The essential absence or obliteration of any references to elk from the immediate cultural horizon within the upper layers of imagery on the Karakol slabs and on the Minusinsk stelae argues that we have here a culture which could only have been dated to the Aeneolithic or early Bronze Age. The undercarvings of elk at Karakol argue that the culture of the horned and feathered deities overlay local outflows of the Baykal Neolithic. Whereas the female elk was the central image for that earlier culture, within the new culture an anthropomorphic deity of bovine and bird characteristics-a female associated with death and with the fullness of life-personified fundamental principles of belief. In some regions her bovine characteristics indeed suggest that this new culture was pastoral, with a primary dependence on cattle. She was also birdlike, however, and that curious aspect, as well as her relationship with deer images at Kalbak-Tash, will require further consideration.
E. CONCLUSION
I have said that there is considerable disagreement regarding the dating and the cultural basis of the Minusinsk stelae, although most scholars consider them to be of the Okunev period, thus post-dating the appearance of the Afanasevo culture in the Minusinsk Basin. Kubarev believes that placement of the Karakol burials within Afanasevo mounds might also indicate a post-Afanasevo chronology, but the lack of any bronze goods within the burials points to the very early Bronze Age at the latest. Novgorodova believes that the images of frontal females, birth scenes, and bulls from Chuluut Gol must belong to the Aeneolithic period. Since the Aeneolithic population of northwestern and central Mongolia appears to have had a clear Europoid component, Novgorodova concludes that here, also, there must have been a culture of Afanasevo characteristics (Novgorodova 1989: 81-86). The Afanasevo culture has been most clearly identified with the regions of present-day Gorno-Altayskaya A.O. and the Minusinsk Basin. As an Aeneolithic culture it formed a transition between the Neolithic and Bronze Ages of that region. The round-bodied pottery with sides ornamented by simple, impressed patterns and flattened or softly pointed bases was sturdy and functional. The scanty remains in Afanasevo
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



114 ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS
burials indicate a dependency on both stock (cattle, horses, sheep), and hunted animals. I have already referred to the significant parallels between the Afanasevo culture of South Siberia and the Yamna (Pitgrave) Neolithic culture identified with the Volga Steppe and western Siberia in the late third millennium B.C. The Yamna culture was primarily stock dependent, with sheep predominating in the east and cattle in the more western region. The Yamna graves were typically constructed in the form of square or rectangular shafts, frequently lined and covered with planks or beams. Yamna groups in the Lower Dnieper region more typically lined and covered their burial shafts with stone slabs. In both types of burial chambers, timber or stone, the bodies of the dead were laid on their backs, legs contracted with knees upright. Although burial goods appear to have been meager, there was frequently a considerable use of ocre in the burials.Burials would be marked on the surface by a characteristic earthen mound (Sulimirski 1970a: 127-136). In terms of the burial ritual, burial structures, and stock dependency, there are striking parallels between the contemporaneous Yamna and Afanasevo cultures. Although osteological evidence is not complete, the Afanasevo appear to have been of a Europoid physical type. On the other hand, although sturdy carts have been found in Yamna burials of the Lower Dnepr at the end of the third and early second millennia B.C., no carts have been found in association with Afanasevo burials. Beyond these facts, however, both the relationship--if any-to European cultures of the Proto-Indo-European period and the details ofthe Afanasevo culture itself remain unclear.Burial structures and ritual varied from the Yenisey to the Altay; and although some scholars see no relationship between the Afanasevo and Okunev cultures (Gryaznov 1969), others find persuasive indicators of continuity (Kiselev 1951; Kyzlasov 1986). It is certain that the particular slab structures of the Karakol burials more closely resemble burial structures associated with the Okunev culture in the Minusinsk Basin than the log construction considered by Gryaznov typical of the Afanasevo burial structure. It is also apparent that the relationship of Afanasevo and Okunev cultures-the former often vaguely associated with a "western" tradition, the latter associated with a Siberian taiga tradition-remains unclear. This discussion leads to the general conclusion that within the Minusinsk Basin and already by the second millennium B.C., the Europoid peoples of the Afanasevo culture and the Mongoloid peoples of the Okunev culture had developed significant cultural interconnections evident in shared aspects of burial ritual, ceramic styles, and
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS 115
economy (Vadetskaya 1986). There were, however, significant differences. The pastoral Afanasevo were primarily dependent on cattle and sheep. Bone finds in Okunev settlements and burials indicate, by contrast, a major dependency on the hunting of wild animals, including bear, elk, deer, and fur-bearing animals such as wolves, sables, fox, and so forth. Reindeer horns, presumably from the wild animal, served extensively for the production of tools. Funerary finds of significant numbers of bear teeth and claws indicate, most probably, a developed bear cult (Vadetskaya 1986: 30-34). There is also ample evidence, however, that over time the culture became increasingly dependent on domestic stock, particularly on cattle and sheep, as well as on horses. Such changes suggest that the Okunev learned stock-raising from the Afanasevo. What is certain is that both cultures, deriving from different regions and ethnicities, achieved a degree of cultural parallelism and even ethnic interpenetration within the Minusinsk Basin by the eighteenth - seventeenth centuries B.C. Whatever problems exist in the dating of the Minusinsk stelae, there is no significant questioning of the Okunev origins of a number of minaturecarved figurines of bone or stone representing females (Kyzlasov 1986: 267-269; Vadetskaya 1986: 31-33). These figurines were found in the burials of young women. In several cases the images are represented as if with long hair combed down over their shoulders and upper chest, and in some cases they wear ornaments on their necks and ears. The columnar base of these images suggests that they were "dressed," and that they may have been intended to be held or set up on some miniature altar. The very care with which they were carved seems to indicate that they were more than dolls. Their impressive frontality and stylized realism contrasts with their miniature size. In that frontality and pillarlike form, these small figurines clearly recall the masked Minusinsk stelae. On the other hand, their realism also suggests that they belong to a related but different culture, or that their reference was markedly different. In either case, there is the visual reference to an image of female attributes, but in one case, the image of the deity is monumental and anti-realistic, in the other, she is realistic and miniaturized. She possibly served as a particular protective deity for women. There are no known analogous male figures. Within the complex ofpost-Baykal Neolithic images discussed in this chapter, many join certain or probable female references with references to bovines and birds. Almost all the imagery refers to beings with quasihuman, quasi-animal characteristics. They are monumental in appear
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



116 ROCK CARVINGS AND PAINTINGS
ance and anti-realistic. Only the small Okunev figurines may be confidently associated with a culture which was on the verge of entering the full Bronze Age and only these figurines demonstrate a concern with realism. It is this aspect which seems to lead into the petroglyphic traditions of the South Siberian Bronze Age of the second and early first millennia B.C. As we turn to that period, however, we will find that imagery in general indicates a revitalized realism. One senses that even if the objects of representation refer to a spirit or mythic realm, they are now rendered in the guise of a real world of humans and animals. The Neolithic and Aeneolithic singularity of representational purpose also disappears: where the mnemonically repeated images of female elk had held sway or where imagery returned again and again to a strange masked figure offemale human and animal characteristics, we find in the Bronze Age a great variety of references. Bulls, cattle, deer, caprids, humans in many ritual settings, carts and wagons-this more fully populated world became the focus of the Bronze Age artist in South Siberia.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



CHAPTER FIVE
THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING The Petroglyphic Tradition of the Middle and Late Bronze Age
A. IMAGE TYPES AND THEIR REALMS OF REFERENCE
When the burials at Karakol were sealed, so, too, was sealed a dictionary of significant images from the period of the Neolithic, Aeneolithic, and early Bronze Age. The importance of those burials lies not only in their having preserved the visual vocabulary of archaic belief. It lies also in what they exclude: what they push, by force of absence, into the succeeding Bronze Age. Where and by what means, however, does one distinguish the lower chronological limits of that Bronze Age vocabulary? To establish that lower horizon, one must turn to a wider regional consideration and to an increased reliance on style. One must also depend upon the principle of exclusion based on iconographic and stylistic evidence derived from materials which can be dated securely in the Iron Age. These images and the styles in which they are rendered point to an invisible line marking the end of the Bronze Age at some point shortly after 1000 B.C. The realistic elk and the feathered and horned mythic figures on the one hand, and the elegant, stylized animals of the Early· Nomadic period on the other, form brackets around the body of material which must be assigned to the second and early first millennia B.C. 1 If style and image type distinguish the deer, caprids, wolves, felines, and boar of the Early Nomadic period from the deer, cattle, human figures, and vehicles of the preceding Bronze Age, it is also possible to assert that any images of humans riding horses must be excluded from a consideration of Bronze Age petroglyphs. After all, it is only with the great burial at Arzhan that we begin to see the bone and bronze psalia, bits, and strap settings which would have allowed the regular use of the
1 Excluded altogether from this discussion are petroglyphic materials which can be assigned to the Turkic and modern periods. The Turkic materials are fairly easy to discern: by the distinctiveness of their style, the use of a scratched-in technique, and by the frequent presence of Turkic writing.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



118 THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING
horse for riding. It is also probable that scenes of herding, such as those from Shivertyn-Am and Gachurt (pl. Xlla,b), and scenes of herds of animals, such as those from Moroy (pl. Xllc) or from Kunin in the Minusinsk Valley (pl. Xllf) must be dated no earlier than the late Bronze Age or more likely within the early Iron Age. Internal evidence from the Great Boyar petroglyphs of the middle Yenisey River, dated to the late Tagar period, would indicate that such domestic scenes should also be dated well into the Iron Age (Devlet 1976a; cf. Okladnikov et al. 1979: pl. 7/9). Those images which include the stylistic marks of the Tashtyk and succeeding Turkic periods must also be set aside. As for the innumerable and indistinguishable images of caprids and deer, repeated again and again on the rocky walls of Gorno-Altayskaya A.O., Tuva, and Mongolia as if they were visual invocations of the real animals, they must also be excluded from this discussion, due to the uncertainty of their dating. In all probability they belong somewhere within the Bronze and early Iron Ages. The so-called "deer stones" of South Siberia and Mongolia are a significant part of that late Bronze Age tradition; because of their particular form and meaning, they will be considered separately in Chapter VII. Excepting them, what remains after considering the chronological brackets I have indicated are a number of image types from across South Siberia and from western and northern Mongolia. All can be placed with some confidence within the second millennium B.C. Within the more northern complexes these include the occasional representations of bear, as at Shalabolin (Pyatkin and Martynov 1985: figs. 75, 84). Much more common are bovine images and images of large antlered deer. These animals are rendered in a variety of styles: silhouette, outline, and a distinctive brocaded or "embroidered" style, in which the animal's body is treated with a variety of linear patterns (e.g., pl. IXe, f; pl. XVlb). Cattle are distinguished by their large bodies and pronounced horns and tails. In some cases, where the bodies of the animals are particularly squared and the tails terminate in a large tuft, the animal represented may be a yak (pl. XIIlf). Occasionally the necks of the animals are ridged or fringed, as if to indicate either folds of skin, a fringe of hair or possibly ritual harnessing (pl. XIIIa). The deer are often, but not always, antlered. Within a group of animals impressive antlered individuals may frequently be found. Together with cattle, deer are among the most common zoomorphic images datable to the Bronze Age. Indeed, the very repetition of cattle, yak, and deer throughout South Siberia and northern Mongolia, calls to mind an artistic imagination
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING 119
which roams on behalf of individual or community between a reverential acknowledgement of the beasts of the forest and mountain-steppe and an invocation of the fertility of domesticated cattle and yak. In addition to the readily recognizable animals are a number of syncretic beasts, merging bovine, deer, and-more rarely--caprid elements. Within the Yelangash complex, for example, a human figure leads a large bodied animal with bovine tail, neck fringe, and a combination of horn and antlered projections on its head (pl. XIIId). Other images from the same complex and from Kalbak-Tash have the distinctive bodies of cattle but wear antlers (pl. IXe, f, g; pl. XIIIg, h). In some cases, the body and horns indicate a goat but the animal has the long tail of a cow or bull (pl. XIIle, i). Among the petroglyphs of the Chuluut Gol complex in north central Mongolia may be found examples of deercattle, bovines in a large style, and elaborate interior patterning reminiscent of that at Kalbak-Tash (Novgorodova 1984: figs. 17, 18, 21, 30). From as far south as the Yin-Shan complex of Inner Mongolia (Gai 1985, fig. 93) comes one example of an animal which appears to combine a large bovine body with deer antlers; its body surface is carved with the circles and double-circles reminiscent of the elaborate patterning of syncretic animals, bovines, and deer at both Kalbak-Tash and Chuluut Gol. In general, however, the center of such syncretic animals appears to have been within Gorno-Altayskaya A.O., that region which served as a kind of hub for the circling and merging of peoples from the North, from the East, and from the Southwest. It seems certain that the pictorial merging of bovine and cervid must indicate the interweaving of symbolic values adhering to each animal and thus the interweaving of cultural traditions. Syncretic deer-cattle are decidedly less frequent in representation than are their "pure" counterparts. Nonetheless, their appearance sideby-side with cattle, yak, and deer done in similar techniques supports the hypothesis that within the Bronze Age, the images of cattle and deer represented two different spheres of reference in the cultures' belief systems. These animals also raise an issue to which we shall repeatedly return: we cannot know from the representations where-in which realm- such animals should be located. Do they refer to real animals which were ritually dressed to become syncretic forms? Do they refer to ideal animals which uniquely joined the conceptual bases of two significant realms? Or do we simply have to do with a playful rendition? The last suggestion may probably be set aside: there are entirely too many such images to justify anything but common ritual or myth as shared
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



120 THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING
reference across South Siberia and northern Mongolia. Whether to locate these images or even those other images of "realistic" cattle and deer in the world of phenomenal reality, the realm of ideal form, or the realm of ritual and myth will necessarily remain, for the present, unclear. In addition to these primary zoomorphic images, there are a number of other images which significantly extend the realm of reference and meaning within South Siberian Bronze Age cultures. These include the representations of wheeled vehicles, reminiscent of carts or wagons, as well as a very distinctive kind of human image, marked by a mushroomshaped headdress (pls. XV, XVII, XVIII). These figures often carry taillike appendages, bows, arrows, and spears. At times they seem to wear pouches at their waists. Most often rendered in outline or silhouette style, they may also appear in the brocade style in which were rendered deer, cattle, and deer-cattle (e.g., Novgorodova 1984: fig. 28). In many cases these mushroom-hatted figures are represented alone, but more frequently they appear in groups, postured as if dancing, performing some kind of elaborate dance-like ritual, hunt, or combination ritual hunt. In a very few cases, they are engaged in real or mock battle or appear associated with vehicles. These image types and broader questions relating to the representations of two and four-wheeled vehicles will be discussed more fully below. One of the most striking aspects of full Bronze Age petroglyphic art is the emergence of compositions of interacting figures, or of figures and animals. These elements are grouped not simply as if together; they seem rather to reflect the process of action and response. Such compositions indicate, in other words, the emergence of the representation of a primitive psychological interaction (Jacobson 1990b). In some cases the indications of physical or psychological interaction are extremely simple. For example, the representation from Chankyr-Kel' of an archer on foot in proximity to a large deer describes in relatively static terms the imminence of cause (release of bow) and effect (the death or startled flight of the animal) (pl. XIVb). In other cases, one senses a more sophisticated interest in the interconnection between action and potential effect. A group from the Yelangash Valley includes several archers whose appearance with readied bows may be directly related to the indication of yak in flight (pl. XIIlf). Elsewhere, the meaning of the grouping is not clear, but the placement of humans among the animals seems purposeful. In the joining of ithyphallic frontal figures and animals such as caprids, horses, or deer (e.g., pl. XVa-c, e), one
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING 121
senses a reference to some ritual moment or to the narration of ritual. 2 The representation of interaction again raises the question of references: whether, for example, the hunt represented is ritual or real, or whether the dance represented is not actually a priestly ritual or even a ritual enacted by spirit ancestors. While it is possible to name the visual sign by reference to the phenomenal world-hunt, copulation, dance, and so forth-it is not possible to determine the accuracy of that name or to know the realm of existence with which that representation should be associated. On the other hand, even if we cannot say what an image group actually represents, it can be acknowledged that the terms in which the individual images are related agree with perceived laws of physical and psychological interaction: that is, with a human logic of action and response. A hunter raises his bow and animals stand on the alert. A wolf appears to challenge a herd of deer, a human leads an animal dressed in ritual regalia. Here, mushroom-hatted figures move together like a solemn chorus. There, they seem to be moving in a more active dance around several deer. If these are not images of real animals or real humans, the figures at least act out theirritual roles in postures which can be named by reference to our world. If they exist only in the realm of myth and ritual, the terms of their visual discourse belongs to our realm of phenomenal being.
B. RITUAL FIGURES AND FIGURES IN INTERACTION
Within the major petroglyphic complexes of Gorno-Altayskaya A.O., Tuva, and Mongolia, among the most commonly repeated subjects are interacting pairs or small groups of figures without the representation of any weapons. From Mugur-Sargol, Yelangash, Khavtsgayt to the Chuluut Gol complex, these simple forms of interaction include scenes of copulation or of two figures holding hands or standing beside each other with unclear purpose (pl. XVb, d, f). More intriguing are the many groups of figures or figures and animals, apparently engaged in some kind of ritual activity. 3 This is exemplified by several groups of images
2 E.g., at Kayancha on the Katun' River; or at Kok-Kan on the Katun' (Okladnikova 1984: pis. 22/1, 37/1). 3 The simplest representation of humans in some kind of interaction is certainly that referred to as figures in a "chorus line." This formulation is well known from the petroglpyhs of the Angara, Lena, and Baykal regions. Many of these groups may belong to the Bronze Age; but similar representations from the Selenga (Selenge) River complex, including figures holding hands or simply standing frontally, or figures which appear to be transformed into birds, are usually dated later, well into the Iron Age (Okladnikov and Zaparozhskaya 1969; Tivanenko 1981).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



122 THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING
from the Mongolian site of Khobd Somon. In one, a large frontal and ithyphallic archer, bow in hand and legs bent as if in dance, holds the horn of a caprid (pl. XVc). Near him are recorded three figures, one of whom has a distinctive headdress with large side loops, the other two of whom may be females squatting in childbirth (pl. XVf). From another site in the same complex, an ithyphallic man holds the lead of a large stallion (pl. XVe). The figure of a person leading a large bovine with ritual "fringe," from Sary-Satak, may also belong in this category (pl. XIIIa), as may the representation of two figures leading a female animal of some kind, from Chankyr-Kel', in the Yelangash Valley (pl. XVa). A particularly complex ritual scene from Chuluut Gol includes animals and figures, several of whom appear to be dancing (pl. XIVe). In contrast to the large number of ritually interacting figures and figures and animals, the number of instances in which figures are represented either in hand-to-hand combat or attacked by predatory animals is so small as to be almost negligible. It is probable that they belong to the early Iron Age. An image from Mugur-Sargol, in which two figures seem to be fighting with pick-axes, probably dates from the later Tagar period (Devlet 1980: pl. 66), as would the images of figures attacking each other from the Minusinsk Basin petroglyphs of Kunin (pl. Xllf). From sites in both the Yelangash Valley and in Mongolia come several instances of figures raising their bows as if to shoot directly at each other; these may indicate an actual battle, or they may indicate a ritual battle from human or mythic experience (pl. XIVa, c, f). On the other hand, a group of figures from the Yin-Shan Mountains of Inner Mongolia may be dated to the Bronze Age by their distinctive mushroom-shaped hats. These figures aim their primed bows at each other; their close range suggests an intense and ferocious interaction (Gai 1985: fig. 1212). Despite this intensity, however, a number of elements suggest that this is not a real battle but one existing on the level of myth or ritual. Several of the figures wear long tail-like appendages. From their waists protrude erect phalluses, weapons, or both. Their mushroom-shaped hats are similar to those found on a significant number of figures throughout South Siberian and Central Asian petroglyphs, but in almost all those instances-to which this group must be compared-the figures are engaged in what only can be understood as ritual activity. In other words, among all the petroglyphic images which seem datable to the Bronze Age, the instances of figures aggressively attacking each other is very small and the actual nature of that agression is open to question. There are occasional image groups which seem to represent specific
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING 123
rituals or mythic events involving one or more archers. In one group from Khobd Somon, an ithyphallic archer aims at (?) a mushroom-hatted figure squatting as if in childbirth (pl. XIVd). At Khavtsgayt, Okladnikov recorded a group of ithyphallic figures with tall head ornaments, tails, and quivers (?), facing each other with drawn bows (pl. XIVf). By contrast to the number of image groups expressing human aggression on an actual, ritual, or mythic level, there are a large number of scenes in which figures appear with animals either in ritual performances or in a ritual or real hunt. As in the scenes which may be expressive of human aggression, here many of the figures are ithyphallic. It is possible that this representational element may function as a sign of fertility ceremonies, but it is also possible that the representation of the phallus was simply a practical way of distinguishing male from female within the representational constraints of rock carving or painting. Figures with mushroom-shaped headdresses are well exemplified by a group of figures from Kalbak-Tash. 4 The largest is approximately two feet tall, the smallest perhaps one-half that size. Large and small, they stand in a three-quarter position facing to their left. In their stylized postures and in the repetition of the sack at their waist and their mushroom-shaped hats, one has the distinct impression of figures involved in some kind of ritual activity. Although this figure type occurs frequently throughout South Siberian petroglyphs, it appears in various forms and contexts. At times, the sacks carried by the figures assume the size and character of a tail; at other times, the sack becomes large enough to suggest a quiver (Devlet 1976b: pis. 36/1, 44). In still other instances, particularly in representations from Yelangash, the large, round "sacks" worn by mushroom-hatted figures seem deliberately to mimic the large, round tufts at the ends of the tails of the bovine animals the figures are hunting (?) (pl. XIIlf). The activities in which these images appear are similarly varied, some with no clear reference and others suggesting a ritual of unclear significance. Examples of this type of representation include that of the large figures from Kalbak-Tash and the group from Chuluut Gol, recorded by Novgorodova, in which seven figures holding bows seem almost to be dancing in proximity to a double-headed(?) stag and a doe led by one of the figures (pl. XIVe). One representation from Khavtsgayt, in western Mongolia, includes two mushroom-hatted figures in copulation (pl. XVd). In a grouping from Khobd Somon, a mushroom-hatted woman crouching as if giving
4 Personal documentation.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



124 THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING
birth is juxtaposed with an archer who aims his bow at a group of cervids, including a male and female deer and a large female elk or reindeer (pl. XIVd).5 This particular scene indicates that the hat itself does not indicate gender; indeed, without the clear indication of a phallus or weapons, it is not possible to distinguish males from females. Moreover, whatever the intended gender, these mushroom-hatted figures occur far more frequently in ritual dances or hunts than in either a real or ritual battle scene, the only clear example of such combat being the scene from the Yin-Shan mountains of Inner Mongolia. The mushroom-hatted figures appear within an arena sufficiently defined to suggest a significant degree of cultural cohesion. One finds them in petroglyphs within the Chuya River basin of southeastern Gorno-Altayskaya A.O., and a large number of them have been recorded in western Mongolia, within the Khobd Somon complex. Other examples have been recorded within the complex ofChuluut Gol, in northcentral Mongolia; and a sprinkling of their images have been found in the YinShan complex of Inner Mongolia. In addition, at least two examples have been recorded in Tuva, at Mugur-Sargol on the Yenisey River (Devlet 1980: figs. 71, 214). These distinctive figures are absent from any representations involving horseback riding, or from any involving pictorial styles associated with the Scythe-Siberians. They are not found in any meaningful association with animal or anthropomorphic representations of the Baykal Neolithic or early Bronze Age or within the image groups described by the Karakol slabs and the Minusinsk stelae. Al-though these facts constitute negative evidence, they confirm that the mushroom-hatted figures must be associated with the middle Bronze Age, that is with the period of the Andronovo-Karasuk cultures. In summary, images of large deer, bovines, and composite deerbovines, as well as figures in ritual or mythic scenes, are among the most significant imagistic elements of the South Siberian and northern Mongolian Bronze Age before the first millennium B.C. Representations of actual aggression between human figures are rare, while scenes of hunting on foot are relatively common. In considering this vocabulary of symbolic forms, one senses a world in which ritual had come to signify not only the reaffirmation of cosmogonic myth, but also the reaffirmation of culture and society. Figures acting together and dressed in similar garb over an extended region-from southeastern Gorno-Altayskaya
5 Some stylistic elements, as indicated in the copy, suggest that these figures all belong together, but there is no way of being certain.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING 125
A.O., Tuva, and through Mongolia down to the Yin-Shan Mountainswould indicate a certain commonality of cultural reference throughout that region. The same petroglyphs, however, indicate that rituals enacted by humans were not the only object of reverence. In some manner that culture was pulled between two poles: one pole represented by bovine animals and probably referring to the significance of pastoralism within the economic life of the people, the other represented by deer images and referring to the taiga as literal and figurative source of being and sustenance. At times those two spheres were joined, as in the syncretic deer-cattle figures. Surely those images reflect both cultural tension and the ritual and mythic resolution of tension, at least during the second millennium. There is, however, one other cluster of images which qualifies such a conclusion: images of vehicles. Although they appear to be quite separate from any study of the deer image, their frequency has decisively shaped scholarly understanding of the cultural context within which the deer image must be located.
C. WHEELED VEHICLES, WARRIORS, AND THEORIES OF CULTURE
Images of two- and four-wheeled vehicles have been found extensively in petroglyphic complexes of eastern Kazakhstan, Gorno-Altayskaya A.O., Tuva, Mongolia, and even Inner Mongolia. These vehicles are almost always represented from above; the figures and animals associated with them are shown frontally or in profile, right side up or inverted. Over the lasnwo decades, these images have caught the attention of numerous scholars, who have found in the vehicles signs of an IndaEuropean invasion and the indication of the diffusion of Inda-European mythic traditions.6 From this persuasion has followed the tendency to interpret all other images-those of deer, bovines, and horses, in particular-in light of Indo-European traditions. I believe that this has led to misunderstandings and interpretations which ignore the ecology of culture and belief. Variation in the presentation of vehicles appears to have had no regional regularity. It is possible to distinguish four-wheeled wagons from two-wheeled carts-vehicles that were appropriate for hauling freight and individuals over rough terrain. More problematic is the
6 See, e..g., Novgorodova 1984; Nowgorodowa (Novgorodova) 1980; Kadyrbayev and Mar'yashev 1977; Kozhin 1968, 1977; Kuz'mina 1974; Piggott 1974, 1975; Smirnov and Kuz'mina 1977. With a few exceptions to be discussed below, the image of the wheeled vehicle does not occur in the Minusinsk Basin nor docs it occur in the Transbaykal region or in the taiga zone to the east and north of Lake Baykal.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



126 THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING
distinction between the two-wheeled cart and the chariot, a two-wheeled vehicle of prestige and warfare. Assuming that the light-bodied chariot is functionally distinguished by its ability to be pulled rapidly, nothing in the petroglyphic images allows us to arrive at a decisive conclusion regarding the nature of the two-wheeled vehicle. Across South Siberia and Mongolia, the wheels of vehicles are represented in a variety of ways: solid, as if of single-piece construction; with four-spokes, as if of cross-bar construction; or with multiple spokes of up to eight or more (e.g., pl. XVIa; cf. representations on pl. XVIII). 7 A wheel of singlepiece wooden construction would clearly offer heavy but slow transport. One would imagine that vehicles with such wheels would function essentially as heavy-load carts or trolleys, similar to those used in the construction of the Pazyryk barrows (Rudenko 1970). Identification of the animals used to pull the vehicles suggests that the representations on the rock carvings indicate carts or that they refer to generalized conceptions. In the majority of cases, the animals associated with these vehicles can be identified as horses; in a significant number of other cases, however, bovines or a combination of bovines and horses are used. At Yelangash, for example, within one image group a man with a mushroom-like hat stands in a two-wheeled vehicle pulled by a horse and bovine; over his head is a standing deer (pl. XVIIIc). If one may distinguish horses from bovines on the basis of the handling of the tail, then it would seem that the pairing of horses and bovines for traction occurred frequently, at least in the northern Altay region (e.g., Okladnikov et al. 1979: pis. 40/2, 41; Novgorodova 1984: fig. 23). Whether the animals used for pulling include one or two bovines, however, the presence of any precludes the identification of two-wheeled vehicles as chariots. In those cases, one can only assume that they are rough carts or carts intended for slower transport. In a small number of cases, deer are represented either as pulling the vehicle or in direct association with the animals pulling the vehicle (pl. XVIIIb, g). A surprisingly large number of vehicles are represented without animals or driver, or with animals alone (pl. XVIIc; pl. XVIIIe, f). On the other hand, representations of vehicles with drivers but without draft animals (e.g., Okladnikov et al. 1982, pl. 37) are extremely unusual. This combination of elements throughout the range of the petroglyphic representation of vehicles suggests that the vehicle itself-its wheels, axle, and box-was the most important element in the
7 Regarding the construction of early wheeled vehicles, based primarily on evidence from European, Near Eastern, and western Siberian materials, see Piggott 1983.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING 127
representation, that draft animals were secondary, and that the driver was the least important element of all (Jacobson 1990c). The problem of identifying the represented vehicle relates not only to the number of wheels and to the animals involved; it also concerns the treatment of the wheels and the actual location of the representation. Theoretically, the greater the number of spokes joining the wheel's nave and rim, the lighter the wheel and the faster the vehicle. A wheel spoked by two cross-bars would be lighter than a solid wheel, but the necessary thickness of the spokes and rim would allow neither great speed nor strength and thus would be inappropriate for either a hunting or war chariot. A pair of two-wheeled vehicles from Arpauzen Vin the Karatau Ridge of Central Kazakhstan has been identified as chariots (Kuz'mina 1986: fig. 38/7; Kadyrbaev and Mar'yashev 1977: fig. 72). Each of the vehicles is pulled by two stallions and is driven by an archer, whose bow is pulled and ready for shooting at a nearby caprid. The vehicle wheels are all four-spoked. If these images can be understood as factual representations, such wheels would indicate a vehicle that was too slow to be useful either in hunting orin war. Furthermore, it is improbable that pairs of stallions would be easily used for traction. Other discrepancies emerge with regard to the representations of vehicles with multi-spoked wheels and pulled by horses that have been found among rock carvings at a site called Ak-Dzhilga, in the northern Alichur Ridge of the Pamir Mountains (Zhukov and Ranov 1974). In this case, the elegance of the vehicle and horses seems to clearly indicate chariots, but the rugged and high nature of the pass precludes the representation of chariots which could actually have been pulled through that mountainous region. Assuming these are chariots, then, they must be understood as conceptual vehicles or as references to a mythic reality, rather than the images of chariots which actually passed through that land. Among the many representations of vehicles in the eastern part of Kazakhstan, in South Siberia, and in northern Mongolia, not one that I am aware of includes any reference to human combat, and very few suggest that the vehicles were actually used for hunting. In the Arpauzen representations, in which hunters are represented shooting from their vehicles, there are no indications of reins connecting horse and driver. Littauer and Crouwel have discussed the skills demanded of the charioteer who functioned simultaneously as a archer. Drawing on examples from the Near East, they conclude that the reins would have had to have been tied at the waist of the archer-charioteer (Littauer and Crouwel 1979: 63). This may have been considered a feat used by racing
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



128 THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING
charioteers, but it would have been highly impractical in the hunt, except in the case of the battue, where the animals would have been driven into the path of the chariot. It was certainly too uncontrolled a manner of driving to have been used in traveling rapidly over rough terrain. A similar set of problems pertain in the only other representation of hunting from a vehicle that I am familiar with. Novgorodova has recorded an image from Chuluut Gol in which an archer "stands" on the platform of a vehicle with two solid wheels, drawn by two bovines (pl. XVIIId). Without any sign of reins, the archer shoots at the figure of a large deer of the type known from the Mongolian deer stones. An ox-drawn cart with solid wheels would obviously have been an impossible vehicle for a successful hunt. One must conclude from this example, as well as from those from Arpauzen, that it is therefore not possible to determine the actual form and function of the vehicles on the basis oftheirpetroglyphic representations, or one must conclude that the scene constitutes a mythic reference or with purely ritual invocation of the successful hunt. The origin and date of the vehicle imagery of Central Asia and South Siberia remain uncertain. There are no archaeological remains of wheeled vehicles earlier than the Pazyryk period (cf. Rudenko 1970). No traces of the wood or bronze fittings have been found that might have been used to secure axels or strengthen wheel naves and rims, nor have the earthen reflections of disintegrated vehicles been identified. On the other hand, the range of distribution of vehicle imagery suggests the diffusion of wheeled vehicles in western Siberia and Kazakhstan eastwards into South Siberia at some time before the age of the Early Nomads. Chronologically, the remains of wheeled vehicles from Bronze Age burials of western Siberia and from Anyang burials of the Shang Dynasty create archaeological brackets for the area between-that of South Siberia and Mongolia-and tend to confirm the probability that the diffusion of the images must have occurred during the second millennium B.C. They also indicate that the four-wheeled vehicle carved onto the upper section of the Znamenka stele (pl. Xld), discussed in the last chapter, represents either an earlier anomolous intrusion or the addition of a second millennium carver. The issue of wheeled vehicle representations brings us back to the primary interpretations of Bronze and early Iron Age imagery. We have seen that bovines have been routinely identified as bulls, carts have been interpreted as chariots, and bulls and wheeled vehicles have been identified as indications of a dominating solar worship. These interpretations certainly reflect the equally persistent persuasion that the "chariot"
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING 129
was a sign of the sun and the vehicle that of the warrior. The vehicle's appearance in rock carvings of South Siberia and Mongolia in the second millennium is said to indicate the invasion of a new culture whose primary instrument of war was the chariot (Kozhin 1968; cf. Novgorodova 1984: 59-79). Unquestionably, these associations by which a wheel or wheeled vehicle becomes a chariot emblematic of the sun and of a warrior class and a warlike culture depend heavily upon a number of assumptions. These include an understanding that wheeled vehicles came into South Siberia from a western and Proto-Indo-Eur.opean or Indo-European culture; that in that culture, wheeled vehicles were a sign of the warrior class and high status; chariots and horses were symbolic of the sun; the wheeled vehicle was emblematic of the chariot. Within these discussions, and without exception, there emerges the assumption that the warrior-charioteer was an honored member of society. Considerable attention has been paid to the cart-burials of males in a group of burials belonging to a transition Timber-grave-Andronovo culture and found near Rimnikskiy in Chelyabinsk Oblast', on the east side of the Urals. These burials were in timbered chambers and five of the burials were furnished with light carts, visible by the negative impression they had left in the soil. The horses associated with these burials were placed in the grave-fill above the roofs of the chambers. These burials have been dated to the mid-second millennium B.C. (Piggott 1975). Earlier Andronovo burials, dating to the seventeenth sixteenth centuries B.C., at Sintashta, Berlik, Ulyubay, and Satan, in northern and central Kazakhstan, have yielded the remains of wheeled vehicles or their imprints (Gening 1977; Smirnov and Kuz' mina 1977; Zdanovich 1983). The size of the kurgans under which the males were buried and the fact that their burials were accompanied by wheeled vehicles and horses that had been laid above the actual burial-as if intended to escort the dead-<:ertainly indicates a degree of status accorded the male dead. Within this Timber-grave-Andronovo complex, women were occasionally accorded burial under large kurgans. Nonetheless, we know relatively little about such burials: they have been so completely plundered that little of the original furnishings remain (Zdanovich 1983). Our ignorance, however, may also reflect a general lack of interest on the part of archaeologists in uncovering funerary rituals which might disturb existing theories of status and ranking. 8 The very fact that female burials
• In this regard, see the critical article by Rowlett on the interpretation of Indo-Europcan burials (1984).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



130 THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING
were so thoroughly plundered would certainly suggest that such burials must have been rich in metals and that their occupants were elite members ofsociety, but that possibility is generally overlooked, as is the fact that the vehicle burials have simply received more attention-and, one might add, more authority-than those without vehicles have. The question arises whether our contemporary social privileging of individuals buried with vehicles actually reflects the reality of the culture in question and its values. It is indeed probable that the Timber-grave-Andronovo connection was essential to the reintroduction of wheeled vehicles into South Siberia. Two major scholars of that connection, Kuz'mina and Smirnov, have argued that from the fourteenth century B.C. the chariot was used as an instrument of war among the Mitanni, Hittites, and Assyrians. This tradition established the association of chariots with war-making and with a warrior elite within the Indo-Iranian culture carried by the Andronovo across Kazakhstan (Smirnov and Kuz'mina 1977: 53). According to Kuz'mina, the appearance of wheeled vehicles or their substitutes in burials probably indicates the high class of the dead and the fact of class differentiation (Kuz'mina 1974). There is general agreement that the four wheeled vehicle developed within the Proto-Indo-European cultures of the eastern European steppe and that its diffusion must be associated with the use of the horse for rapid traction and riding, and with the use of the wagon for indicating status in burials (Gimbutas 1963, 1973; Piggott 1974, 1975, 1983; and cf. Rowlett 1984). On the other hand, Littauer and Crouwel have argued that the light war chariot was developed within the Near East as a practical response to the imperial need for a prestigious vehicle combining speed with maneuverability (1976). It is also clear that from as early as the third millennium the chariot was associated with both gods and goddesses thoughout the Near East. In Hittite representations of wheeled vehicles of the second millennium B.C. and with the appearance of actual vehicles within Mycenae shaft graves, the chariot seems to have been associated with success in war or in the hunt (Anati 1960: figs. 1, 2). By the time the image of the chariot appeared in Central Europe, as in Camonica Valley of northern Italy, its image had come to function more as a sign represented schematically either with or without bovines or horses for traction and frequently accompanied by ithyphallic stick figures with raised arms. Anati associates these images with the emergence of heroic figures or war divinities (Anati 1960, 1961). Elsewhere in the Camonica petroglyphs, two or four wheeled vehicles appear in a
