Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING 131
functional form, as if for hauling. Many of these European representations have been found together with the images of weapons, thus prompting the conclusion that the wheeled vehicle was the sign of the warrior class of an aggressive culture. The image of a wagon pulled by two oxen, together with the images of daggers, is found on a massive stone figure dating to the third millennium B.C., from Lagundo, northern Italy (pl. XXld). Such a combination has only served to confirm in the minds of many observers the intimate relationship between wagons or wheeled vehicles and warriors. In Scandinavian petroglyphs dated to the Bronze Age, the twowheeled vehicle was frequently represented both with and without draft animals and drivers. 9 Individual wheel shapes are also assumed to refer to the chariot and both chariot and wheel are understood as referring to a cult of the sun (Anati 1960; Gelling 1969). The famous "sun chariot" of Trundholm has been cited as a primary example of the relationship between wheels, wheeled vehicles, horses, and the sun in northern Europe. In general, wheels and wheeled vehicles have been understood as referring to an Indo-European worship of the sun; they are thus associated with the sun, the sky, and with a warrior elite. Within these understandings, the distinction between wagons, carts, and chariots has too often been neglected; anything wheeled becomes referred to as a chariot and the "chariot" is associated with both solar worship and with the warrior hero (Gelling 1969; Hawkes 1962). It is certainly this set of associations, born of a fascination with solarism within European scholarship (cf. Dorson 1955), that lies at the basis of the predominant interpretation of South Siberian and Central Asian vehicle images. Within the Indo-European tradition there is considerable association between horse, chariot, sun, and heroic figure. Of all the Vedic-Avestan dieties, Surya is most clearly associated with the sun and the golden chariot. His name is said to have derived from svar, light, a cognate of the Avestan hvare, sun, "which has swift horses and is the eye of Ahura Mazda" (Macdonell 1965, 124). The Avestan Mithra, also, is said to ride before the sun in his bright chariot, but he himself is not a solar deity nor does his chariot represent the sun. 10 Male gods do not, however, have a monopoly on chariots and light. Usas, the Dawn, is also represented as
9 See, e.g., the series of driverless and often horseless chariots from Frannarp, Scania (Gelling 1969: fig. 8). 10 See Yasht 10, translated in Gershevitch 1959.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



132 THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING
coming in a chariot (ratha) of light, bringing the luminosity of heaven. 11 Moreover, within the Vedic tradition, chariots and swift horses are not consistently indicators of male superiority. In demonstrating how in the Inda-European tradition the third function (the cultivators) could gain supremacy over the first two functions (priests and kings, warriors and charioteers), Dumezil cites the tale of Mudgale, from Rig Veda X, who took part in a chariot race by using a cart drawn by a single ox and driven by his wife, Mudgalani (Dumezil 1953: 202, 254-262; Grottanelli 1986: 138-139). In this case, supremacy comes at the hands of a woman driving a slow, ox-drawn cart. Grottanelli has pointed to a number of other instances within Inda-European mythology in which virtuous women driving carts or wagons gain the upper hand over men driving or riding horses. 12 These various instances suggest that the association of chariot, horse, and heroic male is not fully supported even within Inda-European and Indo-Iranian mythology. Among all the images of wheeled vehicles which have been recorded from the petroglyphs of South Siberia and Central Asia, none matches those of the Znamenka stone. Okladnikov has recorded what may be a wagon from the Yelangash complex, but it is pulled by horses and not associated with the images of bovines (pl. XVIlf). I have earlierreferred to the representations of ploughs at Saimaly-Tash in the Tyan-Shan mountains which Golendukhin (1971) identifies with calendrical celebrations of an agricultural society. At Saimaly-Tash, representations of whecle1 implements appear to be used in ploughing (Sher 1980: fig. 107). By contrast, no ploughing images have been recorded at sites further to the northeast, in South Siberia, or in Mongolia. Given the limited agricultural opportunities of that region, the lack of such images may not be surprising. Kozhin has proposed that the wheeled vehicle most strikingly represented in the so-called "Gobi quadriga" (pl. XVIIIa) must be compared with chariots found at Anyang. He dates the Gobi vehicle and the Shang
11 Rig Veda I, 48-49. Even that association is not consistent, however. In Rig Veda IV, Usas is represented as having been humiliated by Indra; enraged by her delay in bringing the dawn, he breaks her cart with his thunder bolt. (Dumezil 1968-1973: 176-188). 12 Even within the founding of the Scythian tradition, the supposed equation of gold, horse, warrior is not consistent. According to Herodotus, to whom we owe the fullest account of the Scythians, the Scythians associated their origins with a golden plough, a golden yoke, a golden battle-axe and a golden cup, all of which fell from heaven. Dumezil has pointed out how, in this account, the Scythians were associated with the implements of the cultivatorplough, cart, oxen-as well as with the cup of the priest and the battle-axe of the warrior. At their very founding, the Scylhians were thus associated with lhe three functions of priest, warrior, cultivator (Dumezil 1953: 263-266).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING 133
Dynasty chariot contemporaneously with the Karasuk period, that is, to the fourteenth - eleventh centuries B.C. (Kozhin 1968, 1977). From this he concludes that all Bronze Age wheeled vehicles in both Central and East Asia must be associated with the Karasuk culture and must be considered to represent the advent of an invading culture dependent on the use of a new war vehicle. Novgorodova also dates the representation of wheeled vehicles in Mongolia to the early Karasuk, which she places as early as the fifteenth to thirteenth centuries B.C. She agrees with Kozhin that the wide spread diffusion of vehicle images represented the pre-eminence of a new battle weapon (Novgorodova 1984: 64). On the basis of the Mongolian representations of wheeled vehicles and, in particular, with reference to the image group from Chuluut Gol comprised of a horse-drawn two-wheeled vehicle, a driver, and a snake-like element (pl. XVIIlb), Novgorodova suggests the relationship of the chariot image to a hero-warrior and thus the vehicle as sign of the emergence of the heroic epic tradition of South Siberia. In those twc instances where deer with large antlers appear near a chariot or its driver, she proposes that the deer should be understood as the warrior's totemic protector (Novgorodova 1984: 70-71 ). Novgorodova's argument and that of Kozhin and other scholars associating wheeled vehicles and specifically "chariots" with a warrior class assume an analogy between the function and symbolic load of the vehicle in eastern Eurasia and the association of the wheeled vehicle with a warrior class within Indo-European traditions. What one actually finds in the petroglyphic images of Central Europe and Scandinavia is a sufficiently varying set of associated images to indicate that cultural values and symbolic meaning must have shifted as traditions moved into new environments. While in Central Europe the image of the wheeled vehicle is sometimes associated with the representations of weapons, the vehicle itself cannot be conceived as a war chariot: in fact, it is usually not a chariot at all, but rather a four-wheeled wagon drawn by oxen. 13 In petroglyphs from Bohuslan, this vehicle is sometimes drawn by a deer, an unlikely animal for a war chariot (Gelling 1969: figs. 7/d, 44/a-g). At other times, it is simply represented without an animal. This variation in draft animals-horses, bovines, or even deer-or their complete absence is repeated in the case of the South Siberian- Mongolian imagery. If one were to assume that the wagon, cart, or chariot were a sign of the warrior,
n See, e.g., a group of rock carvings from Cemmo, Camonica Valley (Anati 1961: 50); or the oxen-drawn wagon represented on the male stcle from Lagundo (pl. XXId).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



134 THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING
then one would also have to conclude that the vehicle itself, without its draft animal, was a sufficient sign of the warrior caste. Whether in the case of European petroglyphs or those from Central Asia and South Siberia, however, or even in the contrast between wagons used in Indo-European burials and chariots used by Near Eastern royalty, one is forced to ask what exactly should be associated with the warrior: the slow wagon, the rough cart, or the chariot? The vehicle drawn by oxen, by horses, by deer or by nothing at all? Which kind of vehicle is actually represented in the South Siberian petroglyphs? We have seen that the images as often as not indicate the solid-wheel construction of a slow cart, rather than the light and multi-spoked wheel construction of a chariot. Sturdy, slow vehicles would be justified by the need to transport goods and people across extensive terrain. The maneuverability of such a vehicle is also appropriate to a landscape characterized by rough, mountainous terrain, narrow high valleys and passes, and the broad grasslands of northern Mongolia. On the other hand, it is difficult to imagine the usefulness of a chariot in most of the terrain of southeastern Gorno-Altayskaya A.O., Tuva, or northwestern Mongolia. The land there is too high in elevation and too rough in surface for a light vehicle of prestige developed in the context of a Near Eastern urbanized society or for warfare on the broad Central Plain of China. In certain parts of the Chuya Steppe chariots might be practical. The inevitable unevenness of terrain and the presence of rugged side valleys, however, raise the possibility that the pursued enemy or hunted animals could quickly find shelter on a higher terrace, inaccessible to a chariot, or that they could rapidly disappear into one of the constant defiles bordering the broader valleys. In the meantime, the pursuing chariot would have fallen to pieces, unable to absorb the vibrations created when a light, wooden, two-wheeled vehicle is pulled rapidly over rough terrain. A number of other considerations, all drawn from the petroglyphic record itself, further challenge the conclusion that the vehicle imagery of South Siberia and Mongolia indicates the intrusion of a warlike society and the presence of a warrior class. In the case of Central Europe, the association of vehicle and warrior is suggested by the occasional representation of weapons together with wheeled vehicles; the association of weapons, bovines, and vehicles with a male figure is particularly indicated by the anthropomorphic stele from Lagundo (pl. XXId). By contrast, in South Siberia and Central Asia there are no representations of weapons found in association with the images of wheeled vehicles, except those few carried by the driver. If one is to insist that the vehicle
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING 135
is a warrior signifier, then it would have to be admitted that in the process of transmission from west to east-from western Siberia and Kazakhstan into South Siberia and Mongolia-an important part of the signifying complex, the weapons of the warrior, were discarded. This would be to admit, however, that the imagery and the symbolic loads the images carried changed with environment and time. However similar may have been the wagons and carts carved onto the rocks of Central Europe and South Siberia, their apparent similarity does not support an argument of identity of substance or signification. The positive arguments joining vehicle representations to a Bronze Age date rely heavily, as we have seen, on material from Bronze Age cultures of western Central Asia and Europe and on the understanding that the Timber-grave culture was a western variation of the Andronovo culture of Central Asia and South Siberia (Chernikov 1960; Gimbutas 1965; Sulimirski 1970a). These finds indicate that wheeled vehicles were used within the burial rituals of Indo-European cultures which had penetrated deep into the Kazakhstan steppe; presumably, these same cultures used wheeled vehicles in their daily life. Where the trail of actual wagons or carts ends, in northern Kazakhstan, their images begin again: in the petroglyphs of the Alichur and Karatau ridges, for example, and in those of Saimaly-Tash. One is led to the conclusion that the wheeled vehicle had to have been reintroduced into northern Central Asia and South Siberia during the Andronovo period and out of the Timber-grave-Andronovo connection. The ploughing representations at Saimaly-Tash indicate, however, an earlier knowledge of the use of animal traction, perhaps with wheeled implements, brought from the Near East to eastern Central Asia. Thus, the Andronovo vehicle may represent a reintroduction of the function of animal traction and with it the introduction of new cultural values. Kozhin and Novgorodova associate the appearance of the vehicle image and, thus, of the "chariot" itself with the Karasuk culture (Kozhin 1968, 1969, 1977; Novgorodova 1984; Nowgorodowa (Novgorodova) 1980). In contrast to the westward-extending Andronovo culture, the Karasuk culture is acknowledged to have been formed out of elements indiginous to Siberia. The people of the Karasuk culture would either have had to reinvent the wheeled vehicle independently-an unlikely circumstance, which no one has tried to argue-or they would have had to receive it from such southern neighbors as the Chinese or from western neighbors, such as the Andronovo. There is no archaeological evidence to indicate that the wheeled vehicle of Shang China could have
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



136 THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING
been indiginously developed; from its Anyang date and the lack of evidence of more primitive forms, it could only have been received from outside. The one remaining source for the reintroduction of the wheeled vehicle, and by far the most convincing one, is the Andronovo culture, with its westward extension into the Timber-grave culture. There are other elements, however, which undermine the theory that the dispersal of the wheeled vehicle was specifically associated with the expansion of the Karasuk culture. In terms of the sheer number of representations, the drift of the wheeled vehicle image seems to have been from the region of eastern Kazakhstan and Gorno-Altayskaya A.O. into Mongolia and present-day Inner Mongolia. There are very few representations of wheeled vehicles preserved in the Minusinsk Basin, the region where the Karasuk culture first clearly emerged. Moreover, while the Mongolian deer stones have been convincingly associated with the Karasuk period on the basis of the weapon typologies represented on the stones (see Chapter VI), there is only one recorded instance of a cart represented on a deer stone and the dating of that image is entirely uncertain (Volkov 1981: pl. 103). There is, also, only one instance of a vehicle associated with the deer image of the type found on the Mongolian deer stones. In that case, from Chuluut Gol, it is possible that the large deer is a later "addition" to the much smaller representation of cart and hunter-driver (pl. XVIIId). If, as Volkov has persuasively argued, the deer stones can be dated to the late Bronze Age, i.e. to approximately 1000 B.C. and later, then it would seem necessary to place the image of the wheeled vehicle earlierthan that date, although within the AndronovoKarasuk chronology. 14 The similar geographic distribution of representations of wheeled vehicles and the mushroom-hatted figures suggests that within the South Siberian-Mongolian arena these two pictorial elements referred back to the same cultural context. Their actual connection is not, however, completely clear. Within the Yelangash complex, two-wheeled vehicles may possibly be associated with the mushroom-hatted figures. I have referred already to one instance (pl. XVIIlc) in which a hatted figure "stands" on the axle of a two-wheeled vehicle drawn by a bovine and a horse. Above the head of the man is a small deer with antlers. Whether deer and man were done together is unclear, as is the exact nature of the man's headwear. From Chankyr-Kel' comes a more certain image of a
14 For discussions of the origins and dating of the arrival of the wheeled vehicle in South Siberia, see, inter alia, Formozov 1969; Kozhin 1968; Novgorodova 1978, 1984; Leont'ev 1980; Sher 1980.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING 137
mushroom-hatted figure. He leads two horses and seems to be approaching a two-wheeled cart, as if to harness the animals (pl. XVIIe). As in the case of the previous relationship of deer to man, however, this relationship of horses to vehicle is merely hypothetical. 15 Only one known representation of a mushroom-hatted figure associated with a wheeled vehicle has been found in Mongolia, in the Chuluut Gol complex (pl. XVIIIb). From all the known images of both mushroom-hatted figures and wheeled vehicles, from Kazakhtan through Gorno-Altayskaya A.O., Tuva, and Mongolia, there are only three instances in w~ich the two elements may possibly be connected, and only one-that from Chuluut Gal-where the connection seems certain. One can only conclude that if indeed the two pictorial elements belonged to the same cultural stratum, they were not necessarily associated.
I have indicated that the images of "chariots" and "warriors" have had an overwhelming influence on the general understanding of Bronze Age South Siberia and northern Mongolia. The Indo-European solar theories into which all petroglyphic imagery has been fit necessarily color the prevailing understanding of images of deer, cattle, syncretic forms, and mushroom-hatted figures. These images have either been ignored, or the cattle are turned into bulls and the deer into solarized animals. Within that interpretative strategy, there has been little possibility for locating the actual focus on rituals which bind community. 16 A careful examination of the petroglyphic record, however, would indicate different circumstances and different conclusions. Both the petroglyphic record and its association with the archaeology of the Aeneolithic and Bronze Age across Eurasia suggest that during the second millennium B.C. there was a gradual intrusion of peoples from the west and southwest into northern Kazakhstan, the northern Altay, and Mongolia. These peoples had originally inherited the Indo-European traditions of the Timber-grave culture as well as elements from the Near Eastern traditions which had preceded the Indo-European overlay but persisted within the agricultural oases of Central Asia. In other words, the Bronze Age cultures reflected in the Saimaly-Tash and Tamgaly petroglyphic complexes were not purely Inda-European and cannot be fathomed using only the tools of a simplified Inda-European
15 I can cite no other representations in which a figure appears to be harnessing animals to a vehicle. 16 For a continuation of the interest in solar interpretations, see, most recently, Martynov 1990.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



138 THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING
mythic tradition. We must assume that as the Andronovo peoples pushed on into South Siberia, their customs and beliefs met, confronted, and were reshaped by the customs and beliefs of the autochthonous populations. The climatic rigors and rugged landscape of South Siberia had to have constrained mythic traditions which had arisen far to the west and south. The very process of penetrating into the Minusinsk Basin and settling in a land which opened to the taiga and tundra of the north must have forced belief systems onto the imagination of the newcomers that had been shaped by the landscape, the weather, the native plants and animals. Physical and cultural survival must have insisted on accommodation and change. As Andronovo met Karasuk and as non-Siberian traditions merged into those of Siberia, reflections of the resulting cultural modifications spread themselves across the petroglyphic record and left signs which contradict the prevailing conceptions of an intruding warlike culture. The image groups that are indicative of interaction or rudimentary narrative emphasize ritual, the hunt (which may also be ritual), and interaction relating to wheeled vehicles. Within those Bronze Age traces, there is no indication that the cultures responsible for the petroglyphs placed a particularly high value on either individual human combat or on war. Indeed, the vast majority of the scenes eschew violence and the hunting scenes are striking for their lack of violence; figures and animals are balanced in size and number, as if to suggest a balance in actuality. There is no indication that the mushroom-hatted figures functioned more frequently as hunters than did any other kind of figure, nor does petroglyphic evidence allow the conclusion that they may have been warriors. While war, combat, and plunder may well have been part of cultural life, within the petroglyphic record we find no validation of these activities. The imagery catalogued above indicates that the hunt for meat, ritual hunting, and ritual dance and sacrifice were valued above all other activities. The vehicle, too, even without a driver, seems to have had a particular, if still unclear, significance. In the vast majority of cases, the wheeled vehicle is drawn by only two animals, usually horses but occasionally bovines. The largest concentration of these representations comes from the valley of the Yelangash River with extensions into the valley of Chuluut Gol. The general aspect of all the represented vehicles is that of a vehicle used for everyday practical purposes rather than for military purposes. The vehicles seem to be driving in all directions or to be facing in all directions; the very arbitrariness of directionality seriously under
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING 139
cuts the assumption that the vehicle referred to the sun in its course through the heavens. Moreover, the appearance of the cart alone indicates that it was valued in and of itself, without any necessary reference to warriors or horses. There are no texts to indicate how the vehicles functioned in the life of Bronze Age peoples of South Siberia and Mongolia. We do know, however, something about the significance of the cart within the life of the Mongols of the Mongol empire. This has been described by Western visitors to the courts of the great khans. The Mongols' carts were apparently essential elements in the wealth of a household. They were small enough to carry the personal chests prized by women or large enough to carry large gers (yurts). The latter could require up to 22 oxen in a team. According to Friar William of Rubruck (journeyed 12531255), such a cart would require an axle as large as the mast of a ship (Komroff 1928: 59-60). William's account is interesting for the light it sheds on the use and significance of the carts. He indicates that these carts were intimately associated with a sense of community. The "Tartars" (as he called them) would carry their houses on their carts and whenever they stopped to make camp, they placed the carts with their chests still on them "here and there, within half a stone's throw of the house: so that the house stands between two ranks of carts, as it were, between two walls." The carts carrying the family wealth seem to have been associated particularly with women: they carried the women's personal chests, often carved with great refinement. The women, according to William, made themselves "most beautiful carts," and a rich man with many wives thus had many carts for all his women. "One woman will guide twenty or thirty carts at once ... and they bind the carts with camels or oxen, one behind another...They go at a slow pace, as fast as a lamb or an ox might walk" (Komroff 1928: 60-61). William says that the duties of women included, among others, driving the carts, laying their houses on the carts and taking them off again (Komroff 1928: 70). 17 Granting the great time difference between the petroglyphs of the Bronze Age and the Mongolian practices observed by William of Rubruck in the thirteenth century, one may still find in the latter an explanation for the former. Rather than referring to hunters or warriors, or even to the aggressive invasion of a foreign culture, the Bronze Age petroglyphs of wheeled vehicles may simply refer to the presence of a
17 Contemporary Mongolians use horse drawn carts, most frequently with only two wheels and drawn by one or two horses. The flat bed of the cart can be loaded up; the axle is held up along the body of the horse.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



140 THE ELABORATION OF IMAGE AND MEANING
new culture in terms of the signs of its understanding of wealth. With or without draft animals, the cart may have functioned as a cultural sign of well-being; it may have referred to the community as a source of identity and to the presence of that community in a new region. It is entirely possible that such a cultural intrusion was identical with the intrusion of the Andronovo culture into South Siberia and its merging with the: contemporary Karasuk culture of the Minusinsk Basin and northern Mongolia. If we are to take the petroglyphs seriously, however, then we must admit that as that culture penetrated into the physical and culturall environment of South Siberia, it is possible that its Indo-European mythic traditions and values were constrained and reshaped by new conditions of survival and other belief systems. The wheeled vehicle may have begun as a sign of the Indo-European warrior and as a reference to the sun, but we have seen that the insistence on conflating wheeled vehicles, solar signification, and warfare raises serious questions as to whether cultural theory leads or is led by historical documentation. However the image of the vehicle functioned within IndoEuropean traditions, it seems to have last appeared in eastern Eurasia in a different form: as a sign of a cultural presence and as the mark of community. Like the images of ritually dressed figures, the images of vehicles carved into the cliffs and boulders of South Siberia and Central Asia trace the gradual infiltration of one culture into a hitherto unknown territory. These images reflect neither warriors nor solar worship nor even the emergence of a dominant warrior caste, but rather community as identity, the cart as a sign of household well-being, and the community reaffirmed by rituals. Within those rituals the primary animals seem to have been cattle and deer. Whether both were used cannot be certain; but their ubiquitous presence and the curious syncretic deer-cattle certainly indicate that they were referred to-one a sign of the pastoral economy, the other a sign of a continued physical and spiritual dependency on forest animals.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



CHAPTER SIX
DEER STONES AND WARRIORS
Anthropomorphic Monoliths of the First Millennium B.C.
A. HISTORICAL AND GEOPHYSICAL CONTEXT
Yalbak-Tash is the name of a small pass on the north side of the Chuya River above the confluence of the Chuya and Katun'. To the casual observer it is only a crumbled mass of rocky outcroppings. Observed more closely, one may begin to discern fragments and broken surfaces on which remain images of deer, caprids, horses, and horsemen, the reflections of centuries of cultural traditions from the Bronze Age to the present. The millennia have taken their toll here and few smooth surfaces remain. High in the center of Yalbak-Tash, a vertical surface stands out from the surrounding stony debris. Its face is marked by a number of silhouetted images, visible even from a distance. When lit by the side light of morning or late afternoon, the chipped-in shapes separate themselves from the mauve-grey stone. Particularly striking are the images of two stylized reindeer (pl. IXh)-their bodies exaggeratedly elongated, their withers sharply raised into a point, their hindquarters rounded as if tensed for flight. By contrast, their legs are short and thin-almost withered appendages. The heads of these deer are no less stylized: small, elegant, with the nose and jaw region elongated into a long beak-like form. The lower animal, a buck, has a large antler which recedes back over his neck. The animal above has the smaller rack of a female. The stylized deer at Yalbak-Tash are late versions of an image which appeared in petroglyphs of South Siberia and Mongolia in the late Bronze Age. Those at Yalbak-Tash are among the western-most instances of this type and date to the period of the Early Nomads, but eastward, in the Chuya Valley at Kalbak-Tash, throughout the Yelangash complex, and on the Barburgazy River flowing down from the Saylyugem Ridge1 this particular deer image is repeated again and again (e.g., pl. Xllc,d,e; pl. XVI). The vast majority of these deer images are antlered and most lack any indication of gender. Only in late instances, as here
1 The Saylyugem Ridge marks the border between Gorno-Altayskaya A.O. and Mongolia.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



142 DEER STONES AND WARRIORS
at Yalbak-Tash or in the case of a pair of images from a site further east on the Barburgazy River (pl. XVId), is there any clear distinction between male and female animals. At Yalbak-Tash, Kalbak-Tash, Yelangash, and Barburgazy, the deer with exaggerated antlers, elongated bodies, and beak-like heads describe a cultural intrusion out of northern Mongolia and westward into presentday Gorno-Altayskaya A.O., an intrusion which also left traces north into Tuva and southwest into Kazakhstan. More importantly, they are the most distinctive aspect of the so-called "deer stones" of South Siberia and northern Central Asia and serve as the source of that peculiar name. In fact, these stylized deer images appear only on the stones associated with Mongolia and the Transbaykal; their anthropomorphic reference, however, is repeated in the deer stones of Tuva and Gorno-Altayskaya A.O. and even in the monolithic sculpture associated with Eastern Europe in the late Bronze and early Iron Ages. As much as the image of the deer itself, the deer stone maps the changing ecology of culture and belief across the Eurasian Steppe in the first millennium B.C. At this time, considerably more than 600 deer stones have been recorded in the region of Central Asia and South Siberia. 2 Related stone images are now known to have been carved across a vast region that included parts of South Siberia, Central Asia, west to the Kuban in Georgia, the region of the lower Bug River, Dubrudzhe in northeastern Bulgaria and even on the Elbe River (Chlenova 1962; Savinov and Chlenova 1978).
B. DEER STONES OF MONGOLIA AND THE TRANSBAYKAL
In a collection of careful drawings first published late in the last century, the archaeologist and explorer V. V. Radlov called attention to the deer stones of ancient Mongolia, as well as to the Turkic stones and runes which succeeded them (Radlov 1892). His reproductions demonstrated the manner in which these stones were regularly enveloped in images of deer both elegant and highly stylized. The drawings also indicated the extent to which the deer stones were set within elaborate stoneworks. In some cases, these stoneworks resembled the vertically protruding walls of a grave; in other cases, they took the form of elaborate circular mounds
2 Already in 1979 Kubarev reported more than 500 known in Mongolia alone, as well as approximately 30 in Tuva, and SO recorded by himself and others in Gorno-Altayskaya A.O.. (Kubarev 1979). A few years earlier, Schul'ts had estimated that there were 42 known images from the Scythian world (Schul'tz 1976).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



DEER STONES AND WARRIORS 143
with "fences," "gates," crosswalks, or repeated circles of seven or eight boulders. Radlov's drawings suggest that the deer stones were part of elaborate ritual constructions, presumably of considerable ancient significance. Interest in the deer stones was rekindled in 1954 by A. P. Okladnikov' s study of a stone which had been collected in 1856 by D. P. Davydov from a place described as "between the Ivolga and Klyuchevskiy stations, along the highway from Verkhne-Udinsk to Selenginsk, 22 verts south of Verkhne-Udinsk" (Okladnikov 1954). According to Davydov's description, the stone had been found lying flat on the ground. Under it was found "rubble" and the bones of a headless horse. Okladnikov believes that it was this deer stone which can now be found in the State Historical Museum in Irkutsk and which has come to be referred to as the Ivolga stone (pl. XIXd). The Ivolga stone measures approximately 3.5 m. in length, 90 cm. in width at the center of the stone, and about 19-20 cm. thick. In its original setting it rose about 2.9 m. over the surface of the ground. The upper end of the stone is cut on a diagonal slant; the resulting high thin side is generally considered to be the "face," or east, side of the stone. Covering the two wide surfaces of the stone are the elegantly carved figures of deer with heads facing towards the taller thin side . The carving of the deer images is shallow: the deer have small triangulated forelegs, truncated hind legs, exaggeratedly long bodies and necks. Each deer's withers are marked by a pronounced rising point, their heads are distinctly and deliberately transformed into long bird-beaks, and their antlers flow back over their bodies in great rising waves. Between the large bodies of deer are smaller ones, as if to fill space, as well as the small bodies of horse-like animals; on the south side a kind of battle axe may be seen. In a few cases, the heads of small "filler" deer are turned backward and their legs are folded under their bodies in a more realistic, recumbent posture. On the narrow east face of the Ivolga stone are a series of objects including, at the top, a small deer and beneath it a round circle, a bow with readied arrow, and several weapons and tools, ending with a double hook. The shorter, narrow west side of the stone is likewise marked by the bodies of small, beaked deer placed vertically with heads up; beneath them is a trapezoidal shield-like object of uncertain significance. In the triangular area at the top of the stone's north face a circular object with a tail descending like a handle is carved. On the south side of the stone, in the same position, are two circles, the upper one smaller than the
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



144 DEER STONES AND WARRIORS
lower. Beneath these two is a line, set as if to separate the space of the circles from the deer. The Ivolga stone offered Okladnikov an opportunity to present the rich vocabulary ofimages which have come to characterize the TransbaykalMongolian deer stone. On the basis of the deers' pronounced forehead tines, Okladnikov identified these animals as reindeer. He argued that the circles on the upper north side of the stone and on the east side were: long-handled mirrors and that the line on the upper south side was a belt. He suggested that the stone should be dated to approximately the seventh - sixth centuries B.C. and that it was contemporary with the early slab graves of the Transbaykal, "of which the inventory, disregarding for a. moment its rarity, retains unquestionable Karasuk characteristics'" (Okladnikov 1954: 218). He surmised that the Ivolga stone was probably the creation of a local late Bronze Age culture, one dependent on stock and, to a lesser extent, agricultural cultivation. Okladnikov believed that the stone had to have been associated with funerary rituals and that it must have functioned as a monument to a particular dead person. On the basis of the weaponry represented on the stone, he concluded that the dead person to whom the stone was dedicated was a warrior, and since the slab graves associated with the stone must have required an expenditure of significant social energy, the warrior memorialized by the stone must have been a leader of high status. Okladnikov's conclusions established the essential interpretative strategy that has characterized the approaches of most scholars. This strategy has assumed that the stones were associated in some way with funerary monuments and that they refer to male warriors. From this identification of the representational value of the stones has emerged the conclusion that, by the late Bronze Age, the nomadic and semi-nomadic cultures of the Eurasian steppe were dominated by a warrior elite. The male reference carried by the weapons and belts of the stones indicates, in turn, an established patriarchal social organization (cf. Dikov 1958: 46). The Ivolga stone is an unusually fine example of the Mongolian deer stone type-that is, of that type found primarily in the Transbaykal and Mongolia. Almost invariably, these stones are covered with the stylized images of recumbent deer with bird bill-like heads and exaggeratedly large antlers. Smaller deer, horses, weapons, and tools were carved into intervening spaces. The belts with which most of the stones are carved are particularly suggestive of an anthropomorpic reference. Even in the case of crudely carved stones, the encircling belt and its hanging
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



DEER STONES AND WARRIORS 145
assortment of tools and weapons evokes the order of a human body from the otherwise mute stone. Among those tools and weapons can be found axes, daggers, hooks, and a peculiar, as-yet unidentified shield-like object. Around the upper part of the stone may appear marks which have been identified by a number of scholars as "necklaces," "earrings," mirrors, and other objects which underscore the essentially anthropomorphic reference of these stones (e.g., pl. XIXa, c). To date, the most extensive study of the deer stones of Mongolia is that by V. V. Volkov (1981). Following the earlier_ observations of Okladnikov and Dikov, Volkov distinguished between two cultural traditions which were joined by the appearance of the characteristic Mongolian stone. In the Transbaykal, eastern Mongolia, and the region to the east of Govi-Altay Aymag, the deer stones appeared in the context of cemeteries, the graves of which were marked above ground by rectangular "walls" of vertically set thin slabs. These complexes have been referred to in scientific literature as slab graves; they are associated with a Siberian cultural tradition centered in the late Bronze and early Iron Ages in the Transbaykal and in northeastern Mongolia (Dikov 1958; Volkov 1967; Grishin 1968, 1975). Through the rare finds of bronze objects such as knives or daggers (e.g., Grishin 1975: fig. 13), the slab-graves have been connected with a late Karasuk bronze culture. The relatively infrequent skeletal remains which have been recovered from the slab-graves also indicate a fairly clear Mongoloid element (Gokhman 1967; Grishin 1975). 3 The deer stones were generally set up near the corners of the slab-graves, or as part of the slab wall. In some cases, a single stone seems to have functioned as a kind of a guard stone, set about 8 to 10 m. east of the graves. In their occasional placement at the corners of the slab graves, they suggested to Volkov both guard stones and a kind of hitching post for tying a horse (Volkov 1981: 81). In several cases they were left lying on the ground, like a kind of hearth before the burial site. Such an arrangement has been recorded at a site near Ulangom in Uvsanyr Aymag and at Khereksurin Denzh, in Bayankhongor Aymag (Nowgorodowa (Novgorodova) 1980, 1989; Volkov 1981: pl. 37). The manner in which the horizontal stones were intended to function is uncertain; it remains possible that at those sites, as elsewhere, their use as covers over burial or sacrificial sites may represent the reuse of stones originally intended for another purpose. While the deer stones are connected with the slab-grave culture
3 On the other hand, the slab-graves with accompanying deer stones have been so lacking in furnishings and skeletal remains as to suggest to Volkov that they were not graves at all, but rather ritual sites (Volkov 1981: 81).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



146 DEER STONES AND WARRIORS
centered in central and northeastern Mongolia and the Transbaykal, they are also associated with a culture whose most impressive remains include large stone kurgans, surface monuments of stones known as "khereksur," and circular enclosures or alters. The khereksur are typically round in plan and surrounded by round or square enclosing walls articulated with considerable variety. 4 The khereksur seem to have functioned as ritual structures of some kind, marking the sanctity of a place and the locus of sacrificial altars. Khereksur complexes have been recorded across northern Mongolia as well as in Tuva and southeastern Gorno-Altayskaya A.O.5 The ritual complexes marked by Mongolian deer stones and khereksur were often elaborated by other surface structures: by stone kurgans, round stone altars laid out in regular patterns, and "walls" and "gates" surrounding the khereksur or kurgan. 6 The complex at Ushkiin-uver, Hovsgol Aymag, in northern Mongolia, is an excellent example of the complexity of these architectural and sculptured ensembles (pl. XIXb). The khereksur took the form of a great mound surrounded by a wall of stones and by a larger circle formed of circular stone altars. Stretching to the south of the khereksur were two rows of large deer stones-fifteen in all-carefully organized among circular altars (Volkov and Novgorodova 1975; Volkov 1981). At Khargalant, in Arhangay Aymag, the combination of deer stones and hundreds of stone altars suggests a gradual elaboration of the site by generations of worshippers (Volkov 1981: pl. 21). In such an instance one has the impression of a site sanctified as much by continuous tradition as by any specific time-bound ritual. 7
4 The term, khereksur, or kereksur, comes from the Mongolian term, "khargias-ur," meaning large, elaborately structured stone mounds, often with walls, gates, crosses, and attendent enclosures or altars; see Khudyakov 1987. 5 In recent years several scholars have called attention to a tradition of khereksur and ritual altars in the Transbaykal, particularly in the region of the Selenga River and its tributaries; see Danilov 1987; Tivanenko 1989. 6 Particularly clear discussion of elaborate stone complexes associated with the khereksur may be found in Grach 1980 and in Mannay-ool 1970. Mannay-ool dates these elaborate forms to the Scythian period, but most scholars date the khereksur to the late Bronze Age and early Iron Age. Khudyakov (1987) has attempted to determine a chronology of deer stones and khereksuron the basis of the organization of parts and the semantics of the stones. The results are not, however, persuasive. 7 The khereksur sites ofTuva could be almost as elaborate as those recorded in Mongolia. At the Tuvan site ofUlug-khorum, for example, the khereksur took the form ofa giant spoked wheel, among the stones of which were found pieces of deer stones. Associated with this khereksur were a large number of regularly placed round stone altars (Grach 1980; and see Grach 1960a, 1960b).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



DEER STONES AND WARRIORS 147
The actual placement of the deer stones within both the slab-grave and khereksur complexes was not consistent. This variation points either to a broad range of functional possibilities or to the regular resetting of stones as needed by ritual (cf. pl. XIXb, c, e). By their placement on or in proximity to the khereksur or altars, they seem to have functioned as guard stones or corner markers. At Khargalant and Ushkiin-uver, the stones were evidently closely connected with the stone rings and small mounds which seem to have served as ritual altars. At the base of many of the stones have been found coals and bone ash, and many of the stones recorded by Volkov had been heat-burned in antiquity (Volkov 1981: 81). In some cases, however, the deer stones have been found lying on their sides, as if purposely disposed, and almost completely covered with earth.8 These peculiar variations in the placement of the stones again suggest their reuse by a culture later than that which had first produced them. Volkov believes that the time between the making and reusing of the stones may not have been very great: first made for a sacrificial altar, they could have later been reused within a burial structure. On the other hand, when the deer stones are found lying on the top of kurgans or khereksur (e.g., pl. XIXc), it is not possible to say whether or not they were actually set up vertically on these mounds and subsequently fell or whether they might have been used in that manner as structural material from the time of the construction of the mound (Volkov 1981: 94). 9 All who have studied the deer stones have been particularly interested in what is referred to as their semantics, about which I will have more to say below. For the time being it should be understood that while there are only six stones in Mongolia which have been carved with human faces, there is a virtually universal understanding that the deer stones carried an anthropomorphic reference. The three slash lines on the upper part of the stones refer to some aspect of the human head, while the circular elements on either side of the "head" are usually understood as earrings, and the bead-like elements around the head appear to be neck ornaments. The belt and hanging weapons and hooks confirm the identity of the figure as that of a warrior, while the curious trapezoidal
8 At Zheregsurin denzh, Bayankhongor Aymag, Volkov found about twenty stones laid flat on the ground. At Temeni chuluu, Ovclrhangay Aymag, the deer stones used in the construction of a tall slab wall had been buried upside down in the ground (Volkov 1981: 81). 9 A well-known case in point is the fragmented deer stone found within the boulders making up the Arzhan. Whether the deer stone was set up intact on the mound or whether it represents the reuse of a discarded stone considerably affects how one would date both the deer stone type and the burial itself; see Gryaznov 1980.

element which occurs regularly on deer stones is considered to be a shield. 10
C. DEER STONES OF THE SAYAN-ALTAY REGION
For many years, the Sayan-Altay deer stone tradition was known only through occasional articles, most of which looked at broader issues of dissemination of style and at questions of meaning. In 1979, V. D. Kubarev published what has been the most comprehensive study to date of the deer stones of Gorno-Altayskaya A.O. His book focused attention on the least well-known deer stone tradition, as well as on the sculptural type itself (Kubarev 1979). In considering the stones ofGorno-Altayskaya A.O., Kubarev established an approach to this sculptural form which correctly returns it to its larger archaeological context. In considering the deer stones of the Chuya and Katun' river valleys, Kubarev distinguished two types: those of a tall, thin type, the "face" of which was always on the thin eastern side; those stones which tend to be short and heavy, square in form and usually no higher than 1 m. In the case of the taller stones, the full length of which may reach 3 m., the upper part often slopes down from east to west in a manner reminiscent of a standing human figure. In some cases these stones are quite rough in cut, suggesting a great pointed slab of stone (e.g., pl. XXf). Both types-tall and thin, and short and heavy-shouldered- seem to have been contemporaneous. In both cases, however, one confronts the problem of use and reuse, and thus the issue of the intertextuality of image and type. There is ample indication that the Gorno-Altayskaya A.O. stones of both kinds were frequently reset and even recarved, first by the Early Nomads of the Pazyryk period (i.e., fourth-second centuries B.C.), and then by the Turks (seventh-ninth centuries A.D.). In the process of being recarved and reset, many of the taller stones seem to have been broken. A particularly clear example of the deer stone complexes of this region was recorded by Kubarev on the high bleak steppe of the Yustyd River, the central tributary to the upper Chuya (Kubarev 1979). The Yustyd complex is remarkable for the appearance of a deliberate order
10 A yoke-like object has been found on a number of the deer stones but its function has not been confidently identified. Some have suggested it was used for the bracing of a bow, others have associated it with a chariot or as the symbolic indicator of a chariot in the form of a kind of model yoke. Bronze counterparts are well known from Tagar-Karasuk, Andronovo, and Zhou Chinese burials but their function and meaning are no clearer; see Belokobyl'skiy 1985).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



DEER STONES AND WARRIORS 149
focused on some sacred ritual (pl. XXg). Within a great terrace several kilometers long, throughout which are scattered ritual rings, kurgans, and rows of stones from many different periods, Kubarev recorded an ordered grouping of perhaps 104 stone rings, running in rough rows of three, from north to south. On the east side of this grouping, also running north to south, were found perhaps 30 stones, either whole or in fragments. Originally, the stones must have been set vertically in the ground, facing to the east. Kubarev believes that they were arranged in pairs, north to south. Within all the rings were found the remains of strongly carbonized material, including animal bones, thus confirming the general hypothesis that these round rings represent ritual altars for burnt offerings of sacrificial animals. Among the stone rings is one square enclosure located roughly halfway between the north and south ends of the complex. At its center stands a large stone, #6, measuring 260 cm. on its longest side and 20 cm. at its greatest width (pl. XXf). In general shape and size, stone #6 appears to fit into the Sayan-Altay deer stone type. On its rough sides only three images have been carved: those of a hammer (south side), a dagger (east side), and a quiver (north side). While suggesting the sides of a person's body and the arrangement of weapons hanging from a belt, the small number of images and their modest size suggests almost an afterthought: as if the weapons had been added long after the stone's cutting and raising. The stone shaft had been set solidly into a mixture of earth and gravel and was wedged with larger stones for added stability. Within the material compressed at the base of the slab a number of unusual elements were found, including two burned horse teeth, some iron slag, and-just under the turf-pieces of four deer stones, recognizable by the presence of carved elements characteristic of the Sayan-Altay deer stone type (Kubarev 1979: pls. II-III). At a depth between 20 and 30 cm. a group of stone pestles was found, one of a decidedly phallic shape; several stones which appear to have been pieces of deer stones; a stone carved with a mask-like image; fragments of thin ceramic and black crystal. 11 The materials gathered and buried at the base of this large stone suggest a complex history of use and reuse, the treasuring of old objects,
11 Pestles, a stone mortar, and a finely worked diorite bear's head, as well as animal teeth, a tusk, and bone necklaces, have been recovered from an group of Afanasevo burials near Kuyum, Gorno-Altayskaya A.O. (Bers 1974). Volkov reports the finds of stone pestle-like objects mixed with the coals and ashes found near a deer stone at Dagan-de!' (Volkov 1981: 80.) The pestle seems to have been associated with rituals involving both sacrifice and the deer stones.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto

and their investment with a significance which went beyond either the value of their material or their function. These elements also complicate the dating of this stone. Its rough aspect and the stone pestles found deep at its base suggest an early date-perhaps as early as the Neolithic period. On the other hand, the pieces of iron slag and the fragments of more elaborately carved deer stones point to a date in the middle of the first millennium B.C. It is possible that the stone itself is ancient but was reset in the Early Nomadic period; the carved weapons would also date to the time of its resetting. At that time-presumably when this present complex was established-other deer stones were broken and distributed around its base. Other stones in the Yustyd group also indicate a deliberate and sometimes more practical breaking and reusing, often for construction purposes. A tall stone at the north end of the Yustyd complex, for example, was deliberately broken (pl. XXIa). 12 This curious process of breaking and reusing remains mysterious. One must presume that the deer stones themselves were invested with significant power, but with a power of an ambivalent nature. Among the deer stones of Gorno-Altayskaya A.O., as among those of Mongolia, a few are carved with clearly anthropomorphic references. One of three stones recently regrouped near the village of Iya, near the confluence of the Katun' and Chuya rivers, is shaped as if with a cowled hood; at its waist is carved a belt into which is tucked a dagger. A smaller stone in the same group also bears the dim traces of a face and belt. 13 Another stone further to the east in the Chuya Steppe is more interesting since it lacks signs of modern tampering. Rising about 2 m. above the surface of the ground, tilting precariously on its axis, its surface worn and discolored by lichen, the shaft appears from the distance like the lone guardian of the grassy terrace. On its upper east side a distinctive face marks the flat surface of the stone. With a large long face, long nose, a mouth down-turned at one end, and close-set eyes carved deeply into the stone the face far more readily recalls a Europoid type than do the usual faces on Turkic stones of the Altay region (cf. Kubarev 1984). On the
12 Other stones were smashed and used either for the stone debris of circles and mounds or for the rings themselves. Yet another stone was found approximately 150 - 200 m. to the northwest of stone #6. Squat in form (30 x 25 x 15 cm.), it had been placed on the west-most end of a row of balbal running to the east from a small enclosure of the Turkic period. north-east side of the shaft a dagger is carved, while on the north side an image of a horse and of another large object, perhaps a bow, are carved. The appearance of realistic faces on the stones of the Chuya Steppe is curious, even unsettling. In each case, the human face seems stylistically and conceptually out of place within the rigidly monumental context of the stone. In facial type and in their lack of satisfactory integration with the rest of the stone, the mask-like faces recall three masks on a large stone found in the context of a Tagar ritual site, near Bel'tir on the Abakan River, Minusinsk Basin (Kyzlasov 1986: 259260).14 The cowl-like treatment of the stone on the larger Iya figure suggests the hoods worn by Early Nomadic horsemen and by their Scytho-Siberian cousins across the Eurasian Steppe. In each of the three cases, there are no deer images emblazoned on the stones. Nonetheless, the stone type, the original organization of imagery, the shape of the stone with reference to an eastward orientation, and the anthropomorphic references carried by belts and weapons join the stones to the larger deer stone tradition. In most cases, the anthropomorphic references on deer stones of the Sayan-Altay type are considerably more muted than those of the Transbaykal-Mongolian stone type. The stones at Yustyd illustrate this point. With the exception of the large central stone, #6, those found both intact and in fragmentary form are considerably more schematic in their human references than are the three stones with carved faces. Carved chains of beads around the "neck" of the stone, three parallel slanted lines on the "face" or at the side of the face, carved marks suggestive of earrings, and a carved belt and hanging weapons: these are the elements which ordinarily distinguish the deer stones of Gomo-Altayskaya A.O. (e.g., pl. XXa-c). The marks are similar to those one finds ubiquitously on the Mongolian stones; so, too, are the representations of weapons and tools which appear on a number of the stones. On the other hand, the images of animals, including deer, boar, horses, caprids, and crouching feline or wolf-like animals seem more indifferently carved and take up considerably less space than do their Mongolian counterparts. Occasionally, as on the tall, thin stone from Yustyd (pl. XXIa), the stylization of the deer recalls that characteristic of the Mongolian deer stone images, and the crouching predators on the stone resemble the curled felines on
14 Kyzlasov dates this stone to the Afanasevo period, but the argument for that dating is not persuasive.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



152 DEER STONES AND WARR IORS
a stone from Gurvanbulag, Bayanhongor Aymag (pl. XXd). By comparison with their Mongolian counterparts, the animal images from Yustyd are lacking in expressive power. The distribution of animal images on the deer stones of GornoAltayskaya A.O. followed a variety of patterns. The tall stone from Yustyd, referred to above, is carved with images of four recumbent deer: one just below the earring and two-one antlered, the other not-as if "hanging" from the belt. Below the uppermost deer and displayed as if on the "chest" or side of the figure are the remains of perhaps four crouched, wolf-like animals. In other cases, animal images were carved among the "beads" of the necklace on the upper part of the stone, suggesting that the zoomorphic plaque was a part of the ornament: on one stone from Yustyd, a recumbent deer occurs in that position; on another from Kara-Dyurgun, an animal reminiscent of a recumbent horse, but possibly with the beard of a goat, is set among the necklace beads; on another stone from Uzuntal, a boar with hanging legs is placed within the necklace (pl. XXa-c). In all cases, the representations suggest animalshaped plaques that could be worn hanging from the belt or as part of a necklace or pectoral. Such implied ornamentation recalls the carved, wooden images covered with gold that were used to dress the hair and neckpieces of the dead, male and female, in Early Nomadic burials from Tuekta, Pazyryk, and Ulandryk. The pairing ofdeer, antlered and unantlered, is unusual on deer stones of the Mongolian type, where virtually all deer images are antlered. On the Yustyd stone, mentioned above (pl. XXla), the distinction between antlered and unantlered animals was deliberate. A similar distinction has been mentioned with respect to the two large deer from the site on the Barburgazy River (pl. XVId), and the pair at Yalbak-Tash (pl. IXh). However, aside from these examples, there are very few instances of a similar pairing, either on the deer stones or within petroglyphic compositions. In the majority of cases, the deer of the Transbaykal-Mongolian and Sayan-Altay stones are antlered and carry no specific sign of the male gender. There has been considerable dispute regarding the chronology of the deer stone types, with some scholars believing that the simpler the carved imagery, the earlier the type, and others arguing the reverse. 15
15 Gryaznov has argued that the Sayan-Altay type preceded that of Mongolia and should be dated as early as the twelth to ninth centuries B.C. (Gryaznov 1978). Kyzlasov has argued that the stones with what he calls "animal style" images (i.e., those of the Sayan-Altay type) date to the Scythian period, while those of the Mongolian type are later (Kyzlasov 1978).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



DEER STONES AND WARRIORS 153
Although the weapons represented on the Mongolian stones are Karasuk in typology, the chronological uncertainty of the Karasuk-Tagar horizon indicates only that the tradition of deer stone carving may have begun by 1000 B.C. and may have lasted for about 500 years (Volkov 1981: 102104). 16 By assigning a relatively early date to the Mongolian deer stone type, Volkov is able to argue that the images found on these stones and on those of the Sayan-Altay type were fundamental to the vocabulary of the Scytho-Siberian "animal style" and that they thus indicate the South Siberian origins of this style of art (Volkov 1981: 115-116). ,tis certainly true that the deer, boar, and feline images from certain stones from Tuva and Mongolia (e.g., pl. XXd, e) are replicated on many objects from Early Nomadic burials, such as in the great feline plaque from Arzhan (pl. Ila). On the other hand, the highly stylized deer of the Mongolian type do not reappear in that exaggerated form in the plaques and fabrics of the Early Nomads. The deer type found there was stylized, to be sure, but it retained a powerful realism. This discrepancy between the Mongolian deer stone image and the deer images of the Early Nomads confirms Volkov's suggestion that the Mongolian stone type may have preceded the Sayan-Altay type, or that the Early Nomads derive much more closely from the culture specifically responsible for the SayanAltay deer stone tradition.
D. DEER STONES AS SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS
I have already mentioned the widespread belief that the deer stones refer to a heroized male ancestor. Most interpretations of these stones represent variations on that theme, but they are variations shaped by a common concern for the affirmation of Inda-European or Indo-Iranian values and traditions. Thus the shape of the stones is taken to refer to cults of clan fertility (e.g., Kubarev 1979). The remains of charred altars, charred horse bones, and other signs of burnt offerings in association with the deer stone complexes have suggested to several observers the association of animal-specifically, horse-sacrifice with the deer stones. Such sacrifices would have been part of the ancestral cult appropriate to
16 Volkov cites two late Karasuk type daggers which were found at Ulangom, a site he dates to the fifth to third centuries B.C. Volkov argues that the tradition may have gone from the more detailed and anthropomorphically clear stone to the more simplified type of the Sayan-Altay stone (Volkov 1981: 118).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



154 DEER STONES AND WARRIORS
the ancestral aspect represented by the deer stone. 17 The erecting of stones and sacrificial altars indicates a heroization of the clan ancestor and specific dead. Given the difficulty of raising the deer stones, their carving and raising must have been reserved only for "particularly respected and heroized ancestors, with an emphasis on marks of male strength" (Kubarev 1979: 86; Ol'khovskiy 1989). The image of the antlered, presumably male, deer emerges within the deer stone cults as a solar and cosmic animal, a kind of substitute for the cosmic horse. Like Kubarev, Volkov believes that within the deer stone complexes, horse sacrifices referred to Indo-Iranian solar cults, but he joins the association of deer/horse/sun to Okladnikov's understanding of a Siberian cosmic stag-that is, to a Siberian version of the solar animal and myth (Volkov 1981: 94-96; Okladnikov 1964; and cf. Martynov 1990). The animal images carved on the sides of the stones are explained as ornaments embroidered or sewn onto the warrior's clothing, or they refer to tattooing of the warrior's body, a tradition attested to among the Early Nomads by the man buried in Pazyryk 2 (Kubarev 1979: 81-83). In contrast to the arguments of some scholars that the circle with handle motif refers to an earring (Chlenova 1962; Kubarev 1979), Vaynshteyn accepts Okladnikov's argument that it refers to a mirror. However, the traditional Siberian mirror, known from Early Nomadic and Zhou period burials, had its handle in the shape of a central knob rather than in the form of a long handle. As a modern but similar instance of an object invested with great power. Vaynshteyn cites the shaman drums of Sayan-Altay peoples such as the Khakass, Shors, and Altaytsy. The trapezoidal object with diagonal lines may refer in highly stylized fashion to the chest cavity with ribs, just as up to the present shamanic robes are marked on the front and back with skeletal references (cf., Savinov and Chlenova 1978: 75). Rather than referring to warrior values and implements, the mirror, deer, trapezoidal shape, and other marks on the stones refer to ancient shamanistic rites (Vaynshteyn 1974: 30-32). In this respect, Vaynshteyn moves the interpretation of the deer stones closer to specifically Siberian traditions, rather than seeking their meaning within lndo-European traditions. In his opinion, the deer stones functioned not as anthropomorphic funerary monuments but as protective powers, appearing within the context of specific constructs. He
17 The offerings also suggest to Kubarcv the desire to lend to the dead the sexual and physical strength of the horse (Kubarev 1979). In these arguments Kubarev agrees with Kuz'mina's argument for the Indo-European basis of late Bronze Age society in South Siberia (Kuz'mina 1977).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



DEER STONES AND WARRIORS 155
suggests that their function may have been similar to that of the stone walls found around so many of the burial mounds of South Siberian peoples of the late Bronze and early Iron Ages. Although the meaning of those walls also remains unclear, they suggest the establishment of protective precincts: figurative dividers between the realms of the living and the dead. In an early study of the Mongolian stones, N. L. Chlenova proposed that the deer stones in general referred to warrior leaders of a people who invaded South Siberia and Mongolia from a region in Central Asia-the same region, perhaps, from where emerged the very image of the Scythian deer (Chlenova 1962a, 1962b, 1963). Referring to Abayev's argument (Abayev 1949) that the deer was the totemic animal of the Sak:as, Chlenova explained the size and number of the deer on the Mongolian stones as a reflection of the need of an intruding peoplepresumably ancestors of the Sakas-to ressert the sign of their cultural identity (Chlenova 1962a: 34-35; cf. Abayev 1949). The stones thus refer to the carriers of lndo-Iranian traditions and must be interpreted in light of Vedic-Avestan beliefs. She has argued that the Mongolian and South Siberian deer stones, with their zoomorphic imagery, must be related to the stone "baba" of Scythia (about which I shall speak later): in both cases, the stones were associated with burial ritual and structure and in both cases they referred to warriors (Chlenova 1962b, 1984). The deer stone conceived as the sign of a heroized warrior also serves as the basis of Novgorodova's critical interpetation of the Mongolian deer stones. Like Chlenova, Savinov, and Volkov, she points to the comparison between weapon imagery and the Karasuk-Tagar bronze weapons and she dates the stones ofTuva, Mongolia, and the Transbaykal to the late Bronze Age (Nowgorodowa (Novgorodova) 1980; Novgorodova 1989; Volkov and Novgorodova 1975). Novgorodova interprets the peculiar trapezoidal object as a shield, indicative of the stone's martial reference. Within their cultural setting, the deer stones functioned as "cultic monuments," dedicated first to the deer and later to the horse as sign of the ancestor, the sun, and the totemic source. In reading the deer stone as symbol, Novgorodova tends to push interpretation to an extreme. In the upper part of the stone, she finds reference to heaven and the connection between this world and that of the spirits; the middle section, usually filled with the representations of hoofed animals, refers to the middle world; the lower section of the stone, including the representations of weapons hanging from the belt, refers to death and sacrifice and hence to the lower world (Novgorodova 1984: 108-109).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



156 DEER STONES AND WARRIORS
As provocative as such a tripartite interpretation is, on too many stones the order is considerably less clear, with animals and weapons occurring both above and decidedly below the belt of the stone. 18 This variety of interpretative strategies indicates both the complexity of issues involved in the deer stone tradition and something of the centrality of this tradition to the artistic inheritance of the Early Nomads. The stones associated with Mongolia and the Transbaykal, and characterized by distinctive stylized images of deer, were apparently used by two cultural groups, one responsible for the so-called slab graves of Transbaykal and eastern and central Mongolia, the other responsible for the khereksur, kurgans, and stone altars common to western Mongolia, Tuva, and southeastern Gorno-Altayskaya A.O. At this time, both the kherekur and slab-grave structures are believed to have emerged in the late Bronze Age and to have lasted well into the Payzyrk period. Lacking more certain evidence than now exists, it is not possible to say whether the slab-grave culture inherited the Mongolian deer stone type from a culture to the west, one associated with the khereksur complexes, or the reverse. The combination of two different types of ritual and funerary complexes (slab-grave and khereksur) and one kind of ritual stone (deer stone) would indicate a non-belligerent meeting of cultures in which certain fundamental elements of belief were so similar that one kind of monumental carving functioned effectively within two different types of complexes. Similar observations may be made with regard to cultural change in the region ofTuva, Gorno-Altayskaya A.O., and northwestern and central Mongolia. As we have seen, in those areas the khereksur complexes formed a common cultural tradition across present-day political borders, but the deer stone type could and did vary, from one dominated by the elaborate Mongolian deer image to one dominated by the somewhat more realistic images of elk, reindeer, and boar, and even to a relatively uncarved monolith. Within a single general tradition characterized by monolithic carved stones a variety of carving styles coexisted. Despite the number of scholars who associate the deer stones with an Indo-Iranian tradition which privileges an ancestral male warrior and which joins him to a solar signification, there remain a number of facts which are difficult to reconcile with these theories. The stones do, indeed, all appear to represent male figures with implements which may
18 Novogorodova's claim that the part of the stone below the belt would be buried underground is open to considerable question, particularly in light of the stones found buried inverted in the ground, or simply left lying on the ground.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



DEER STONES AND WARRIORS 157
be interpreted as weapons or tools, depending on one's point of view. Nothing else in the aspect of these figures indicates a particularly martial aspect. All the images seem to have been located within ritual and sometimes funerary complexes, and all appear to have been originally placed facing to the east. While a few images representing predatory animals-e.g., wolves, felines, and boar-may be found on the stones, there is not a single instance of the representation of an actual animal attack and certainly no reference to human combat. Moreover, although there are a few cases in which the male deer is distinguished by the indication of his sex, such definition is unusual, and belongs, I believe, to a late phase in these representations. Antlers alone do not indicate gender since antlers are carried by both male and female reindeeranimals which seem to have been represented on the classic deer stone type of the Transbaykal and Mongolia. Finally, deducing a phallic and male emphasis on the basis of the shape of the deer stones seems to be a case of creating meaning (phallic cults) where there is simply a straightforward functional necessity: in this case, the need to set a marking stone into the ground. The deer stone tradition of South Siberia and Mongolia appears to indicate the source of what came to be known as the Scytho-Siberian animal style. The very image of the deer-stylized, monumental, dominating the stones of Mongolia and Tuva and marking the stones of Gorno-Altayskaya A.0.-would seem, necessarily, to precede the deer images of the Early Nomads and the Scythians. The kurgan of Arzhan, dated by its excavator to the eighth century, is perhaps the earliest burial associated with the Scytho-Siberians. The great bronze feline plaque found among the Arzhan ruins may well be the earliest of a series of such coiled felines, including the undated Mayemir gold plaques from GornoAltayskaya A.O., the small gold felines dated to the seventh-sixth century, from Chiliktin in eastern Kazakhstan, and the undated gold plaque from the Siberian Treasure of Peter the Great. Yet the images of coiled or crouching felines on the deer stones from Gurvanbulag, Bayanhongor Aymag, and Yustyd (pis. XXd, XXIa), may be even earlier than the date of Arzhan. At the least, the wolf or feline images from the Yustyd and Gurvanbulag stones, the coiled feline from Arzhan, and the coiled gold felines found in the Mayemir Steppe indicate that South Siberia was the source of at least one of the three images fundamental to the Scytho-Siberian artistic tradition. The deer stones confirm that the deer image also had to have derived from South Siberia and northern Central Asia. Although almost all the deer stones carry the imagery of
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



158 DEER STONES AND WARRIORS
weapons and although one may confidently argue that the deer stones do refer in their surface carvings to the figure of an armed male, there are no signs of a specifically belligerent stance: neither in the postures of the stones nor in the representation of animals on the stones. The steadfast lack of imagery representing the actual struggle attendant on animall predation confirms the hypothesis that the deer stones should be dated\ no later than the very early stages of the Scytho-Siberian period, when in the zoomorphic imagery of the burials themselves no portrayals of actual animal combat can be found. At most, the images of predators and prey were simply juxtaposed.
E. CIMMERIAN STELAE AND ScYTHIAN "BABA"
One of the most curious elements within the Scythian artistic tradition are the stone human images traditionally referred to as "baba." Awkward at times to the point of being ungainly, these images immediately call to mind the deer stones of South Siberia and Mongolia; they also bear an undeniable resemblance to the even cruder stone images associated with the Cimmerians, those people who preceded the Scythians in the region north and east of the Black Sea. Any study of these two traditions is hampered by the fact that virtually all the stones-Cimmerian and Scythian-were found within disrupted sites. Most are badly damaged, some to the point of unrecognizability. None have been adequately published. The Cimmerian tradition of anthropomorphic monoliths is represented by a stone found within the embankment of a Cimmerian kurgan known as the Ptichata burial, opened in 1971 in Belogradets, Bulgaria (Toncheva 1972: fig. 6; Savinov and Chlenova 1978: fig. 2/6). The stone is a tall, thick block, the top of which has been broken. A row of beads indicates the neck region, while a belt with gorytus and knife confirms the stone's crude anthropomorphic reference. On the lower part of the stone is visible a section of an interwoven pattern. Because of its placement as a reused stone within the Cimmerian context, the Belogradets stone must be dated earlier than the seventh century B.C. There are a number of other stones associated with the Cimmerian period of the North Pontic Steppe (Toncheva 1972; Savinov and Chlenova 1978; Chlenova 1975, 1984). Like the Belogradets stone, they tend to be crudely anthropomorphic in reference (e.g., pl. XXlc). On two or three of the stones assigned to the Kuban Cimmerian culture of the eighth century B.C. may be found images of horses or goats. In contrast to the
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



DEER STONES AND WARRIORS 159
zoomorphic images on the Mongolian deer stones, these are enclosed within circles and suggest a distinctively Kuban style (Savinov and Chlenova 1978: figs. 2/1, 2, 3). Some of these stones have a curious aspect: they seem to have been carved similarly at both ends. For example, both ends of a stone from Ust'-Labinsk, in the Kuban, are carved with the circular line and a round circle that suggests a "head." Another stone, from Zubovskiy khutor in the Kuban, is carved at both ends with a necklace and eye-like circles as well as with circled animals (pl. XXlb).
