This deliberate treatment of the stone as an object which can be inverted echoes the actual inversion of deer stones at a number of Mongolian sites. 19 It is also reminiscent of the manner in which the deer images on the Mongolian stones had no regular directionality: they could be placed facing to the right, to the left, to the upper end of the stone, or to the theoretically lower end of the stone. Chlenova has proposed that the Cimmerian stones refer to a god of thunder borrowed by the Cimmerians from the Hattians. She and Savinov have identified the Cimmerian stones with a common CimmerianKarasuk tradition, manifested particularly in the weapon typologies found on the stone figures (Savinov and Chlenova 1978; Chlenova 1984; cf. Terenozhkin 1978). The particular treatment of both the Cimmerian stones and of the deer stones of the Sayan-Altay type-with necklaces, belts, circular elements which may refer to earrings or ornaments, but generally without a definition of the human face-certainly supports the hypothesis of an essential link between these two anthropomorphizing traditions. Most striking, perhaps, is the summary character of the anthropomorphic reference and the occasional treatment of the "head" in terms of a flat area sectioned from the lower part of the stone by a band or necklace.20 These parallelisms serve as yet one more element in the still unclear connection between South Siberia and the South Russian Steppe in the late Bronze Age. The male figure type represented with belt and weapons suggests the possibility, too, of an essential connection with anthropomorphic stelae from Central Europe, even though these are dated considerably earlier in the Bronze Age (Gimbutas 1973; Bernardini 1975).21 This monolithic
19 Personal observation at an unnamed site in the Khangay Mountains; and see above regarding the stones at Temini chuluu, OvOrhangay Aymag. 20 Compare, for example, stones from Gumarovo in the southern Urals and Ust'-Labinsk in the Kuban with stones from Sosnovka and Saygyn Mountain in Tuva (Savinov and Chlenova 1978: fig. 2/1, 2; Kyzlasov 1978: fig. 3). 11 Because of the weapon imagery which is so frequently carved on these stelae, the figures have been identified by Gimbutas with the "earliest known visual images of IndoEuropean warrior gods" (Gimbutas 1973: 201).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



160 DEER STONES AND WARRIORS
figure type is exemplified by the famous stele from Lagundo, TrentinoAlto Adige, in northern Italy, engraved with daggers, a belt, and a four-wheeled vehicle pulled by oxen (pl. XXId). Among the Bronze Age stelae from Central Europe, however, a number ofcarved stones refer not to male figures, but to female figures identified as such by their clearly indicated breasts.22 The fabrication of monumental female figures in Bronze Age Europe suggests that if the deer stones of South Siberia were linked to the warrior figures of the Alpine area through the transmission of a Bronze Age tradition, either the female figure was dropped out of that tradition in its gradual passage eastward, or that we are not reading the deer stones correctly. On the other hand, among the few published stones associated with the Cimmerian culture, all seem to refer to male figures by virtue of their belts and weapons. One is left with problematic options. If there was an historical connection between the South Sibe-rian-Mongolian deer stones and the Central European stone figure tradition, and unless there are as-yet unstudied Cimmerian female figures, then the apparent male reference of both the Cimmerian and South Siberian stone figures may indicate the transformation of an older Bronze Age tradition as it was transmitted eastward across Central Asia. At some point in space and in time, the specific representation of the female disappeared, replaced, or perhaps masked, by the male image. In recent years, approximately forty-five so-called Scythian baba from the Kuban, the Black Sea region, and Bulgaria have been published (Shul'ts 1973, 1976; Goranka 1972; Petrenko 1986). Virtually all are crudely carved stone figures representing male warriors holding rhyta (pl. XXle). In several instances they appear to be wearing armor. Tucked under and hanging from their belts are long swords, short swords, and goryti. None of the figures are dated and almost all were found in conjunction with severely disrupted sites. Schul'ts believes that many were originally erected on the tops of kurgans; as the kurgans collapsed,, the stone figures fell and broke, becoming part of the stone rubble. He believes that the phallic shape and martial character of the figures referred to hero-ancestors and that the stones functioned in ancestor cults associated with funerary rituals (Schul'ts 1976: 228-229). The baba are considerably more realistic in their human representation than are the
22 See, e.g., a stele from Val Pellice, with a stick figure of a female and with the indications of her sex emphasized; and one of a group of stelae located in the Museum de Casola, Lunigiana (Bernardini 1975: figs. 32, 84). A number of such figures from the Catacomb-grave period of the North Pontic region have indications of both (female) breasts and of the male sexual organs. Gimbutas has suggested, somewhat unconvincingly, that the female breasts may represent a later "reshaping" of the stone [Gimbutas 1965: 497].
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



DEER STONES AND WARRIORS 161
older Cimmerian stelae. This stylistic change might reflect the inherent maturation of a representational process. One also suspects, however, that such maturation more directly reflects the impact of Hellenic taste on the artists of the Scythian world after the sixth century B.C. Like Schul'ts, Rayevskiy has emphasized the phallic aspect of the Scythian baba. This aspect is located not simply in the shape of the figures; it is figuratively referred to in such carved weapon imagery as the whetstone and, literally, in apparent representation of the male sex on one figure (Rayevskiy 1983: 88, pl. XII). 23 Rayevskiy joins these perceived phallic references to his conviction that Scythian mythology and belief systems were fundamentally charged with an Indo-Iranian cosmogony (Rayevskiy 1970, 1978b, 1979, 1985). The underlying conception of the Indo-Iranian universe, in Rayevskiy's understanding, is three-leveled: heaven, earth, and the underworld. The most important symbolic references to that tripartite structure are the World Tree and the human body, the paradigm of which is Purusha, the Vedic Cosmic Man. The head of Purusha is the sign of the heavens; the reproductive organs and digestive organs refer to the earth. From this it follows that the Scythian baba also refer to the tripartite structure of the universe; the indication of their sex refers to origin and stability within the universe. Like other scholars who have attempted to interpret Scythian art in light of Indo-lranian traditions, Rayevskiy is troubled by the discrepancy between the male-centered universe they find in the Inda-Iranian traditions and Herodotus's terse description of the Scythian' s religious beliefs:
The only gods the Scythians worship are Hestia (their chief deity), Zeus and Earth (whom they believe to be the wife of Zeus), and, as deities of secondary importance, Apollo, Celestial Aphrodite, Heracles, and Ares. These are recognized by the entire nation; the Royal Scythians also offer sacrifices to Poseidon (Herodotus (1954]: 260).
Hestia, Herodotus informs us, is the Greek name for Tabiti; he uses Zeus for Papaeus, Earth for Api, Apollo for Oetosyrus, Aprohodite for Argimpasa, and Poseidon for Thagimasadas. Tabiti, the chief deity, was the goddess of the hearth. In her person was located the source of familial and social stability. However, since the Scythian baba refer to male figures, Rayevskiy concludes that their presence at funerary sites refers them back to Targitaus who, according to Scythian legends recounted by Herodotus, was the son of Zeus and the ancestor and first king of the
23 The stones to which Rayevskiy refers are badly damaged and available only through sketches which do not offer convincing evidence for his argument.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



162 DEER STONES AND WARRIORS
Scythians. The three sons of Targitaus, each the ancestor of one of the Scytho-Siberian nations, also represent the three zones of the tripartite universe (Herodotus [1954]: 243-244; Rayevskiy 1983b: 52). Rayevskiy's interpretative strategies rest upon assumptions which have wide and long-standing support. They include the belief that the Inda-Iranian tradition manifested in Scythian antiquities represents a relatively pure continuation of traditions rooted in Vedic-Avestan texts. However, even if the Scythians themselves derived from an Iranian language group, that fact in itself does not predict the particular details of Scytho-Siberian culture. The Scythians' pastoral economy, their zoomorphic imagery, and even many of their weapon types find their most clearly traceable origins within the eastern region of the Eurasian Steppe. Even the tradition of carving stone figures is more clearly associated with the South Siberian-Mongolian deer stone traditions than with the still obscure figure-carving tradition of the Cimmerians. In other words, the interpretation of Scythian cultural artifacts in IndoIranian terms must be tempered by the awareness that there is as yet little knowledge of Scytho-Siberian languages and virtually no agreement as to what set of archaeological facts would predict an Inda-European or Inda-Iranian culture as distinct from a culture which had adopted and adapted some traditions with Inda-European or Inda-Iranian elements. In the vicissitudes which brought the nomads from South Siberia and Central Asia, belief and myth must have undergone changes as profound as the transformation of their environment. Under these circumstances and even if the Scytho-Siberians derived from ludo-European or IndoIranian roots, it is unlikely that interpretations taken directly from Vedic-Avestan texts would be applicable to Scythian antiquities. This point may be emphasized by returning to one object, the rhyton, which is a particular attribute of many of the Scythian baba (e.g., pl. XXIe). The rhyton, or horn cup, has attracted the attention of students of Scythian art not only because of its frequent appearance among burial furnishings and representations in Scythian goldwork, but also because it figures prominently in the Scythian account of their origins as told by Herodotus. It must be recalled that the Scythians traced their lineage and territories back to the youngest son ofTargitaus, who claimed the golden plough, yoke, battle-axe, and cup which fell from heaven (Herodotus [1954]: 243-244). 24 It is possible that the cup here should or could be understood as a rhyton.
24 It is curious to note that the golden objects would indicate a cultural value placed on
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



DEER STONES AND WARRIORS 163
Many of the Scythian baba appear to be males carrying weapons and holding rhyta against their chests. One of the most interesting is a broken statue from the Kuban, located in the Krasnodar Museum (pl. XXlf). 25 The figure is headless and dressed in armour. Hanging from his belt and across his belly is an akinakes. Like many of the other Scythian baba, this one presses his forearms against his upper body. In his right hand and against his chest he holds a large rhyton. According to the Schul'ts drawing, the figure carries the image of a recumbent deer under his throat as if it were a clasp.26 There are presently no other known Scythian stone figures which carry deer or other animal plaques in the same place, but the suggestion of an animal-shaped plaque does recall the animals in circular frames found on the upper parts of several of the Cimmerian stelae. It also recalls the inclusion of animal images in the necklaces worn by a number of the Sayan-Altay deer stone figures (pl. XXa-c). The Krasnodar figure is unusual in its relatively sophisticated realism. The representation of a figure holding a rhyton is not, however, unusual among the baba; there are at least seven other recorded figures which hold rhyta.
Schul 'ts dates the Scythian baba between the sixth and the third centuries B.C. He dates the Krasnodar figure to the fifth century on the basis of its akinakes. By virtue of their references to male figures with weapons, the Krasnodar figure and the other Scythian baba invite a comparison with the South Siberian deer stones and the Cimmerian stelae. They differ from those other sculptured images in the clarity and relative realism of their anthropomorphic references. It is certain that this greater concern for the representation of a human figure is based on the observation and emulation of Greek human representation (Schul 'ts 1976: 228-229). On the other hand, the baba with rhyta may be directly related to the many gold plaque representations of Scythians holding rhyta in the act of pledge or consecration. A fine gold plaque from the fourth century barrow of Kul Oba represents two Scythian males drinking from one rhyton as if in a pledge of loyalty (Piotrovsky 1987: pl. 196). On a silver rhyton from Karagodeuashkh the representation of two Scythians on facing horses was engraved, the rider on the left holding a rhyton in his right hand and an upraised sword in his left, and the much
ploughing and warfare, but not on the kind of pastoral economy out of which the Scythians are believed to have emerged. "Compare Schul'ts 1973, fig. 2; Savinov and Chlenova 1978, fig. l/l; Goranka 1972, fig. 9/a. Unfortunately, there are no photographs available to me of this figure and noone has commented on the discrepancies in detail between the published drawings. 26 This, as well as the rhyton, is also missing from the Goranka drawing.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



164 DEER STONES AND WARRIORS
damaged rider on the left raising his right hand in response (Minns [1913] 1971: fig. 121). Figures holding rhyta appear, also, in more complex scenes from Scythian burials; in those complex scenes, I believe, may well lie the key to the meaning of the Scythian baba. In Chapter II, mention was made of a series of plaques from Chertomlyk and Merzhdana and of a gold headdress frontlet from Karagodeuashkh-all representing male Scythians approaching seated women (pl. Vic, d, e). In some of the Chertomlyk plaques, a standing male figure drinks from a goblet before the seated figure of a woman holding up a mirror. In other plaques from Chertomlyk, the woman is frontal. With both hands she holds a goblet(?) over her lap. On her left is an altar topped by a flame or plant-like crown. To her right stands a male figure holding a rhyton in his right hand and raising his left towards the woman. In both representations, the woman is considerably larger than the male figure. Both images are reminiscent of the crude plaque from Merdzhana, representing a female figure seated frontally on a throne holding in her right hand a cup-like object (Artamonov 1969: 331). Hanging on the top of the left-hand back post of her throne is the head of a horse; to the right of her throne stands a large tree. Back and to the left of the throne is the figure of a male rider on a small horse. This figure holds up arhyton, as ifto honor the female figure. The gold plaque from the Karagodeuashkh headdress repeats this theme with greater elaboration. Thus in each of the plaques the subject is similar: two men address each other with rhyta, or male figures stand or sit on horses with raised rhyta before a seated woman. The woman may be frontal or in profile, and she may hold a mirror or a cup or vase. She may be accompanied by an alter, a tree, and a horse-head. The problems of identification of these figures can only be touched on here, but the subject will be returned to in the last chapter. Rostovtzeff identified the female figures from the Chertomlyk plaques and from the crown plaque from Karagodeuashkh with a goddess of the Ishtar-Astarte type, here handing the sacraments of power to a young Scythian lord. Artamonov rejected this interpretation, believing that these scenes, like that from Merdzhana, refer to the acceptance of the gift of divine abundance and strength from the goddess through the drinking of sacrificial liquid (Artamonov 1961). Artamonov argued that the female figures here actually represent a Scythian conflation of two or more deities: Tabiti, the most important deity of the Scythians and referred to by Herodotus as Hestia, and Artimpasa, to whom Herodotus gave the name Aphrodite. Tabiti-Hestia, according to Artamonov, must be asso
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



DEER STONES AND WARRIORS 165
ciated with the altar, while Artimpasa-Aphrodite must be joined with the mirror. Hestia was the Greek goddess of the hearth and of sacrifice, as well as the protector of the household. Artamonov points out that while Hestia may have been in some respects a Greek equivalent to Tabiti, in fact the latter was significantly more important in Scythian religious traditions than was the former. As the goddess of the hearth, Tabiti was the ancestral mother in whose person was indicated the unity of the clan lineage. Animal sacrifice was consecrated to her and to honor her the cup of sacrificial liquid was consumed. While the crude representations on the Merdzhana plaque reflect a provincial hand and possibly a more Asiatic conception of the goddess, the narrative realism of the Chertomlyk and Karagodeuarshkh representations reflects the taste for Hellenistic realism which had overtaken late Scythian art.27 The several representations also establish the rhyton or cup as an implement central to some ritual of consecration. This series of images, however, is particularly intriguing for another reason: their image combinations indicate that the stone baba with rhyta must be placed within a larger conceptual context. In speaking of the composition with horsemen from the Karagodeuashkh rhyton, Artamonov argues that there, in contrast to the cult scenes represented on the Merdzhana plaque or on the Pazyryk 5 felt hanging (pl. Via), "the main personage is no longer a goddess but rather a god," perhaps the very god referred to by Herodotus as Papaeus, better known to the Greeks as Zeus (Artamonov 1961: 74). There is nothing in the Karagodeuashkh representation, however, to distinguish a "god" from a horseman with rhyton: both represented figures are roughly equal in appearance, size, and position. I would suggest that these figures simply represent horsemen in the act of devotion-symbolized by the raised rhyton-to some unrepresented deity. The rhyton scene is thus a variation on the Kul Oba representation of two Scythians drinking from the same rhyton. The Karagodeuashkh headdress plaque and the Chertomlyk and Merzdhana plaques suggest that the unrepresented focus of that devotion was a female deity. In a like manner, the baba-usually found alone, without an accompanying figure-referred not to their own
27 Onayko has argued that neither the "great goddess" nor the "mounted god-hero" represented, for example in the Merdzhana plaque, could have come out of Hellenic or Ancient Near Eastern traditions. They rather refer to an Asiatic response to Mediterranean traditions (Onayko 1976). This conception is also reflected in Artamonov' s characterization of the Merdzhana and Pazyryk 5 scenes as significantly different from Greco-Scythian work and indicative of a tradition outside mainstream Scythian conceptions (Artamonov 1961: 63).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



166 DEER STONES AND WARRIORS
significance and not to the significance of an unseen god, but rather to the implicit presence of a female figure, for whom the raised rhyton was a sign of obeisance and honor. It is as if the plaques filled out with narrative clarity the event merely implied by the stone baba: that the presence of the latter implied the honoring of another figure. That other was not a heroized male ancestor; she was a woman, or at least a being who in the context of Hellenized late Scythian art was given the body and gestures of a woman. Whether to call her Hestia-Tabiti or another may be set aside for the time being. What is certain is that she was a figure of major significance for the Scythians. This hypothesis is supported by a late and poorly published group of monumental stone figures from the Kuban. This group is a set of three large images, one female between two males, which was found late in the last century near Prigradnaya. The figures had apparently stood near a kurgan and represented some kind of ritual site. 28 One of the male statues was originally of monumental proportions: with head (now lost), it may have been as much as 4 m. high. This figure carried a gorytus at its left hip and held a rhyton in its left hand and against its chest. The second, poorly described male figure was smaller: without its head it measured 2.80 m. The size of the female figure is not given, but she, too, was presumably of monumental size. Like the seated women in the Chertomlyk plaques, this one wore a long robe and her head was covered with a soft cloth that fell over her shoulders. In her right hand she carried an unusually long rhyton. Schul 'ts has dated these figures to the latter half of the fourth century B.C. The monumental Prigradnaya set involving a male on either side of a female, with at least one of the males and the female carrying ritual objects, reflects the group of figures in the Karagodeuashkh plaque and echoes the scenes in the plaques from the royal Scythian burial of Chertomlyk. The Prigradnaya figures lend support to the hypothesis that the male baba with rhyta indicate not heroized ancestors and not even the ancestor as sign of a tripartite IndoIranian universe, but rather the presence of a female deity, one which under the sway of Hellenic realism took the form of a woman. Information regarding find sites and find conditions for most of the Scythian baba are too incomplete to conclude that these stones figures were intended as funerary monuments or as part of a ritual complex.
28 All available photographs of these figures are extremely unclear. Schul 'ts argues that because of their size and because of their combination of unusual elements, the figures must represent Meotian deities rather than Scythian deities (Shul'ts 1973: 197-201). The distinction between Scythian and Maeotian cultures, much emphasized in his day by Artamonov, remains vague and unsatisfactory, however.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



DEER STONES AND WARRIORS 167
Within burial furnishings, rhyta have been found associated with both male and female skeletons. The plaque from Kul-Oba showing two Scythian males drinking from one rhyton indicates that this kind of vessel could be used in some kind of ritual oath of brotherhood. In all other representations of drinking, however, males dressed in typical Scythian garb are indicated in the act of addressing female figures. The rhyta raised by both males and females apparently functioned as signs of consecration directed to the female of the group. Although the monumental size of the Prigradnaya figures indicates their late date and although the clumsiness of their carving also indicates a provincial hand, the group confirms the signficance of a scene represented more gracefully in the Chertomlyk plaques. When Herodotus described the Scythian pantheon he also pointed out that the Scythians neither made statues nor raised altars or temples. The anthropomorphic representation of that pantheon apparently did not exist yet. This suggests that the earliest stone baba, datable perhaps to the fifth century B.C., represented not deities but male warriors or guardians or similarly significant members of a human social fabric. The Prigradnaya triad was the result of a slow process by which Scythian deities and their mythic traditions, originally embedded in zoomorphic imagery, gradually acquired anthropomorphized formulation. One can only assume that the Kul Oba representations of Scythians pledging brotherhood had had some earlier pictorial indicator, but that lies hidden behind a combination of zoomorphic forms. Similarly, one must imagine that the statement of consecration performed by a Scythian male before a seated female must earlier have been designated by its own zoomorphic sign, one unreadable to eyes which demand stories told by human figures and human actions.
F. GATHERING TOGETHER: SCYTHIAN BABA, CIMMERIAN STELAE,
AND THE SOUTH SIBERIAN-MONGOLIAN DEER STONES
In figure type, in male reference, and in the general association of stones with burial mounds or ritual sites, the Scythian baba point back to some degree of connection with the Cimmerian stone figures. Both, in turn, seem to refer to two more ancient points of origin. On the one hand, it is impossible to ignore the similarities between the Cimmerian stones and the great stone figures associated with Bronze Age Central Europe. In the end, however, there are no continuous routes which lead from those ancient figures down to the Cimmerian and Scythian monolithic
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



168 DEER STONES AND WARRIORS
figures. We may simply have to acknowledge an idea too good to be ignored within two cultures widely separated by time and relatively separated by space: by virtue of their own histories and by virtue of available materials, both cultures turned to stone and to the anthropomorphic image as the medium for artistic and mythic expression. There is a possibility, however, that impulses which lay behind the male and female stelae of Central Europe made their way across Eurasia in the course of the Bronze Age, entering South Siberia perhaps only late in the second millennium B.C. The images of carts which began to appear at that time in southeastern and eastern Kazakhstan, and which then spread into Mongolia, Gorno-Altayskaya A.O., and Tuva, argue that together with the intrusion of the Andronovo culture, new values and expressive traditions must have appeared in that far eastern region of Eurasia. Unfortunately, such a map of the universe of carved stone figures is purely theoretical. We lack any material to indicate that the value placed on anthropomorphic stone sculpture was actually continuous from west to east. Perhaps between those distant points, wooden sculpture, now lost, served the same purpose. It is also possible that the anthropomorphic figure as concept, not image, was carried in some other form or lodged within some other expressive context across the long trail running irregularly from Central and eastern Europe to South Siberia. Within this morass of uncertainty, however, several facts are indisputable. The Central European stone images and those of the North Pontic region, both Cimmerian and Scythian, are clearly anthropomorphic in reference. By contrast, the South Siberian-Mongolian deer stone tradition indicates only an inconsistent and uncertain interest in anthropomorphization. The general shape of the stone, some belts and weapons, and the possibility that some of the signs around the "head" of the stone refer to a human visage: these are the only elements indicating that emergent in the deer stone tradition was the idea of the stone as a human shape, and as a male image at that. Far more explicit is the sign of the deer-like a shroud enfolding the Mongolian stones, like plaques ornamenting those of the Sayan-Altay region. Sometimes more elk or maral-like in appearance and sometimes accompanied by coiled felines or wolves, it is nonetheless the deer which creates the imagistic power of the stone and not the stone's larger carving. In the end, the deer stone may accurately be so named, at least within the South Siberian-Mongolian arena, and insofar as the deer stone appears to lie behind the tradition of the Scythian baba and Cimmerian stelae, those types, also, may be
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



DEER STONES AND WARRIORS 169
seen as distant referents to the deer stone, even if the deer is no longer visible. Earlier in this chapter I referred to the explicit sexual definition of two pairs of deer at Yalbak-Tash and at Barburgazy in the Chuya Steppe. At that point I suggested that the male definition of the large deer was necessitated by a need to distinguish it from the female deer and that the appearance of antlers would not, in this case, suffice. I also suggested that the deer image there and on the Mongolian deer stones-exaggerated, elaborated, clearly suggestive of the merging of deer and birdactually referred to the reindeer, both sexes of which are ·antlered. Here I will state this more clearly. I would submit that in carving the large pair of deer and in feeling the need to define their sexuality, the artist betrayed a loss of archaic meaning. In carving a definitely heterosexual pair of animals, the artist altered the terms of visual discourse within which there was no indication of maleness or femaleness. The deer of the Mongolian deer stones referred in fact to the female of the species: first to that of the reindeer, but through a process of generalization of reference also to the female maral and elk as well. Within the context of whatever mythic tradition motivated the deer stones and the animal images with which they were emblazoned, the female of the species was endowed with magnificent antlers indicative of her pre-eminent mythic position. Within the South Siberian petroglyphic context, only a demythologizing of the antlered female can explain the large pair of deer at Barburgazy. The femaleness which had been obvious to others had become for that artist not obvious at all, and the mythic load with which the female deer had earlier been freighted had likewise been forgotten. The concern for myth reiterated so often on the deer stones and petroglyphs of Mongolia and South Siberia at Yalbak-Tash and at Bargurgazy gave way to a concern for the pairing of elements and for the measuring and contrasting of power inherent in male and female sexuality. As for the Scythian baba, they only confirm my hypothesis that the actual referent of the deer stone was female. In the Scythian case, that ultimate source is revealed not in the stone itself and not in what was present, but rather in what was absent. The male figures with rhyta must have had some specific reference, but their presence within a ritual context referred to someone other than themselves. In the context of Scythia, that "someone" was a seated female: the women to whom the males of the Chertomlyk and Karagodeushsk plaques raised their cups of consecration. With the exception of the surviving figures from Prigradnaya, her presence was indicated in effect by her material
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



170 DEER STONES AND WARRIORS
absence. In the case of the Scythian baba, the stones clearly refer to anthropomorphic forms, just as the goldwork of the Scythians after the fifth century was dominated by anthropomorphic representation. The Cimmerian stelae are considerably less readable, in part because of the uncertainty of their original context, in part because of the crudeness of their carving. On the basis of similarities to the Siberian and Scythian figures, one is justified in assuming that the Cimmerian stelae may fit into the mythic constructs indicated by the Scythian baba and the South Siberian-Mongolian deer stones. Just as the Scythian baba rewrote the deer stones in terms of clear anthropomorphic references, so there, too, did maleness and femaleness become clearly distinguished. If the vast majority of Scythian stone images referred to a human male, we now know that the absent presence was that of a female. This absent presence suggests that the South Siberian-Mongolian deer stones also encompassed a similar distinction, but in a more generalized form. Just as the stone itself was only vaguely anthropomorphic and male in reference, so the deer with which it was emblazoned was female, her essence formulated in terms of an antlered animal. If the absent woman gave meaning to the Scythians' stone warriors, then, likewise, the female deer was the reason for the existence of the deer stones. Facing toward the east, arranged within elaborate ceremonial structures, and accompanied by indications of sacrifice and honor, the deer stones served as the signs of a presence intimated in the body of the deer.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



CHAPTER SEVEN
IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER Pre-shamanic, Shamanic, and Mythic Traditions
In search of the mythic source glimpsed in the materials of archaeology, we come to Siberian ethnography-the one area which may offer decisive verbal and visual clues by which to decipher ancient Siberia's shifting systems of belief. The ethnographic record is, however, no less complex than what we have been considering: indeed, the layering and interweaving of references found within the archaeological sanctuaries of Siberia serve as a metaphor for the layering of objects, myths, and rituals recorded by ethnographers. Just as the representation of a deerbovine at Kalbak-Tash may be the result of a late Bronze Age recarving of an earlier Bronze Age image and the consequent melding of very different myths and values, so may the tale told of a shaman's journey include elements of both archaic and more contemporary development. The investigator of Siberia's past faces the ethnographic record with some of the delight and bewilderment experienced by a hungry diner confronted by a huge buffet: reasoned need is easily overwhelmed by a desire to try everything. The academic result is too often a melange of fascinating possibilities that are offered without regard to chronological or spatial probability. 1 To be useful, the ethnography of Siberia, like its archaeology, must be divided and organized: according to space, time, and the larger context of artifact and belief. It must be considered, in other words, according to its own ecology. Siberian ethnography comprises made objects, oral traditions, and descriptions of rituals and practices. Most ethnographic sources from Siberia have been gathered within the last several hundred years. Those made objects usually include the embellished tools of everyday life, the costumes of everyday and ritual wear, and some objects of purely decorative purpose. The most interesting materials are probably those associated with the religious rituals of community and family (clan) cults, but here complications emerge. While shamanism has been the
1 The belief in the power of ethnography to illuminate the complexities of prehistoric Siberia has been mostrecently and forcefully expressed in Devlet 1988 and Martynov 1990. Throughout this discussion it should be understood that both males and females can be shamans, the use of the masculine pronoun not withstanding.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



172 IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER
most thoroughly examined religious tradition among Siberian peoples and while its artifacts have been accorded the most attention, shamanism has been joined, ousted, or contaminated by other traditions among many peoples: most notably by lamaism among the peoples of Mongolia and among some of the peoples of Tuva and Buryatskaya ASSR; and by Russian Orthodoxy. In a number of cases, the artifacts associated with shamanism have been contaminated by non-shamanic, non-Siberian traditions. Fortunately, such intrusions are relatively easy to spot and separate from the earlier layers-those of pre-shamanic cults and myths and those of shamanism proper (Dyrenkova 1937). The most complex interweaving of ethnographic traditions occurs between those which are centered on the figure of the shaman and those which certainly represent a more ancient, pre-shamanic period. In general, the shamanic cults were maintained by the community as a whole. Even in the event of shamanic intervention on behalf of a sick or dying individual, the larger community would become involved in the preparations for the rite and in the actual ritual itself. On the other hand, ethnographers have recorded many cults common to the community as a whole but the rituals for which were maintained by households or clan representatives. They include cults of the family hearth and ofindividual protective spirits, and cults of the spirits of mountains and rivers to insure good hunting, fishing, or safe passage. These traditions may be distinguished from shamanism in several ways, even when they have been integrated into the shamanic cults. They are characterized by the direct relationship between subject (individual) and object (indwelling spirit), while shamanism is characterized by the mediation between human community and spirit world undertaken by a priesthood (the shamans). The cults are concerned primarily with the natural world and with essential aspects of well-being within that world: the protection of children, for example, or of household stock, or of the fire which centers a family's self-identification. By contrast, shamanism concerns itself with issues of life and death: with sickness and the wandering soul, with death and the necessity to conduct the soul of the dead to another world; with threats which affect the community as a whole. While the household member who maintains the family cults is firmly integrated into the social fabric, shamans usually stand somewhat apart by virtue of the process of their selection and by virtue of their activities. Finally, while the paraphernalia of clan practices are usually simple objects which could be fabricated by any knowledgable individual, the artifacts of shamanism are characterized by an unusually rich symbolism of object
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER 173
and image: elaborated through the artistic imagination of the shaman, specially fabricated by carefully selected individuals, and consecrated by elaborate rituals. The objects and implements used within the pre-shamanic and shamanic cults are explained by a rich body of oral tradition. This tradition contains the degree of consistency and variation one would expect in cultures dependent on oral transmission. While the general themes and structures remain relatively constant, the elaboration of these elements bear the hallmarks of individual creativity. This is apparent in the songs chanted by shamans or in the individual statements by shamans as to how they became initiated (Vasilevic [Vasilevich] 1968). More ancient is the tradition of stories and legends which relate to the natural world and the great pantheon of indwelling spirits by which Siberian peoples felt themselves surrounded, protected, and threatened. Within these tales one finds the accumulated knowledge and understanding of human societies which sought to find order and meaning in the physical features of their land, in seasonal changes, and in the animals, birds, and fish on which they depended for survival. Within this body of pre-shamanic lore and related to this spirit world was another layer of power, elaborated sometimes in the imagery of animals and sometimes in human form. These are the male and female figures which have the power to control cosmic forces, to determine order and disorder on a cosmic scale. By association with cosmic time and space, they reaffirm the shape of the significant universe laid out according to the cardinal directions, the flow of rivers, and the passage of time. Within the oral tradition one may thus distinguish between deities who effect cosmic order/disorder, heroic figures who confront that cosmic order/disorder, shamans who mediate between humans and cosmic forces, and household protective spirits and indwelling spirits who serve as an immediate support to the survival of individual and family.
A. THE SHAMANIC TRADITION
Although shamanic implements and costumes vary from one people to the next, there are general patterns which can be traced throughout Siberian shamanism and which indicate a commonality of tradition (cf. Levin and Potapov 1956, 1961). Throughout the vast area including the Allay Turkic peoples, the Turkic and Mongol peoples of Tuva and Mongolia, and the Paleosiberian and Tungusic peoples of more northern regions, the implements and clothing used by shamans effectively
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



174 IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER
represent a reassignment to themselves of the signs and symbols of an ancient pantheon formulated in the bodies and powers of sacred animals. Indeed, the most obvious referents carried by the clothing and implements of the shaman were to animals, and within virtually all Siberian societies the most significant animals have been deer and elk, a variety of birds, and bear. In donning his cloak and headdress and in commencing the shamanic ritual, the shaman did not simply assume the powers of animal helpers; he also became that animal and was reborn into its body and knowledge. In so far as the cloak and headdress referred to totemic beings, it may be said that the shaman returned to the body and being of the tribal progenitor. This ritual reimbodiment of the shaman becomes clear in the consideration of the shamanic costume of cloak, headdress, gloves, and footwear as universal insignia of the Siberian shaman. Eliade has referred to the shaman's costume as representing "a religious microcosm" by which the shaman's immediate self and the space through which he or she moved became sacralized, in contrast to the surrounding profane space (Eliade 1972). Most typically that cloak would be the whole skin of a specially consecrated deer or bear (Anuchin 1914); made from deer skin, it could be cut in the shape of a bird (Anisimov 1958: 170ff.). Similar references to deer, elk, bear, and birds would be fashioned into the shaman's headdresses, gloves, and footwear. The metal images and objects which hung from the body of the shaman indicated both the shaman's animal-helpers and his animal double (Anisimov 1958; Anuchin 1914; Dioszegi 1968). Overarching all the animal references woven into the materials and imagery of the shaman's costume are specific and general references to the female generative force. Among the Ket, the very shapes and designs of worn objects refer to the female sexuality of the enveloping natural world and to the female character of the originating animal (Anuchin 1914). In wearing the shape of the vulva or its stylized reference in reindeer hair upon his deerskin cloak, the shaman assumed the power invested in the generative aspect of nature, its primary metaphor being the deer as generative force. The Evenk believed that the first shamans and the primogenitors of all later shamans were the old women who guarded the road to the dead. Because of this, the drawings on their robes had to be executed by women (Anisimov 1963: 97). Like those of virtually all Siberian shamans, the Evenk shaman's cloak was similar in cut and elaborate decoration to female robes (cf. Levin and Potapov 1961; Anokhin 1924). The dressed shaman would thus present the appearance of an elaborately gowned woman wrapped in references to
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER 175
antlered animals, birds, and sometimes bear. Within that elaborate set of references would appear signs of the reaffirmation of the half-human, half-animal aspect of the shaman, while specific indicators of male sexuality would be muted or absent. The shaman's caftan-shaped robe was prepared so that the power of the living animal was retained within its skin and transferred to the shaman. This practice undoubtedly refers to the widely held conception among Altayans, Tungus, and Ket of the shaman's animal ancestor as a wild cervid (Dioszegi 1968; Anokhin 1924; Anuchin 1914; Eliade 1972; Anisimov 1963; Potapov 1935). When the shaman died, the robe had to be ritually disposed of, either destroyed or, more often, hung on a tree somewhere in the forest, far from human settlement. The headdresses of Ket shamans and those of the chief shamans of the Evenk took the form of a metal cap with reindeer antlers (Eliade 1972: 155; Anuchin 1914: figs. 103, 104; Anisimov 1963: 100). References to deer antlers were repeated in the designs of various objects hung from the cloak of the shaman and in the branched form of the shaman's staff. This object was also made of metal; its several branches and the occasional appearance of a bird at its crest referred both to the tree by which the shaman effected his journey into the realm of the spirits and to the antlers of the shaman's deer-being. Dioszegi has detailed how the gowns of the Tofa shamans were made from the skin of a specifically consecrated male or female reindeer. Front and back of the gown were decorated with strips referring to the bones of the shaman-as-animal (Dioszegi 1968) and to the belief shared with other Siberian peoples that the bones represent the source of life (Eliade 1972: 158-165).2 Attached to the cloak were streamers of textile, reindeer hair, or reindeer skin. These refer to the body of the reindeer, but also to wings and feathers by which the shaman-deer regained the ability to fly (Dioszegi 1968). This conflation of deer and bird reminds one of the Ket belief that reindeer were formerly winged: their wings disappeared when it became apparent that wings prevented people from harnessing and saddling their deer (Anuchin 1914). In so far as the shaman was the tribal intermediary between the human realm and that of the spirits, the totemic aspect of his clothing reveals more ancient totemic sources of the clan itself. The robe of the shaman referred to the deer or elk as a primary tribal progenitor as well as the
2 When the shaman-to-be is "killed" in order to be reborn as a shaman, the process involves the (imagined) stripping of the flesh from the bones and the reconstitution of the reborn shaman from the rearticulated skeleton. See, also, the very similar gowns of the Altaytsy (Anokhin 1924).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



176 IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER
shaman's animal ancestor (Potapov 1935). The preparation of the shaman's drum also reveals archaic totemic beliefs. The skin from which the drum was made had to be taken from a specially hunted and consecrated deer. The drum frame had to be made from slats taken from a living and consecrated tree. Should the tree die as a result of the cutting of its wood, then the drum would die, too. 3 Within virtually all Siberian shamanic societies, the drum had to be "enlivened" and consecrated through a special ceremony (Vajnstejn [Vaynshteyn] 1968; Potapov 1947). Conversely, when the drum reached a certain age-perhaps 14 or 15, the age of an old deer-it was said to be too old and had to be replaced by a new drum made from the skin of a younger animal (Dioszegi 1968). When the shaman died, his or her drum had to be ritually destroyed. To drop the drum during the shamanic seance was equivalent to the killing of a deer and it was considered to presage the death of the shaman (Anuchin 1914). The concepts revolving around the making and using of the drum indicate that it was conceived in terms of the shaman's deer progenitor and the shaman's steed. In its "body" was reaffirmed the essential association of deer progenitor and Tree of Life and, thus, of the source of the tribe and the location of the tree within the most important tribal rituals. Among the Evenk, the deer-drum was the steed of the shaman, but it was also conceived as the cosmic deer who lay at the base of the Tree of Life. From that tree was fashioned the drum frame (Anisimov 1958: 156 ff.). In this complex of elements, the drum appears as the progeny of both the Animal Mother and of the tree and the mediator between the shaman and the locus of tribal generation. A similar complex of ideas are found, for example, in Selkup rituals accompanying the vivification of the drum. This ceremony would occur in the spring, at the time of the all-important migration of birds. At that time., the shaman, accompanied by his spirit-helpers, would go into the forest to the tree from which had been fashioned the frame of his drum. They would gather the chips that remained on the ground and place them in a pile in the swamp where lived the Ylyunda kotta, the old WomanMistress of the universe. That place was also the point where converged the two cosmic rivers, that of the eagle and that of the nutbreaker (Anisimov 1963, 188). In this complex of ideas the tree of the shaman's drum is returned to the locus of the tribal progenitor, the old woman from
3 See the description of drum making and drum symbolism in Dioszegi 1968; Potapov 1935, 1968; Ivanov 1955.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER 177
whom flows the tribal rivers of life. The fundamental association of deer and tree and of the relationship of the deer to a female progenitor is again evident in the beliefs surrounding the fabrication of the Ket shaman's drum. According to Anuchin, the skin of the drum had be made of deer skin and the frame had to be taken from a south-facing branch of a ceder tree. The Ket allowed only women to prepare the deerskin, but the rest of the drum was prepared by men (Anuchin 1914: 48). The drum was the very heart of the shaman's steed._ In the sounding of the drum was described the shaman's arduous journey in search of the souls of the sick or to accompany the dead. The framework by which the shaman held the drum was frequently shaped like an anthropomophic doll, the "master of the drum." Among Altayic peoples, the name of that part of the frame preserved a reference to the reindeer ancestor represented by and re-embodied in the drum (Potapov 1935). The resonators on the frame of the drum were sometimes referred to as the arteries of the deer totem (Tofa), sometimes to antlers (Yakut), sometimes to ears (Selkup), and sometimes to the deer teats by which the shaman would derive vital sustenance in his or her journey (Khakass) (Dioszegi 1968; Potapov 1935). Within such conceptions is reaffirmed the understanding of the deer-drum as progenitor, nurturer, and protective steed. Over the long course of the shamanic ritual, the shaman would beat his drum and recount with words and movements the journey he had undertaken. As he chanted, the drum would rise and fall and its pendants and streamers move and sound in imitation of the breathing of the shaman's deer-steed. The dependency of the shaman on the deer-steed and the conviction that the drum would be transformed into that steed is eloquently suggested in a the words of a Soyot shaman addressing his drum, "O my beautiful drum, you are limping my beauty," and "Heed, heed, my horse-stag.... my supreme animals, stag and doe..." (Potapov 1935: 143). The shaman's journey was frequently structured by a real or imaginary tree, perhaps represented by a pole or ladder. This "tree" served as the axis which connected the land of people, the underworld, and the upper realms of the spirits. At the same time, the tree referred to the clan tree and to the tribal understanding of the World Tree, or Axis Mundi. 4 Among the Evenk, the tree figuratively climbed by the shaman was set within the shaman's tent and extended through its smokehole. The tree also referred, however, to the center of the clan lands and to what
4 See, e.g., Anisimov 1958: 162-164. Such ideas regarding the tree as Axis Mundi or Tree of Life are, of course, universal and have been commented on by a number of scholars; cf. inter alia, Cook 1988.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



178 IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER
Anisimov has referred to as the topogentilic center of the clan itself (Anisimov 1963; Vasilevich 1963). In this construct may be traced what must have been a gradual absorption into the shamanic ritual of the concept of an archaic World Tree joining the upper, middle and lower realms. Among the Ket, the tree of the shamanic ritual also referred to the tree of Es, the high god, which grew in the East and was associated with the rising sun. This is certainly the same tree as that from which the Selkup shaman's drum was made and which was "returned" by the shaman to the swamp of the old Woman-Mother of the tribe. It is ultimately the same tree-that is, the same symbol of generation and passage-as that which was said to grow from the body of the Evenk elk or deer Animal Mother, the bugady enintyn (Anisimov 1963).5 Within the shamanic ritual, birds were hardly less significant than deer and trees; the three elements-birds, deer, and trees-are constantly reunited in tale and ritual. 6 The Selkup envision the raven-mother and raven-father as key elements in the vivification of the shaman's drum. These birds are said to fly to the swamp where dwells the Ylyunda kotta. There they take water from the whirlpool of life and sprinkle it on the deer-drum which will serve as the shaman's steed. The shaman then erects a sacrificial "tree" next to his tent and hangs his deer-drum on it, thus returning the deer, now revitalized by the birds, to the tree from which it came (Anisimov 1963: 189). Among the Ket, the primary birds were the eagle, the swan, and the grebe (Anuchin 1914: 54-57). The Ket considered the eagle to be the chief helping spirit of the shaman. This bird referred back to the ancient myth of Dokh, sometimes referred as the first shaman, who was instructed by an eagle. Indicative of that bird's central role were the implements carried by the Ket shaman: a metal eagle leg with claws, by which the shaman caught and destroyed evil spirits; the wand with double eagle heads, by which the shaman was endowed with the great foresight and knowledge of the eagle (Anuchin 1914). The swan was the messenger of good tidings and was associated with Tomam of the warm South. By contrast, the grebe inhabited the far North, the kingdom of Khosedam. Represented by metal images worn on the shaman's cloak, the grebe would tell the shaman all that happened
5 The close connection between deer and trees indicated in the shaman's staff and drum and their essential association with sun and moon (that is, with the signs of cosmic order) are reaffirmed by the frequent repetition of stylized images of all four elements on the backboards and doors of the shamans' sleds and huts; see Anuchin 1914: 65, figs. 67, 68. 6 Regarding the general nature of bird symbolism, cf. Eliade 1972: 156-158; Shtemberg 1925; and regarding the extensive bird symbolism in Tofa shamanic headdresses, cf. Dioszegi 1968.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER 179
in the world of the tundra; its cry was said to be that of a weeping child or woman (Anuchin 1914: 57). The three birds-eagle, swan, and grebe-thus reflected the basic structure of the Ket universe: the vertical rise represented by Dokh's journey along the Yenisey was the "direction" of knowledge; the South, referred to by the swan, was associated with the return of life to the middle world, and the North, represented by the grebe, was the region of cold and death. The reiteration of references to deer, birds, and trees in shamanic paraphernalia, their conflation, and the transformation of shaman into deer and bird affirm the foundation of these elements within a universe considerably older than shamanism. Shamanism seems to have absorbed constructs which already existed and through which deer/tree/bird already referred to tribal progenitor, intercessor, and the transformation attendent on death. While nothing within known shamanic paraphernalia reproduces the Scytho-Siberian construct of the deer with bird-headed antler-tines, one cannot help but find in the early images of the deer with tree-like antlers a prefiguration of the insistent shamanic conflation of deer, trees, and birds. A significant qualification, however, is that within that earlier period (the early Iron Age), the conflation of these elements seems to have been the deathright of all members of society. Many individuals, male and female, adult and child, carried the images of that conflation with them to the grave, perhaps as a sign of passage to the next world. At some point between the early Iron Age and the emergence of a documented shamanic tradition, a monopoly over those animal references was shifted to and concentrated within the person of the shaman. Within the nomadic burial rituals of the late Bronze and early Iron Ages the structures indicative of passage may also be glimpsed, the structures within which were foreshadowed the shape of the journey of the shaman as psychopomp and the symbolic structures inherent in that journey.
B. PRE-SHAMANIC CULTS
The foregoing discussion has focussed attention on the implements of shamanism and on the archaic elements within shamanic practice and belief. The examples drawn from shamanic traditions of such peoples as the Ket, Evenk, Selkup, and Tofa draw attention, in turn, to those traditions which may be identified as pre-shamanic cults: that is, cults which appear to have developed before shamanism emerged and which point back to archaic beliefs and practices and to an ancient social order. In general, these cults involved a belief in the indwelling spirits of
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



180 IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER
natural objects and forces. The rituals associated with these cults were undertaken and maintained by individuals on behalf of their clan. Indeed, such cults tended to reinforce the idea of clan unity rather than the integrity of nuclear family or tribe. The deities referred to in those cults were clearly indicative of an archaic belief in a clan mother, an earth mother, or an animal mother of life and death. Within the ethnographic tradition, one frequently finds a combination of elements: shamanic societies within which survive to a greater or lesser degree preshamanic cults of the hearth, of the deity-protectress of children and of the herd; and even bear and mountain cults. Among the Turkic peoples of the Altay or the Mongol peoples of Mongolia and South Siberia, these cults have frequently been obscured by the overlay of shamanism and lamaism. They tend to be more clearly preserved in their archaic forms among more northern peoples, such as the Ket and the reindeer-dependent Evenk.7 In many cases, and particularly among South Siberian peoples closer to the Eurasian Steppe zone, pre-shamanic as well as shamanic cults reveal a complex intertwining of elements suggestive of sexual and social tension. This sexual tension unquestionably reflects slowly changing political structures dependent on an ancient shift ofpolitical power away from a female-centered clan structure, and the reintegration of that power within a male tribal elite. These cults also reveal tensions between pre-shamaic belief systems and the later shamanic cults into which they were ultimately integrated. Such examples of sexual and social tension, lodged within the sedimentary overlay of shamanism on pre-shamanic cults, can be found throughout the ethnographic record. For example, among peoples of the Sayan-Altay region, the drum of the shaman serving a particular lineage or tribe had to be made from trees on the lineage's sacred mountain (Potapov 1958: 320-321). In this tradition may be glimpsed a continuing tension between the pre-shamanic mountain cult-assertive of lineage and family identity-and the shamanic cult of a later period. The South Siberian mountain cult shared by Turkic peoples of the Sayan-Altay region as well as by Mongols, Buryats and Tibetans is another case in point. As a tradition which reaffirmed the identity of the clan lineage, it was most certainly pre-shamanic in origin.
7In fact, the further north the location of a people, the more significant remained the cults of clan deities carried out by individuals on behalf of household and clan. Conversely, the greater strength of shamanic traditions among peoples closer to the Eurasian Steppe may be explained in part as a response to the powerful articulation ofstatehood among cultures south of the steppeland.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER 181
The cult involved both the establishment of cult sites (obo, obaa) in the form of stone or stick cairns on the tops of mountains or at the top of significant passes, and the reaffirmation of the power of the mountain deity by ritual offerings (Potapov 1946, 1958). In passing over the mountain at the time of a hunt, the hunter would offer bits of meat or tea to the mountain "master" represented by the obo. Each sacred mountain was associated with a particular lineage or tribe. The resulting sacralization of mountain and lineage and the understanding that a mountain might have its own protective deity was indicated by the establishment of the obo .8 In a real sense, the cult of the mountain indicated a culture of place: a recognition that the well-being of the lineage-insured through the successful hunt and through rich pasture and water-was dependent on the mountain deity. In Tuva, it was only in the nineteenth century that the relationship of lineage to obo, sacred mountain, and mountain deity become displaced and monopolized by shamanic and lamaistic elements (D'yakonova 1975a: 159-160). The mountain cult and its mythology, however, also reveal sexual tensions which reflect the conflict of larger social issues over time. The sexual identity of the "master" of the mountain seems to have varied from male to female. Potapov recounts how in recent times, the "master" of the mountain is said to take the form of a naked female. She approaches the hunter at evening, when he is in the taiga, and sleeps with him. From this encounter is ensured his success in the hunt (Potapov 1946: 159). Sexual tension in relation to the control of power is also expressed in the same region by the fact that the cult of the mountain was often reserved for men alone; women would not be allowed to take part, or even to go to sacred places (the obo, for example); stock might not be allowed to graze on the slopes of the mountain (Potapov 1960: 213 ff). The complexity of the association of mountain cult with a male or female power and the indication of the complexity of sexual reference over time is also suggested by elements in heroic epics which have been gathered from among the peoples of Khakassiya. There one finds that the hero of the epic has been born in a mountain cave or that he finds protective shelter in the caves of mountains considered to have been ancestral places. I. L. Kyzlasov (1982) believes that in these cases the cave refers to the generative and protective female ancestor, the spirit of the mountain. Related to this is the widespread belief in the mountain as the
'Throughout northern Mongolia, Tuva, and the northern Altay, the appearance of obo at the tops of mountains and at passes attests to the continuing vitality of the mountain cult, even in the post-Soviet period.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



182 IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER
source of the lineage and the place to which the dead of the lineage return. The mountain is thus understood as the generative source. In commenting on the mountain cult of Khakassiya, Kyzlasov has associated the transferance of cult attention from the female "mother of the mountain" to a "stone father," presumably a stone cairn, as indicative of the rise of patriarchal structures within tribal society. The Siberian bear cult even more clearly reveals references and proccupations which go back to a pre-shamanic stage of prehistory. The cult is laced through with sexual tension reflecting a gradual diminution of female power and the concurrent rise of shamanic traditions and the attendent cults related to Heaven.9 The Evenk consider the bear to be a culture hero from whom people received fire and tools, including such primitive implements as stones and scrapers and a knowledge of tanning skins and harnessing reindeer. This bear-hero gave tools and knowledge not to the tribe as a whole, but rather to a woman. The nature of the bear's gifts suggests the deep antiquity of the Evenk bear cult, seemingly referring back to the Neolithic period and to the later acquisition of the tools for using reindeer. At the same time, the fact that this culture hero gave his knowledge to a woman indicates the survival of an archaic understanding that control over the natural world was vested in a female figure. The Ket and the Evenk believed that the bear had the ability to think and to talk like a human. Indeed, the Ket attribute the existence of a soul only to people and bears, whom they refer to as the "tsar" of animals (Alekseyenko 1960: 91). They ascribe the ability to think only to people, bears, and to the evil Khosedam (Alekseyenko 1977). If an orphaned bear was found, it was raised as a member of the family, fed, clothed, ornamented, and taken on social visits, until it was old enough to be released in the forest (Alekseyenko 1960: 92-93). Among both Ket and Evenk, as well as among other related peoples, there existed numerous requirements which governed how one addressed a bear, how one hunted a bear and drove it from its cave, how one had to appease its spirit, and how one "buried" the bear's skull and bones on a specially selected tree so that it would be reborn (Vasilevich 1971: 160 ff.). 10 These rituals all
9 Among the best sources on the bear cult, see Vasilevich 's discussion of its form among the Evenk, and Alekceyenko's discussion of its form among the Ket (Vasilevich 1971; Alekseyenko 1960; cf. Anisimov 1958). 10 Among the Turkic peoples of the Sayan-Altay, the bear was also considered to have the qualities and abilities ofa human. In killing a bear, it was necessary to ask its foregiveness and to appease it with rituals expressing concern and regret. After the killing of the bear, its head had to be left in the field, in a tree. Arrows would be shot into it, apparently to scare off its spirit from following the men and seeking revenge (Potapov 1958: 317).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER 183
indicate the complex interweaving of bear and human origins. They also suggest the emergence of the bear cult at a period when both Ket and Evenk inhabited the mountain taiga zone, that is, the region of the SayanAltay, in the case of the Ket, and, in the case of the Evenk, the PrebaykalTransbaykal. These traces of an ancient habitation of mountainous taiga indicate that the origins of the bear cult lay within the Bronze Age, or even earlier. Other signs of the bear cult's archaic origins lie in the complexity of its themes of human/bear generative relations. These involve myths regarding marriage between bears and human girls, the birth of heroic figures from the marriage of bear and girl, and even myths involving the birth to a young girl of twin babies, human and bear (Vasilevich 1971; Anisimov 1958: 129-131). In that union of girl and bear may be glimpsed totemic conceptions regarding the origin of a clan, within which the union is inevitably between girl/woman and bear, while the boy or man emerges from that union or is in conflict with the bear. This special relationship between bear and girl/woman may be documented in both the myths and the folktales of the Evenk, Even, Orochey, Ul'chey, Nanay, as well as among the Evenk of the Amur region, where the woman is said to have been transformed into a bear when she had finished raising her children (Vasilevich 1971).'1 The Siberian bear cult obviously reflects archaic traditions which go back before shamanism. In its references to the caves, cliffs, and rivers of a homeland in the mountain taiga zone of Siberia, and in the contrasting relationships of bear/woman and bear/man, are glimpsed totemic understandings of tribal origins, as well as a deeply rooted ambivalence regarding the particular interrelationship of bear and woman. In the variations on the Ket understanding of the essential nature and function of the bear is suggested the layering of historical stages of development. Among the more archaic stages reflected here is the sense of the bear as the animal double of the woman and the essential unity of woman and bear. At the same time, the fearfulness of the bear-reflective, certainly, of its actual power as a forest animal-and its role as source and as destroyer echoes the ferocious capacity which the Ket attribute, also, to Khosedam, who ate the souls of the living but who was also, by that act, the catalyst for their rebirth. This construct parallels the dual function
11 The concept of the bear as source is also reflected among the Ket by the fact that the robe of the shaman who takes Mother Earth as his source is sometimes made of a bear skin (Alekseyenko 1977: 31). The shaman thus integrated on his or her person the assimilation of bear and female in the form of generative source.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



184 IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER
which the Evenk would attribute to the giant elk mother, who was said to consume the souls of the living. The archaic traditions embodied in both the mountain and bear cults are combined with elements which have been continued up into the modern period; they are laced with the threads of ancient tensions which emerged within a slowly changing social order. Somewhat clearer references back to a pre-shamanic tradition have been maintained in the tradition of household and clan cults which could also be found among virtually all Siberian peoples up into this century. With few exceptions, these cults centered on deities who were female in essence and whose function was protective and generative. This female-centeredness and its archaic elements are particularly clearly reflected in Ket traditions. Among the Ket, as among many other Siberian peoples, the elements of the natural world--earth, sun, moon, water, and so forth-are personified as female. This female personification appears foremost in the conception of the earth as Mother Earth (bangam-earth-mother), navel, that all creatures of the world are connected to. Hidden here is the understanding of death's interconnectedness with earth, i.e. that human life and death emerge equally from Mother Earth (Alekseyenko 1977: 30). This conception became embedded in the Ket shamanic cult, where the shaman is understood to be protected by Mother Earth. The earth is sometimes referred to as mother of the kaigus', that is, the mother of the being who ensures success in the hunt, the mother of the wife of the bear and of the bear-woman, and the mother of all living things. Although within Ket mythic traditions the sun was associated with the great god, Es, within their everyday life the Ket personified the sun in female form and associated her with the regions from which renewal and warmth come-that is, with East and South (Alekseyenko 1977: 32 ff). These directions were considered positive and pure; they regulated the organization of life, from the placement of encampment and the spatial organization of ritual, to the orienting of events such as the birth of a child. To the east rose the sacred tree of Es, the deity of heaven; in its branches were placed the bones of the animals waiting to be reborn. The significance of the sun was reflected in the shamanic rite in which the sun was seen as a protecting spirit of the shaman and as the end of one of the seven roads taken by the shaman in his ecstatic journey . The moon, too, was conceived of as female, as was the north (polar) star-the Mother of Stars-and Venus (Alekseyenko 1977: 33-34). Thus, the directions of life and death and the major celestial markers of the universe retained in whole or in part a female character.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER 185
In the same way, the closer elements of the natural world were charged through with aspects of the female. The Ket personified their watercourses as female deities with the aspect of a mother-as Ulemam (water-ul' and mother-mam ). The Yenisey, the most important of their rivers, was a Great Mother, while all its tributaries were lesser mothers or grandmothers-the mothers of mothers. 12 Trees were also considered female. As sources of life and as the means by which earth and heaven were joined, they were treated with great respect. Although the Heavenly Tree was associated with Es, the tree itself was considered to be female. The fearful elements of the world-the north, islands, cliffs, and rapids, all that threatened the Ket in their daily life-were also female in essential aspect, but they were associated with Khosedam (Alekseyenko 1977: 36-44). The positive and negative by which the world was charged were thus bound together by a female aspect. Among the Ket existed the belief in a being of remarkable abilities, the kaigus' . On the one hand, a kaigus' was a female being, the mother offurbearing animals such as the squirrel and sable. In this form, kaigus' had the ability to take on both animal and human form. It was said that in the form of a woman, kaigus' would meet and mate with hunters in the taiga, thereby ensuring their success. On the other hand, kaigus' was a bear who held dominion over the animals of the forest. Alekceenko points out that in this duality was embedded a firm belief in the interconnection between human and bear, an interconnection which is considered essential to the success of the hunt (Alekseyenko 1977: 44). The belief in kaigus' extended itself to stringent rules regarding how one hunted: not to take more than was necessary, not to inflict unnecessary pain, to observe the rituals required to ensure the animal's rebirth. Moreover, a hunter could lose his kaigus' if his wife did not observe monthly rituals of purification. Like so much within the mountain cult, this last belief seems to mask deep tensions between the underlying female aspect of the bear cult and a developed male domination of the cult. Nonetheless, here and throughout the Ket bear cult emerge the outlines of the female as dual in nature and power. She was both Animal Mother and mother of the animals. Conversely, the bear was believed to be a reincarnated relative; when a hunter killed a bear, it was believed that the bear/relative had come to be released for rebirth (Anuchin 1914: 12-13).
12 In calling on the rivers to loosen the ice with which they are covered, the Ket referred to them as "grandmothers" (Alekseyenko 1977: 35).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



186 IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER
Less fraught with ambivalence was the Ket attitude toward their protecting spirits of the household. Represented in the form of fetishistic female dolls, these spirits were considered to be female, but they were passed down through the male line (Anuchin 1914; Alekseyenko 1967, 1971, 1977). These protecting spirits thus reaffirmed clan lineage while also echoing an ancient dependency on female origins. The antiquity, even pre-shamanic existence, of these traditions is suggested by the independence of these cults from shamanic practice (Alekseyenko 1977: 57). Foremost among the household protecting spirits was bokarn , "firemother," the protector of hearth and household. When the members of the household ate, the fire was also fed with small bits of meat or tea; it was spoken to as if it were a grandmother. When the encampment moved, it was carefully carried along in a special box in order to be redistributed to family members at the site of the new encampment. Redistributed it became, again, the source of household well-being in terms of warmth, light, and food. The Ket personification of the fire as a beautiful woman was not unusual: fire is also given female personification among the Altaytsy, Shors, Khanty, Mansi, Evenk, Nganasan, as well as among other Siberian peoples (Alekseyenko 1971: 264-265; Dyrenkova 1927; Tokarev 1961). The Ket alel (alal, alel barn, barn), protecting spirits of the hearth and family, are also significant survivors of a pre-shamanic tradition. The alel were made from carved and painted pieces of ceder (Anuchin 1914: 86-89). Theyweredressedinclothandfursandomamented with beads and copper. Sometimes the entire face was covered with a sheet of copper or with old pieces of silver money and then hammered and marked with human features.13 In recent times, ale! were invested with domestic tasks: they were believed to be the special protectors of children, to watch over and caress them as they slept. They were also used in divination: when thrown into the air, if they landed face up, the answer to the question was positive (Alekseyenko 1971: 269). The ale! are the latest and probably last expressions of an ancient tradition which invested protective power in female spirits and deities. As personal and prosaic as the alel appear and as reduced in power as may be the "fire-mother," one senses in their cults threads leading back
13 See, e.g., Alekseyenko 1971: fig. 2. Copper or silver could be used in metal ornaments but iron could never be brought near the a/el (Alekseyenko 1971: 266). Attached to one ale/ in the Museum of Anthropology and Ethnography, St. Petersburg, is a cast copper openwork ornament with protruberances on its rim; altogether it looks very much like the "sun-disks" found on early petroglyphs; see Alekseyenko 1971: fig. 3; cf. Devlet 1978: fig. 6.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER 187
to a more powerful past, one bound to the powers possessed by the duality of Khosedam and Tomam. These figures will be considered more fully below.
