For the moment it may be said that these greater entities, Khosedam and Tomam, and Khosedam' s unending battle against male gods and male heroes themselves point back to an ancient and possibly unified source, female in character, associated with deer and birds, and ultimately the source of life and death. The levels of the Ket universe, the polarities into which those levels were arranged, further suggest the gradual splitting of individual deity and function over time and the relocation of those functions and powers in the hands of more specialized deities, spirits, heroes, and shamans. Such a model of cultural change, wherein the functional catalysts of awe and mythic elaboration may be traced back to an ancient female source, is reaffirmed when one considers the overwhelming female character of those spirits associated with natural elements and with the well-being of the family and the individual within Ket tradition. Indeed, the Ket universe, like that of so many Siberian peoples, seems threaded through with traces of a femalecentered belief or with what has been called a mother cult, with roots as ancient as the Paleolithic period (Nahodil 1968; Alekseyenko 1977). Ket cults related to the natural world were all rooted in the worship of Mother Earth. From that worship descended the more particular cults, virtually all of which were focussed, also, on female spirits. They represent a world vitalized by a female generative spirit, but a spirit also acknowledged as the source of death. These female-centered cults seem to have existed in tension with the cult of Heaven, centered on the male god Es and forming a major aspect of the shamanic cult. While Es was credited with bestowing well-being on the Ket, the sources of life and death were more concretely located in the female powers represented by Tomam and Khosedam. Similarly, although the sun was associated with heaven and thus with Es, it was personified as a female figure, as were the moon and the (North) Pole star. The Ket cult of the hearth fire was not unique among Siberian peoples. Among the Evenk, the cults of hearth and fire and the related ancestor cults point back to an ancient process of change characterized by tension between an archaic order centered on the female and that which ultimately culminated in the patriarchal social order known in the modern period. The cult of the protective ancestors of the family was focused on those ancestors considered to be the source of the agnate family. The cult was handed down from generation to generation through the oldest scion of the male line or, in case of his death, through the nearest of his blood
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



188 IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER
relatives (Anisimov 1958: 92). The maintainance of the cult, involving the feeding and honoring of the cult symbols, was thus the prerogative of the oldest male within the family. Similar cults, also maintained by the male members of the family and also complex in their references, are characteristic of other Siberian peoples. Anisimov refers to Shternberg' s observation of the Goldi (Nanay) ancestor cult:
In almost every home... is a large wooden representation, sometimes one, sometimes two, male and female-dzhuolin, in the form of a person with a large chest. The fact that almost all dzhulin'y, even those which are male, are represented with female breasts, indicates that the original dzhulin'y were considered to be the family ancestor; and only with the passage of time, with the change in the position of the woman, did the dzhulin'y, even though male (in form) continue to be represented in their original aspect, that is, as women, out of the fear of destroying the tradition (Anisimov 1958: 99).
Anisimov has emphasized how among the Evenk such complexities have resulted in a peculiar conflict between the patriarchal nature of the family ancestral cult and the mother-centered, and even more important, cult of the hearth fire. Within this cult, the hearth fire, addressed as "grandmother," is understood as the mistress of the tent, of the family hearth, and as the protecting spirit of the family and clan. Her significance appears in the elaborate rituals involving the removal of the hearth fire at the time of the moving of camp and the carrying of coals to the gathered herd of reindeer. In both cases, it is the mistress of the household who maintains the cult and effects its protective rituals. It is she who carries the coals of that fire to the new encampment, she who redivides it among the clan members, and she who takes it to the reindeer herd as reaffirmation of the practical and spiritual link between the clan and its animals. The significance of the hearth fire is made clearer when one realizes that it is believed to be the habitation of the family protector and the clan mother. It marks the place of passage of the soul from the land of the souls to the land of living people. Coming from the realm of the souls and passing through the opening of the smoke hole of the tent, the soul-to-be-born finds a resting place within the womb of a woman. The fire is, in other words, the site of passage from one world to the next (Anisimov 1958: 94 ff.) and a metaphor for birth. The hearth fire cannot be given to individuals other than those who belong to the clan. In this respect, according to Anisimov, the cult of the hearth fire reaffirms the unity and indivisibility of the clan and the identity of the household and clan with the specific place marked by the hearth fire. The members of the clan are "the people of one fire, one hearth" (Anisimov 1958: 97). In this respect, and in the cult's relation
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER 189
ship to the female line of a family, the fire cult appears to be considerably older than the agnatic cult of the family ancestors. Anisimov points out that the Evenk term, kharan , refers to the yurt site and to the place of the yurt hearth, to the fire in the yurt, and to the general encampment: "In this manner, the encampment, the hearth, and the place for the hearth fire and the dwelling at one time had the same meaning and were expressed by one and the same word" (Anisimov 1958: 97). From this he concludes that the spirit of the hearth fire and the mistress of the hearth, the tent, and the clan were all reflections of the matriarchal aspect of social organization. The beliefs and rituals revolving around the mistress of the hearth fire all involve concepts of protection and regeneration; in their relationship to the continuity of family and herd, they are protective and generative. In these several aspects the cult of the hearth fire stands in sharp contrast to the cult of the ancestors maintained by the male line and to the elaborate shamanic rituals related to the spirits of the dead. In these rituals, the spirits of the dead are conceived as potentially harmful and vengeful elements which must be forced to leave the land of the living. Tension and conflict between the living and the dead are reflected throughout the shamanic rites. In the rituals related to the hearth fire, on the other hand, consistent concern for the continuity between the two realms is reflected. The mistress of the hearth fire is associated with the reindeer herd and the locus of the clan; as such, and in her association with the Evenk mistress of the taiga and animals (bugady enintyn), represented in the form of an elk or wild deer, the mistress of the hearth fire is the source of a lineage's well-being. In contrast to the anthropomorphic aspect of the ancestors and of the dead within the shamanic rituals, the zoomorphic imaging of the Evenk cult mistresses reaffirms the greater antiquity of the cult of the hearth fire and of the matriarchal social order it implies. That this discussion has continually returned to two Siberian people, the Evenk and the Ket, is not accidental. Although the symbolic systems involving an animal mother, the Tree of Life, the transformation of the individual soul, and the nature and function of the shaman are shared by many other Siberian peoples, the Evenk and Ket are most relevant to this discussion. More clearly than any other Siberian or Central Asian peoples, their predecessors are traceable back to a probable identity with the Early Nomads, their Siberian relatives, and their Siberian predecessors. I have already referred to many aspects of their shamanic and preshamanic traditions. It is useful, now, to review the Evenk and Ket
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



190 IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER
mythic traditions, within which appear even richer resources for a study of early Siberian symbolic systems.
C. THE EVENK AND EVENK MYTHIC TRADITIONS
The Tungusic Evenk are the most numerous of the Siberian "small peoples." They may be found across the whole of Siberia, from the far southeast in the region of the Amur River, around Baykal, and further north, east and west beyond the taiga, into the tundra region. The forerunners of the Evenk are believed to have emerged from the forested lands west ofLake Baykal, an area now known as the Prebaykal. Perhaps first nudged through population pressures in the Bronze Age and then in the late first millennium B.C. pressed by more aggressive groups of Xiongnu ("Huns"), the forerunners of the Evenk spread out from the Baykal taiga zone. Moving to the north along the Lena River, to the west and up the Angara and Yenisey, the Evenk spread into the Siberian taiga-tundra zone. As they moved, they assimilated some Paleoasiatic groups and pushed others further to the north. This large, northern group of Evenk peoples came to be characterized by an economy dependent on hunting and on reindeer herding. The theory of Evenk origins in the region of the Baykal taiga was first articulated by Okladnikov on the basis of archaeological excavations in the Prebaykal and was elaborated further in succeeding years by Okladnikov and other scholars (Okladnikov 1950, 1975; Tivanenko 1989; Levin and Potapov 1956: 701 ff.). This theory has also found support in Vasilevich' s meticulous analysis of the Tungusic Evenk bear cult and the mythic tradition with which it is surrounded. Vasilevich has argued that the Evenk bear cult had to have emerged in a region of mountainous taiga; in the case of the Evenk, that would have been the region to the east and west of Lake Baykal. The cult must have emerged as early as the Neolithic and Aeneolithic periods (Vasilevich 1969, 1971). 14 The northern Evenk designate significant space by the term buga.
14 Vasilevich also places the emergence of shamanism in the same period, but her arguments for such a dating are not convincing. As the proto-Tungusic peoples spread out of the Baykal region, other groups moved to the east and south as far as the mouth of the Amur River. These are the so-called "mounted" Tungusics who over time absorbed cultural and! linguistic elements from the horse and stock-dependent forerunners of Turkic-Mongol groups. The dialects of these Tungusic groups are now strongly Mongolized. Most scholars reject the theory according to which the Evenk originated in the region of the Ordos and Yellow rivers and moved north through Manchuria where they learned reindeer herding (Levin and Potapov 1956: 703-704). The Evenk of that area are of less interest in this discussion of ethnography than those reindeer-dependent groups to the north.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER 191
The upper world, of the taiga, animals, and people, and inhabited by the deities of natural phenomena, was referred to as ugu (upper) buga. Ugu buga was similar in all respects to the world of people. That world, the realm of living people, was called dulugu (middle) buga, while the lower world was called khergu (lower) buga. Khergu-buga is just like the middle world, except that everything is reversed: what is alive above is dead below and what is dead above is alive below. All the worlds in turn have their own layers, each of which is reserved for different groupsthe living, the dead, the rulers of the worlds, the spirits, and so forth (Anisimov 1963). In its extensions, however, the term bug!bugalbualba is considerably more complex, revealing Evenk conceptions of significant space in terms of the Evenk lands, the phenomenal world surrounding and effecting tribal existence, and the hostile space outside the Evenk world. Thus, bug refers to land, forest, nature, country, and homeland while buga /bua /ba refers also to homeland, weather, firmament, heaven, universe and world, among other meanings. Semantic qualifications of the term vary meaning and even invert its underlying sense: thus, desolate space-taiga-is referred to as bua khueni ; exterior space, and space outside is referred to as bugadya. (Anisimov 1963: 181-182). One of the most central terms in Evenk cosmology is that of bugady, "of the buga, belonging to the buga," i.e. the sacred clan rocks and trees which belong to the clan land (buga). In the concept of bugady Anisimov finds an essential key to Evenk understanding of the world in terms of genetic and kinship relations. The bugady mark the originating places of the clan; they are sacred because of their significance as clan sourceliterally, as the place from which the clan's spirit ancestors emerged from their totemic source. The topogentilic (Anisimov's term) aspect of the bugady is revealed by the Evenk concept of the bugady mushunin, the mistress of the clan, and bugady enintyn, the mistress-mother of the clan. Bugady mushunin is not only the spirit mistress of the clan but also the mistress of the world and nature. She is conceived in the form of a woman but also as an cow elk or wild cow reindeer. Bugady enintyn, with its element enin meaning both mother and cow elk, also combines the concepts of cow elk (eneche) and mother (eni) (Anisimov 1958: 102105). The two mythic figures together point back to the ancient totemic aspect of the cow elk and cow reindeer within the Evenk belief system. The combination of clan mistress and mistress-mother of the clan suggests that the human aspects of these sacred figures emerged only later as the concept of clan origins became separated from the primary image of the totem animal mother (Anisimov 1963: 169). The
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



192 IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER
Podkamennaya Tunguska Evenk concept of bugady enintyn as the mistress of the universe and the mother of animals and people reaffirms her merged anthropomorphic and zoomorphic aspects. As "Grand-· mother bugady," she is called upon to give animals and fish to the Evenk (Anisimov 1963: 177). In the shamanic ritual of shingkelevun, of which more will be said below, she is unequivocably conceived as having the body and character of an cow elk or cow reindeer. The reconstruction ofEvenk mythic traditions carried out by Anisimov and Vasilevich, among others, indicates the manner in which shamanic: traditions recapitulate the historical development of belief systems central to the life of a specific people. Enfolded within these shamanic traditions are the most archaic layers-layers which refer back to an animal mother, to the transformation of that originating totemic source into a form half-human and half-animal, to the degeneration of that totemic source into a cult object, and, finally, to the subordination of fundamental aspects of archaic beliefs to the shamanic cult, that is, to the, shaman's power and functions. Because the shamanic cult and these: archaic levels are so intertwined within the ethnographic record, it is best to approach the latter through a discussion of rites and objects characteristic of shamanism among the northern Evenk. The Evenk ritual of shingkelevun was performed to ensure success in the hunt (Anisimov 1958, 1963). In this ritual, the shaman would be charged with traveling to the dwelling of the mistress of the clan lands, the dunne mushunin, who was thought to live under a rock or under the roots of the sacred clan tree. From her he learns that she may release the animals to the taiga only if the mother of the universe, the bugady enintyn, agrees. The mistress of the clan lands tells the shaman how to go to the dwelling place of the bugady enintyn, at the origin of the clan lands. Within this tale, the bugady enintyn is clearly conceived as a giant cow elk or doe. She, in tum, allows the shaman to lasso some animals which are transformed into magical woolen threads, shingken, which the shaman carries in his drum. He shows the dunne mushunin his catch of shingken and she lets him pass back through her bugady to the land of the taiga. When he releases the shingkin in the taiga, they are transformed back into animals for the Altaytsy to hunt. Within the shamanic rite of shingkelevun, which joins passage to other worlds with the bugady, the guardian of that passage is also the mistress of the clan lands. Thus the shaman's journey to find animals which his people depend on takes him to a sacred place marked by a sacred rock or tree, and to the locus of a female figure, visually conceived
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER 193
in the form of a woman. This figure, in turn, functions as the sentinel guarding the entrance to the route the shaman will pursue to arrive at the place of the giant cow elk or doe, the maternal source of all animals and people. The drum which the Evenk shaman used was likewise made from the skin of a deer born specifically for the shaman at the base of the very Cosmic Tree from which the drum's frame was fa~hioned (Anisimov 1958: 156). The transformation of the animals he has lassooed into threads carried within the deerskin drum and the reembodiment of the animals when he empties these threads from the drum onto the taiga recapitulates the process by which the shaman-to-be becomes reconstituted. In that process, the anthropomorphic soul of the future shaman is taken into the underworld of shamans, kherekshe, by his or her spirit ancestor. The soul is led to the turu , the tree which is at once the sacred tree of the clan and the sacred tree of the clan's shamans. Among the roots of the uru lies the great Animal Mother, a cow elk or cow reindeer. She eats the shaman's anthropomorphic soul and then gives birth to the khargi, the soul of the shaman's animal double. The khargi is then placed by the spirit ancestors in an iron cradle hanging from the branches of the larch, or turu. The khargi are half-human and half-animal or bird. They are still represented, for example, by the bird-men figures on the cloaks of Evenk shamans (Anisimov 1958: 74, 136-137). 15 This khargi will function as the chief soul of the shaman. When the shaman is reborn, the animal again slips into its human form. Thus the khargi is able to assume any human or animal form. The transformation and reconstitution of the shaman through the doubling of his being and through the power of the deer or elk mother at the base of the tree recalls the transformative and reconstitutive power of the shaman's deerskin drum (Anisimov 1963: 183). 16 The turu, the tree which grows over or from the body of the deer or elk, is synonymous with clan origin: it marks the place of emergence from the animal mother and the place where one passes to the upper or lower worlds. The tree is joined to the body of the Animal Mother; it "cradles" the soul of the shaman-to-be as if the latter were cradled in the womb of its mother. 17 At the same time, the actual birth of the shaman
"The fusing of bird and human retained in the shamanic ritual forcefully recalls the birdmen of the Selcnga River petroglyphs; cf. Okladnikov and Zaparozhskaya 1969. 1• Among the Evenk, the shaman's animal-mother, in the form of a cow-elk or reindeer doe, a bear, or a bird, is also called khargi (Anisimov 1958, 138). 17 This belief, with slight variation, is common to many Siberian peoples; cf. Anisimov 1958, 83-84.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



194 IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER
his passage from cradle to reappearance in an anthropomorphic formdepends upon a larger conceptual transformation in the myth of shamanic selection. The tree is reformulated as a tent ofreindeer skins, the Animal Mother appears as the old woman of the tent, and the spirit ancestors who had perched on the branches of the tree are transformed into the old woman's kinsmen (Anisimov 1963: 183). 18 Anisimov has convincingly argued that this process of shamanic selection and rebirth recapitulates the transformation of a totemic system based upon an animal mother into a shamanism which reduced the totemic object to a cult object and which transformed the animal source into an anthropomorphic form (Anisimov 1958). Similarly, the assimilation of the turu, the sacred clan tree, to the central pole of the shaman's tent effectively wrests power from the clan and the animal mother, and relocates it in the persona of the shaman. The complex staging that accompanied the Evenk shamanic ritual reflects the process by which earlier, independent powers associated with concepts of clan origins were reformulated to serve the shamanic cult. The staging would include setting the symbolic clan tree marking the clan lands within the shaman's tent and erecting around the tent wooden replicas of female spirits and tribal deities-stag, elk, bear, and fish-that served as the shaman's animal helpers (Anisimov 1963). In this reconstruction of the significant universe, the center of the shaman's "world" (the tent) was identified with the clan tree, turu, and the shaman was assisted by animal helpers which had formerly had greater, even independent power. The compression of distinct historical stages within the development of a belief system and the accompanying tension which, over time, emerges between human and animal, male and female, are also reflected in the Evenk concept of the cosmic elk, Kheglen (Anisimov 1958: 6872). Kheglen is identified with the Big Dipper (Great Bear) constellation; since the Little Dipper (Little Bear) is said to be Kheglen's calf, it is clear that Kheglen is a cow elk of gigantic proportions. Kheglen and her calf are said to go into the thickets of the heavenly taiga during the day and to reappear at night. Nightly, the movement of the constellations recapitulate a great hunt. According to most recent versions of the tale, Kheglen is followed by three hunters, represented by the three stars of the Dipper's handle: a Russian (the furthest from the elk-dipper), a Ket, and an Evenk right behind her. The Milky Way represents the hunters'
18 The elements of this myth, including the reindeer tent as the tree and animal spirits, reemerge in the organization of the Evenk shaman's tent at the time of his or her ecstatic journey.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER 195
tracks. Every night the Evenk overtakes Kheglen and kills her; but the next night her calf reappears as Kheglen and the hunt is repeated (Anisimov 1963: 161-162). Among the petroglyphs from the Stone Island on the Angara river is a representation of a man on skis following closely behind a cow elk. Okladnikov and Anisimov have concluded that this scene, dating by style to the Neolithic period, refers to the nightly hunt after Kheglen (Okladnikov 1966: pis. 38-39; Anisimov 1958: 3436).19 In what is certainly an older version of the myth, the cosmic Kheglen ran out of the heavenly taiga and impaled the sun on its antlers, carrying it back into the taiga thicket and bringing darkness to the world. To save the world from this darkness, the hero, Main, pursued Kheglen and finally killed the elk at midnight. In taking the sun from Kheglen, Main restored day to the middle world, but he himself was not able to leave heaven. Thus Main is fated to repeat his pursuit every night (Anisimov 1963: 162-163). Within this legend, the great elk Kheglen has both male and female aspects. It is antlered, but it is also accompanied by its calf. In the aspect of antlers may be found, perhaps, a later addition or a conflation of cow elk and cow reindeer, the only female cervid to carry antlers. Furthermore, the antlers may refer to the tension between an archaic female center and an evolving emphasis on a male-centered social order, represented here by Main. Within the perpetual reiteration of the hunt, the conflict between the human hero and the animal female for control of the sun remains unresolved. In a still older version of the Main-Kheglen myth, according to Anisimov, the hunter takes the form of the bear, Mangi, who nightly pursues and devours the sun-elk of the Great Dipper. Anisimov points out that in Evenk, mangi means not only "bear," but also "spirit of the ancestors, "master of the lower world," and "devil" (Anisimov 1963: 164). In this respect, the bear and the elk appear as the most significant cosmic animals, but the bear embodies a complex duality and plays the role of the challenger to the primary animal, the cosmic elk. These patterns of meaning recall the complex relationships of bear and female humans within the wide spread Siberian bear cult. Like many Siberian peoples who lived in proximity to great rivers, the Evenk believed that the soul of the deceased would be transported by the shaman down the river to the land of the dead, but that the shaman must
19 It is curious that among the thousands of representations of cow elk dating to the Baykal Neolithic and gathered from the Lena, Angara, upper Yenisey and Tom' rivers, there is only one other instance, on the Tom' River, which may include a hunter on skis and cow elk (Okladnikov and Martynov 1972: plate opposite p. 70, no. 71).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



196 IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER
obtain agreement from the mistress of the world of the dead (of the buni) if the soul is to be admitted there. Only with her agreement could the soul be transferred to the birch bark boat of the dead (Anisimov 1958: 78). The understanding that the river or road to the land of the dead is guarded by a woman is ubiquitous among Siberian peoples and may have deep roots in the Baykal Neolithic or Aeneolithic. The concept of the river as the path to the land of the dead may be particularly reflected in the petroglyphic images of boats found along the rocky shores of the Lena, Angara, upper Yenisey and Tom' rivers, which were referred to in Chapter IV. Like the Evenk, the Nanay and the Nivkh believe that the guardian of the land of the dead is a woman (Anisimov 1963: 167-168). Among the Orochey, a small nationality related to the Evenk, instead of a single mistress of the universe who both devours and gives birth to souls, at the center of the cosmogonic process are two old women in the form of a tigress and a bear. They live on opposite sides of the moon land, one along the Tiger River and the other along the Bear River. These animal mothers nurse the spirits of future humans and animals and toss them up to the middle world. When the animals and people die, their spirits return to the old bear and tigress and the cycle begins anew (Anisimov 1963: 180).20 The Evenk cosmogonic myths are not unique. Throughout the Siberian mythic tradition, one finds an insistent reference back to ancient traditions by which the original progenitor is a female animal, an animalwoman, or a being with aspects of both women and animals. According to a Nganasan shamanic tale, to obtain the powers essential to his functions, the shaman followed a long road ending at a tent on a high hillock. Inside the tent were two naked women covered with reindeer hair and wearing reindeer antlers. The fire between them on the tent's hearth was like the rays of the sun. From these reindeer-women the shaman received the wild and domesticated deer and the hairs necessary for his shaman's cloak (Anisimov 1963: 186). In this case the generative sources are clearly half-human, half-animal. The association of the animal mothers with the fire of the sun and thus with the sun itself parallels the association of the cosmic Kheglen who carries the sun on its antlers. It also offers a parallel to the pan-Siberian association of the
20 Shamanic songs frequently refer to the river as the road of the shaman's journey back to the land of the dead. In one Evenk shaman song published by Vasilevich, for example, the shaman is challenged at successive waterfalls as he makes his way down to the place where the river enters the lower world and the locus of"little mother-night" (Vasilevic [Vasilevich] 1968: 352-359).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



IN SEARCH OF THE ANiMAL MOTHER 197
sun and fire with female deities. 21 In another Nganasan tale, the shaman makes his way to a swamp where he finds naked women covered with the hair of deer and wearing antlers. From these animal mothers come forth the wild and domesticated deer by which the Nganasan will live (Anisimov 1963: 187). Here again, within a setting charged with the force of creation-a swamp within the tundra, a place of rich sustenance for reindeer and elk-appears the vision of the ancestor in the form of an animal-woman. In both cases, the deer-women are the center of the life-giving universe and the source of life itself. Their location at the end of the shaman's journey serves also as a metaphor for the great antiquity of this rich conception. For all the wealth of imagery surrounding these shamanic visions, however, one senses the loss of power once accorded the original Animal Mother. These animal-women are successors to more ancient concepts lodged in the Evenk bugady enintyn, the giant elk who devours and gives birth,who lies at the base of the clan tree, and whose antlers become the tree itself. A still later formulation of cosmogenesis is represented by the Ket duality of Khosedam and Tomam. Khosedam, associated with the wild reindeer and having the footprints of an animal, was the source of putrification and death, the one who ate souls in order that they may be reborn. Only after a sojourn in Khosedam's womb could the souls return to earth in a new generation (Anisimov 1958: 80). Her complement was Tomam, the source of the bird-harbingers of spring. With the bodies of women but the powers and aspects of animals, they together reiterated the cycle of birth and death referred to by spring-summer and fall-winter. They represent yet another reformulation of the concept lodged in the image of the Evenk buny enintyn, who lay at the base of the clan tree.
D. THE KET AND KET MYTHIC TRADITIONS
The Ket are an almost extinct nationality inhabiting the middle Yenisey River between the mouth of the Sym to the mouth of the Kureyka rivers. They are also found along the lower Podkamennaya Tunguska River and throughout the length of the Yeloguy River. The Ket live primarily by hunting and fishing. They use reindeer for winter transport but they do not ride them. Indeed, there is much to indicate that a reliance on domesticated reindeer has been acquired relatively recently, perhaps from contact with Samoyedic neighbors (Alekseyenko 1969). Accord
21 See, e.g., the Gilyak tale of the mother of the universe who holds in her horns the sun and moon (Anisimov 1963: 172-173).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



198 IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER
ing to the census of 1926-27, only 1428 Ket existed, of whom 1225 retained knowledge of the Ket language. The language of the Ket is related to a number of now-dead languages-Assan, Aris, Kott, and Pompokol; thus the remaining Ket are virtually the last of what was once a group of related peoples in northern Siberia, all of whom are believed to have moved into that region from the south (Levin and Potapov 1956: 687-700; Jakobson 1957: 222). Ethnographers have long suspected that the Ket originally lived considerably further to the south, in the region of the Sayan Mountains. In their mythology are found references to an event long ago when they were attacked by a more powerful people and forced to move north across a great divide, possibly referring to the Sayan Ridge. One of the major positive figures in Ket mythology, Tomam, is said to live in the far south, on the upper Yenisey. The Ket association ofTomam with lifegiving warmth and migratory birds suggests a now muted memory of cultural origins. Now extinct peoples to whom the Ket were relatede.g., the Arin and the Kott-were reported in the seventeenth century to be still practicing traditions which indicate a southern source: stock breeding and some agriculture and iron smelting (Levin and Potapov 1956). Vaynshteyn, among others, has pointed to the fact that the outer cloak of the Ket-full, straight in cut, crossing from right to left and secured by a belt-is similar to that ofthe Central Asian khalat (Vaynshteyn 1951). The most convincing indicators that the Ket came from a region much further to the south, possibly from the area of the Sayan Ridge, are offered through linguistics. Dul'zon has pointed to shared elements in Ket and Caucasian which must indicate an ancient association, going back at least 4000 years, when the two groups may have been in significantly close contact. Moreover, many close elements between Ket and related but dead languages of northern Siberia (Assan, Arin, Kott, Pompokol) as well as apparent connections with the language of the Xiongnu ("Huns") strengthen the premise that the Ket must have originated in southeastern Siberia, between the upper Irtysh and the upper Yenisey (Dul'zon 1968a, 1968b; Pulleyblank 1963, 1983). Toponymes of Ket derivation within the Krasnoyarsk Krai and in the region of Tomsk and Kemerovo appear to trace the movement of the Ket out of the area of the upper Yenisey and Irtysh to their present region (Dul'zon 1962b).22 Several scholars have suggested that during the
22 Others have pointed to shared elements in Ket and Tibetan. See references to work of Ramstedt and Donner in Vaynshteyn 1951: 5.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER 199
Tagar and Tashtik periods, in the late first millennium B.C., the Ket inhabited the Minusinsk Basin. With the influx of a people known in Chinese sources as the Dinlin, some of the proto-Ket stayed in the Minusinsk Basin and over generations mixed with the newcomers; others moved north, up the Yenisey River (Vaynshteyn 1951; Nikolaev 1960).23 From the mixture of that migratory group with the autochthonous peoples north of the Krasnoyarsk region emerged the forebears of the Samoyedic peoples, including the Nenet, Nganasan, Enet, and Selkup; the Mansi and Khantsi (Ostyak); and the Yenisey Ket and now extinct related groups (Prokof'ev 1940).24 Like the mythic traditions of all Siberian peoples, those of the Ket may be roughly separated into three general types: traditions which center on personal and household protective deities considered essential to the well-being of a social unit; traditions which refer to mythic figures whose powers and realms transcend concerns for daily well-being and the geographical specificity of tribal lands; traditions which center on shamanic figures and interweave the heroic exploit with shamanic functions. All three areas of myth are constantly interwoven, and within all three traditions, vehicles of power continually reaffirm the interconnection between individual human well-being and the events which govern the perceived universe. In excavating the layers of belief making up this tripartite mythic universe, one finds much that indicates that the most archaic layers-those going back to the most ancient roots-are embedded in traditions which refer to personal and household protective deities. The great personages who make up the Ket mythic universe and who explain the positive and negative aspects of existence are unquestionably later developments, the accumulated result of a centuries-old impulse toward the heroic and the epic. Those mythic figures also offer a glimpse back into an archaic past, one which preceded the emergence of heroic figures, male gods, and the most recent tradition of the shamans. Drawing on the rich body of folkloric materials gathered over the last century, Ivanov and Toporov have proposed a reconstruction of the Ket mythic universe which understands primary, secondary, and tertiary
23 Dolgikh has argued for a relationship between Ket and ancient Karasuk burial ritual, albeit not convincingly (Dolgikh 1961). 24 One may note in this respect that there are significant parallels between the mythic tradition of the Ket and that of far northern Samoyedic peoples such as the Entsi, Nentsi, and Nganasan, suggesting a greater proximity in the past (Prokof'eva 1953).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



200 IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER
figures in terms of interactive structures (Ivanov and Toporov 1965, 1969; cf. Anuchin 1914; Alekseyenko 1976). Atthe highest level, above the highest of the seven levels of heaven, dwells Es. He is associated with thunder, storm clouds, heaven and the sun; his sacrificial animal is a wild reindeer. Es is in many respects an otiose god; his very being and the manner in which he reflects a monotheism centered on a male god may indicate ancient impulses from Indo-European traditions or more recent impulses from Russian Christianity (Ivanov and Toporov 1969: 149150). Paired with Es is Kales, the embodiment of evil. For all his potential power, however, Kales is invoked primarily in curses; the obscurity of his power suggests that he may once have been joined with Es, and that the present unbalanced duality reflects a more ancient unity. A similar duality, but one of far greater color and elaboration may be found in the positive and negative female figures of the second level. These include Tomam and Khosedam, embodiments also of well-being, on the one hand and of destruction on the other. Like Es, Tomam is associated with the sun: with the south, with warmth, with the return of life to the frozen north in the form of birds. Tomam invokes the renewal oflife and fertility. It is said that she lives far to the south, where the edge of earth and heaven are joined by an opening. That southern land, where even iron glows red, is too warm for people but good for birds. In spring she is said to stand on a cliff over the upper Yenisey. She shakes her arms and from her sleeves emerge the feathers of the geese, swans, and ducks which will fly to the north, to the lands of the Ket. It is to "mother Tomam" that the migratory birds are said to return every fall (Alekseyenko 1976: 88-89). It is precisely this association of Tomam with the upper Yenisey which has suggested to many scholars a poeticized memory of southern lands from which the Ket migrated long ago. For all the beauty and lyricism lodged within the imagery ofTomam, it is Khosedam who cuts the most powerful figure within Ket mythology and the one who appears to intersect most frequently with the figures above and below her in the cosmic structure. Khosedam lives in the north: she is said to inhabit a cold island in the mouth of the Yenisey. She is the source of the cold, fever, and sickness which brings on death and she is said to devour the souls of people and retain them in her womb until they are ready to be reborn. Khosedam' s name refers both to herself as mother (am /aem ) and as old woman. Through her female aspect her existence is joined by marriage and blood to the existence of other mythic figures. At one time in the distant past, Khosedam was the wife
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER 201
of Es; she left either of her own volition or was forced to leave. 25 Her daughter, apparently the offspring ofEs, is the wife of Dokh, Khosedam' s major protagonist. Khosedam is joined to the heroic figures of the third level, Alba and Dokh, who are sometimes also considered to have been the first shamans. Alba is often referred to as the "first man," and as the hero-creator of the Ket world. It was Alba who freed the human souls consumed by Khosedam. His stay in this world ended with a great journey-one might say, the paradigmatic shamanic journey-from south to north down the Yenisey, and from earth to heaven. Dokh, by contrast, is fated to repeat his journey from south to north, along with the birds which emerge from Tomaro' s sleeves. In his annual return to the south, to the upper Yenisey, Dokh is joined with Tomaro, but his recurring battles with Khosedam draw him to the far north. Just as Dokh's existence is drawn between south and north, so his life is bound by forces of good and evil. He is associated with Es as a positive force; both figures are connected with Heaven and the sun, with whom Dokh is sometimes said to have been joined within marriage (Ivanov and Toporov 1969: 155). At the same time, Dokh was driven from Heaven by Es who was offended by the ritual impurities of Dokh's wife, the daughter of Khosedam. At every turn, Dokh's existence is thus qualified by the character and power of Khosedam. Within this complex relationship are echoed ancient tensions between an archaic understanding of the cosmos organized by reference to a omnipotent, unvanquishable, and complex female principle on the one hand, and, on the other, a social and political order centered on human energy and order, particularly on that of males. Below Alba and Dokh are ranged a series of other figures: lesser heroes; the indwelling spirits of the natural world; the spirits or deities of sun, moon, and stars; the protective spirits of the family hearth and the individual. Many of these spirits echo the bipolarity of positive and negative articulated in the Es/Kales, Tomam/Khosedam constructions. The levels of power which these nature and household spirits represent continually intersect to reaffirm a world which is totally invested with supra-human elements. In the last analysis, that world is characterized by an overarching distinction between that which is Ket and that which is non-Ket. Of all the deities and heroes traced within the Ket folkloric tradition,
25 This tradition, as well as the tradition of sacrificing reindeer to Khosedam, suggests an ancient unity, in some form, of Es and Khosedam; cf. Ivanov and Toporov 1969: 150-152.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



202 IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER
however, the most powerful imagery and fluid presence is centered on the figure of Khosedam-on her raging malevolence, her black tent in the north, her sled of destruction. This complex figure seems to have had an unending vitality within the mythic tradition. She is human in form, but she leaves animal prints behind her in the snow. Her lifetime is described in terms of continual battle. She is at once the former wife and constant opponent of Es, but to her, too, is owed the sacrificial reindeer of Es. She is the mother-in-law of Dokh, but she is also his arch enemy. Khosedam's daughter is the offspring of Es and she shares with him an association with the sun. Through that daughter, Khosedam emerges as the motherof the sun, but also as the motherof the defiler of Heaven. She sends putrification to the world and swallows human souls, but it was she who, harried by the first hero, Alba, vomited the souls back onto earth. More than Es, Khosedam thus reveals the complexities of an original creator: life-giving mother and death-bringing hag, the source of both birth and putrification/death. By comparison to Khosedam, Kales is a weak opposite to Es, a figure seemingly stuck onto the god of Heaven for symmetry. Alba and Dokh are both positive figures, two variations of the heroic. Within the upper layers of the Ket cosmic structure, only Khosedam and Tomam appear to be clearly drawn opposites: in function, in imagery, in geographical reference (north/south, lower/upper Yenisey), and in time and condition (summer/winter, warmth/cold). One senses that both figures point back to an archaic unity in which were joined the powers of life and death, ofearth and the underworld. One also senses that in the vivid powers and imagery accorded Khosedam and Tomam is revealed an original layer, one preceding and ultimately ousted by the more muted figures of Es and Kales. That layer was female in character and function; its unity organized the significant space (south/north) of the Kets' river-bound universe and the significant periods of their lives (summer/winter, life/sickness, birth/death). At some unclear period, this dominant female center was deposed from its first rank by male gods-one positive but increasingly otiose, the other negative but weakly defined. At the same time, perhaps, the female unity was gradually split apart into positive and negative, into Tomam and Khosedam. In that duality, however, were retained the most fundamental existential polarities affecting human society. In the power of imagery surrounding both figures, but particularly in that of Khosedam, are revealed the tell-tale traces of the archaic source of the Ket and their mythic tradition. Below Es/Kales, Tomam/Khosedam, Alba and Dokh, exist the shamans and lesser spirits. Just as in folklore the fates of the first
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER 203
shamans, Alba and Dokh, are bound up in battle with Khosedam and in the eternal journey to the place of her tent, the north, so too are the fates of the lesser heroes and shamans entangled by the whims and power of Khosedam. Within the Ket folkloric tradition the traces of an archaeology of myth and belief are preserved. Those traces take the form of tales which reiterate the feats of shamans, the beneficence of Tomam, and the destructive sallies of Khosedam. In their references to the everyday world or mortals, the tales defuse an underlying tension between the power of elemental forces to wreak havoc in human lives and the neverending attempts of humans to hold back those forces. Dominating those elemental forces is the archaic figure of Khosedam. Demoted and demonized, it is primarily she and, to a lesser extent, her sister, who turns the wheel of the year and human lives, who prompts the annual journey of Dokh and the never-ending shamanic search for sick and wandering souls. Beneath the mythic structure headed by Es and beneath the shamanic tradition, the Ket universe is clearly charged through with powers located in a female source of life and death. Over the millennia, that source has been elaborated in specific figures of highly vivid aspect and actions and in tales of conflict and heroism. Beneath those elaborations, however, the original source and its association with the organizing principles of the Ket world are still clearly discernible: the Yenisey and its tributaries; the sun, moon, and stars; the cardinal directions and the seasons of the year. Finally, the association of birds and Tomam, and of reindeer and Khosedam, root the ultimately zoomorphic character of the primary deity within the aspect of bird and deer (Alekseyenko 1977: 3233).26 The river which joins the locus of both figures, Tomam and Khosedam, recapitulates the journey of Dokh, the first shaman. In recapitulating that journey, the river becomes equivalent to the Cosmic Tree by which the Evenk worlds are joined and to the Cosmic Tree which is replanted in the maternal swampland of the Selkup between two rivers. In the persona of the Tomam/Khosedam duality, what had been inextricably bound together into a single sign of the cycle of birth, sickness, death, and rebirth was torn apart and redistributed to a locus of beneficence and a locus of evil. To those now opposed aspects of being were
26 In the east grows the sacred tree of Es, and in its branches on the east side are placed the bones of animals in order that they may be reborn. It is possible that these considerations are reflected in the manner in which Minusinsk stelae, deer stones, and the organization of khereksur complexes appear to refer to the East as the main direction.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



204 IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER
attached symbolic qualifiers by which humans could organize their world. Tomam came to represent the south, warmth, spring, the lifegiving birds. Khosedam was bound forever to the frozen north, barren islands, ice, sickness, death. Khosedam retained enough ambivalent elements, however, to recall her earlier, richer existence. Her bird, the grebe, referred both to the upper world and to that below the waters. She bought death, but only through a sojourn in her body may a soul be reborn. Her raging, ferocious character continually draws the attention and poetic imagination of frail humans. Perhaps more than any other aspect of her character, it is this ability to attract and hold human poetic imagination that suggests something of Khosedam's original, all-powerful self.
E. THE ORIGINS OF SHAMANISM AND THE PREFIGURING OF SHAMANISM
WITHIN EARLY NOMADIC SYMBOLIC SYSTEMS
The preceding discussion has suggested that shamanism was a relatively late development in Siberian religious beliefs and practices. There has been no successful attempt, however, at dating the actual appearance of shamanism as it is known today. Vasilevich, following Okladnikov, associates the emergence of shamanism with the Neolithic-Aeneolithic periods and specifically with the Baykal Glazkov culture. In burials from that period the differentiation in status, wealth, and function which Vasilevich believes indicates the appearance of a shamanic tradition may be found(Vasilevich 1969; Okladnikov 1950). Other scholars find persuasive evidence for the existence of Siberian shamanism during the Bronze Age. They point for evidence to such painted and carved images as the horned figures of the Tom', Angara, and upper Yenisey complexes as well as to the masks of Mugur-Sargol in Tuva (Devlet 1978, 1980, 1988). There is no good reason, however, why distinctions in burial wealth should necessarily indicate a class of individuals who hold the extraordinary power which we associate today with the shamanic function, and there is no way of knowing whether rock representations of horned figures actually refer to a socially defined shaman. It is entirely possible, for example, that the antlered or feathered headdresses of modem shamans derive from another early tradition, one which could not be defined as "shamanic" in the sense of "psychopomp." Anisimov's study ofEvenk religion uses the archaeology of ethnography to demonstrate that shamanism could have emerged only with the disintegration of a matriarchal social order. The specific local and clan
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER 205
totemic deities, expressed in terms of animal mothers (totems), gradually became subordinated to the idea of family and tribal deities associated with a male-centered social order. The clan totems retained their zoomorphic character, at least in part, but they were dispatched to serve as spirits of the lower world, of the ancestors, and as spirit helpers of the shamans. According to Anisimov, the rise of shamanism thus coincides with the profound change from a matriarchal to a patriarchal social order (Anisimov 1958, 1963). Anisimov speculates that within the area of the Prebaykal, the homeland of the Evenk, this change occurred during the Neolithic-Aeneolithic period; that is, at the point when burials shifted from an orientation towards the rising sun to an orientation towards the river. The former orientation was a sign of the supremacy of a matriarchal clan order, Anisimov argues, while orientation to the river indicates the appearance in religious belief of the idea of the clan river along which the shaman must conduct the soul of the dead to the land of the ancestors. The appearance of boat images among the Neolithic and Aeneolithic rock representations signals the emergence of the concept of the river of the dead and thus the emergence of shamanism. While Anisimov's theory of social development has a convincing logic, particularly within the context of his superb analysis of Evenk religion, it remains ideal and unverifiable. The fact remains that we define shamanism in terms derived from the observation of the institution known only in the last few hundred years. The function most central to the role of the shaman-the ecstatic journey-leaves no material traces in burials; its existence could be known only through the records of a written tradition. While there is nothing to indicate that the gowns, drums, and headdresses which are the mark of the "modern" Siberian shaman can be duplicated in burials of the Bronze or early Iron Ages, there is also no way of knowing whether there may have been other implements, unrecognizable as such to us, which would have signaled the burial of a shaman and the existence of shamanism. There is good indication that the shaman as psychopomp existed as an institution in southern China by the late first millennium B.C. The songs from the late Zhou state of Ch'u refer to wu (shamans) and to ecstatic flights ofa shamanic nature. If one were to agree with Eliade that central to the institution of shamanism is the role of psychopomp, then neither on the basis of textual or archaeological evidence is it possible to maintain that the shaman as psychopomp existed within ScythoSiberian culture. The absence of shamanism from that culture--even within its South Siberian branches-would suggest that North Asian
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



206 IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER
shamanism had not yet evolved into a widespread and relatively uniform cultural tradition by the late first millennium B.C., or at least not in a form recognizable to modern observers. The term saman or shaman is not universally used by those Siberian peoples among whom shamanic traditions are recognized, but whether the term is kam, kham, or oium, in every case the term refers to an individual, male or female, invested with powers to intervene on behalf of individuals or on behalf of the group to control suprahuman powers. Some kind of shamanism seems to have emerged within Central Asia by the middle of the first millennium A.D. In 568 A.D., the Byzantine emissary to the Turkic Kagan recorded that his embassy was visited by individuals who claimed an ability to control fortune, ward off evil spirits, make fire, and who made use of bells and drums in their incantations. In the Tang chronicles, the term kam is used with reference to individuals with unusual powers among the Yenisey Kirgiz (Potapov 1978b: 12-13). In one of the early Turkic Yenisey inscriptions, there is reference to a ritual similiar in kind to the shamanic kamlenie , even if the term kam or shaman is not used (Potapov 1981: 124-125). The term shaman (saman ), used by Russian emissaries of the Tsar to the Chinese court, appeared only in the seventeenth century with reference to shamans within Tungus society (Potapov 1978b: 7). In drawing on ethnographic traditions to explain the visual images and structures of Neolithic, Bronze and early Iron Age Siberia, the general practice has been to use whatever materials seem to fit the situation. Because of its visibility and theatricality, shamanism has been most frequently turned to in order to explain the strange or the unusual. In using shamanic elements to explain archaeological facts, however, scholarship routinely ignores the essential fact: that the date of the emergence of shamanism is absolutely unclear. Scholars of Neolithic, Bronze, and early Iron Age archaeology regularly refer to symbolic objects and systems as "shamanic," as if one could assume that the societies of those periods were already shamanic in organization and substance. Thus, as has been noted, the frontal figures on petroglyphs and even the deer stones themselves have been seen as signs of the shaman, or of shamanic power; masks have been understood as the sign of shamans. Battling animals have frequently been interpreted as marks of the conflict between shamans (Dioszegi 1952). Support for such an interpretative strategy depends, however, only on analogy and inference. Aside from the masked images or horned figures or the battling animals of Early Nomadic art, one finds no other potential signs of
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER 207
shamanism anywhere within the Siberian Neolithic, Bronze or early Iron Age context: no drums, no costume, none of the paraphernalia associated almost universely with "modern" shamans. On the other hand, modern ethnography makes clear that strange images, half-human, half-animal, may "stand for" deities who were ascribed with having human form or as being half-human, half-animal. Such beings were given both human and animal form in the cults, such as those dedicated to the bear. Admittedly, the absence of burials marked as those of shamans by the preservation of shamanic paraphernalia is not good evidence against the institution of shamanism. Ethnography indicates that in Siberian societies which retain shamanic traditions to the present day, the bodies and instruments of shamans were frequently disposed in such a manner that there would be no lasting remains. If able to select their burial place, shamans would prefer isolated, unvisited areas. They might be actually buried, but more frequently they seem to have been placed in wooden structures directly on the ground, or on raised platforms, or their wrapped bodies would be placed directly on the platforms themselves. The drum, gown, and headdress of the shaman would typically be placed within the wooden structure, or draped over it, or even hung on a tree nearby. In any of these instances, time and weather would rapidly dispose ofany traces ofthe shaman and of his belongings (e.g., D'yakonova 1975: 72-84). Given the ephemerality of such objects, one must conclude that there is nothing either to confirm or to deny the appearance of shamanism as we know it, at any time within the archaeological record. The Siberian shamanism revealed by modern ethnographic research involves a number of distinctive social elements. The purposes and practices of a shamanic tradition are similar to those of a priestly tradition. Shamanism is founded on the belief that approach to higher, more powerful forces in the universe-ancestors or gods-requires the assistance of a specially chosen intermediary, one marked by his or her sensibilities and intelligence. Conversely, the success of the intermediary is dependent on social acceptance and reaffirmation of the shaman's unique ability to intercede on behalf of society. The institution of shamanism thus implies a willingness to cede power to a single individual. In so far as that function has to do with issues of life and death, the shaman's powers may be even more "powerful" than the privileges and authority ceded by society to its political leaders. Shamanic cults thus reflect a society which has accepted and reaffirmed the location of unusual power within the hands of a single individual. At the same time, the act of locating such powers in a single individual catalyzes ambiva
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



208 IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER
lent responses from those who depend on the shaman for matters of intense significance. Hence the fact that in many societies the shaman is held somewhat apart; shamans are forced to defend their territory against other shamans, figuratively if not literally; and the power of the shaman is invested as much in destructible objects-his or her gown, drum, or drumstick-as in the person. Until the existence of shamanic institutions can be determined by clearer evidence than the petroglyphic images of masks or of horned figures, it is premature to explain the archaeological traces of Siberian Neolithic, Bronze and early Iron Age cultures with reference to shamanic practices known only from a relatively recent time. On the other hand, shamanism as understood through relatively recent ethnographic research has its own multi-layered accumulation of assumptions, implements, and practices. Indeed, a part of the function of the shaman has been to preserve in tale, song, and dance society's accumulated store of legends-the very stuff of its self-conciousness and the means by which it gave order to the world. Among Siberian peoples, the terms used to indicate the telling of tales also refer to shamanizing, thus locating the shaman at the center of the great tradition of oral legend and folktale. Furthermore, we have seen that the institution of shamanism arrogated to itself signs, symbols, and explanatory formulations from pre-shamanic beliefs and rituals in order to reaffirm its legitimacy and authority. In the particular case of the Ket and the Evenk, the materials of folktales and myths recounted by shamans and preserved in shamanic ritual represent the survival of beliefs which date from a pre-shamanic period. Like any other tradition within highly conservative cultures, Siberian shamanism must have emerged slowly, over generations and centuries. The forms manifested earlier in this century must be regarded as late refinements of considerably earlier and more complex traditions. Even if we cannot find specific indicators of shamanism within the archaeology of the Early Nomads or in that of any of their ScythoSiberian relatives, we are able to discern a number of elements which suggest at the least a prefiguring of shamanism and of its symbolic structures. Central to the institution of shamanism is the journey made by the shaman to the underworld or to the land of the ancestors, to recall the wandering soul of the sick or to accompany the souls of the dead and assure their non-return. The shaman undertakes this journey during the shamanic seance (kamlenie) and on behalf of his social group. The shaman's ability to complete the journey is dependent on the implements referred to earlier: a drum which serves as the shaman's steed; a robe and
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER 209
headdress decked with imagery and amulets which at once protect, empower and transform the shaman, and a vertical axis, real or imaginary, which serves as the pole linking the underworld, earth and heaven. The latter might be, for example, the center pole of the shaman's tent, or a ladder, or a tree referring to the World Tree, or even an imaginary pole. Underlying the functions and implements of shamanism is the understanding that death is another stage of existence, an inversion of that known in this world, or a continuation of this existence, but in another far-off land: a land of the spirits, the ancestors, or the dead. Sickness denotes a wandering soul; death releases the soul from the body, with potentially destructive results for those who remain in life. As pyschopomp, the shaman travels between this world and the other. The shaman's transubstantiation depends upon an elaborate understanding of the manner in which forces of good and evil can become embodied in both human and animal shapes. By taking on the body of an animal double, or by mounting a specially empowered steed, and with the vigilant assistance of animal helpers, the shaman alone is able to seek the wandering soul of the sick, or to accompany the souls of the dead to made certain they do not return. The shaman's ecstatic journey presumes that the significant universe was formed in a system of layers ranged along a central pole, or World Tree. In poetic formulations, that arrangement might be articulated in terms of the flow of rivers (from south to north, or from above to below) or in terms of the cardinal directions, each of which is associated with forces of good or evil, life or death, and is bound to extensions of space and time.27 After the elaborate preparations for the shamanic kamlenie, the shaman signals the undertaking of his journey through the slow beginnings of drumming and dance. In the ensuing patterns of quickening movement and sound, the ideal order of the universe is reaffirmed and projected onto the phenomenal world. The projection of that order as a river marked by treacherous rapids or as the day marked by the rising and setting of the sun and moon functioned as the shamans' mapping out of the paths they would follow in their quest. No less effective as a map of the shamanic journey were the images painted on shaman drums. On a number of drum faces gathered from the Teleut, for example, suns and moons indicate the heavens in the upper part of the drum, while below
27 See, for example, the descriptions and drawings of the shaman's journey, gathered from among the Evenk (Anisimov 1958); and the song gathered from the Evenk shaman, I. Bajaki in 1940 (Vasilevich) 1968: 352-359.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



210 IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER
may be found reaffirmations of the earth. Connecting the two spheres are images of trees or of antler-like trees over which hover the figures of birds (S. V. Ivanov 1955). In this reaffirmation of order on a cosmic scale, the shaman confronted and responded to the potential chaos created by sickness and death. In search of the wandering soul and as psychopomp, the shaman's human form was ineffective. Through the ordeal of selection, the shaman lost his human body and learned the language of animals; through the donning of a special robe and headdress, the shaman was at once enveloped by the body of the Animal Mother and transformed into that body. In the course of the journey, the shaman would require the aid of animal helpers: to see, hear, and combat evil forces. Thus, shamanic empowerment was predicated upon a theory ofpotential physical change, as well as upon a conception of the universe which placed extraordinary powers in the being of animals. In seeking the traces of such theoretical assumptions within an ancient archaeology, one is dependent upon the appearance of coherent symbolic systems. We have seen that those coherent systems appear regularly in Scytho-Siberian archaeology: in the orientation of burial structures within organized cemeteries and in the regular placement of the dead, their furnishings, and their horses by reference to the cardinal directions and the movement of heavenly bodies. Moreover, throughout the Early Nomadic culture of South Siberia and Central Asia and throughout the larger Scytho-Siberian culture, the dead and the horses sacrificed with the dead were laid out in clothing and ornaments which conformed to specific and consistent understandings regarding imagery and order. Within that order, the body of the person or the horse was redefined in terms of a central vertical axis. The imagery associated with that axis, along its rise or symmetrically on either side of the rise, affirmed the association of axis with tree or with mountain, and of axis with wings, with antlers, or with horns. Dense and controlled, that imagery may have functioned to project the deceased and their horses onto an ordered passage from the realm of the living to that of the dead, along the path of a pole, a tree, or a mountain. At the same time, the signs of transformation reaffirmed the anticipation and process of transforma-tion which the dead would undergo with their animal helpers or in the bodies of animal doubles. While there is no firm basis in Early Nomadic archaeology for assuming that culture was shamanic, there are many indications that the Early Nomads organized their world around certain theoretical assump
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER 211
tions which parallel principles underlying shamanic traditions in Siberia and Central Asia. Those assumptions include a human dependency on animals, and an understanding of being as essentially transformative, of death as a process of transformation and passage in space and time, and of that passage as one symbolized by a pole, a mountain, or a tree. They seem to have also included a conviction that the order of the universe was knowable and representable, and that it had to be reaffirmed in the rituals accompanying death. Whereas shamanic societies reserve the most articulate affirmation of those principles for the body and actions of the shaman, however, Early Nomadic society appears to have believed that at death each individual was necessarily and legitimately bound by the principle of transformation and enwrapped in the diagrams of universal order. Death was a potential tearing of the social fabric: a threat of chaos devolving on human society. The funerary arrangement of body and burial served as an opportunity to repair that tear, to reaffirm the ordered structure of the significant universe. Without a shaman, however, one cannot assume the existence of shamanism. It is at least a possibility that the powers accorded the modern shaman were originally lodged in all individuals and that the order invoked by the shaman was in that age still considered the anticipated deathright of each person. The symbolic structures arranged on the bodies of the Early Nomadic dead and on their horses perhaps foretell the symbolic structure underlying the shamanic journey, but they do not predict it. Conversely, the poetry and process of the shaman's journey offers undeniable parallels with zoomorphic imagery in the period of the Early Nomads, but the parallelism remains only suggestive. It is as if Bronze and early Iron Age beliefs and practices contained the seeds for the germination of shamanism. The symbolic and essential constructs uniting deer, tree, and bird, as well as the understanding of death as a process ofjourney and change, were gathered together into the rituals and paraphernalia of the shaman: in the deer/robe, deer/drum, and tree/staff with bird claws. What had been the focus of concern and attention by all members of a social group--the process of death and rebirth-seems at some time after the age of the Early Nomads to have become monopolized by a single individual, the shaman, as that figure is defined through modern ethnography.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



212 IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHE\R
F. CONCLUSION
To move from ethnography to archaeology requires a leap in imagination and a conviction that the symbolic structures encoded in Siberian mythic traditions have parallels within the material remains of long vanished cultures. Many scholars have made that shift with great ease, even with too much ease. In the process of making analogies between ethnographic materials gathered in recent decades and archaeological materials of the Bronze Age, some fundamental issues have been simply ignored. In fact, as this chapter has attempted to indicate and as some ethnographers have demonstrated, mythic traditions have their own archaeology just as surely as do the material remains of cultures. Only in a careful consideration of the possibility of parallel developments within the mythic tradition and within materials of archaeology can a cautious use of analogy pertain. The results of analogical analysis, however, must be predicated upon the understanding that any material remains-seemingly discrete, seemingly bound in time and space-mask a signification which is essentially an organic weaving of complex beliefs and symbolic imagery reaching back into many levels of time, and at each level varying in shape and substance. The Evenk concept of bugady and of the generative cow elk, bugady enintyn, and the split of the Ket's Khosedam(fomam must surely serve as indicators of meaning within the material remains of ancient South Siberian cultures. It was said above that both Anisimov and Okladnikov have argued that the great images of elk painted on the cliffs of the upper and middle Lena and dating back to the Baykal Neolithic period referred to concepts of the cosmic elk, Kheglen, or to the cow elk totemic mother, bugady enintyn. The cliffs on which the elk images appeared were, in effect, bugady : places marked by trees or stones which indicated the sacred and generative clan source, the point at which the three worlds describing the cycle of birth, death, and regeneration were figuratively joined (Anisimov 1958: 34-35). Both scholars have also found evidence for the significance of the hearth-fire in the archaeology of the Prebaykal Neolithic. In burials from that period, according to Okladnikov, females buried with bows and arrows suggest a social order which allowed female participation in a broad range of economic activities and a female pre-eminence within the household. Similarly, the finds of large and continuously used hearths from the Baykal Neolithic may also reflect a social organization focused on the household and particularly on the hearth fire (Okladnikov 1950: 263 ff; Anisimov 1958: 100). Those
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



IN SEARCH OF THE ANIMAL MOTHER 213
hearth fires and that female-centeredness are distantly echoed in the panSiberian cult of the mistress of the fire. However it might vary in time and place, the Evenk concept of bugady suggests a rationale behind the persistent South Siberian appearance of specific places marked by sacrificial altars and stelae. Regardless of the specific culture to which it is attached-Altayic Turk, Paleosiberian, or Mongol-the shamanic cult also consists of layers which may be peeled back to an earlier, preshamanic level. The Evenk shamanic cult serves as a model for processes which may have occurred in cultures across South Siberia: it reflects a shift in significance from the primary animal mother, the cow elk or cow reindeer, to a preoccupation with the shaman assisted by now demoted animal helpers. The Ket organization of their universe and particularly the hierarchy described by Es, Khosedam{fomam, Alba and Dokh, also reflects an archaeology of belief: a process of demotion, denigration, splitting, and reassignment of values which ended in the apparent supremacy of the male god and the assignment of the most powerful negative elements to a female force. For all the effectiveness of that process of demotion and reassignment, however, the essential female aspect of the household cults and particularly of the all-important cult of the hearth-fire points back to a persistent, generative source which was female, animal, and the place marker and sign of clan identity. It is probable that the processes reflected in the Ket and Evenk mythic traditions, evolving over millennia, must also lay behind the elaborate stone structures of the Early Nomads and behind the enigmatic symbolic structures with which their dead and their horses were laid to rest.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



CHAPTER EIGHT
ABSENCE AND PRESENCE The Deer Goddess of Ancient Siberia
At each stage of this investigation we have found a curious, elusive, fluctuating image. Although first cast in the shape of a female elk, her preeminance with the passage of time was replaced by an image halfhuman, half-animal, and certainly female. Carved onto the surfaces of monolithic stelae in the Minusinsk Basin, it is probably she, too, who emerges among the strange "feather-heads" on the grave slabs from Karakol and on the images from Tamgaly and Saimaly-Tash; indeed, it may be her presence which is acknowledged by the loop-headed figures at Karakol and Shalabolin. The strange bird-women of Kalbak-Tash and Chuluut Gol are most likely another of her many aspects. Female in gender and associated by her signs and placement with life and death, she disappeared in the Bronze Age, only to reappear in the guise of a cow and deer. As a cow, she derived from a tradition which earlier had crowned her with horns, and as a deer she derived from the northern tradition which earlier had given her the body of an elk. As a deer she also came to dominate the iconography of the late Bronze Age, carved ubiquitously on rock walls and boulders or wrapped around monolithic figures of the emerging male hero. She entered the age of the Early Nomads as a recumbent deer, more rarely as a standing deer. Ultimately, however, her fate was obscurity: burdened by overelaboration, smothered under the press ofpredatory animals, her animal form was finally dismissed by the increasing fascination with human imagery and realism. At the very end of the Scytho-Siberian period, she assumed the guise of a woman seated before a male holding a rhyton, but as woman she is finally eclipsed, her invisible presence only referred to by a male figure. Where one might have expected to find the reaffirmation of a supreme male god, heroized male ancestors, or solarized warriors, is instead found their contradiction: a female animal; a monstrous female head; a deer twisted under the grasp of a wolf or cat. It was that image which centered the axis carried by the Early Nomads and their horses to the land of the dead. Reformulated as a bird, caprid, branches of a tree, or the shape and substance of a woman, the metamorphic deer of the Early Nomads established the center of a bilateral symmetry around which the
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ABSENCE AND PRESENCE 215
parable of animal predation was enacted again and again. Horns, antlers, wings, and branches were its sign and the reaffirmation of the axial center. But who or what was the entity behind that fluid image? What was the significance of the symbolic systems for which that image served as both source and center? And by which threads can that image and its symbolic context be connected with the much more archaic images of the Neolithic elk and Aeneolithic-Bronze Age horned and feathered women? The only early literary reference to the deities worshipped by the Scytho-Siberians comes from Herodotus. At the end of Bk.
