I of his Histories, he tells us that the milk-drinking Massagetae worship the sun, to whom they sacrifice horses (Herodotus [1954]: 101). From that terse phrase there is no way of knowing in what manner the sun was worshipped, or whether it was considered male or female. The historian's comments regarding the Scythian pantheon, however, are more detailed. In Bk. IV, we have seen, Herodotus writes that the chief deity of the Scythians was Tabiti, and was equivalent, he says, to Hestia. Together with Hestia he grouped Papaeus-the Scythians' Zeus-and his wife, Api, or Earth. Among Scythian deities of secondary importance Herodotus included Apollo (Oetosyrus), Aphrodite (Argimpasa), Heracles, and Ares, the only deity whom the Scythians honored by building structures. "It is not their custom," Herodotus asserts, "to make statues, or to build altars and temples, in honour of any god except Ares" (Herodotus [1954]: 260). It would be rash to accept without question what Herodotus writes and particularly his facile equivalencies. After a11, Herodotus looked at the world with the eyes of a Greek and measured what he saw according to Greek standards and Greek concepts. On the other hand, the historian's report that the chief deity of the Scythians' was female and that of the three most important deities, two were female, seems reliable. This structure, after all, was significantly different from the religious pantheon of the Greeks, just as the Scythian' s pantheon and cosmogony, as described by Herodotus, differed radica11y from the Greek pantheon and cosmogony. From Herodotus's account, we may conclude that the Scythians' chief deity was a goddess named Tabiti and that she shared some elements in common with-or reminded Herodotus of-Hestia, and that just below Hestia/fabiti in the religious hierarchy was a male and female generative couple, roughly equivalent to Heaven and Earth. 1
1 Within the Greek pantheon, Zeus was the pre-eminent god and the son-rather than the husband-of Earth/Gaea. Hestia played a relatively minor role: the eldest daughter of Kronos and Rhea, she was the protector of public and domestic hearths and the goddess of
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



216 ABSENCE AND PRESENCE
The Greek pantheon to which Herodotus compared the Scythian deities represented a complex intertwining of pre-Hellenic and Near Eastern deities with Inda-European deities and concepts. Aphrodite (the Scythians' Artimpasa) is a case in point: she derived from a combination of a local cult goddess and the Near Eastern Ishtar, who was the result of a long, syncretic process. One must assume that the application of Greek names to Scythian deities would be at best only approximate, and that the Scythian connections with traditions rooted in Central Asia would markedly heighten the complexity of those figures referred to as "Hestia," "Zeus," and "Aphrodite." Artamonov has proposed that, within Scythia, the two goddesses, Hestia/Tahiti and Aphrodite/Argimpasa could be combined into a generalized primary goddess. When shown with the mirror, this primary goddess was identified in her Argimpasa aspect; when represented with an altar, however, it was her aspect as goddess of the hearth and fertility which was most significant (Artamonov 1961). Citing the historian's account of Scythian origins from the union of a snake-woman and Heracles, Artamonov associates the snake-bodied goddess represented on many late Scythian objects with Tahiti. There are two arguments against that position, however. It is only in the Pontic Greek version of Scythian origins that this snake-woman appears. Furthermore, the images of the snake-bodied maiden occur only in highly decorative representations, as if their value was primarily ornamental (Artamonov 1961: figs. 9, 12). In no case do the female representations convey the authority expressed by the seated females in the small plaques from Chertomlyk or even in the crude plaque from Merdzhana. One ends by suspecting that the figures of women with snake or leaf shaped bodies are of little significance in locating images of the Scythians' gods and goddesses. In contrast, the seated woman addressed or surrounded by males with rhyta clearly refers to a female of some importance and authority. It is interesting to note that there are no male images in the Scythian pantheon which are identifiable with a Zeus-like god. It is possible to discern two categories of female figures in late Scythian art. The first must include figures who may have referred to deities; the second group would include those figures who may have once referred to cultic deities but who by the fourth century had entered the domain of decorative elements. Within the former group, those
fire. While her name indicates an Indo-European derivation, it is probable that her cult comes out of a pre-Hellenic, pre-Indo-European cult of the hearth (Eliade 1978: vol. I, 247-289, 455-456).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ABSENCE AND PRESENCE 217
figures addressed or surrounded by male and female officiants, such as the images on the Chertomlyk, Karagodeuashkh, and Merzhdana plaques, would be included. The "queen" represented on the Novocherkassk crown may be a late variation on the same theme of a Scythian female deity. By contrast, within the surviving art of the Early Nomads there are virtually no images of human beings, and very few of women. The most important exception is the felt wall hanging from }:>azyryk 5. The significance of that hanging and its images increases when considered in relationship to collateral materials, both excavated and unprovenanced. The appearance of parallel images across the Scytho-Siberian world ends by supporting certain aspects of Herodotus' account. The Early Nomads and their relatives across Kazakhstan and the North Pontic region held in particular reverence a female deity who came to be represented in the headgear and garments of high ranking members of the nomadic world. Her spiritual domain probably included elements which were also associated with the Greek Hestia and Aphrodite: with human and animal fecundity, with the bounty of the earth, and with the protection of the hearth. She was almost invariably represented in a seated position, addressed by young male figures. The latter might be represented on horseback or in the company of a horse. In some cases, the male figures visibly carried goryti or other signs of warfare or the hunt. In other cases, however, no weaponry is visible. Rhyta and cups seem to have been as frequent a mark of the male figure's rank and respect as were weapons. The few remaining representations of male and females together indicate that the primary attributes of the women included rhyta, as well as post-like altars and branches or trees-none of which are associated with male figures. In addition, the Merdzhana plaque adds the intriguing element of a horse head hanging from the female's throne. The lateness of all these representations indicates that her incarnation in a visible, human form occurred only with the advent of human representation in the Scytho-Siberian imagistic tradition. On the other hand, the persistence of many other representational elements across the Scytho-Siberian world indicates that there was a shared and, thus, considerably more ancient understanding of this female deity than is indicated by the actual representational record. Other than the Scythians, within the Scytho-Siberian world the Yuezhi (Kushans) had the most extensive and recorded pantheon of female deities, many of whom reflect Iranian traditions. Sarianidi has identified Anahita-the Iranian ancestor of Aphrodite-as the principle goddess of the Yuezhi culture as attested to by the great finds at Tillya
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



218 ABSENCE AND PRESENCE
Tepe. Represented as the mistress of animals and as a naked frontal figure, Anahita was the goddess of love, plants, fertility, and water (Sarianidi 1989: 121-123). On the coins of Kushan kings, Nana, a particular version of Anahita, is represented. Nana was a composite nature goddess derived from the Iranian Anahita and the ancient Mesopotamian deity Innana/lshtar. Her most distinctive attributes were a sword, reminiscent oflshtar's association with war, and a staff terminating in the protome of a horse or stag (Rosenfield 1967: 88). This last element lacks any precedent in Iranian traditions, but it recalls the horse head hanging on the throne of the woman in the Merdzhana plaque. It also seems to point back in the direction of the Siberian world of the nomads, or at least to that of the nomads beyond the settled agrarian lands. Before turning in that direction, however, it would be useful to briefly consider the ancient tradition of goddesses referred to by the Yuezhi pair, Anahita/Nana. As the last and the most elaborately represented of Scytho-Siberian female deities, their importance and that of their ancestral traditions cannot be minimized. At the same time, their combination of martial and erotic elements indicates a complexity which needs clarification if we are to understand the nature of the female figures in Scythian and Early Nomadic art. Clarification of the Near Eastern goddess tradition may also assist in the untangling of mythic elements attached to the Siberian image of the deer. As the goddess of love and war, Nana's roots may be traced back beyond Inanna/Ishtar to the fourth millennium B.C. and the first great deity of the Mesopotamian world, Ninhursag: the "lady of the stony ground"; the "lady of the foothills"; the "great mother-goddess"; perhaps even Mother Earth (Gimbutas 1989: 110; Kramer 1964: 148). Ninhursag was the first to establish the eight plants in the paradise of the gods; when Enki, the water god and god of wisdom had them cut so that he could eat them, the outraged Ninhursag placed the curse ofdeath upon him. She was thus associated with the gift oflife and the cause of death. Ninhursag, however, was also mistress of wild animals. In her function as birth-giver, she was known as Nintur, meaning the "lady of the birth hut," or ofthe cattle pen. In her association with cattle, Ninhursag/Nintur was the source oflife-giving milk; her presence was indicated by the cow pen and the pillars or posts which indicated the all-important entrance derness in the lovely inlaid frieze from Al 'Ubaid, in which cows being nuzzled by their calfs are milked in the sacred cow pen, while on the other side of the gateway, marked by posts, the milk is being prepared (Hall 1927: pl. XXXI). Thus Ninhursag, the mother of the gods and humanity
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ABSENCE AND PRESENCE 219
and referred to as the mother of kings, could be characterized as a cow and referred to as a post and as the giver of milk (cf. Hall 1927: pl. 142). Her post thus refers to the entrance to a place of generation and sustenance. Ninhursag' s consort was Enlil, the lord of the storm. As he gradually acquired the power to protect and generate vegetation, he was more frequently envisioned as a bull whose image indicated the presence of the goddess (Jastrow [1911) 1971: 74-75). At Al 'Ubaid, one finds the reference to the life-giving force of the goddess in the form of cows, the copper bulls indicative of her fecundating presence and the cemeteries on the west side of the temple indicative ofNinhursag's association with the dead and with the power to restore life (Hall 1927: 143-146). In this regard, she was frequently represented seated in a boat which would transport her to the great city of Ur to renew her sacred marriage with the Moon god. The power of this most ancient and majestic goddess certainly resides within a complexity of functions, as well as in the variety of her symbolic forms. By the second millennium B.C., Ninhursag had been supplanted in absolute power by the male god, Enki. Her double powers over life and death were now embodied in the sisters Inanna and Ereshkigal, the goddess of death and gloom and ruler of the underworld. In other words, as Ninhursag-the giver of milk, life, and death-became challenged by a male god for pride of place, her realm became split between two females, sisters from the same mother. Inanna's Akkadian incarnation, Ishtar, is particularly clear. She was most frequently represented standing on a lion and wearing a girded robe and horned tiara, or seen driving a war chariot and carrying a bow, arrows, and a scimitar. As the queen of heaven, Ishtar was identified with Venus; her sign was the eightpointed star, usually embedded in a circle with rays extending beyond the perimeter. Ishtar was either accompanied by that sign or indicated by it; in the latter case, her sign was flanked by the crescent moon of Sin and the sun-disk of Shamash, the three signs together referring to the Heavens (cf. Pritchard 1954: figs. 518, 522, 526). Ishtar was not only associated with war and martial power; she was also considered the "protector of flocks...the merciful progenitor of mankind" (Jastrow [1911) 1971: 136); she appears with exposed breasts nursing her children. In the Gilgamesh epic, she is described as weeping for her offspring destroyed in the great flood. Either in combination with her consort Tammuz (the personification of the sun of the springtime"the first of Ishtar's lovers"-who was slain by her) or in combination
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



220 ABSENCE AND PRESENCE
with Gilgamesh in the Gilgamesh epic, the pair represents sun and earth, "the combination of the two principles which bring about life; and upon their separation follow decay and death." In association with the sun god, she becomes "the light of heaven and earth," "the shining torch of heaven," light of all dwellings" (Jastrow [1911] 1971: 128). These associations probably derive from her connection with the sun god, as well as from her association with Venus. In association with both Tammuz and Gilgamesh, Ishtar is the passionate lady, but she also appears in these traditions as the earth which drains the sun of its energy. 2 Here, again, one encounters the contradictory, or complementary, aspects of early goddess figures, exemplified first in the supreme goddess Ninhursag. At any point in history, the emphasized aspect appears to have been closely connected with the political ambitions of the current ruler. For example, Ishtar's power as the goddess of war and victory was particularly pronounced in Assyria where Ishtar was revered as the lady of war and battles. Ashurbanapal's father, Esarhaddon, had restored her temple at Uruk, and when Ashurbanapal retook Susa, the capital ofElam, he restored the statue of Ishtar to the temple of E-Anna, from which it had been taken by the Elamites in 2300 B.C. (1635 yrs. earlier) (Jastrow [1911] 1971: 134-135). Under Ashurbanapal (who reigned 668-626 B.C.), Ishtar was often associated with Ashur, the war god, and the two deities were represented standing by the King in his battles, representing the duality of sun and earth. In one of his inscriptions, Ashurbanapal described how Ishtar appeared to him in a dream, armed with bow and sword and promising to lead the him into battle with the Elamites. Among the deities revealed in the Ras Shamra texts was Astarte (Ashtarte), yet another incarnation of Inanna/lshtar. She seems to have been associated in some significant but unclear way with Baal, the chief god. In Egypt, where she appeared as a foreign goddess, her image is clearer; she was represented as a nude figure on a horse, frequently holding the shield, spear, and bow of war. In the Ptolemaic period,, Astarte was referred to as the "lady of the horses and chariots," "repre-• sented by Sechmet standing on a chariot." Pritchard surmises that Astarte's association with the horse and war and herrole as patroness of war in both epithet and representation derived from being brought into Egypt by the Hyksos. As Ashtoreth in the Old Testament, the goddess may have carried some reference to the fecundity of flocks. She appears
2 Gilgamesh rejects her love out of fear of encountering the same loss of vitality as her other lovers encounter (Jastrow [1911) 1971: 127).
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ABSENCE AND PRESENCE 221
as Ashtarte in Phoenician inscriptions only in the first millennium B.C.; although her cult is attested to throughout that millennium, she does not seem to have had a major place in the Phoenician pantheon (Pritchard 1943: 66-72).3 Second to Baal in importance were the two goddesses, Anat and Athirat, whom many believe to have been the biblical Asherah (Obermann 1948). Anat seems to have been a deity of ferocious passion and warlike temperament, unafraid of taunting and threatening her father, El, the supreme god of the pantheon before power shifted to Baal. In the case of Anat, as in that of Ninhursag and Ishtar, one finds associations with cows and cow horns; here, however, Anat is often referred to as a wild cow with "horns of strength" or as an "amiable Virgin" with "scourging horn" (Pritchard 1943: 77; Obermann 1948). Interestingly, she is never shown naked. Invoked by pharaohs as the daughter of Re, the mistress of heaven and the mother of rulers, Anat is also called a "woman being a warrior," a warlike goddess who sought revenge after being raped by the god Seth (Pritchard 1943: 80-81).4 Asherah appears in Palestinian South Arabic texts as a solar deity, the consort of the two moon-gods, Amm and Wadd (cf. Pritchard 1943: 6365). Pritchard believes that in the second millennium, in Syria and, perhaps, South Arabia, Athirat was the chief goddess; by the first millennium, however, her place was taken first by Ashtart, and then by Astarte. Until relatively recently, it was believed that Asherah was not a goddess; other attempts were made to identify her with Ashtart and Ishtar. Only with the Ras Shamra texts did it become clear that such a goddess had existed in the pantheon and that her existence was attested to in texts from many other sources. It is possible that at least a part of the difficulty in believing in her existence was due to the challenge she posed to the Yahwehists of the biblical period. The goddess Asherah was associated with the penetration of the Hathor cult into Syria - Palestine during the period of the Egyptian rule, and with the consequent merging of native Egyptian cults with those of Syria. By the tenth century B.C., Asherah was frequently represented as

(Albright 1938: pis. 26/#1, 27/#1); and Astarte plaques (e.g., two clay plaques representing the goddess naked, frontal, holding two lotus stems and wearing a feather headdress), dated to latter second millennium (Albright 1938: pis. 26/#3,5; 27/ #2,4). 4 She is often represented in a long dress with a plumed or horned crown. Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto
