a naked pillar figure, her pronounced pubic triangle indicating her association with fertility. In Egypt and in some late Bronze Age materials from Syria, she appears with a lion and a tree. On the well-known Taanach cult stand from the tenth century, the goddess still appears in association with lions and trees (Hestrin 1987: 220-221). Like her sister goddesses of the Near East, Asherah of the Old Testament was a complex goddess, exemplifying the syncretism and intertwining of deities in Egypt and the Near East throughout the Bronze Age and well into the Iron Age. In her Qudshu form-naked, frontal, frequently carrying a lotus and wearing the horned Hathor wig-she symbolized beauty, grace, and erotic vitality, as well as fecundity. In the Ras Shamra texts she appears as the wife consort of the supreme god, El; in the biblical tradition, with reference to Yahweh, that position becomes ambivalent, contested, and contradicted. The full meaning of the Old Testament "Asherah" has been the subject of much debate. In some contexts, Asherah seems frequently to have referred to a post or a grove as the symbol of the goddess. She could be represented as a pillar of clay with breasts and head, the pillar thus referring to "the Asherah," or sacred tree, and the whole indicating the Mother Goddess or the Tree of Life, which gives nourishment and life (Hestrin 1987: 222-223).5 InDeuteronomy 16:21-22, reference is made to the nature of Asherah as a tree and a pillar, but this passage also documents the conflict between Asherah' s followers in her popular cult and the new religion centering on Yahweh:
You shall not plant any tree as an 'Asherah beside the altar of the Lord your God which you shall make. And you shall not set up a pillar, which the Lord your God hates.
In her association with trees and milk-giving, Asherah recalls the Egyptian goddess, Hathor, marked by her cow horns (the "Hathor wig") and frequently merged with a tree which kings would drink nourishment from. In the tomb of King Tuthmosis III, the pharaoh is represented drinking milk from Hathor, who is represented in the form of a sycamore tree. In this case, one finds the association of death, rebirth (through the
5 See, e.g., the many examples of the naked pillar figure, female, recovered from Geze, in Palestine, from the first millenium B.C. (late fourth Semitic Period, c. 1000-500 B.C., through the Persian and Hellenistic periods (Macalister I 9 I2: vol. III, pl. CCXXI, #23-26; and see vol. II, 411-423, and fig. 502). At the Geze site, with strata representing culture from the Bronze Age through the Hellenistic and early Christian periods, many terra-cotta figures of females, most naked and frontal, and of what Macalister calls the "Hathor-Ashtoreth" type, were found and dated by him to the late Bronze Age-early Iron Age.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ABSENCE AND PRESENCE 223
milk of the nourishing goddess), and tree (Hestrin 1987: 219; Keel 1978: fig. 254; cf. Bisi 1967). Anahita was the last major incarnation of the great tradition of Near Eastern goddesses. She emerged as a significant goddess in the first millennium B.C. Gershevitch argues that she must have been purely Iranian in origin, since her name is not attested to in Indo-lranian texts, i.e., neither in the Mitanni treaty with the Hittites of the early fourteenth century, where the names of five gods including MithraNaruna are recorded, nor in the corresponding Rig Veda texts. Thus, neither she nor many of the other goddesses in the Iranian branch of the Indo-Iranian tradition seem to have been lndo-European in origin (Gershevitch 1959). Others believe that she was an amalgam of an Indo-Iranian deity and ancient Near Eastern goddess types. Anahita, meaning "immaculate," was the "goddess of celestial waters and fertility." This was the goddess who was known to the Scythians as Argimpasa, or, after Herodotus, as Aphrodite (Herzfeld 1947: 516,527; Masson 1959: 19). According to Herzfeld, Anahita was not associated with any specific earthly home: as the goddess of pure water, she flows down from the mountain Hukarya to the sea, and from the stars down to earth. In verses which, perhaps, are of late date she is represented as driving a chariot drawn by four white animals; she is described as a magnificent, strong young woman (Herzfeld 1947: 517-522). Until the reign of Artaxerxes II (404-359 B.C.), her cult, ifany existed, was aniconic, as were the IndoIranian traditions involving Mithra. Only during the reign of Artaxerxes II do there appear images of both Anahita and Mithra. In that period, Artaxerxes had great statues of Anahita set up in Babylon, Susa, Ekbatana, Persepolis, Bactria, and elsewhere (Masson 1959: 20). In general, however, her position became weakened within the Zoroastrian pantheon. One finds Anahita worshipped primarily in those areas which lay outside the domination of Zoroastrianism, i.e., in the lands of the Yuezhi/Kushans, where she was also given the form of Nana. Within the Iranian lands, no anthropomorphic imagery corresponding to any of the deities of the lndo-Iranian mythic traditions can be found before approximately fourth century B.C. On the other hand, one finds a persistent reference to Bronze Age female deities, frequently in association with snakes or birds. The bottom of a cup from Altyn-Tepe in Turkmenia (third- second millennia B.C.) is painted with images of a schematic bird-headed goddess, her body bent as if seated, before whom stands another schematic figure with upraised arms. Above, on the side of the cup, is a vertically placed snake. A broken terracotta
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



224 ABSENCE AND PRESENCE
fragment from the same site apparently represented a female figure with a snake coiled on her chest (Kozhin 1968: figs. 1, 2). Another deliber-ately bird-headed "goddess" figure was painted on a cup from Kara-tepe in southern Turkmenistan (Masson 1959: fig. 1). As in the case of the figure on the cup from Altyn-Tepe, this figure is bent in the posture of one seated on a throne. On either side of the bird-headed figure on the shard are the remains of two (probably male) figures; judging from the position of their feet, they were facing her, in heraldic fashion. The strata from which this fragment was gathered-Namazga III-is dated by Masson to the first half of the third millennium B.C. In the same site and strata were also found several terracotta figures of bird-headed seated women. According to Masson, the frequency of such terracotta figures in the archaeology of this period testifies to the popularity of a cult focussed on a goddess figure among the early agrarian peoples of Turkmenia. This cult was maintained in the region of Turkmenia until comparatively late in the second millennium B.C. The tradition of making goddess figures probably emerged out of many different cults dedicated to goddesses of the agricultural and stock-dependent peoples of the Aeneolithic and Bronze Ages. They formed the basis for the development of the Avestan deity, Anahita, the protector of cattle and the deity of heaven and fertility (Masson 1959: 20-21). The cult of a goddess-whether of Anahita or, as some have suggested, of the Zoroastrian goddess of order, prosperity, and fertility Asi-Urti-Rti (Ashi)-lasted with force late into the first millennium B.C. and into the common era. The strength of these cults in the region of Margian, Khorezm, Tokaristan and Sogdia is well attested to through archaeological finds, particularly of small terracotta figures. These cults also revealed their strength in the Yuezhi finds at Tillya-Tepe, in which representations of female goddesses serve as a major theme on the thousands of gold plaques. Naked and clothed, carrying implements indicative both of their martial character and their attributes as goddesses of love, the goddesses of the Tillya-Tepe finds indicate the persistence into the Iron Age and into the end of the Scytho-Siberian tradition of the ancient and complex Near Eastern goddess tradition. This overview of the ancestors of the Nana/Anahita (Aphrodite) goddess type leads to a number of general conclusions. It is clear that the goddesses of the ancient Near East tended to blur into each other. This is particularly certain in the second millennium, where the coming of one goddess into the area of another (e.g., Anat into the Egypt of Hathor) resulted in a merging of both function and representation and in both text
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ABSENCE AND PRESENCE 225
and ritual. It is also clear that the goddess figure carried to a greater or lesser degree, depending on the period and the place, values, powers, and associations which would seem vastly contradictory to the modern mind, but which were apparently linked in the minds of earlier peoples. Thus, in Ninhursag and Inanna/lshtar one finds a powerful combination of generative, life-giving forces bound in one goddess with a destructive, warlike character. More striking, perhaps, is that with the passage of time and the rise of male challengers, the two sides of the female goddess become more extreme: fertility, fecundity, and love become bound together with more ferocious, vengeful characters. When one considers the larger tradition of Near Eastern goddesses beginning with Ninhursag and ending with the Anahita/Nana duality, one finds persistent patterns of behavior and recurring iconographic attributes. The combination of life and death, of life-giving and destroying, seems to have been fundamental to the earliest of these goddesses. Down to the representation of Nana, they could be associated with weapons, war chariots, lions-the signs of a warrior. Through cow horns or the Hathor wig they were joined back to the cow, a vessel of life and of life-giving milk. In the form of Hathor and Asherah, there was a regular conflation of goddess as source of life and tree which gave sustanance. The naked, frontal aspects of the Qudshu figures, of her Palestinean variation, 'Elat (Hestrin 1987: 215-217, figs. 1, 2, 3), and of her Old Testament variation, Asherah, wearing cow-horns and having the form of a post or tree, indicate the widespread understanding of the goddess as being associated with those fundamental aspects. In many ways, Asherah exemplifies the syncretism and intertwining of deities in the Near East throughout the Bronze Age and well into the Iron Age. The perseverance of her cult suggests something of the tenacity with which culture clung to an ancient goddess and endowed her with the powers of giving life and death. In her Qudshu form, she symbolized beauty, grace, and sexual vitality. The fecundity that she represented was indicated also in her naked body, as well as in her cow horns and in her manifestation as a post or tree. At the same time, the transformations attendant on the Asherah figure indicate, I believe, something of the hidden struggle for spiritual power which increasingly swirled around her and which increasingly challenged the authority of the ancient goddesses. In several of her incarnations-most spectacularly as Ishtar and most ferociously as Anat-the goddess is associated with symbols usually joined to the male god or ruler: the sun, the lion, the bull, the chariot, and
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



226 ABSENCE AND PRESENCE
weapons of war, as well as brightness, strength, speed, and bellicosity. The association of the bull with Ninhursag and Inanna/Ishtar and of the lion and chariot with Ishtar clearly indicate that even when referring primarily to another deity, symbols may indicate the presence of the goddess and may transfer to her the power they represent. Thus the bull as the source of seed refers at least in part to the fecund nature of the cow; and the lion as a solar animal lends his glory to the warlike Ishtar. As we shall see, these martial aspects are particularly enlightening with respect to the association in Scytho-Siberian scholarship of weapons, bulls, chariots, sun, and fire with the male warrior. These ancient goddesses could be naked or clothed; their crowns were frequently a variation on horns or feathers. They could also be associated with birds through the birdlike treatment of their heads and faces. 6 The appearance of twisting snakes on the body of the goddess and the manifestation of feathers, used to indicate divinity, suggest ways of understanding the so-called "feather-heads" of Karakol and the Minusinsk stelae in a manner other than as "sun heads." In both cases, it is possible that the rayed elements refer to feathers or serpents. This is particularly possible in the case of the vertically waving elements which emerge at the top of the masks on many of the Minusinsk stelae and continue to the upper part of the stones. The persistence of the ancient Near Eastern goddess in varying forms and under varying names, from as early as the third millennium down late into the first millennium B.C., surely indicates the tenacity of her cult even in the face of the emergence of male god-centered religions. Her replication in thousands of small clay images throughout that period and throughout the cultures of the Near East reveals her extreme popularity and the immediacy of her presence. To say this is not to negate the equally frequent veneration of male gods; it is, however, to insist that, regardless of the apotheosis of the latter and despite the contemporary male domination of political power across the Near East and into Central Asia, the cult of a complex goddess persisted well into the end of the first millennium B.C. and well into those areas which were dominated by Scytho-Siberian groups. Furthermore, her archaic tradition repeatedly
6 The feather headdress was actually the attribute of both gods and goddesses from the ancient Orient. It is represented as a high-sided headdress, the feathers bound around approximately 1/2 way up the sides. In Assyria, Shamash, Ashur, and Ishtar were often pictured wearing a crown of horns and feathers (Pritchard 1943: 38-40; Smith 1928: fig. 17) By the first half of the second millennium it was regularly used in the area of Babylonia to indicate divinity. The feather headdress continued to be used down into first millennium Assyria.
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ABSENCE AND PRESENCE 227
reiterated the complexity of her character and her association with animals-cows, birds, and snakes, in particular-as well as reaffirming the concept of milk as a source of life and trees or posts as the sign of fecundity. Whether she was called Hestia or Tabiti, Argimpasis or Aphrodite, Nana, Ardoxsho, or Anahita, her cult remained a major aspect of religious belief throughout the Scythian, Saka, and Yuezhi hegemony. Even if her material form or image were absent, she was indicated by one of her own attributes, or by the body or attributes of others. In other words, if the tree or the post symbolized the goddess, then the bull and the chariot may also refer to her presence. This discussion brings us back to the world of the Scytho-Siberians and to the possibility of extending to that world some of the insights offered by Near Eastern traditions. The lack of written documents characteristic of the Scytho-Siberian world in general and of the Early Nomads and their predecessors in particular makes absolute certainty impossible. There is no way of proving that the seated woman on the Pazyryk 5 (pl. Via) hanging is a South Siberian expression of the seated woman in the Chertomlyk, Karagodeuashkh (pl. Vic, e, e), and Merdzhana plaques. We cannot argue from analogy with Near Eastern symbolic structures that the frontal figures on the Minusinsk stelae, the feathered and horned figures from Karakol, and the strange bird-women from petroglyphs of the Chuluut and Chuya rivers must refer to a fundamental goddess figure. It is not possible to say this figure means that, or that here we certainly have influence from there. It is possible, however, to take parallel structures seriously. In considering the range of female imagery from the Scytho-Siberian world-images as early as those of the Pazyryk 5 hanging and as late as the female bust from the Novocherkassk crown-a number of elements continually reemerge, albeit in different positions and in varying relationships to the female figures. These elements include trees or branches, horses, deer, caprids, and a vertical axis. In the case of Nana and the woman of the Merdzhana plaque, the horse head hangs from a staff or post. In the case of the woman from the Pazyryk hanging, the horse appears before her and she holds the tree-staff. In the unprovenanced anecdotal plaques from the Siberian Treasure, considered as a pair, the woman sits at the base of the tree flanked on either side by men and horses. In the plaques from Chertomlyk and Karagodeuashkh, the woman is addressed by men holding cups or rhyta, the sign of the caprid, or she holds the rhyton herself, thereby indicating her conceptual centrality within the composition. In the case of the more elaborate tree
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



228 ABSENCE AND PRESENCE
crown from Tillya-Tepe, the woman is the central axis of the tree. In fact and in image, she is the center of the tree crown from Novocherkassk, but there she is also flanked by antlered and horned animals. The crown which was placed close to the head of the man buried at Tillya-Tepe reaffirmed the association of horned animal, tree, and death. There-and as in the case of the mountain headdress at Issyk-the symbolism of central axis marked by tree or mountain was transferred to the male from its original source, the female; by that transposition the vitality ascribed to the central and transcendant axis was also reconferred. The association of horse head and staff or post represents a late reformulation of the symbolic system at the center of which was placed the deer as tree or centering axis. We have seen that within the cluster of goddess associations in the ancient Near East, there was a frequent conflation of goddess, tree, post, and source of sustenance (milk). In this connection it is worth noting that among the Yakut there is a very similar cluster of symbolic elements. The Yakut are a Turkic people who retreated northward from their homeland in the Prebaykal perhaps as early as the late first millennium A.D. They are theoretically the principal inhabitants of the vast Siberian region known as Yakutia (Levin and Potapov 1956: 268-271; Okladnikov 1970). Because of their early withdrawal out of South Siberia, the Yakuts have preserved archaic cultural elements more clearly than any other Turkic group has. Their carved, wooden horse-hitches offer a particularly interesting example of archaic elements within their material culture. The horse-hitches are similar in form and function to the horse hitches one sees to this day throughout Mongolia and South Siberia. The southernmost hitch in a row, the one closest to a dwelling, would be considered the most prestigious; that furthest away would command the least respect and authority. Indicative of the centrality of the dwelling and of the respect commanded by the head of the household, the hitches were also believed to function as the resting places of good spirits capable of bringing wellbeing to a household. Although these observations are drawn from modern ethnography, Kubarev has argued that the organization of hitches in relationship to a dwelling is reminiscent of the rows of stones (balbal) in relationship to ritual and burial mounds from the Turkic and perhaps Early Nomadic periods (Kubarev 1979). Although the primary purpose of the Yakut horse-hitches refers to the function of holding a horse and, thus, honoring the head of a household, there are a number of secondary associations which bear upon the discussion of ancient goddess figures. S. V. Ivanov tells us that, until
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ABSENCE AND PRESENCE 229
recently, the hitching posts were used in rituals to assure the fertility of women. Special hitches would be set up under a tree in summer and cupshapes carved at their crowns would be used to hold koumiss for mother earth. In this restructuring of the horse-hitch an ancient association of pole/tree/milk/earth/fertility is indicated, all associated with the horse, the primary Yakut source of milk and koumiss. Ivanov has published one elaborate Yakut tether, collected in the nineteenth century, the upper part of which takes the form of elaborate tree branches with a small human figure at the base and a bird at the top (pl. Ve). The potential power of such hitches is also suggested by the fact that the Yakut designated special horse-hitches only for the "winged" or "humpbacked" horse of a big shaman; to hitch any ordinary horse to such a tether would be to kill it (S.V. Ivanov 1976). There is absolutely no way of tracing the real signs of a connection between the pole or tree form of the ancient Near Eastern goddesses and the Yakut horse-hitches, and there is no way of documenting any certain connection between the latter and the woman from the Pazyryk 5 hanging. There is, however, a striking analogy between the clusters of associations, one that argues for a widespread and archaic understanding of the relationship between tree/pole, woman/milk, and a power so vital as to be capable of giving life and at the same time inflicting harm and destuction. The duality of character embodied in Ninhursag and all her female descendants is distantly glimpsed in the thin line between life and death epitomized in the act of bringing a being into the world, when the female body is both the source of life and the threat of enclosing death. In those early goddesses, as in the special Yakut horsehitches, that which gives life is also capable of destruction. It is no wonder that the tree should become the metaphor for that complex aspect of being: rooted in the ground, reaching toward heaven, displaying in its foliage the eternal cycle of emergence, florescence, and death, and bearing on its branches the fruits of life, the tree was the ideal sign of the Mother Goddess and of all that she embodied. Whether translated into a post in the form of a frontal woman, into a seated female holding a branch, or into a woman wearing a tree crown, the combination of elements remains consistent and points back to parallel religious and mythic conceptions.
We have seen that beyond their funerary purposes, the slab-grave and khereksur complexes of South Siberia were the sites of elaborate ritual, including rituals of sacrifice. In intention and to some degree in structure, these complexes refer back to a period considerably earlier than the
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



230 ABSENCE AND PRESENCE
late Bronze Age date to which the khereksur are usually assigned. While Kubarev has emphasized the difficulty of determining chronologies for all parts of the khereksur and deer stone complexes, Tivanenko has emphasized the antiquity and continuity of ritual sites in the Baykal region, and especially those sites which included sacrifical altars (Kubarev 1979; Tivanenko 1989). Like the ritual sites of the Baykal region, Yustyd is the kind of archaeological complex which has come to be called a "sanctuary," a place sanctified by the practices and structures of successive generations. It is generally acknowledged that South Siberia is marked everywhere by sanctuaries which may include the images or structures of cultures as early as the Neolithic and as late as those of the present-day. We have seen this process of overlay and sanctification in the case of petroglyphs. It is no less evident in the interpenetration of Afanasevo, Early Nomadic, and Turkic cemeteries in Gorno-Altayskaya A.O., in the ritual sites reused over centuries in the Transbaykal, or in the reuse and reconstruction of Mongolian deer stones and circular stone altars. With the exception of the Turkic structures, most parts of these sanctuaries resist certain dating. What is clear is that their roots go back to at least the early Bronze Age and possibly earlier. Throughout South Siberia there is ample evidence that the location of sanctuaries was dependent on the character of a place. That is, the natural environment conferred a signification on the site of the ritual structures and it, in turn, was sanctified by the deposition of human artifacts. The petroglyphs of South Siberia are regularly located beside or above rivers, as if the river itself were a metaphor for significant experience and the petroglyphs were witness to the sanctification of that experience (cf. Okladnikov 1950, 1966; Tivanenko 1989). In many cases, ritual sites marked by sacrificial altars have been found at the base of or directly under cliffs with petroglyphs, as if the images in stone represented or referred to the spirits to whom ritual sacrifice was made. Such ritual sites relative to petroglyphic complexes have been particularly noted in the region of the Prebaykal where images of female elk dominate the cliffs, their presence affirming a date in the Neolithic period. Along the upper Angara and Lena rivers and their tributaries there is a clear relationship of petroglyphs and ritual sites to stretches of the river marked by dangerous rapids (Tivanenko 1989: 82 ff.). From this combination of elements the river emerges as a metaphor for the river to the land of the dead, and the rock paintings and carvings functioned as an invocation to the spirits who would guide the dead to that other world. In the Neolithic
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ABSENCE AND PRESENCE 231
period, the image most frequently invoked was that of the female elk; it must have been she to whom the ancient cultures along South Siberia's rivers appealed for assistance in their fearful passage down the waterways. The distribution of ritual structures in South Siberia indicates that, with the passage of time from the Neolithic into the Bronze Age, rituals relating to death were freed from a necessary association with rivers. Certainly this appears to be the case in those regions capable of supporting a gradual shift to a pastoral economy. In the Minusinsk Basin, the stelae dominating what may have been late Neolithic or Aeneolithic ritual sites faced to the East rather than being oriented by reference to rivers. Elsewhere during the South Siberian Bronze Age, a similar orientation became more widely spread. Ritual sites were frequently placed in broad valleys surrounded by mountains. Most of those valleys are marked by the rivers which helped to form them. The deer stones themselves, however, were turned toward the East; and as far as can be gathered from recorded sites throughout Mongolia, the Transbaykal, Tuva and the northern Altay, the complexes of enclosures, khereksur, and burials which were marked by deer stones also became oriented by reference to the cardinal directions. This change in orientation suggests that the passage to another world had come to refer to a larger universe than was earlier indicated by specific rivers. The rivers that flowed from the warmer south towards the ice-bound north-the Yenisey, the Tom', the Angara and Lena, for example-had served as metaphors for the passage from the land of the living to the land of the dead for the people of the Baykal Neolithic. Within the Bronze Age Minusinsk Basin, the Sayan-Altay Ridge, and Mongolia, the significant universe became organized by reference to the four directions, and within that universe it is probable that mountains, valleys, and the movement ofcelestial bodies played a more important role than rivers did. Such a transition in sanctified places-from river to mountain valley-should not be wondered at. It was precisely during the Bronze Age that South Siberian peoples made the critical transition from a dependency on hunting and fishing to a dependency on livestock. The grass-rich plains of the Transbaykal and northern Mongolia and the pasture land marking the terraced valleys of the Sayan - Altay became the source of well-being for Bronze Age South Siberia and the fitting locus of ritual sites. From the mountains flowed the water on which pasture depended, and on the spring and summer flanks of the mountains both wild animals and grazing stock pastured. Within such an ecologically adjusted niche,
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



232 ABSENCE AND PRESENCE
it was fitting that mountains became the locus of helping spirits and the source of a lineage's life and well-being. Within both the deer stone/khereksur and deer stone/slab grave contexts, the essential, common element is the vertical post. Gyaznov has proposed that the stone stele is simply a more permanent form of the ancient wooden posts used to mark ritual sites (Gryaznov 1978, 1984), and there is some evidence to support that hypothesis. One of the ritual sites at Lower Burgultae, on the Dzhide River in the southern Transbaykal, included slab-graves, khereksur, and over fifty circular enclosures. Each of the enclosures included the skull, backbone, and hoofs of a horse. A significant number included other animal bones as well as signs of burning. Within a number of the enclosures the remains of wooden posts that had risen from the center of the ring were found (Tivanenko 1989: 107-109) At Shan Tengerintal, in the Arhangay Aymag, Volkov recorded a complex that included two gigantic khereksur and a squared enclosure defined by several deer stones. In the southwest corner of that enclosure and at its center were found the remains of large wooden posts. At the base of the post in the center part of a calf's backbone was also recovered (Volkov 1981: 26). The association of posts or trees with sacrificial altars was not limited to the Bronze and early Iron Ages. Indeed, the burial and ritual sites of the early Turkic period indicate that this association was maintained for many centuries. At an early Turkic period site in the Uzuntay River basin of Gorno-Altayskaya A.O., Kubarev found a number of ritual enclosures within which had been placed the root sections of large trees (Kubarev 1978). In light of known mythic traditions of the early Turks, it is probable that these root sections symbolized sacred trees or the World Tree, which offered passage from the land of the living to the land of the dead. The appearance of such symbolic trees within a Turkic ritual site suggests a fundamental link between Turkic ritual structures and those which can be found as early as the late Bronze Age (cf. Savinov 1981). It is the Minusinsk stelae carved with masks which offer the most striking parallels and precedents for the later deer stones. It is probable that these great monoliths were originally intended to serve as the central elements in ritual sites that also included stone hearths or altars for ritual animal sacrifice. In some cases, such as that of the large complex in the Sorga Valley which included burials, sacrificial altars, and huge stones, the stelae were clearly ranged from north to south and were turned to the East. The sacrificial altars were placed to the west of the stelae. This regular ordering, as well as the combination of structures and functions
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ABSENCE AND PRESENCE 233
within a single complex, closely parallel those established in the great deer stone complexes of Mongolia and Tuva. It is also significant that the Minusinsk stelae were buried deep in the ground, with as much as one-third or one-half of the stone's length beneath the surface of the earth. Certainly a part of that deep burial was practical: in order to remain upright, they required such deep rooting. There is much evidence, however, that the purpose of burying the stone was more than functional. A number of the stones were apparently buried deeply enough so that parts of their carved surface were lost from view. Other stones were placed in the ground in an inverted position, or in such a manner that the masks were inverted (e.g., pl. Xa). A similar inversion is suggested by the irregular directionality of the images of deer on the Mongolian deer stones, as well as by the carving of Cimmerian anthropomorphic stelae, which sometimes have two similar ends (e.g., pl. XXIb). In all these instances, one senses ancient parallels to the common Siberian belief, exemplified in the case of the Evenk, that the land of the dead is like that of the living, but in reverse. It seems as if the placement of the stones effectively functioned as a metaphor for the joining of two different spheres, an underworld and an upper world, and as if that function was necessarily accompanied by sacrificial altars. The female aspect of the masks dominating the Minusinsk stelae has impressed many observers, as have her peculiar animal characteristics: her large nostrils, horns, and staring eyes. Khlobystina has identified the animalistic masks as reflecting the conflation of the female ancestor and her totemic husband, the bull (Khlobystina 1971 a: 170). Kyzlasov associates the female mask with what he calls the First Mother; her stele refers to the axis of the world, analogous to the World Mountain and the World Tree (Kyzlasov 1986). The possibility of developing, as has Kyzlasov, an elaborate interpretative strategy to account for all elements on the stelae is questionable in the absence of sufficient contingent material. It is certain, however, that the female and animal aspects of the carved images were significant and dominating elements within the iconographic program of the stelae. It is also certain that within a ritual context involving animal sacrifice, the stelae represent the closest parallels to the deer stones of the Sayan-Altay and Mongolia-Transbaykal types. We lack a continuous thread between the Minusinsk stelae and the deer stones of the late Bronze Age, and occasional carved stelae only suggest that continuity. One from the Sorga complex, carved with a mask on its eastern face but the upper end carved in the shape of an elk head,
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



234 ABSENCE AND PRESENCE
was reused within a Tagar site (pl. Xlb). Other potentially transitional stones of the Sayan-Altay type were discussed in Chapter V. However, assuming that the classic deer stones cannot be dated earlier than approximately 1000 B.C., it is obvious that within the chronology of the South Siberian monolithic stelae there is a gap between the Neolithic and late Bronze Ages traditions-a gap which may or may not be filled by deer stones exemplied by a number from the Chuya Steppe. Assuming that the Minusinsk stelae do not represent a cultural dead-end, that they were indeed the "ancestors" of the later deer stone tradition, it is reasonable to expect that the beliefs and myths consecrated in those great monoliths flowed into the cultural base of succeeding periods, albeit in changed form. The tradition represented by petroglyphs of female elk associated with ritual altars and animal sacrifice belonged to cultures dependent on rivers and forests for their survival, cultures with an economy rooted in hunting and fishing. In contrast, the stelae of the Minusinsk Basin must be associated with the emergence of stock raising. The transition between the two economic traditions is suggested by the combination of elk representations and female masks on the Sorga and Sartygoy stones (Kyzlasov 1986: figs. 32, 124) and in the overlapping cultural layers of petroglyphs at Shalobolin. That transition, however, can also be traced in the petroglyphs and Bronze Age ritual sites in the region around Lake Baykal, where forest gives way to grassland and the possibility of pasture. Within Bronze Age South Siberia a series of symbolic transformations occurred indicative of the essential transformations of underlying myth. The female elk image had long since been discarded, and the female mask-quasi-human, quasi-animal-had also been left behind, as were the curious bird-women recorded in petroglyphs along the Chuluut and Chuya rivers. These female images were replaced in Bronze Age petroglyphs by images of antlered deer, bovines, and horned and hatted figures. At Kalbak-Tash and Chuluut Gol, animals seemingly combining the bovine and deer mark the overlay of two traditions: one rooted in a forest/river economy, and the other rooted in pastoral stockbreeding. These syncretic animals suggest the deliberate fusing of zoomorphic images in response to the actual fusing of cultures at the figurative and real borders between forest and steppe. It is not surprising that such images appear precisely in areas which could have supported both pastoralism and hunting. Furthermore, the continued appearance of thousands of images of deer and cattle throughout the petroglyphic
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ABSENCE AND PRESENCE 235
record of Bronze Age South Siberia attests to a continuing process of cultural influx and interchange. I have referred on a number of occasions to the ritual organization of space with reference to the cardinal directions characteristic of both the Minusinsk stelae complexes and those of the deer stones. Indeed, so similar are these traditions in their sacrificial dedication and in the use of carved monolithic stelae facing decisively to the East that one would be hard pressed to explain away the similarities through the arguments of coincidence or convergence. Decisive images, however, had changed. The Minusinsk stelae were carved with the monumental heads of beings resembling a cross between humans and animals. Breasts and full bellies indicate the female gender of these strange beings; horns and wide-set eyes most frequently seem to refer to bovines. Snake-like streamers and unidentifiable four-cornered marks complete the complex of images. In contrast, the deer stones are marked most obviously by the image of the antlered deer. In its appearance on Mongolian and Transbaykal stones and in its reappearance on petroglyphs throughout northern Mongolia, Tuva, and the northern Altay, that deer image almost certainly derives from the reindeer, rather than from the elk or maral. The stone itself unquestionably refers to a human male. Whether one concludes that it is indeed the image of a warrior or whether it may simply be the image of the male in ritual regalia-his prized tools and weapons hanging from his belt-the male reference of the stone is beyond doubt. While it is possible to theorize about the meaning of the quasi-human, quasi-animal masks on the Minusinsk stelae, certainty remains out of reach. One is impressed, however, by a certain coincidence of elements. The stelae combine monumental posts with images that would best be described as animal-women. The appearance of breasts and swollen bellies and the location of these stones at sacrificial sites, set with the masks facing to the East, convey the impression of a primary concern for beginnings of time and space and for regeneration. On the other hand, the deep anchoring of the stones in the soil, the appearance of menacing beasts on a number of the stones, and even the ferocious aspect of some of the masks suggest, also, a concern for the negative and deathconnected aspects of existence. In this combination of characteristics, the stelae offer a South Siberian parallel to the goddess figures of the Near East, particularly in their post or tree formulations. The combination ofpositive and negative and life and death, lodged in stelae and mask offers a northern alternative to the goddess of love and death, and to the erotic and ferocious character of an Ishtar or Anat. In the Minusinsk
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



236 ABSENCE AND PRESENCE
stelae there seems to be indicated either another expression of what may have been a virtually universal understanding of the Mother Goddess or the result of the infiltration of cultures from the southwest, from the region of Kazakhstan, and of the resulting merging with autochthonous cultures. We know that in the region ofTurkmenia there was a widespread and tenacious cult of a great goddess figure from the beginning of the Bronze Age and that this goddess seems to have shared in the complex symbolism of her Mesopotamian sisters. The images preserved in the petroglyphs of Saimaly-Tash surely indicate, as Golendukhin has argued, a cult associated with the first ploughing of the earth (Golendukhin 1971). While the "sun-heads" there may refer to a solar cult, given the virtually certain pre-Indo-European date of those petroglyphs it is probable that the cult was dedicated to an earth goddess and that the curious feather or ray-headed creatures may refer to her, rather than to a god. So, too, may the heads with radiating rays from the pre-Bronze Age petroglyphs at Tamgaly refer to a cult of the goddess, rather than to the cult of a sun god. This is particularly true of the impressive frontal figure with circular head, standing on a long-horned bull (pl. VIIIg). In contrast to the ithyphallic animal, the standing figure has no indication of male gender, leading one to believe that here, as in the case of similar frontal figures from the Karakol slabs, a female deity is represented, rather than a male. The interior of the figure's head is filled with large dots arranged in a manner reminiscent of a constellation: one in the center surrounded by nine others. From the circle of the figure's head extend rays, more reminiscent of the extended rays of Ishtar's star sign than of the enclosed rayed sun disk of Shamash (e.g., Pritchard 1954: figs. 518, 529). The: relationship of bulls and lions to Mesopotamian goddess figures raises. the possibility that the bull here refers to the goddess's fecund presence; figure and bull are the necessary duality for the beginnings of life. These and similar images from Saimaly-Tash and Tamgaly appear to offer iconographic evidence for an ultimately distant source for the images found at Karakol and those appearing on the Minusinsk stelae. The masks on the Minusinsk stelae suggest the association of an anthropomorphic-bovine image with the influx of a culture more dependent on pastoralism than had been true of previous cultures in the region. At the same time, the mixed bones and teeth found in related sacrificial sites indicate the transitional dependency of that culture on wild animals, as well as on domesticated stock. The parallels between stele image and its post-like presentation and the post and tree versions
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ABSENCE AND PRESENCE 237
of the Great Goddess of the Near East may not, in other words, have been entirely coincidental. The idiosyncratic nature of all these images beyond the Mesopotamian world, however, indicates the independent elements that the masks refer to. The strange figures at Tamgaly, Karakol, Shalobolin, and those carved into the Minusinsk stelae trace the imagistic and conceptual reworking of an idea as it was carried further and further from its homeland. Lacking texts to clarify the transitions we see in symbolic structures, we may turn to those structures themselves to inform our understanding of underlying processes. There are two considerations which may illuminate the transition indicated between, on the one hand, the dominant images of Neolithic South Siberia-the female mask/stele and the female elk/petroglyph-and, on the other hand, the sign of late Bronze Age South Siberia-the male anthropomorphic stelae with deer images. Of all female cervids, the female reindeer (or, in North America, the caribou) bears antlers. The importance placed on the reindeer image reflects the real economic value of the animal within the Siberian woodland and tundra-an animal which is both wild and domesticable, an animal which would ultimately be herded in Siberia like domesticated cattle, horses, and sheep. As late as the nineteenth century, the wild Siberian reindeer were said to move in herds estimated in the thousands, their huge numbers promising human groups security from hunger and cold. There has been considerable, if inconclusive, discussion regarding the origins of reindeer domestication and riding, with calculations of its appearance ranging from the Bronze Age to the middle of the first millennium B.C. (Kyzlasov 195; Vaynshteyn 1968; Zeuner 1963). The Great Boyar petroglyphs on the Sukhaya-tes River in the Minusinsk Basin, dated to the late Tagar period, include the representation of two riders apparently herding deer; one of these deer is shown being ridden (Devlet 1976a). On the basis of the extensive use of reindeer products in Pazyryk 1, Gryaznov has argued that the inhabitants of the Sayan -Altay region may have already herded them by the early Iron Age (Gryaznov 1978b). Regardless of the actual date of the reindeer's domestication, however, the finds of reindeer bone in Neolithic and Bronze Age Siberian sites and the dependency of the Early Nomads on reindeer products indicate that even in its wild state the animal was a primary source of meat, leather, and bone for the inhabitants of Bronze Age Tuva, northern Mongolia, and within the Pre-and Transbaykal zones (Flerov 1960). It is not difficult to imagine that the reindeer herds of Siberia would serve early cultures as a metaphor for the wealth of the natural world
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



238 ABSENCE AND PRESENCE
and-for those cultures which increasingly depended on stock-as a metaphor for the desired wealth in domesticated herds. Like the female elk of more wooded regions, the female reindeer would seem to double the invocation for fertility and well-being: as part of the herd and as the source of its numbers. In the Baykal Neolithic period, it was the female elk which served as the sign of life and rebirth; it was most certainly the shape of the Great Mother to which the dead would return along the clan rivers. With the emergence of a stock dependency brought to South Siberia by cultures coming out of the southwest and west the image of the Great Mother took on the form of a strange being, half-cow, halfwoman. It is to her that the Minusinsk monoliths were dedicated, and her association with beginning and end, life, death, and renewal, is indicated by the stones' rootedness in the ground and by the masks' facing towards the East. It is as if the underlying object of reverence---call it the Great Mother, the Animal Mother, or the Great Goddess-had simply changed shape, from elk to cow-human, with the transformation of economic dependency from hunting and fishing to pastoralism. The female elk disappeared because her mythic substance had been reformulated under the impact of new cultural elements. It takes little imagination to understand how the antlered but female reindeer could function to join early beliefs rooted in a hunting economy with the beliefs which emerged from an increasing dependency on livestock. From the basic understanding that the deer stones refer to male warrior figures in general and to the tribal progenitor in particular, has followed the assumption that the deer stone complexes must be associated with cults dedicated to the male ancestor. The phallic appearance of the stones has also been repeatedly cited as indicating the dedication of the stone to rites offertility vested in the male. The position taken here is that the phallic appearance of the deer stone is purely coincidental. The argu-ment for its phallic significance on the basis of its vertical shape is no more justified than would be the argument that the circular mirror of the Scytho-Siberians was symbolic of the female sex and thus of female power. If the deer stone in its various types refers to a male figure in dress regalia without necessarily representing a phallus and without necessar-ily referring specifically to a warrior, how is one to reconcile this male figure with what has been said above: that within the deer stone ritual complexes the deer emerges as the inheritor of traditions which were: embodied in the female mask and female elk? Some of the answer may be found within the tradition of Scythian art, including that of the carved
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ABSENCE AND PRESENCE 239
monolithic figures. Of all the Scytho-Siberian artistic traditions which inherited the South Siberian Bronze Age mythic and iconographic vocabulary, that of the Scythians was most clearly shaped by the realism of the Hellenic world. Through the adoption and adaptation of Hellenic realism to Scytho-Siberian mythic traditions, the Scythians translated into human and naturalistic terms the religious beliefs which lie behind the images of deer, feline, and bird. The plaques from Chertomlyk, Karagodeuashk, Merdzhana, and the Prigradnaya figures argue that the male stelae alone must have been intended to indicate the presence of a woman or goddess. The combination of the male who honors and the female who is honored is, of course, the same combination represented in the great felt hanging of Pazyryk 5. Missing from this Siberian version and from those of Chertomlyk and Karagodeuashkh, as well as from almost all the Scythian stone figures, is the image of the deer. Only on the Krasnodar figure is it recalled by the plaque of a deer on the figure's chest. That figure, however, raises a rhyton to its chest, as if silently honoring the absent seated female. If the Scythian figure is representation, then the unseen female is the indicated presence, and the significance of the stone figures lie in a balance implied between the male officiant and the enthroned female. In the earlier deer stones of South Siberia, the deer that wraps itself around the male figure was the sign of that female power. Yet even there in South Siberia, the stones reveal a tension between sources of power, both spiritual and worldly: a tension between the power of the archaic female as source and the power increasingly claimed by those who would officiate at her altar. Against this background, the principles we found associated with the Early Nomads' symbolic systems become clarified. The vertical axis with images displayed symmetrically on either side referred to a pole or tree associated with the source of the cycle of birth, death, and regeneration. In the materials from Tuekta and Pazyryk, we find a persistent implicit designation of that axis by the placement of bird wings, foliage, antlers, and horns, and by motifs which seem to shift between these elements, as if the very fact of their visual transformations would invoke the cycle of death into life. The antlered and homed animals displayed on either side of the axis were the sign of that essential source. The images of predation-in which deer or caprids are attacked, but never killed-reiterate symbolically that cycle of existence, but the images now refer to the natural world which surrounded the Early Nomads. The predation of large cats and wolves on deer and caprids had become a metaphor for the pattern of existence which earlier had been fully
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



240 ABSENCE AND PRESENCE
summed-up in the singular image of an elk or deer. This recourse to the natural world surely measures the impact on Early Nomadic culture-as on the culture of the Sakas and Scythians-of the realism of Near Eastern and Hellenic styles. The Achaemenid mirrors, rugs, and belt buckles found in the Pazyryk burials indicate that foreign standard of representation to which the archaic Siberian symbolic systems were now adjusted. The adaptation of such foreign motifs as lions and eaglegriffins measures, also, the shifting taste of the Pazyryk culture as it figuratively turned away from its ancient Siberian focus and towards an increasing awareness of the signs of Near Eastern wealth and power. More reflective of the gradually rising wave of realism inspired by Persian and Hellenic taste is the mountain headdress from the Issyk burial and the realistic axial combinations of trees, birds, deer, caprids, and humans embedded in the crowns from Tillya-Tepe and Novocherkassk. So, too, on the Aluchaideng crown, the bird hovering over the predation of wolf on caprid restates the literal translation of the motif of death and regeneration. Even the headdresses of Ulandryk-much further removed from the influence of Persian and Hellenic taste--demonstrate the intrusion ofrealism. There, however, one senses a loss or weakening of symbolic systems, a reduction of once powerful metaphor to the symmetrical display of skillfully carved images. Against this discussion it is useful to reconsider one of the most unusual images from the Scythe-Siberian world, that of the deer with bird-headed antler tines. It is not possible to determine the actual source of that image since it has been found on objects as separated in time and space as fifth century Bulgaria, the North Pontic region, the Caucasus, the fourth century northern Altay, and extensively from Xiongnu finds in Mongolia and Inner Mongolia. It appears frequently in objects from the Siberian Treasure of Peter the Great and is recapitulated on belt plaques worn by the man buried in Issyk kurgan. Whatever its source, this image apparently carried an immediately comprehended meaning across the nomadic world. In an earlier study I suggested that the image of the deer, its antlers reminiscent of tree branches and its head and antler-tines transformed into birds, must have referred to a tree of life. In those cases where the deer is twisted as if under the impact of a predator-as in the case of the images tattooed on the man from Payzryk 2-the twisting refers to the death from which regeneration will emerge. The placement of such images on either side of the central axis of the man surely reaffirmed the location of that cyclical process of change at the center, which is ultimately signalled by the antlers of the animals, and
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ABSENCE AND PRESENCE 241
at that center are the antler-trees in which the birds of souls to be returned to the world are perched. The deer with bird-headed antler tines was a relatively late restatement of an idea already undergoing change. As in the case of the literal representations of predation from the Pazyryk saddle blankets, the explicit nature of the transformation of antlers into bird_heads, of deer heads into great beaked heads, and the evocative twisting of the body of the deer suggest an emerging concern for explicit representation. In contrast, the great feline plaque from Arzhan, the recumbent deer and crouched felines on the Kelermes gorytus cover, and the recumbent deer from Kostromskaya kurgan refer in monumental formality to the same cyclical nature of being treated in greater detail in the recumbent deer from Ak-Mechet, Zhurovka, Ilyichovo, and Axjutintsy. The increasingly detailed rendition of that process points ultimately to the weakening of archaic myth and the concept of the deer as Great Mother within the Scytho-Siberian world. Detail and realism, the translation of the archaic deer image into the prettified, weak Kul Oba deer, the transformation of the deer into the image of a female goddess: these are the signs that the archaic mythic tradition of the early nomadic world had been buffeted and thrown into question by impulses from the Hellenic and Achaemenid worlds. It is as if the closer the nomads moved to those sedentary lands-the closer physically and culturally, that is-the more their ancient traditions were compromised by new concepts, new understandings, and new goddesses and cults.
Considered against the background of the fluidity of Siberian cultures and mythic traditions, the symbolic systems revealed in Siberian ethnography acquire greater meaning, and the mythic traditions of the Ket and Evenk begin to fill in the gaps of the story we have been trying to reconstruct through the broken archaeological record. It becomes clearer that even up to the present time aspects of a mother cult have been recorded throughout Siberian societies. It is also apparent that they are rooted in an ancient and more magisterial, more unified understanding of cosmogenesis and in the continued dependency of human society on this originating source. Over the centuries, the transformation of the archaic tradition has demoted that original spiritual center, restricting her powers to lesser cults, remnants of which are preserved in the bear cult and in the Ket kaigus' and ale/. They are also glimpsed in the Nanay cult of the ancestors, centered on the wooden dzhuolin . The sexual and social tensions lodged in those beliefs become more comprehensible in
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



242 ABSENCE AND PRESENCE
light of the traditions which were lost. The ubiquitous fire cult-the cult of the hearth, the family's center both literally and figuratively-emerges as a more substantial echo of the deer-mother whose locus referred to the beginning of a lineage, to the place of return to the clan river, and to the vital center of the lineage itself. Of all surviving Siberian peoples, the Ket and the Evenk are most easily traceable back to Bronze Age roots in the very regions from which probably emerged the ancestors of the Early Nomads: the region around Lake Baykal and the upper Yenisey. For that reason, their archaic: traditions have been of particular interest in this study. The Evenk concepts of bugady mushunin and bugady enintyn-mistress of the clan and mistress-mother of the clan-both combining the concepts of woman and cow elk or wild cow reindeer point directly back to the female elk and deer images of the Siberian Neolithic and Bronze Ages. In the concept of dunne mushunin, the Mistress of the clan lands who lives under a rock or under the roots of the sacred clan tree and points. out the shaman's way to the dwelling place of the bugady enintyn, one finds the reaffirmation of the concept of the post or tree which marks the passage to the underworld. In the image of a giant cow elk or cow reindeer, lying under the clan lands, one seems to catch distant sight of the impulses behind the great stelae wrapped in the images of deer, or of the deer with bird-headed antler tines. The complex cycle of birth, destruction, and rebirth appears in the Evenk rite of shingkelevun as well as in the concept of an animal mother referred to in the deer stones and within the symbolic systems of the Early Nomads. The shifting conception of these references, from woman to deer or elk and back again, reflects, I believe, the complex reference of the Bronze Age deer image. It also anticipates the animal's eventual transformation into the figure of a goddess. The shifting reference from deer to woman to deer, found in many archaic Siberian mythic traditions, is vividly reflected in the Nganasan belief in a swamp where their animal mothers live as naked women with the hair and antlers of deer. It will be recalled, however, that the fire in the tent of these animal mothers is analogized to the sun, and that the great female elk, Kheglun, is variously said to carry the sun on its antlers and to be the body of the Great Dipper constellation. Considering these traditions together with the almost universal Siberian association of fire with a female protective deity, one begins to question the appropriateness of imposing a male solarism on South Siberian cultural traditions. The archaic aspect of the association of fire and sun with female
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ABSENCE AND PRESENCE 243
entities-anthropomorphic or zoomorphic-which has been attested to throughout Siberian cultures by modern ethnography strongly suggests that its roots are ancient and deep, preceding even the period of the Early Nomads. The stability of these traditions makes it probable that one need not have recourse to an Indo-European solar tradition to "explain" Scytho-Siberian myths. In the age of the Early Nomads, the sun was not necessarily associated with a male warrior or male ancestor; it was much more likely connected with a female aspect and was referred to through the image of the deer. One must recall the repeated association of the Scytho-Siberian deer and its Bronze Age predecessors with a post-tree or antler-tree in which appear the heads of birds-the birds of the souls to be born. In that treereferred to as post or stele or branching antlers-appears the pictorial counterpart to the mythic traditions lying behind the Evenk concept of the turu , the sacred tree of the clan and the sacred tree of the clan's shamans. Among the roots of the turu lies the great elk mother of the clan. It is she who eats the soul of the shaman to be born, and it is the turu and the elk mother who mark the place of clan origin. Within the mythic tradition, the concept of the tree rooted in the Animal Mother and marking the point ofreturn and origin clarifies, I believe, the purpose and function of the deer stones, marking the sacred places of sacrificial enclosure, ritual mound, and burial. The Evenk mythic tradition animates the powerful images of female elk and deer from the Siberian Neolithic and Bronze Ages, lending a surging vitality to the image of the female animal, to the deer wrapped around the vertical stelae, to the deer with the heads of birds and with antlers transformed into branches tipped with birds. Yet the Ket mythic tradition illuminates the tension which appears in the Early Nomads' visions of predator and prey, of elk, deer, and caprid crushed by the weight of wolf and feline. That mythic tradition also goes a long way in explaining the process which finally resulted in the loss of the deer image as mythic symbol and in the eventual diminution of the fundamental concept of a single great mother. It will be recalled that the Ket mythic universe is theoretically dominated by the figure of Es, the male god of Heaven, who was associated with the sun and to whom wild reindeer are sacrificed. In fact, however, the most compelling figure of that pantheon and the one who functions as a perpetual source of disruption is Khosedam. She is the former wife of Es; to her, as to Es, a reindeer must be sacrificed, and it is Khosedam who is locked in eternal battle with the champion of human society, the hero Dokh. In Khosedam and her sister
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



244 ABSENCE AND PRESENCE
Tomaro one sees the splitting of power which accompanied the history of the Great Goddess wherever she occurred, the gradual exaggeration of her power to destroy and her horrific aspects, on the one hand, and the elaboration of her beauty and goodness in human terms on the other. The rages of Khosedam, her passage up the Yenisey to afflict human society and her flights back to her ice-bound island in the north, her eternally recurring battle with Dokh, and, most importantly, the fact that Khosedam remains both the source of human sickness and death and her womb the necessary passage to rebirth: all these aspects reveal the conflicting forces within which the deer-mother of the Bronze Age became dislodged, diminished, and backed into extreme positions in the Iron Age. Like one act following another, the Ket mythic tradition seems to offer the dramatic, turbulent sequel to that of the Evenk. Something of the ambivalence toward the Animal Mother was eventually institutionalized in the practices of shamanism. The deer aspect of the shaman's robe, the references to deer and birds in the shaman's headdress, and the deerskin drum conceived in terms of the animal mother at the base of the Tree of Life and as the shaman's steed: all these major aspects of Siberian shamanism point back to a time when knowledge of the source of human life and death did not require shamanic mediation. In comparing the signs and symbols associated with the shaman with the ubiquitous appearance in Early Nomadic burials of the signs of the journey after death, it becomes apparent that there was a displacement of power since the early Iron Age. At some point-perhaps early in the first millennium A.D.-the institution of shamanism as we know it today emerged, gathering into its rituals reformulations of the symbolic systems of an earlier period. The antlered deer became the mother and cloak ofthe shaman. The deer and the tree were reconsecrated to the shaman's drum. The drum emerged as the shaman's steed, a late reflection of that same fusing of deer and horse which appears in the Early Nomadic deer with bird-headed antlers. Even the female sex of the deer would be reaffirmed in imagery found so often on the shaman's costume, or in the terms of endearment by which the shaman would address his drum. It must also be acknowledged, however, that if the shamanic tradition reformulates and reallocates concepts of beginning, mediation, and return that were earlier attached to all people, it is also true that the shamanic tradition represents what we might call a specifically Siberian solution to the sexual and social tensions apparent in late Scytho-Siberian art. Within that solution, tension and its resolution were negotiated through zoomorphic imagery and through the consistent
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



ABSENCE AND PRESENCE 245
reaffirmation of animal doubles and an animal source. In contrast, one may point to the Near East, where realism and anthropomorphism gave the female element the form of a goddess. Among the Scytho-Siberians, when she was translated into a female goddess she became one of many, all displaced from the seat of power by male gods and by male-centered values. Within Siberia, it was the Siberian solution to the allocation of power which survived: the Animal Mother was reformulated in terms of cultic spirits-of the fire, the sun or moon, the hunt. In the shamanic rite she retained a powerful position; but in that tradition, without the shaman as mediator there is effectively no animal mother, no tree of life, and no access to the realm of the spirits.
Considerable distance separates the deer images at Yalbak-Tash and the Mongolian deer stones from which they emerged. Even greater space and time divides these remnants of Bronze and early Iron Age Siberian cultures and the plaques from Chertomlyk and Karagodeuashkh, the crowns from Tillya-Tepe and Novocherkassk. Joining these objects and images is a whole chain of finds, stretching across the Eurasian Steppe and tracing the gradual spread of an early nomadic culture out of the forest-steppe and steppelands of Mongolia and South Siberia, across Kazakhstan and western Siberia, down into the steppe and mountainous regions north and east of the Black Sea. Within that spatial and temporal transition is written the history of the emergence and disappearance of the Scytho-Siberians' culture. We know the materials of that culture through the structures and objects recovered by archaeology. They focus our view on the people themselves, on the trappings in which were embedded their understanding of the meaning of death within some larger cosmic process. Within those trappings, the image which returns again and again is that of the deer: alone in magnificent isolation; caught in the talons of a bird; crushed by the weight of a cat; transformed into some kind of composite beast, part-deer, part-bird, part-horse. There are no texts, no words to explain what we see. Turning to a mythic tradition which emerged and developed far away from the origins of the nomadic world, scholarship has attempted to flesh out the unknown by recourse to the known, even when the only basis for doing so is simple analogy. The words of Herodotus have dominated the translation of Scytho-Siberian antiquities, despite the patently Greek orientation of Herodotus' account and even though Herodotus almost certainly depended on the uncertain testimony of Greek travelers and of Greek inhabitants of the Black Sea colonies. Given the fact that evidence from
Esther Jacobson - 978-90-04-37878-0 Downloaded from Brill.com02/09/2020 08:01:21PM via University of Toronto



246 ABSENCE AND PRESENCE
outside the Scytho-Siberian world and particularly distant from that of the Early Nomads has been seized upon and used to interpret the objects and rituals of the nomads, it is no wonder that the result is such a pastiche of bits and pieces: of solarized warriors, heroized ancestors, winged deer, and sun horses. No wonder that there can be no agreement on the origins of the so-called "animal style": depending on what the images "mean," their origins must vary from theory to theory. The purpose of this study has been to locate the meaning of the deer image in ancient South Siberia and to decipher its symbolic systems. In searching for the symbolic load of that one image and its permutations, we have found that the transformation of motifs and styles within the art of the Early Nomads and of their larger culture, the Scytho-Siberian, was coherent and meaningful. There was signification in the slow elaboration of animal images, from archaic and stylized to realistic and Hellenized. Meaning is revealed, also, in the curious emergence of an anthropomorphic tendency, first suggested in the deer stones of the late Bronze Age, and triumphant, finally, in the late precious-metal work of the Scythians and Yuezhi. Rather than finding clarification outside that world, however, one must look within it and back in time. In the centuries of overlapping South Siberian cultures, the mythic traditions and their variations which shaped the roots of Early Nomadic culture were developed. That culture and its iconography were the results of the interweaving and mixing of traditions reaching up into the Siberian forest land and down to the borders of ancient Iran. Only by taking such a broad view is it possible to reconcile elk and anthropomorphic mask, deer, bovine, deer stone, deer and predator, and deer and woman. Ultimately, it is the deer of the Early Nomads which is the key to the anthropomorphic figures of late Scytho-Siberian art, just as it is the key to deciphering the deer stones of the late Bronze Age. Icon and symbol, absence and presence, this animal is laden with values forged in the South Siberian Neolithic and reshaped in the Bronze Age. Whether ultimately referring to elk, reindeer, or maral, even when challenged by the image of the bovine, the image of the deer never lost its preeminent value. One senses in the history of its image a drama of epic proportions, but one long drawn out and intensely complicated by the intertwining of cultures. For hundreds of years the South Siberian deer was the center of a fluid symbolic system which traced in its shifts and fluctuations the passage of time and the transformation of culture. Within the art of the Early Nomads, the deer's image represents the final statement of an ecology of belief.
